Advance Praise for The Dao of Complexity





“In the West we are shifting from seeing the world as ordered, inherently perfect, me-chanical, and in equilibrium, to seeing it as alive, messy, organic, and ever in process. Jean Boulton captures this shift in thinking beautifully, with insight and precision.”

—W. Brian Arthur, author of Complexity and the Economy

“This is my book of the year. Some books fill your head, some your heart, some educate, some set you alight; Jean’s book does all of these. Her sparkling irrepressible scholar-ship takes us on a journey from the profound to the practical and incites us to cultivate ourselves and face the future. Essential fare for any progressive business school, and I’d also recommend it as a refreshing holiday brain reboot.”

— Jon Foster-Pedley, Dean and Director,

Henley Business School, Africa

“Wonderfully wide-ranging and thoughtful, Jean Boulton doesn’t explain complexity, she inhabits it. Through musings, stories, and a breath-taking range of ideas, Boulton is an inspiring companion who teases out the connections to Daoist cosmology with deceptive ease. An essential read for anyone wanting to make a difference.»

— Margaret Gearty, Associate Professor,

Hult International Business School (Ashridge)

“Drawing together her deep understanding of the complexity sciences, and a voracious appetite for ideas from other disciplines, Jean Boulton writes with wit and wisdom to argue that the contemporary dilemmas we face on our planet today demand new, processual ways of thinking. Wide-ranging and compelling, this is an absorbing and fascinating read.”

— Chris Mowles, Professor of Complexity and Management,

Hertfordshire Business School

Jean has gathered a lifetime of thought and reflection and draws on sources that are eclectic yet pertinent and lead to practical insights that anybody can appreciate. Knowing Jean, I see her behind every word and her authenticity is emboldening. A must for academics, practitioners and leaders looking for fresh perspectives and humane answers to the critical challenges of today’s world.”

—Jonathan Davies, Head of International School, Mumbai

“Dialogues between so-called natural scientists, social scientists and practitioners matter more than ever. But they often start badly. Sharing our struggles with complexity can help, and those willing to reflect on their most basic assumptions will gain much from Jean Boulton’s account of how great thinking about complexity converges.”

— James Copestake, Professor of International Development,

University of Bath

“The very accessible tone in which Jean writes feels like a personal conversation, making challenging topics like process complexity, Daoism, and quantum physics remarkably easy to connect with. Jean invites us into a great conversation, providing profound insights and practical strategies, of benefit to anyone seeking to understand and address the complexities of our ever-changing world.”

— Rika Preiser, UNESCO Chair in Complexity and

— Andrey Pavlov, Professor of Strategy and Performance,

Dedication




I would like to dedicate this book to the memory of Peter Allen, a wonderful

engaging warm human being, a teller of stories, a jazz pianist – as well as a leading

thinker and founding pioneer of complexity science.

And to my parents, who would, every so often, ask me what complexity meant and

then regard me with marvel.





Introduction




What is this book about?



Global heating, rising inequality, diminishing resources, geo-political unrest – just a few of the issues causing turbulence in today’s world. We are living in times that are complex and fast-changing. If we can get a handle on the dynamics of this complex global landscape, might we be better equipped to act more skilfully?

The Dao of Complexity starts with an intention to make sense of the nature of complex-ity, a complexity that shows up at all levels: the global, the organisational and the per-sonal. The thesis is that we have adopted the wrong science to guide us in how to live and work in a way that values the sustainability of the future. I hope to convince you that viewing the world as complex will provide good guidance for how to live well, har-moniously and with resilience. The inquiry begins with the physics of open systems, en-gages with other diverse bodies of knowledge, and leads to the articulation of ‘process complexity’ – a framing of the world as processual, contextual and emergent.

The second half of the book then addresses, in a variety of ways, what a complexity worldview suggests for ‘making waves’ – for making a difference in a world of increas-ing connectivity, polarisation and fragility. What does this mean for leadership, change, governance and the way we conduct our personal lives? I chose the phrase making waves as it suggests that what emerges from our actions may not entirely be what we intended. We can make a difference, but we cannot march inexorably towards a cho-sen endpoint.



A chance encounter with the Dao



The seeds for this book were sown one day back in 2004. I was part of Cranfield Uni-versity’s Complex Systems Management Centre under the leadership of Peter Allen. Peter’s background was in theoretical physics, and he had worked for many years with Ilya Prigogine.1 Peter came into the office this particular day clutching a copy of the Dao de Jing, an ancient Chinese philosophical text. ‘Look,’ he said, ‘it reads just like a complexity theory textbook.’ I was intrigued.

The question raised for me that afternoon was this: How is it that the cosmology or worldview emerging from complexity theory – with its roots in the science of open systems – is more or less identical to an experientially informed perspective developed in the Far East in the fourth century B.C.E? It struck me that if inquiries flowing from such unbelievably different starting points arrive at very similar destinations, then maybe we are homing in on something really important. xvi Introduction



After devouring that particular version of the Dao de Jing,2 a translation and com-mentary by Ames and Hall, I started to notice similar resonances with other bodies of knowledge – neuroscience, theories of personal development, early modern pro-cess philosophies. When I came across Carlo Rovelli’s3 views on quantum physics and quantum gravity, and that work also seemed to be reaching not-dissimilar conclusions, I really started to become excited. Simultaneously, I discovered the work of political commentators who had lived through the rise and fall of various kinds of despotism. They, too, had developed perspectives on this complex world that seemed very reso-nant. Here are key words shared by these very different disciplines:

flow, process, pattern, becoming, difference, context, history, diversity,

evolution, paradox, relationship, indeterminism, emergence



Process complexity



I develop the concept of process complexity4 in Section III. This incorporates the idea of a world always in process of ‘becoming’, but, more than that, forsakes the idea of a world made of things; it considers a world constituted of intersecting processes and patterns. The development of process complexity is a central feature of this book: what does it mean to see oneself as more of a process than a thing? What does that mean for the way our various webs of relationships can become ‘established’ and yet are always subject to change? One expression that encapsulates this perspective is to view the world as:

the emerging, stabilising and dissolving of patterns of relationship



The power of mindset

We can’t solve problems by using the same kind of thinking we used when we created

them. Albert Einstein

I registered the central and primary importance of mindset or worldview when writing Embracing Complexity with Peter Allen and Cliff Bowman.5 Mindset, not method, is the thing.6 Mindsets and beliefs hugely impact what we value, to what we pay attention, how we make sense of what we see and how that, in turn, shapes our choice of methods and mediates our decisions and actions. Without a shift in mindset, we all concluded, nothing really changes. I have become evangelical on this point and this theme imbues the approach I have taken in this book.

I set out to trace the development of the worldview of process complexity and through this I invite you, the reader, to consider your own views about ‘the way the world works’. Is the world, indeed the cosmos, largely static or always changing? Can the future be predicted, or does it emerge? How ‘real’ are things? Are there universal understand-ings to be uncovered or are matters more locally and contextually driven? Complexity theory conveys a view of ‘the way the world works’ that is different from the picture

Introduction xvii



we receive from classical physics. What is that view, with what other bodies of inquiry does it resonate and how might this be of use to those of us, concerned – in both senses of the word – with the social and natural world?

The challenge of changing our worldview is central to our times. Peter Reason7



The still-dominant worldviews from classical physics



Meanwhile, it still seems that the world is primarily in the grip of worldviews driven by two classical – and outdated – physics theories. One, the mechanical worldview, is based on the work of Isaac Newton. Newton essentially was focused on two questions: how the planets travel around the sun, and (essentially) how two billiard balls knock into each other.8 His work was of enormous significance to science; the problem comes when we want it to apply as a ‘theory of everything’. It presents a picture of a world that works like a machine – a world made of bits that operate under the direction of forces, a world that is as predictable as the cogs, wheels and gears of a train engine or a cotton mill. This world is ‘closed’. It is unaffected by anything outside of its own regime. It is reducible to its separate parts. It is predictable. It offers the promise of certainty and control. It is a very tempting metaphor to adopt when we are wanting to organise and manage the human world.

The other physics theory that has gained salience as a worldview focused on the be-haviour of gases and liquids. This theory, from equilibrium thermodynamics,9 also ap-plies to situations closed to outside influences. It promises that things will reach equi-librium and not deviate very far from there.

These classical physics theories, both applicable to closed systems, established the ground in physics for future developments, such as relativity and quantum mechan-ics. They were a huge step forwards in the field of science. The problem arises when we wish to assert that these theories apply to the human world. One particular differ-ence, as I will explore in Section I, is that situations in the human and natural world, and indeed in the galaxies of the heavens, are almost always open to their wider con-texts. They can almost never be regarded as isolated and unaffected by their sur-roundings.

However, starting with the French Enlightenment, those concerned with gover-nance and organisation adopted the mechanical worldview and focused on man-aging through plans, hierarchies, measures and controls. Economists preferred the equilibrium worldview and felt assured that if you minimise control, leave things alone and adopt a laissez-faire approach, then economies will naturally tend towards balance. As one colleague10 pointed out, these are orthodox views, still commonly held. They both in their different ways, simplify things, offer the power of mathe-matics to understand what is going on, and appeal to reason. They make us feel that our human world can be understood through rational means. But, as he said, they xviii Introduction



have become more than merely orthodox; they are hegemonic. They offer dominance and power to those who are in control of a hierarchy or to those who benefit from laissez-faire economics. And those who benefit have a vested interest in leaving things be.

But are these theories really applicable to the dynamics of the social and natural world? Clearly not, when we recognise that situations open to their wider context do not necessarily find balance or reach equilibrium, nor do they function predictably like machines. This was the key breakthrough of Prigogine and others: the word for which, in effect, Prigogine received the Nobel Prize, was open.11 For open systems, we have to dig deeper to explore if there are any general principles that would help in our under-standing. I am hoping that this book plays a part in rattling the iron cage of the machine metaphor, a cage in which our imaginations seem to be imprisoned, held in place by the interests of those who hold its key.



Ontological resonance



But there is a conundrum here. I would be contradicting myself if I were merely to assert that this science of open systems necessarily applies to the social and natural world. I think there are good grounds for this assertion in that I struggle to think of any examples of human or natural situations which, as with the situations of interest to Prigogine and others, are not messy, interconnected and open to their wider surroundings. But nevertheless, I don’t wish merely to assert the validity of transferring complexity ideas from the physical to the natural realm and hence require you, the reader, to accept my word for it and take a leap of faith. The approach I have taken to support this position has been to seek ‘ontological resonance’ with other bodies of knowledge. This began with Peter Allen’s recognition of the resonance between Daoism and complexity theory. But I then found equally strong resonances with other schools of thought – from neuroscience to politics to Pragmatism. I want to share with you my perspective on some of these other bodies of knowledge – ones that are not based on science but seem to reach similar conclusions – so that, armed with this material, you can take your own view.

In addition, I notice that almost all of these bodies of knowledge that resonate with process complexity are experimentally or experientially driven. They are not just ab-stract conceptions or idealisations, but derive understanding from the bottom up, a naturalistic approach to meaning making. Prigogine, too, started with experimental ev-idence. He came across the work of two Russian scientists12 who had found swirls and patterns appearing in chemical systems. This ‘order’ seemed to appear out of nowhere, it seemed counter to expectations and was indeed discounted at the time as probably due to bad experimentation. Prigogine compared this to the work on evolution and found congruence, as I will discuss. The point for me is that his understanding was em-pirically based.

Introduction xix



I am arguing that evolutionary perspectives, the science open systems in the physi-cal-chemical world, and Daoist and other processual empirically derived views of the behaviour of human societies, all seem to be pointing in the same direction. They are surfacing a worldview that is almost indistinguishable from that emerging through the science of complexity. This worldview, in a nutshell, says the world is: intrinsically in-terconnected, diverse, organic, shaped by history and context and ripe with potential. It is a world in which messiness is generative, and where the future emerges in ways that are neither random nor predictable.

I think that this is worth exploring.



What this book covers



The book is in two parts: the first part – Making Sense – starts with theory – of science, complexity, Daoism and other process philosophies. I develop a processual view of complexity. I also explore experiential ways to get to know about this contextual, ever-changing complex world.

The second part – Making Waves – focuses more on what this means we should do – at the level of the individual, the organisation and the global.



Making Sense



There are five sections in this ‘first half’: The long walk of science towards ‘freedom’ as it moves from determinism to indeterminism, the development of and meaning of process complexity, a dive into Daoism, an overview of other resonant perspectives, and a perspective on experiential knowing.

I The Long Walk to Freedom

In this first section, I aim to demonstrate that even the physical universe does not merely unfold according to its universal laws. This is due in part to its inherent messiness and variation, in part to the fact that the universe is full of holes and discontinuities, and in part to its paradoxical nature arising at the tiniest scales. The universe emerges and evolves in ways that are dependent on local conditions, shaped by history and are uncer-tain. There are laws but how they link up is not straightforward, and even if it were, the laws are working on clouds of dust and energy that are not and never were uniform. The universe is interdependent yet incoherent, and it is this incoherence that allows novelty to emerge – including life. This intrinsic indeterminism also provides space for ‘mystery’ and has the potential to return us to a natural philosophy that does not split science from the mystical or spiritual. It provides for a new perspective on what to regard as science.

In ‘Metaphysics and chocolate’, I consider some of the terminology that is relevant to discussions of worldview and ontology. Then I head off into science, starting with xx Introduction



Newton and ending with Prigogine, detouring into quantum physics and evolutionary theory along the way.

II Process Complexity

A processual worldview conveys a world that is in flow, is ‘always becoming’. Further-more, it is a world in which ‘things’ are better thought of as patterns or processes. This may sound hard to swallow, but it is not as far out as it might appear at first glance. It is a view embedded in relational quantum mechanics, it underpins the work of other complexity thinkers such as Ralph Stacey,13 Chris Mowles14 and Paul Cilliers,15 and is postulated by other process thinkers in sociology, biology and philosophy. In this sec-tion, I lay out this processual view of complexity – systemic, patterned, paradoxical, particular to context, path-dependent, punctuated and emergent.

III Daoist Cosmology: a Better Way?

The third section is an exploration of Daoism, building in particular on the translation and exegesis of Ames and Hall.16 I find their work compelling, scholarly and meticulous. They portray the processual nature of Daoism in language that is both clear and illu-minating and often come to conclusions similar to those reached through the lens of complexity, albeit sometimes using different words and emphasising slightly different aspects.

I have gained a great deal through engaging with this work. Indeed, I don’t think I would have arrived at process complexity by merely steeping myself in complexity thinking. For example, one phrase that encapsulates Daoism and captured my attention is ‘the path is made through walking’. In other words, the future is co-created by everything we, collectively and reflexively, intend, value and put into action.

Wanderer, the road is your footstep, nothing else;

Wanderer, there is no path, you lay down a path in walking.

In walking you lay down a path and when turning around

You see the road you’ll never step on again.

Wanderer, path there is none, only tracks on the ocean foam. Antonio Machado

To give another example: There is emphasis, in Daoism, on the importance of being alert to glimmers of change and how this can help the ‘wise leader’ to seize the mo-ment. I might have reached this notion via complexity thinking, but Daoist texts really bring this alive.

So, I am attracted to Daoism not only because it shares an ontology with complexity but also because it adds perspective and provides suggestion for skilful practice. There is emphasis on how to cultivate our personal capacity to act with the collective future in mind.

Introduction xxi



IV Other Resonant Perspectives

I’ve already considered one processual tradition and that is Daoism. In this section, I briefly explore other processual traditions – process philosophies from Heraclitus to Alfred North Whitehead, process sociology and Pragmatism. I also survey other ap-proaches to complexity – those from more sociological and philosophical traditions, those emerging from biology, and those coming from a more computational founda-tion. I reflect, in all these pieces, on similarities with process complexity, and some dif-ferences too.

V On Experiential Knowing

This fifth section asks: how to explore the complex world? If the world is complex, then ‘knowing’ that world is best approached with methods that reflect that complexity and capture its contextual, path-dependent, patterned nature. As that complex world is not reducible to ‘things connected by forces’, it cannot in general be captured through objective, rational means. To aim to make sense of complex situations requires meth-ods that allow for multiple perspectives and draw on our intuition and instinct as well as our rational faculties. Methods must allow for paradox and must allow for situations to shift over time and for new features to emerge. We also cannot assume we are pas-sive observers with no impact on the situations that we are seeking to explore. Engag-ing holistically and through narrative – not just relying on hard data and analysis – thus become appropriate ‘scientific’ methods when aiming to know the processual complex world. To use academic language, the epistemology has to mirror the ontology. It is not scientific to analyse a situation as if it operates like a machine when it does not. Methods need more subtlety and need to engage with a sometimes poetic and intui-tive sense of what is happening. We are sometimes ‘glimpsing through a glass darkly’ things that are not objectively existent.

This section focuses on experiential knowing. As I discuss in ‘The raw and the cooked’, I am not disputing that there is a place for mapping and modelling, but I feel others can cover this field better than myself. My interest lies in what managers, policymakers, parents and civil society leaders face every day – how to decide what is happening in this situation and what to do about it.



Making Waves



The second half of the book focuses on three different levels – the individual, the organisational and the global. This is not to suggest that these levels are distinct or independent. Equally, as I explore, insights from one level are often relevant in other contexts and at other levels. In these sections, I also consider how some of the bodies of knowledge I draw on resonate with complexity.

xxii Introduction



VI Becoming Ourselves

In Becoming Ourselves, I take a walk around the literature on brain science, consider the implications of left and right hemispherical difference, look at approaches to work-ing with trauma and discuss Gestalt psychology. I end with a memoir17 that explores how embracing personal uniqueness and accepting the ‘life you have’ rather than ‘the life you want’ provides an interesting perspective on the path-dependent, context- specific nature of the complex world.

What I discovered is that the understanding of the brain and the mind-body and the ways we impact and change each other seems extraordinarily consistent with a com-plexity worldview. This is exciting for two reasons:

First, this resonance gives power and substance to the complexity perspective. Views from brain science seem to accord with views from Daoism, and also the work on the physics of open systems, the biology of cells and the ecology of forests. Maybe we are uncovering something about the nature of things that has depth, salience and sub-stance?

Secondly, I find that there are methods embedded in this broad work on the develop-ment of the individual that have wider applicability and can be built on. One example is the way many of these psychological theories emphasise the need to work slowly and consciously with a moment-by-moment stepping through current experience. This method can be adapted to working with organisational change. It also provides a way to unravel any locked-in patterns that have developed due to what has happened to us along the way – the path dependency of our lives.

VII Perspectives on Organisational Practice

Here I include many short perspectives and reflections that pertain to issues of con-cern for managers and policy makers. I include stories and examples: the sagacity of leadership, the structuring of education, the dangers of over-reliance on rules, and the importance of engendering trust. I explore the ramifications of hope, consider the role of ‘agonistic dissent’ in decision-making, promote the practice of focusing on outliers not norms, and explore how to work with paradox without ‘going beige’.

VIII On the Global

In this last section, I start by emphasising the way that we cannot separate the socio-economic from the environmental. I focus the lens of complexity on the field of economics. I tackle the big questions of inequality and limits to growth. I discuss the power of beliefs to shape our economic and political systems and describe how hard it is to come to terms with the potential for runaway change, and the potential for collapse.

In ‘On the political’, I find a fascinating ontological resonance between complexity and the work of various political writers – academics and journalists and those who have lived through difficult regimes. These authors have reflected on what creates power

Introduction xxiii



inequalities, how to arrest their concentration before it becomes too late and how, historically, change has happened. These stories align with complexity concepts – the path dependency of society and how power can become unassailably locked-in to the hands of the few. I find great inspiration in their insights.

Finally, I consider the developing polycrisis of our times and draw together some con-clusions as to what ‘embracing complexity’ suggests we should be doing.



How to read this book



In order to try to bring the contextual uniqueness of complexity to the fore, this book is written not in chapters, but in the form of short essays, which I refer to as ‘pieces’. I want to bring alive the concepts embedded in complexity and offer perspectives that might engage your imagination, evoke your determination, and cause you to question your beliefs and motivations. I don’t just want to explain concepts or provide methods. These pieces aim to be dialogic. You are invited to disagree, consider what is missing, maybe become energised, annoyed, amused or thoughtful, touched or piqued. I want to say to you – look, the world really is complex! How does that show itself in different contexts? What does it mean to you to say the world is complex, what do you think we should do differently? What should you do?

For this reason, I did not set out to write a primer or a textbook nor aim to be com-prehensive.18 I have wanted to allow myself to be inspired by chance encounters, be energised by what I read or by what comes up in conversation. I have wanted to write in a way that offers up a situational, relational take on complexity and brings it alive. My fear has been, if I were to overly sanitise this work, would I leave out the very nu-ances that matter? You will thus find examples from my own experience showing up in this writing. And you will find that many of the examples are UK-based, as that is where I live.

It also occurred to me that the Dao de Jing, the Daoist book that has entranced me for decades, is also written in this way – short, paradoxical, overlapping, often ambiguous chapters – poetic, mysterious, visceral, allegorical – and sometimes clear, angry, chal-lenging. Its chapters invite you to wrestle with them, respond to the feelings evoked, circle around, gain or lose clarity and let meaning and any implications for practice sur-face. I have gone back to these chapters in the Dao de Jing many times, over decades, and I always see new things and gain some deeper realisation. My writing is not so po-etic, and, I hope, less ambiguous – but it is from the Dao that I draw my inspiration for its form.

I am invoking in this approach the idea of the ‘slow read’ movement.19 These pieces are designed to be approached slowly, picked up and put down, digested and considered. Methods, principles that shape action, and suggestions for practice are there – but emerge in many cases through the medium of stories. There are no checklists and few summaries.

xxiv Introduction



I hope that engaging with these vignettes and examples engenders in you a shift in per-spective, an evaluation and potential reframing of your worldview. I hope they subtly change your mind. Cliff Bowman once said to me, somewhat inelegantly, that complex-ity is like a virus – once you are infected, you can’t get rid of it. There is no going back; you see things differently. There are few explicitly complex methods, but with com-plexity in mind, we may use common tools and methods in a different way and con-clude that some may be more useful than others. But there are methods: how do you find the simplicity on the other side of complexity? How do you get a sense of the whole and spot nascent signs of change? How do you attune to the flow and yet act to make a difference? The methods, though, tend to be contingent and suggestive, and each step towards clarity can tend to offer up more questions. Indeed, finding better questions is arguably an important method.

The intellect has little to do on the road to discovery. There comes a leap in conscious-

ness, an intuition or what you will, the solution comes to you, and you don’t know how

or why … I stop thinking, swim in silence, and the truth comes to me. Einstein

So, with the second half of the book in particular, there is an invitation to dip in, to reflect, to let the ideas seep in, irritate or inspire; there is no need to read it in order and no need to read it all.

I recommend a slightly different approach to the ‘first half’. Section I, The Long Walk to Freedom, develops the shift in science from determinism to indeterminism in a fairly systematic way. You may choose to read this section later, as it is not an easy read, but it probably works best to read it through starting at the beginning. I did in the end banish two particularly dense pieces – on indeterminism and quantum physics – to the Appendix.

Regarding Process Complexity, I would suggest you read the first four pieces before heading off more randomly. The sections on Daoism, Other Resonant Perspectives and Experiential Knowing don’t need such a systematic approach.

Before you dive into each section, it would also be beneficial to read their introduc-tions to in order to get a flavour of what they cover.

In general, though, I invite you to browse, read that which attracts you, circle in and around. Attend to your emotional reactions, notice what memories are provoked, read and absorb through your own lens and experience. Converse with it through other texts and ideas. Let yourself be critical and reflective and yet sometimes let the words permeate, not fully digested. Notice your feelings and reactions but don’t always try to make sense of them. You may sometimes weave insights and at other times just feel uncomfortable or heartily disagree. Working with the pieces in such a manner also re-flects the indeterminate paradoxical quality of life that process complexity, as well as Daoism, can show us. Perhaps we can creep up on complexity, dialogue with it, tus-sle with it as it weaves between our inner and outer worlds. Maybe we will discover a sense of its import and value as we go.

Introduction xxv



One further thought: In the section on experiential knowing, I make the point that knowing and meaning making are often best approached through working dia-logically with multiple perspectives. Suggest The Dao of Complexity to your study group, as readers may pick up on different aspects and interpret what they read in different ways. Group discussion can both surface what I was trying to say and what I might have said instead!

Housekeeping: I employ a number of concepts – for example, emergence, pattern, self-organisation, lock-in – and they occur in many places throughout the text. I have included a Glossary of terms at the end of the book. I have also indicated, in the text, where I have covered an idea from a different angle in another part of the book. Such cross-references are indicated by including, in square brackets, the number of the relevant piece.



Why did I write this book?



I am a theoretical physicist by background. My field of study was quantum physics. I then moved through various random steps, and through further academic study, into applied physics, strategy and organisational consultancy, lecturing in business schools and hands-on management until I finally emerged in the social sciences. When it comes to complexity theory, I feel reasonably equipped to bridge the gap between hard and soft sciences and between theory and practice.

My motivation comes from my anxiety about the state of the world. I feel enormously concerned about the insufficient focus on global heating, rising inequality and increase in global conflict. We seem to be ‘fiddling while Rome burns’. Indeed, we seem to be losing our humanity. I notice in business and politics a move away from the values of community, a lack of importance placed on integrity and truth, an unassailable focus on profit and on the short term, and a seeming unconcern about the precariousness of the future. In my lifetime, I have never been so concerned. This book, then, is my form of activism. It is written in the hope that it changes minds about how to act, manage, and consider the future, how to value others, value nature and see that we are all in this together. But is written in an open way. I am not insisting you share my values; I am inviting you to consider that our values drive our actions, and through our actions, we co-create the future.



It takes a village to raise a book



This book might have been conceived by me, but it has developed through all the con-versations I have had along the way. It has been nudged, challenged and re-formed through discourse; it has matured through the engagement with the ‘village’. This has sometimes led me into despondency as I peered through the holes in my thinking xxvi Introduction



and the gaps in my knowledge. But I cheered up when one colleague, Sharon Varney, pointed out that the universe is full of holes and that is what makes it work. I am not presenting you with a tidied-up coherent ‘whole’; holes and gaps invite you in.

There is a crack in everything, that is where the light gets in. Leonard Cohen

These dialogues with colleagues have been very fruitful and absolutely central to what this book has become. There are very many people to whom I owe heartfelt thanks – for robust conversations, suggestions, challenges and sometimes just cups of tea and encouragement.

I particularly want to mention ….

Zoe Arden, David Bent, Charles Boulton, Cliff Bowman, Mike Collins, Joanna Crowson, Greg Fisher, Margaret Gearty, Esther Maughan-McLachlan, Uschi May, Chris Mowles, Peter Reason, Rika Preiser, Fern Smith, Sharon Varney and Sam Woolley.

In 2015, Melissa Hawkins, Chris James and I set up a Complexity Interest Group at the University of Bath. This has gradually morphed into an online bi-monthly meeting with participants from across the globe. I would like to thank all the members of that group for sharing experiences from so many different spheres, and for all the interesting and challenging debates.

I started working on this book in 2019 during my research fellowship with the Stellen-bosch Institute of Advanced Study. I am very grateful for that time – the joy of engag-ing with scholars from many disciplines and geographies, and the space to research, reflect and write.

Finally, I would like to express my appreciation to Steve Hardman and Jaya Dalal of De Gruyter who have been an absolute pleasure to work with.



The artwork



I want to give particular mention to the artwork in the book. Putting together these twenty-five illustrations has been a journey in itself. You will see that the sketches and paintings evoke aspects of a processual worldview – but indirectly, aiming to engender a visceral rather than an intellectual reaction. There are flowing rivers, and precarious mountain ridges, weeds growing out of cracks in abandoned factories, piles of shoes and paths worn by use. They convey, variously, the sense of life’s journey – in the feet of an old woman, the way something new emerges in the most derelict of situations, the flow and emotional impact of history and change.

In particular, I have worked closely with Simon Blakeman, and we evolved a way of working through finding photographs that captured the essence of what I was trying to convey, which he used as a base. It was a form of dialogue and cooperative endeavour that was complex in itself. He needed to be inspired by my words and intentions but

Introduction xxvii



also be free to find his own artistic expression. I needed to find ways to express what I was seeing in the photos. Sometimes I just found art from other sources that fitted the ‘feeling sense’ of what I was after. I include pieces from a Ukrainian artist and one from a Palestinian artist who, as I write, is living with his family in a tent in Gaza. I list the artwork and the artists at the end of the book.



Ode ’ere



The Yorkshire folks were in a dither

Because of Heraclitus’ river

They knew they could not cross it twice

But were not clear – would once suffice?



And then at last a quite astute ’un

Remembered dear old Isaac Newton

Who was a marvel in his season.

And never was he contradicted,

He said that all could be predicted

And we could all rely on Reason.



And so, they dropped all their ferment

And settled on Enlightenment.

And all would have continued charmin’

Apart from that annoying Darwin

And his ideas of Evolution –

For which, it seems there’s no solution.



This led to quite a sense of urgenc-

Y – and focus on Emergence;

The physicists tried to ignore it

Those rich economists deplored it,

And Management was not too keen …



But then into this sorry scene

Came Russian Belgian Prigogine

Who said ‘Don’t fear, science isn’t broken

‘Cos things thought closed are in truth open;

There is a theory quite supreme

Which fits experience like a dream.’



The Yorkshire folk who’d felt at sea

Poured yet another cup of tea

And marvelled at Complexity.



—Jean Boulton, 2009





Section I




The Long Walk to Freedom:

the Developing Story of the Scientific Worldview





1


Introducing the long walk to freedom1



Why physics, when applied to the actual messiness of the universe, paints a much more

complex picture than we might expect. And why this is important, given the tendency to

adopt physics theories to explain and manage the social and natural world.



In this section, I start with Isaac Newton’s theories of gravity, out of which emerged the mechanical worldview. This presents a picture of a universe obeying over-

arching universal laws and unfolding to plan. I explore the reasons why this scientific worldview has been superseded. I explain how Charles Darwin’s theories of evolution sparked interest amongst physicists as well as philosophers and how this led to the view of an evolving, contextual, indeterminate universe – a universe of richness, free-dom and novelty.

Before I embark on this journey, I focus in the first piece, ‘Metaphysics and chocolate’ [2], on a discussion of the meaning and importance of words like metaphysics and worldview.



Worldviews



Much of this book is about the implications of the world being complex. It explores the way we understand ourselves as individuals, the way we view organisational life, and engage with big global issues such as economics, politics and climate change. It considers how we gain knowledge and how that shapes our actions, both individually and as part of institutions. But what drives all of this?

The enduring theme to which I constantly return is that it is our worldviews that are fundamental to shaping how we think, what we deem as important, and what we then do. Without addressing our implicit assumptions and beliefs, nothing really changes. We merely try and do better at what we were already doing. So, one of the purposes of this particular section is to explore, unpick and challenge scientific assumptions about ‘the way things work’.

The chance of surviving with dignity on this planet hinges on the acquisition of a new

mind. F. J. Varela2



I think the language that pertains to this broad notion of the way things work is quite tricky to navigate, and in ‘Metaphysics and chocolate’ [2], I describe the concepts that circle around all of this – ontology, metaphysics, worldview, cosmology – and try to get to the nub of it all. I am not introducing a complexity perspective at this stage; I am seeking to lay the ground as to what these philosophical concepts are concerned with, 4 Section I The Long Walk to Freedom: the Developing Story of the Scientific Worldview



and why asking ourselves about our own often opaque and taken-for-granted assump-tions and beliefs is so important.



Science and the nature of the universe



The second focus of this section is to endeavour to show that the physical sciences, despite being made up of lots of laws, do not actually infer that the universe works like a predictable machine. The physical sciences are not painting a picture of a universe proceeding in an orderly fashion – and this is for all sorts of reasons that I will explore. I believe it is really important to debunk the mechanical worldview encapsulated by: things work to plan, like a machine, and science supports this.



Walras and equilibrium physics

Léon Walras, in 1874, expressed the view, that “this pure theory of economics is a

science which resembles the physico-mathematical sciences in every respect.”3 This

is a very strong assertion and Edward Fulbrook had this to say:

What matters to … Walras is not the methodological fit but rather the method itself. …Instead of being led by ontological inquiry [he] defines a priori the ontol-ogy to fit the method. Nothing could have been more against the procedures and mindset that have dominated the natural sciences from Copernicus on. … For a scientist … the mathematics is supposed to illuminate empirical reality, rather than

the other way around.4

In other words, Walras merely asserted, with a paucity of exploration or evidence,

that the mathematics of classical physics (in this case that pertaining to equilibrium

thermodynamics) would be a good fit for economics and hence that would make

economics ‘scientific’. Joseph Stiglitz calls this “a triumph of ideology over science”.5

These days it would be called scientism.6



Science tends to be regarded as the gold standard of thinking and concepts, and models from physics in particular are often adopted by those seeking to understand the social and natural world. But if these models and worldviews are inappropriate to the situa-tion, we run the risk of choosing actions and policies that do not give us what we want. They might lead to strategies that are ineffective or, worse, are actually harmful for the future – think climate change, increasing inequality, political polarisation, and loss of resilience. Underneath policy and strategy, a ‘classical’ scientific worldview often lurks.

Most of the pieces in this section address physics, arguably the most fundamental of sciences. People in all sorts of ways love to quote physics and use it as a justification for all manner of schools of thought. Social scientists, for example, are wont to look to quantum physics to re-imagine new approaches and this can be a great thing provided there is a differentiation between metaphor and actuality. And the mainstream of eco-nomics and management theory is still based on classical physics; the economists still

1 Introducing the long walk to freedom 5



like to build on the idea of balance and equilibrium, and the management people con-tinue to love a good machine metaphor.

So, it feels to me that because ‘science says this’ is such a powerful argument, it is im-portant to try and unpick as much as possible what science does say. This is the key motivation for writing this section, which takes the long walk from the ticking clock [3], iron block [5] view of Newtonian physics, to the freedom [8] entailed in the science of open systems. The science of open systems is what led to complexity science.

This temptation of certainty is the solidification of self against other, of delimitation

of national boundaries in opposition to other human societies, in brief, the source of

suffering. My hope is that if modern science can rediscover in its own way this profound

truth, then the ears of our contemporaries will be more open and receptive because of

the authority that science carries in our Western world. Varela7

Has this not been tackled elsewhere? My feeling is that popular science texts are often more focused on explaining to non-scientists the sexy bits of pure physics rather than explaining the way many different aspects of physics weave together when acting on the messy non-uniform clouds of dust and energy and ‘stuff’.8 And yet it is this ‘woven- together’ perspective that is most likely to have relevance for our understanding of the messy open complex social and natural world ‘down here’.

Some scientists are more focused on the wider complex picture and its metaphysical meanings. My heroes are Roger Penrose, Carlo Rovelli, Martin Rees, Lee Smolin, Stuart Kauffman, Chris Clarke and Ilya Prigogine. Interestingly and perhaps not surprisingly, many of them, as did Einstein, also ponder the human condition, comment on social and world events, flirt with things spiritual, and see themselves as global citizens with a responsibility to play their part. They are aware of the power of science to shape narratives and for science potentially to be misleading when taken outside of its remit.

A human being experiences himself, his thoughts and feelings as something separate

from the rest, a kind of optical delusion of consciousness. This delusion is a kind of

prison for us, restricting us to our personal desires and to affection for a few persons

nearest to us. Our task must be to free ourselves from this prison by widening our

circle of compassion to embrace all living creature and the whole of nature in its

beauty. Einstein



So, my endeavour in this section is to make plain the various and varying ontological assumptions embedded, over its history, within the physical sciences, and discuss their limitations exposed by later theories and through experimental evidence. In particular, I want to examine the ‘mechanical worldview’ that emerged from Newton’s science, challenge the assumptions on which it is built and expose its weaknesses. This world-view still holds enormous power and sway in the world of policy development and management. But it is not scientific to adopt a view from outdated science. And it is not scientific to apply it to a problem which is outside the remit for which it was developed. 6 Section I The Long Walk to Freedom: the Developing Story of the Scientific Worldview



Furthermore, I believe that if we continue to act as if the world operates like a machine, it will severely impede our ability to find solutions to the developing crises of our time.

I spotted Rovelli had written this, at the end of his Seven Brief Lessons on Physics:

I believe that our species will not last long. … The brutal climate and environmental

changes which we have triggered are unlikely to spare us. For the earth they may turn

out to be a small irrelevant blip, but I do not think that we will outlast them unscathed. …

I fear that soon we shall also have to become the only species that will knowingly watch

the coming of its own collective demise, or at least the demise of its civilisation.9



Rovelli uses strong words. My hope in writing this section is to show that if we start to think differently about ‘the way the world works’, if we challenge our scientific world-views, we might see things differently and do a better job of engendering a sustainable future. Gripping an inappropriate scientific worldview is not the only reason why we continue to behave as if the present will continue indefinitely. But it is an important reason; it is used as a powerful justification, a comfortable sop for us to continue on our fateful path. There is still hope if we are willing to ‘change our minds’.



Content of this section



Below is a list of the pieces in this section, with a brief description of what they are about.



• Metaphysics and chocolate



Exploring the vocabulary that focuses on ‘the way things are’, contrasting this with ‘the way we know about the way things are’ and suggesting why this is important. Our scientific worldviews matter.



• The ticking clock



Explaining why the mechanical worldview, that bastion of science coming from Newton, was built on shaky ground, and relied on God to sort out the difficult bits.



• The indeterminate universe



Summarising two longer pieces, now banished to an Appendix, which list reasons why the universe is not deterministic, but is full of holes, discontinuities, messiness and paradox. How these features offer the potential for an ever-changing, ever-forming, impossible to predict complex connecting cosmos.

1 Introducing the long walk to freedom 7



• Unity and uniqueness

Unpicking our predisposition to adopt over-arching maxims that apply universally. How early scientists believed they were revealing the mind of God and His ability to have an oversight of past, present and future all at the same time. And how this con-nects with seeking absolutes in qualities such as Beauty, Truth, Purpose or Oneness. Exploring the flaws in this reasoning.



• Is there no thing?



Showing how theories of physics gradually do away with ‘things’ altogether, and how this paves the way for conceiving of a world of ‘processes’.



• Darwin and evolution



How Darwin changed the game for science, philosophy, economics and even literature and showed that, without messiness and uncertainty, nothing changes.



• Messiness is next to openness



How Prigogine focused on open systems, connected it to evolutionary theory, got the Nobel prize for so doing and established the foundations of complexity theory.

In summary



This section, mainly on science, is not easy. You may not want to start with this section of the book, and it won’t appeal to everyone. The aim is to ground subsequent discussions on complexity in its historical scientific context and really try to bury the ‘science tells us the future unfolds to plan’ argument. What we believe has such an impact on what we do.

I do suggest, if you read it, that you start at the beginning and read through; the pieces are designed to build on each other. The complex explanations of quantum physics [73] and a detailed view of what creates indeterminism [72] are now safely hidden away in the Appendix but are there if you are interested.

In a nutshell, I am arguing, in this section, that physics does not paint a picture of a world working like a machine. Rather, it offers a picture of a world that depends on context and history, has a level of variation and messiness without which little would happen, has an intrinsic level of uncertainty that cannot be removed – and gives room for mystery, novelty, and emergence. This paves the way nicely for the beginnings of complexity theory, which is where I end this section.





2


Metaphysics and chocolate



How our beliefs about ‘the nature of things’ fundamentally shape how we investigate and

learn, and also impact what actions we take.

The more I learn of physics, the more I am drawn to metaphysics. Einstein

There is no metaphysics on earth like chocolate. Fernando Pessoa



As early as my teens, I mused about the nature of reality, including the question as to whether there is a reality. This was amplified by my study of physics. It hasn’t translated for me into a search for a universal tidy theory that explains everything. Rather, I have been intrigued by how change happens, how the very big relates to the very small, and about the mystery of beginnings (was there a beginning – just one – at a particular time and place – why?) and endings.

These are scientific but also philosophical preoccupations, and I eventually came to understand this as a metaphysical or ontological quest. I became interested in how my view of ‘reality’ affects how I think about life in general and my own life. Am I stepping into a flowing river of life, or am I just an individual with my own particular history and make-up, muddling along? Is the spiritual, whatever that means, separate from the material, whatever that means – or not? How do I make sense of intuitions or synchronicities? Why do I feel so passionately about certain things, so drawn in? Why have certain questions stayed with me all my life?

Have patience with all that is unresolved in your heart and try to love the questions

themselves, like closed rooms, like books written in a foreign tongue. … Live the ques-

tions now. Perhaps you will then gradually, without noticing, one day in the future, live

into the answers. Rainer Maria Rilke10

The terms that span this terrain – ontology, metaphysics, cosmology, worldview – are not, I find, easy to pin down. But I am not too concerned here with crystallising definitions; rather, I want to show that all these concepts share a common interest in asking how the universe and everything in it exists, changes, connects and so on. My focus is on exploring the nature of reality, the nature of being, the way things are – all slightly different expressions but homing in on the same territory. I am trying to explore whether there are fundamental principles that underpin the nature of things at many scales – principles that might hold true for universes, galaxies, societies, ecol-ogies and even brains. Is that a step too far, or is there something to be reached for? What do we find when we explore different philosophical and scientific perspectives, and different types of experiential evidence? Are there common features and if so, how can we express them?

10 Section I The Long Walk to Freedom: the Developing Story of the Scientific Worldview



Definitions

Ontology is concerned with the nature of things, the so-called nature of being.11 Onto-

logy can embrace universal big picture concerns but can also be used to explore more

specific situations.12

The notion of worldview can contain our ideas about the nature of being but also in-

clude our over-arching views and beliefs more generally – on ethics, gender or religion,

for example.

Cosmology has, in fact, related but different meanings in philosophy and in science. In

philosophy, it encompasses the philosophical contemplation of the universe as a totality.

In physics, cosmology is limited to the scientific study of the material universe – its struc-

ture, origins and future development.

So, as is evident, there is some overlap and also certain differences of emphasis when

it comes to defining these terms and grappling with what they are seeking to convey.

These questions about the nature of things are also deemed to be metaphysical. Meta-

physics conveys a more over-arching, a ‘grander’ perspective than ontology. But, as

well as considering the nature of reality, metaphysics also covers more human-centred

concerns such as: what is knowledge, what is consciousness, what is the role of human

perception?



It is interesting to note that metaphysics went out of fashion as our modern world placed such emphasis on evidence and reason, and objective ‘hard science’. It was seen as unfounded speculation. But it is gaining importance once more.13 Rovelli argues that metaphysics often inspires and opens up the vista for scientists to explore.14

The imagination often leads where reason cannot, as reason is often constrained by the

scientific norms of the time. Rovelli15

There is also an argument for seeking to develop metaphysical perspectives through empirical means, bottom-up – a form of naturalistic metaphysics.16 This is an approach with which I would concur; it also underpins Daoist thinking. As I explore in ‘Unity and uniqueness’ [5], Daoists disavow over-arching universal concepts. Their metaphysics is more focused on the contextual and the particular – gleaned through experience rather than through grand analysis and logic. Consider this snippet from the Dao de Jing:

The events of the world arise from the determinate

And the determinate arises from the indeterminate.17

This snippet captures the essence of Daoist cosmology, as I will go on to explore, and reminds me of the idea of Order out of Chaos,18 the title of one of Prigogine’s books.

I really believe this is important stuff. How we think about the nature of reality has such an impact on what we see as salient, how we interpret our experience, and what

2 Metaphysics and chocolate 11



we include in our scientific experiments and explorations. If, for example, we think that the universe is deterministic, that it follows a set path, then why tackle climate change or inequality if they are pre-determined? If we think that the future is entirely determined by fixed laws, then why bother to explore local contexts or histories? If the universe is understandable – at least in principle – by reason alone, then why give any value to intuitions or subjective experience – or even dare to imagine that the inter-actions between aspects of the universe are less than objectively existent and may in actuality be fuzzy and paradoxical?

Getting the right term (ontology or cosmology or whatever) and pinning down its meaning is not crucial. These are ideas to wrestle with and let any meanings seep in. Every time I go around this loop, I gain a richer understanding of the territory these concepts are staking out. It is taking seriously the territory that is important.



Ontology, epistemology and methodology



Thus far, I have primarily focused on the language and concepts that dance around the ontological/metaphysical question as to ‘how things are’. There is a related, epistemo-logical question, which is to ask: ‘how do we get to know about how things are’?

Our ontological stance – how we view the nature of things, how we view the way things stabilise or change – needs to be linked to our epistemological stance – what knowledge we deem important, and how best to gain access to this knowledge.

To give an example: If we think that the social world behaves rather like a machine and can be considered stable and connected by well-defined forces, then it makes sense to investigate that world through measuring the strength of those forces and measuring the connection between outputs and inputs. However, if we think the social world is more organic – more like a pond or a wood than a machine – then we may feel we need to explore ‘webs of interdependence’ and be more cognisant of local conditions, rather than look for simple interactions between unchanging things.

The link between how we theorise about the nature of the world (our ontology) and how we believe we can know about it (our epistemology) leads to the methodological question as to how we then choose to investigate it, and how we then choose to act. These need to be self-consistent; otherwise, we risk missing the very information that is key to furthering our understanding.

So, if the world is complex, then choosing methods of investigation that ignore or slide over that complexity is unlikely to surface the very features that may be key to under-standing what is happening. This is a point I return to many times [27] [41] [56].

This then sets the stage, for exploring, in what follows, the ontology of complexity and its implications. Viewing the world as complex, uncertain and emergent (a complexity ontology), rather than objective, predictable and controllable (a mechanistic onto logy), has a significant impact on how we choose ‘ways of knowing’ and how we subsequently work out what to do and how to do it.

12 Section I The Long Walk to Freedom: the Developing Story of the Scientific Worldview



Furthermore, our worldviews and ontological positions bias what we select as impor-tant to explore, what we notice, how we interpret what we notice, and what consequent actions we take [65].



What about the human?



I want to end on an important point, which concerns the role of the human in all this. Clearly, the universe as a whole got on pretty well for billions of years developing its trillions of galaxies, its planets, clouds and fields of energy, without human conscious-ness and in the absence of humans. Our human observing and engaging cannot be vital, as the universe, planet formation and emerging life were doing quite well without us.

I am thus wary of explanations that put too much emphasis on the role of the human observer [73].

To think that human beings, their minds, their tools or the numbers they use play any

special role in the [ontological] grammar of nature is nonsense. Rovelli19



The relationship between humans and everything else is just one relationship that is occurring in the universe. Indeed, the relational view of quantum physics suggests that ‘reality’ is ‘subjective to itself’ [73] and that properties are dependent on ‘what is interacting with what’ in the broadest sense. In other words, this quantum fluctuation is impacted by the interaction with that gravitational fluctuation and vice versa. One could say they ‘observe’ each other. This is an important notion to reflect on, as it pulls us away from seeing ourselves as somehow central. I further explore this topic – of relational quantum physics and ‘ontological subjectivity’ – in ‘Smoke and mirrors’ [73]. And I recommend Helgoland by Rovelli on this topic.20

Within the immense ocean of galaxies and stars we are in a remote corner … we are

merely a flourish among many such flourishes. Rovelli21



Secondly, for more prosaic reasons, it is unlikely that human thinking or even human experiencing could ever reveal all aspects of the universe. There are, no doubt, factors existent in the universe that are intrinsic to its functioning, but yet are beyond both our cognition and our physical apparatus to sense them. I cannot know these factors, but I can allow, in my musings, for their existence. And maybe they show themselves darkly, tangentially, in glimpses, in ways not expected. I may notice these glimpses if I am open to the possibility of their existence.

One thing I have learned in a long life; that all our science, measured against reality, is

primitive and childlike. We still do not know one thousandth of one per cent of what

nature has revealed to us. It is entirely possible that behind the perception of our

senses, worlds are hidden of which we are unaware. Albert Einstein

2 Metaphysics and chocolate 13



Since we are not the culmination of intelligence, we’ve got to be mindful that there

could be aspects of reality of which we are unaware, which our brains couldn’t grasp.

Martin Rees22



Having said all this, I do expect that any metaphysics that deals with the universe will still be relevant when humans are in the picture too. I am not separating us humans from everything else. Neither am I excluding our inner worlds. But I am working on the assumption that the nature of reality is not dependent on us, or shaped by us to any greater extent than it is shaped by any other type of interaction.

We are an integral part of the world … we are not external observers. … We are made

up of the same atoms and the same light signals as are exchanged between pine trees

in the forest and stars in the galaxies. Rovelli23

Lee Smolin, more generally, argues for not treating physics and biology as different in kind, because, as he says, living biological systems are made of the same stuff as rocks and clouds. He states: “An approach to physics that does not make the existence of life comprehensible must eventually give way to one that does.”24



In summary



This piece aims to provide a language and understanding concerning key concepts on which the rest of the book builds. There are four points to make by way of summary.

First, as I explained in the Introduction to the book, the starting point for this book is an ontological quest. This is an important point to emphasise, as many approaches to complexity are less focused on teasing out the nature of reality and more interested in methods. As Ron Martin and Peter Sunley point out: “In complexity theory, epistemo-logy has dominated over ontology.”25 So, I hope the discussion here – if you’re not al-ready steeped in these philosophical concepts – has put some ‘flesh on the bones’ of notions of metaphysics, cosmology, ontology and so on, as these terms will come up many times in future sections.

Second – and this is a recurring theme in the book – beliefs matter. What we believe about all sorts of things – including our ontological views of the nature of reality or the nature of change – drives everything that follows. As I argue in ‘It’s only make believe’ [65], without surfacing and exploring our beliefs, nothing substantive really alters.

Thirdly, how we view the nature of things – whether or not we think the world is com-plex and what that means – will drive how we think we can explore that world (our epistemology) and what we do. In Section V On Experiential Knowing in a Complex World and in ‘The raw and the cooked’ [31], I take up this issue of epistemology.

Finally, a good science should apply to humans as part of everything, but a science that relies on human interaction for its framing should be viewed with suspicion.





3


The ticking clock



Why the universe does not work like a machine and why Isaac Newton had not discovered a

‘theory of everything’.



The machine worldview – the idea that “nature works according to mechanical laws” and that “everything in the material world can be explained in terms of the arrange-ment and movement of its parts” – was first proposed by René Descartes and then built on by Newton.26 Science has moved on from this view, but its power in the world of organisations and management is still very evident. In this piece, I want to illustrate its limitations and show, as Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz27 pointed out at the time, that it cannot account for the diverse, rich, evolving structure of the universe.

The mechanical worldview, articulated by Descartes, was brought to life through Newton’s calculations as to how the planets in the solar system move around the sun. Until that point, others had tried to get the maths to work by assuming that the earth was the centre. Indeed Galileo Galilei, in 1633, was condemned for heresy, and put under house arrest until his death in 1642 for daring to suggest that it was the sun around which the planets revolved. It was Newton who showed, in 1686, that the mathematical equations become elegantly simple when the sun is placed in the centre. It was a huge step forwards and indeed loosened the power of the church to dominate philosophical thinking and strangle this new field of science.



The mechanical worldview

The mechanical worldview, applied to the social world, assumes that things interact in

a consistent, predictable fashion, like a machine of interacting parts that behaves like

clockwork. The progress over time of a mechanical system, such as a ticking clock, can

be predicted. Causes and effects derive from simple, linear interactions between things

and can be traced. Outcomes are measurable and can be attributed to inputs. Differ-

ent aspects of the situation – for example, the social and the environmental – can be

handled independently, and projects and organisations can be divided into separate

independent parts. There is no possibility, however, of learning or change, of anything

new entering the system or anything leaving the system. If organisations truly were me-

chanical, there would be no possibility of innovation, learning or adaptation.



Newton’s theories, on which the mechanical worldview is based, are deterministic. That is to say, if we know everything at a particular point, we can know everything that hap-pens before or after. They are also reductive – it is assumed that an interaction between two things is unaffected by anything else, present or past. These are simplifications and later science shows that things are not so uncomplicated. Nevertheless, Newton’s science allowed, to an astonishingly accurate degree, the mapping of the planets and trajectories around the sun. And for many people, ever since, the mechanical 16 Section I The Long Walk to Freedom: the Developing Story of the Scientific Worldview



worldview has epitomised what science is – a search for certainty, for clear causal relationships between elements of the situation, for objectivity, for universal laws and for predictable outcomes.

At the time, it caught the imagination of many – including the natural philosopher and mathematician Emilie du Châtelet.28 She translated the Principia Mathematica, Newton’s great work, from Latin into French. She and her lover Voltaire felt this had great relevance for the social world and it fuelled their vision of being able to design and build a new society that would operate like a well-oiled machine. This idea contributed to the naissance of the French Enlightenment with its focus on reason – the idea that reason, with its logical, objective, analytical standpoint, can uncover everything important and can stand against unprovable doctrines, superstitions or unreliable emotional whims. Newton lived at the beginning of the Modern period – where religion and science, reason and emotion, body and mind were seen as separate things, each with its own sphere.

It has been the rationalist, plausible, and apparently logical argument of the French

tradition, with its flattering assumptions about the unlimited powers of human reason,

which has progressively gained influence. Friedrich Hayek29



This then is the mechanical worldview. It revolutionised science at the time and also underpinned the French Enlightenment. It continues to have a massive impact on methods of management.



So, what is wrong with the mechanical worldview?



Were Newton’s theories as comprehensive as we might imagine? As I will explore, Newton’s theories cannot explain the structure, particularity and the path-dependent irreversibility of the universe. They also rely on God to play a part.



(1) God had to set off the clockwork universe in order for it to behave

like clockwork



What is not immediately obvious is that Newton’s theories have no way of incorporat-ing a beginning, a creation – or indeed an ending. Motion just goes on forever. Secondly, for his theories to apply to the universe as a whole, and hence to form the basis of a cosmology, he needed a frame of reference in which to place the universe – something that oriented the universe in a context wider than it, a sort of notional box in which the universe sat, against which he could define left or right, or up and down. As cosmologist Lee Smolin discusses in The Life of the Cosmos:

The problem of how to make a theory of a whole universe is the problem of how to

construct a theory without making any reference to anything … outside of the system

we are describing. Newton’s physics … relied heavily on the use of a fixed background

and for this reason, could never have stood as a theory of the whole universe.30

3 The ticking clock 17



Smolin continues: “One cannot speak of where the universe is, or when it happened … the only reference points available to describe when or where something happens are other events.”31

These assumptions of a ‘time zero’ and of an absolute spatial framework in which things happen are axiomatic;32 they have to be assumed and cannot be proven. Newton solved this by calling upon God to orient the universe and set it off. In other words, Newton’s classical mechanics, in order to apply it as a universal theory, depended on assertions about what God would do.

When challenged on these axiomatic assumptions at the time by Leibniz, Newton replied that God decided – on when the universe started, on which way up it is. When Leibniz asked how this would change if God had set off the universe half an hour later, or further to the left, Newton merely replied that that was for God to choose.33

The point of sharing this story is to emphasise that what is regarded as the most scienti-fic and rational of sciences, the standard for all other scientific endeavours, is based on non-obvious axiomatic assumptions (attributed by Newton to what God did) without which the theory becomes much more limited and much less of a contender for a universal ‘theory of everything’.

(2) Particularity and patterning



Although Newton’s laws allow, to a good approximation, the mapping of the motions of the planets around the sun, they only explain some of the phenomena of the universe. They cannot explain for example why the solar system is flat, or the particularity of the detail of the universe – the specificity of each galaxy, planet and meteor shower. The universe is not uniform; every piece has its own uniqueness, its own history. Leibniz pointed out that Newton’s theories have nothing to say about this patterning of the universe.34 Leibniz described this as the “order of coexistences” giving rise to the struc-tures of galaxies and planets.

The fact that we live in a universe of structures at so many scales is so commonplace, so

manifest, that it is easy to miss its significance. Smolin35

I hold space to be something merely relative ... an order of coexistences. Leibniz36



(3) Irreversibility and path dependence



Finally, a machine-like universe would not only be entirely predictable but would also be reversible. If things are reversible, you can’t tell the difference between the past and the future. The passage of time has no discernible impact, leaves no trace. This provides one more nail in the coffin of the machine perspective. In practice, things change irreversibly. Things grow, age and die; new things emerge. There is no possibility of smoothly going back, no retracing of steps; things never exactly repeat. Stars are born and eventually use up all their fuel and die. Flora and fauna evolve, many become 18 Section I The Long Walk to Freedom: the Developing Story of the Scientific Worldview



extinct, and others take their place. If we watched a time lapse film of a forest or a drama on the television, it would be entirely obvious if it were played backwards.

As Prigogine said, time’s irreversibility is a “fundamental aspect of the universe”.37

This irreversible process is also path dependent. Structures of galaxies, stars, meteor clouds, and energy fields are constantly changing. What happens next is shaped by what already exists, coupled with the influence of the longer-range interactions with other galaxies and structures.



What does this mean for the world of management?



Newton’s physics was without doubt an amazing achievement. It brought clarity to certain aspects of dynamics of the universe (gravity), but it cannot explain many others. Since then, the mathematics has been challenged and extended by quantum physics and relativity, and it no longer stands as ‘good science’ in its own terms, for the problems for which it was derived.

Nevertheless, this mechanical worldview – of prediction and certainty – still forms the basis of many management practices and still forms the gold standard for what we think science can offer. And yet it is clear that social systems do not behave in this fashion. And if they did, if the future really were that predictable, there would be no newness and no potential for adaptation. Machines can’t change, they can merely chug along.

We [modern scientists] have arrived very far from the mechanical world of Newton

and Laplace, where minute cold stones eternally travelled on long precise trajectories

in geometrically immutable space. Rovelli38

Mock on, mock on Voltaire Rousseau

Mock on mock on, tis all in vain!

You throw the sand against the wind

And the wind blows it back again. William Blake



The extent to which Newton’s science still dominates approaches to management seems rather astonishing. In Section VII, Perspectives on Organisational Practice, I share many examples of where this view continues to prevail – often to the detriment of achieving what was intended.





4


The indeterminate universe



What causes indeterminacy in the universe?



Newton made a remarkable contribution to science. His work on gravity and mechanics was of enormous importance. But in practice gravity is not the only

factor driving the universe. There are light, heat, sound, electricity and magnetism. And eventually scientists discovered nuclear forces and quantum mechanics. Like Newton’s theories, many of these theories are also deterministic, so, it does beg the question as to whether the universe could be deterministic – still a machine, just a more complicated one than envisaged by Newton.

In the Appendix, I have included quite a detailed breakdown of what causes the uni-verse not to work like a machine. There are two pieces: ‘Science and indeterminism’ [72] and ‘Smoke and mirrors’ [73], which is an exploration of quantum physics. In case you don’t wish to tackle them, I summarise the key points here.

The same dull round, even of a universe, would soon become a mill with complicated

wheels. Blake

Messiness: Even if the laws of physics are deterministic, they operate on messy stuff. We don’t really know much about the Big Bang, including whether there was only one of them, or a series of them, or whether it happened somewhere or everywhere. But the cosmic microwave background39 shows that its aftermath was not uniform. This non-uniformity may have been the result of the remains of a previous universe,40 or due to quantum fluctuations or heat fluctuations [72] or, more likely, all of these.

A second after the Big Bang, nothing in the laws of particle physics hinted at the possi-

bility of rivers, misty dawns, or human beings. These only became possible as more and

more constraints piled onto the initial soup of particles. Karl Schroeder41



Indeed, had there been uniformity, there would not have been the clumping of energy and matter that concentrates things sufficiently for stars to be born. The emergence and evolution of stars and galaxies in all their glory fundamentally depends on non- uniformity, on messiness.

Another source of indeterminacy: These messy clouds of energy are subject to the pushes and pulls of different ‘forces’. These forces – nuclear, magnetic, gravitational – cannot be considered merely additive. They operate together in complex ways.



20 Section I The Long Walk to Freedom: the Developing Story of the Scientific Worldview



Star birth

Stars are created from turbulence and vortices in clouds of simple molecules. Some

patches need to be dense enough for gravity to create enough force for nuclear
