reactions to occur. In addition, the clouds tend to be strongly magnetic, and this resists

gravity, meaning that more material is needed before there is a collapse into star forma-

tion. The particularity of the shape and size of a molecular cloud and the exact timing

of other matter arriving from the wider universe will shape what sort of star is formed.

And when there are births and explosions leading to the formation of new heavier el-

ements, these events are complex. Scientists have an understanding of the conditions

under which stars can form – but being able to predict when, how and into what a par-

ticular situation might lead to is another matter.



Closed systems and continuity: Newton’s theories and other deterministic theories apply to closed systems. These theories can’t deal with and don’t apply to beginnings, endings and discontinuities.

And evidence shows that the universe is full of discontinuities – black holes and various sorts of ‘tears in the fabric’.

Clarke describes quantum theory as “a crack in the notion of perfection”, and he regards the crack as “the most beautiful element in the whole picture”.42 Leonard Cohen take note!

Thus, we can’t think of space as continuous. And indeed, as Einstein showed, we can’t even think space as being distinct from matter.

Quantum physics messes up everything ontologically: Quantum physics also changes the game when it comes to determinism. As I describe in more detail in ‘Smoke and mirrors’ [73], properties are held in the relationship between things, not in the things themselves. Properties are context dependent and paradoxical – showing different ‘faces’ depending on what is interacting with what.

Furthermore, quantum physics shows that there are really no ‘things’ anyway when it comes down to it, as I explore further in ‘Is there no thing at all?’ [6]. Quantum physics points to an ontological form of indeterminism. Go to the Appendix if you are interested.



So …



The universe shows its nature as path-dependent, context-sensitive, loosely inter-connected and evolving. And above all, it is indeterminate. It really doesn’t look like a machine. In this piece, I have listed some of the reasons why.





5




Unity and uniqueness



Why we assume that there are over-arching universal truths and why this is to be

questioned. An emergent view of imperfect, incoherent, self-organising wholeness.



In this piece, I want to explore a particular aspect of the machine worldview which centres on the concepts of Universality and Unity.



The iron block



Classical physics of Newton in the seventeenth century had its origins in theology. “Its aim”, Prigogine explained, “was to bring Man closer to the knowledge of God.” He continued: “For God, there is no difference between past, present and future. Everything is there, eternally.”43 Thus science, in the view of Newton, reveals itself in the form of time-independent, universal, unchanging laws – laws that mirror the mind of God [3]. As Toulmin says, “Descartes and Newton set out to build mathematical structures and looked to science for theological not technological dividends.”44

These top-down universal laws were deemed to apply everywhere and forever and give no opportunity for change or novelty. From this perspective, the universe, as dis-cussed in [3], is viewed as a gigantic deterministic machine where everything merely unfolds according to God’s plan. As William James says, Newton’s science conveys the “absolute unity” of an “iron block”.

To quote James more fully:

What does determinism profess? It professes that those parts of the universe already

laid down absolutely appoint and decree what the other parts shall be. The future has

no ambiguous possibilities hidden in its womb … the whole is in each and every part

and welds it with the rest into an absolute unity, an iron block, in which there can be no

equivocation or shadow of turning.45

Absolute space, in its own nature, without regard to anything external, remains always

similar and immovable. Newton



These universal laws had a further quality. As they were of God’s making, they were deemed to be ‘perfect’ in the sense that they provided the optimal universe that God could create.

The idea of Perfection is the subject of Plato’s ideal forms.46 Plato suggested that Man’s task was to overcome the chaos of our realm and strive for this Perfection. Indeed, this fuelled the idea, centuries later, of the Great Chain of Being in which each evolutionary 22 Section I The Long Walk to Freedom: the Developing Story of the Scientific Worldview



step – from amoeba to Man – was moving the animal realm closer to this Perfection, closer to God, with Man as its greatest expression.

This perpetual chain of causes and effects, whose first link is riveted to the throne of

God, divides itself into innumerable diverging branches, which, like the nerves arising

from the brain, permeate the most minute and most remote extremities of the system,

diffusing motion and sensation to the whole. … We trace the operations of nature from

cause to cause, climbing up the links of these chains of being, till we ascend to the Great

Source of all things. Erasmus Darwin47



This view of Perfection included qualities such as Goodness, Truth and Beauty. These absolute qualities were not deemed to change with circumstances, were not matters of perspective or context, but were of an eternal, ‘perfect’ nature.

What we now regard as universal laws prevailing throughout the observable universe

may, in the grander perspective of the multiverse, be just parochial bylaws applied to

our cosmic patch. Rees48



Daoism embraces uniqueness not Unity



Daoist philosophers David Hall and Roger Ames really brought home to me just how wedded we are in the West to the idea of Unity. As they say: “In classical Western metaphysics, the equivocation between unity and uniqueness has largely been re-solved in favour of unity.”49 In other words, we in the West, despite scientific evidence that brings into question whether the universe is entirely determined by over-arching laws, still have a predisposition for seeking universality.

To give an example of our predisposition for universality: In the field of management, we seek ‘best practice’ and are attracted to standardised solutions where ‘one size fits all’. We continue to see science as the territory of certainty and prediction, obeying fixed rules and continue to want to mimic that in managing the social world.

Complexity theory, as explored in Section II, presents a view of science as much more contextual, much more about uniqueness than Unity. In similar vein, as explored in Section III, the Daoist perspective starts with the particular and local and does not invoke assumptions of universality or unity. There is no “transcending pattern” that determines order. There is no “superordinate One to which the many reduce.”50 An over-arching Unity “would dominate the order of the parts.”51

In other words, what goes on here and now – the local order – is shaped by the particularities of the local conditions – those “particular elements that come together to make it” which are “unique and irreplaceable” and can’t be substituted without everything changing.52 Change even one thing and you change the local pattern. This is self-organisation shaped by local conditions [14].

5 Unity and uniqueness 23



Coherence and Unity – wholes but not a Whole



I want to bring in one more related theme into this section, and that is the idea of coherence. Coherence in science and mathematics refers to situations in which no information is lost as things unfold. There is perfect, unbroken connection. If you dropped a stone on a flat pond, the ripples would be coherent; if you viewed water from a bubbling stream, the ripples and swirls would not be coherent.

One example of coherence is a so-called standing wave pattern in sound vibrations.



Sound vibrations displayed with salt



You can create a ‘Chladni Plate’ by pouring

salt or sand onto a taut membrane.53 Play

music and watch the salt move, making

the vibration of sound waves visible to all.

You find patterns that transform with each

pitch change, forming a mesmerising visu-

al representation of standing waves that is

usually hidden from sight.54



If the universe were coherent, unbreakable or indivisible, then it would satisfy the con-ditions for determinacy. In practice, as discussed in ‘The indeterminate universe’ [4], the universe is full of holes and discontinuities and does not function as a coherent, indivisible Whole. Indeed, without discontinuities and messy asymmetries, we could not account for the forms and structures of which the universe is composed [4] [72].

Why am I labouring this point? According to Aristotle, the Platonists proffered an argument for the existence of Forms that was called the “One Over Many.”55 This is an idea that has attracted philosophers ever since. Some philosophers suggest that out of this Oneness can spring individuality and differentiable features (the Many). Freya Mathews, for example, asserts that the universe is “primordially an indivisible whole”56 and that the nature of space is a “vast, unbounded, unbroken, field-like continuum”.57 Yet within that “seamless whole” Mathews suggests that beings and ecosystems can self-determine.58 In similar vein, Iain McGilchrist says “the history of the cosmos looks like one of constant divergence into multiplicity and uniqueness, yet a uniqueness that is always subsumed within, and understood against the background of, a coherent whole.”59

The issue I am bringing into question is the assumption, in these statements of Mathews and McGilchrist, of coherence or indivisibility or unbreakability. From the scientific point of view, these terms infer a pre-ordained, deterministic process in which there is no possibility of the emergence of novelty, nor any freedom or uniqueness. If the One is a coherent, seamless, indivisible Whole, then, from the perspective of science, nothing unexpected or novel can happen. If the One is unbreakable then ‘the many’ can only emerge in a way that is pre-ordained and pre-determined.

24 Section I The Long Walk to Freedom: the Developing Story of the Scientific Worldview



But evidence shows that the universe is not perfectly deterministic – neither in the sense of working like clockwork, nor in the sense of being a coherent indivisible Whole [72]. On the other hand, nor is it merely random and chaotic. Rather – and this is the important point – it is incoherently interconnected, allowing for patterns of relation-ships or wholes to emerge, stabilise and ultimately fall away. These patterns intersect, blend and mutually affect each other.

And it is this incoherent, imperfect connectedness that allows for novelty, adaptability, evolution and surprise. It leads to temporary stabilisation of form, it gives primacy to relationship yet also embraces impermanence, flow and creative change.

There is not a whole, but many such wholes. David Hall and Roger Ames60



To summarise



Embracing this idea of incoherent connectivity, of imperfect connection leads to a universe of ‘wholes’ – temporary, intersecting, imprecise – but not an indivisible Whole. It is this connectivity and local patterning coupled with cracks and sinkholes and fractures in the ‘fabric’ that allows for novelty, freshness and response to context and circumstance.

[The universe is] a vast, self-organizing, complex system, the emergent properties of

which are … everything. Neil Theise61



Wholes in society

Let me apply this thinking to the ways societies have emerged over centuries.

First, there were small groups that settled together, sometimes traded, sometimes

fought, sometimes joined together. Eventually groups grew into cities and these warred,

traded and connected. Sometimes nations or empires emerged, with their own cultures

and with power to impact the cities and citizens within their reach. These constellations

of people, at every level, were unique and distinct; and they constantly interacted and

shaped each other, upwards, downwards and sideways. Often the higher level – the na-

tion – could constrain and shape the future behaviour of its citizens and groups. They

are wholes embedded in larger wholes – ‘wholes’ at every level.

This negotiated, shifting, indefinite, incomplete interconnecting society, described

above, is a good metaphor, I think, for the way the universe is, in some sense, a ‘whole’.



So …



Platonic philosophical ideas deeply influenced the tradition of classical science. Classi-cal science focused on universal laws and tended to ignore, and had no explanation for, the rich, ever-changing diversity of form in the universe.

5 Unity and uniqueness 25



From the point of view of the old Newtonian-style physics, the structure of the world

is accidental … it gives a picture of the universe where life, variety and structure are

improbable accidents. Smolin62



Daoists, as I will explore in Section III, are much more comfortable with the ‘particular’ rather than the universal – and the particular is the territory of complexity too. I hope this piece provides some additional background for this topic.





6


Is there no thing at all?



How successive developments in physics gradually removed notions of matter, time

and space.



I want at this stage to explore how successive waves of physics further deconstruct the machine view of reality. I’ve considered determinism [3] [4] and I’ve brought into

question a universe defined by universals and absolutes [5]. Here I am examining the assumption that the universe is made of solid, fixed ‘things’ moved around by forces.63



Forces as fields



By the nineteenth century, Michael Faraday and James Clerk Maxwell showed how electromagnetic objects interact via electromagnetic fields.64 These classical force-fields demonstrate how the forces that act on objects change over space.

A great way to envisage a forcefield in two dimensions is to place two magnets on a piece of paper and scatter iron filings around them. The magnetic field is then revealed in the patterns taken up by the iron filings. Another sort of field occurs in sound, illus-trated by connecting a metal plate covered in sand to an audio source and seeing the patterns that arise, as illustrated in ‘Unity and uniqueness’ [5]. These are interference patterns, whereby the edges of the plate reflect back the sound waves which then interact with those going outwards. The same thing occurs when throwing a stone into a pond; you can notice the ensuing ripples bouncing back from the banks.

These examples show how forces present themselves, in many cases, as dispersed properties of objects. Planets ‘create’ gravitational fields around them, and planets mutually impact each other through these fields. Objects with electric charge and/or magnetic properties interact in the same way.

What all this work on field theories demonstrates is that forces cannot be thought of as separate from objects; things create forcefields and forcefields act on things.



Gravitational forces are really distortions of space



The next chronological step occurred as a result of Einstein’s work on relativity.65 This new theory of gravity further challenged the notion that forces and objects were separate, through reconsidering the nature of space. Up to then, space had been a sort of unchanging background against which, or in which, events happened. Einstein showed how an object in space distorts the shape of space itself. In two dimensions this looks like a heavy ball on a rubber membrane – the ball sinks into the membrane and 28 Section I The Long Walk to Freedom: the Developing Story of the Scientific Worldview



other objects ‘fall towards’ the heavier one. Gravity is no longer a force but is under-stood as a reshaping of space itself.



E=mc2: things aren’t things forever



The next nail in the coffin of things: Einstein intuited the relationship between energy and matter in his famous equation, E=mc2. Energy begets matter (as in the heart of a fiercely burning star which creates elements of higher mass, such as iron) and matter begets energy (think nuclear bomb). Things aren’t things forever.



Quantum physics – things dissolve



Finally, quantum physics destroys the idea that there are little building blocks – particles – out of which matter is formed. In Newton’s view, things are made of smaller things which are made of yet smaller things. But now, with the advent of quantum physics, matter is no longer seen as ‘real’ in this way, but ‘dissolves’ into quantum fields. These fields are dispersed in space; they are ambiguously, paradoxically there and not there; they are not really real at all.

I came across the following long quote by Bernard Kastrup, which I include as I cannot better it as a description of the way quantum physics destroys the foundations of classical materialism.

But we’ve known at least since the late 1940s … that what we call ‘particles’ aren’t par-

ticles at all: they are merely local patterns of excitation of a spatially unbound quantum

field. Think of ‘particles’ as ripples on a lake: each ripple has a certain height, thickness,

speed and direction of movement, which are the ripple’s defined physical properties.

They also have defined locations in space: you can point at a part of the lake and say,

“there’s a ripple!” Yet, there is nothing to the ripple but the lake itself. The ripple is not a

standalone entity, but a behaviour of the lake; it’s not a thing but a ‘doing.’ This is why

you cannot grab a ripple and lift it off the lake.



In precisely the same way, Quantum Field Theory66 … tells us that the so-called parti-

cles are just ‘ripples’ of a quantum field. In essence, there are no true particles; we use this

word today only metaphorically, and for historical reasons. There are only quantum

fields, which are spatially unbound.67



Even time is not time as we know it



The final blow to our everyday view of reality comes from quantum gravity – an area of physics that has certainly not yet landed but is aiming to bring together quantum physics and relativity. Einstein’s relativity had already put an end to absolute time – a universe moving along according to God’s ‘ticking clock’ [3]. Einstein introduced

6 Is there no thing at all? 29



relative time encapsulated in the story of two twins, one of whom time travels and the other does not; when the time traveller returns, he is younger than his twin.

But quantum gravity overturns even the level of precision inherent in relative time. Furthermore, it also destroys the illusion of the existence of absolute or even relative space. It suggests that space and time can only be defined in relation to two (or more) interacting ‘objects’ [73].

Rovelli writes clearly and evocatively about these ideas in The Order of Time.68 Time, Rovelli says, is absent as part of the constitutive fabric of the universe. “The present,” he says, “is defined only in our vicinity in an approximate way.”69 Daoist cosmology takes the same view [23:6]. Ames and Hall say: “The Chinese tradition does not have the separation between time and entities that would allow for either time without entities or entities without time”.70

Rovelli continues: “The absence of time does not mean that everything is frozen and unmoving.”71 The world, he says, “is in a ceaseless process of change.”72

The incessant happening that wearies the world is not ordered along a timeline, is not

measured by a gigantic tick-tocking. Rovelli73



In summary



You may be wondering, why am I so keen to spell this out? Partly because this history of the dissolution of ‘things’ and the questioning of the nature of time and space does not just bring into question – but even more firmly buries – the idea that being scientific is to adopt a machine worldview.

Furthermore, it creates a foundation stone for process complexity [9], and for process philosophies more generally [35].

Rovelli explains that quantum gravity implies:

The world is not a collection of things, it is a collection of events. … Even the things

that are most ‘thing-like’ are nothing more than long events. … The world is not so much

made of stones as of fleeting sounds or waves moving through the sea.74



This idea of things being better thought of as events – some long, some small – is the stuff of process philosophies [35], forms the basis of process complexity [10] and is inherent to Daoism.





7


Darwin and evolution: another sort of science



Showing how Darwin changed the game in science and philosophy and introduced the

fundamental role of variation in engendering change.



On the Origin of Species, published in 1859, had an enormous impact on philosophy, science and even on literature. 75 Up to that point, philosophy and science had searched for uniformity and viewed chaos or messiness as something to overcome [5]. Darwin’s great contribution was to recognise that messiness was generative; variation was a fundamental condition for nature to be able to adapt to changing conditions and for the emergence of novelty.

Furthermore, Darwin showed that evolutionary change starts locally, bottom up. And evolution is path-dependent – what has emerged shapes and limits what can happen next [15]. For example, animals early on developed the basic structure of two eyes and four limbs and that structure has tended to endure. Nature does not generally start again from scratch.

A science dependent on variation, where what happens is shaped by history and by the local context, is a very different sort of science to that embraced by Newton and others; it embeds uncertainty within it. We cannot know in advance what will emerge locally, it depends on local conditions and features. We also cannot know if any changes will sustain and spread. Even if we understand the core mechanisms, we cannot predict the outcomes. It is not possible to predict what new species and ecological patterns will emerge. The concept of emergence was established [19].

Darwinism involves a general theory of the evolution of all open, complex systems

[and] a basic philosophical commitment to detailed, cumulative causal explanations.

Geoffrey Hodgson76

Darwin’s ideas had an amazing influence on intellectual thought at the time and influ-enced many diverse fields including philosophy, science, literature and economics. His ideas sparked the interest, in the late nineteenth century, of a group of philosophers who came to be called process philosophers [35]. They started to consider whether it wasn’t more useful and realistic to regard the world as ‘in process’ – a world that is constantly shifting and changing and emerging, rather than, in line with classical physics, as fixed and predictable. Henri Bergson’s Creative Evolution77 shaped Prigogine’s considerations of open systems [8].

Darwin’s work also sparked the interest of writers such as Thomas Hardy and D. H. Lawrence, as described by Gillian Beer in Darwin’s Plots.78

As Beer tells us, Darwin also approached and wrote about science in a way that was untypical for scientists at the time, when scientists wished to be certain and assured. 32 Section I The Long Walk to Freedom: the Developing Story of the Scientific Worldview



Beer explains that Darwin wrote The Origin as a tentative and time-sequenced story of how he came to the still-provisional conclusion that evolution had at its heart the principles of variation and selection. As you can see from the excerpt below, Beer views his writing as an example of how the medium embraces the message:

Darwin’s language does not close itself off authoritatively nor describe its own circum-

ference. … He sought to move out beyond the false security of authority or even of

the assumption that full knowledge may be reached.79 … Evolutionary theory is first a

form of imaginative history. It cannot be experimentally demonstrated sufficiently in

any present moment.80 … Darwin’s work … is the description of a process of becoming

and such a process does not move constantly in one direction.81



Evolutionary science also impacted the thinking of Thorstein Veblen, who, in 1898, wrote Why is economics not an evolutionary science?82 He scathingly suggests that eco-nomics is “helplessly behind the times”83 in sticking to the view that the economy tends to reach a balanced equilibrium. He introduced the term “dispassionate cumulative causation”84 to describe the path-dependent evolutionary process.

Evolution also impacted the physical sciences and was of central importance to Prigogine’s work on open systems [8]. Smolin suggests that the evolutionary principle applies even at the level of the universe. He says:

I remember wondering whether, if the earth can be understood as a self-organizing

system, maybe the same thing is true for the universe as a whole. … It seemed to me

that the only principle powerful enough to explain the high degree of organization of

our universe – compared to a universe with the particles and forces chosen randomly –

was natural selection itself.85



Smolin emphasises that “the environment that determines how successful any particular organism will be is largely made up of other organisms. … So, there is a community of organisms which in some sense collectively determine the different niches as they evolve together.”86 For this to work requires synergistic interdependencies and diversity, themes that are taken up in complexity thinking [11]. This is a development of the initial idea of survival of the fittest where the primary emphasis was on competition.



In summary



Evolutionary theory was a very different sort of science to what had gone before. It provided an understanding of how evolution happens but showed that we cannot pre-dict what will evolve. It showed how variation leads to path dependence and emer-gence. It was a central inspiration for the science of open systems and the development of complexity theory. Embracing diversity, messiness, the non-standard and the con-textual stands in contrast to the fixation, of the classical physicist, with the universal and the eternal.





8


Messiness is next to openness87



Showing how Prigogine worked with ideas from evolutionary science and the science of

open systems and arrived at complexity theory.



Here I focus on the science of open systems and the way this led into complexity science. The ontological view we arrive at – a contextual, emerging, patterned,

path-dependent perspective – is already emerging through the science explored thus far. Ilya Prigogine, on whom this piece focuses, unravels more of the reasons why.

Prigogine was a Russian-born physical chemist; he lived and worked most of his life in Brussels. Evolutionary theory brought home to Prigogine that classical physics tends to focus on closed systems. In closed systems [4] the problem is relatively straight-forward as there are definite, impermeable boundaries; thus nothing outside the prob-lem of interest can affect it. Prigogine, however, realised that for open systems, systems that can interact with their surroundings, it is possible for ‘form’ or ‘structure’ – in the sense of patterns – to emerge [14]. What particular patterns emerge depends on his-tory and context and is reliant on the particular details that happen to be present. It is the openness to the surroundings that provides the fuel to allow the creation of more sophisticated and complex structures.

In one fell swoop, with this word open, Prigogine was able to connect physics with bi-ology, and to show the resonance between the physics of open systems and the theory of evolution. In this, he laid the foundations for complexity theory. He wrote his first paper on non-equilibrium thermodynamics in 1947,88 and he received the Nobel Prize for this insight in 1977.89

Prigogine recalls in his Nobel autobiographical speech that inspiration came to him as a young man when reading Creative Evolution by Henri Bergson. In particular, he was struck by Bergson asking: “Why does life mount the incline that matter descends?”90 Bergson was referring to the seeming contradiction between physics and evolution. Life has a tendency to evolve from simple forms, like amoeba, to ever-more-complex forms, like primates. But physics only seems to have two types of theories. Either things behave in a machine-like way91 and stay the same forever or advance in pre-dictable ways; or there is the physics of equilibrium thermodynamics which suggests that gases and liquids tend to lose structure and energy and move towards featureless, uniform states of lowest energy (associated with increases of entropy, a measure of disorder). This minimum energy state is called equilibrium or heat death [72]. Both of these physics theories assume closed situations.

What Prigogine realised, is that for open systems that can exchange energy or matter or information with the wider world, the tendency towards loss of order and eventual death can be reversed; order can emerge locally and entropy, locally, can decrease. 34 Section I The Long Walk to Freedom: the Developing Story of the Scientific Worldview



The situation sucks energy into itself from its surroundings to fuel this reversal of the norm. This does not change the inevitable plod of the whole towards heat death, but it is what gives us life and stars in the meantime.

The thermodynamics of open situations not in equilibrium, where there is interaction with the wider context, thus brings congruence between physics and evolutionary theory. Both can lead to an increase of order locally. This was very exciting. It is not suggesting that such ordering lasts forever or always happens; death, dissolution and decay happen too and indeed are inevitable in a universe that does not have an inexhaustible supply of energy. But recognising that open systems, be they living or not, can demonstrate a tendency for patterns and order to emerge, was a huge realisation.

Prigogine, being Russian-born, was able to find experimental examples of such local reversals of entropy, where patterns emerge, in the work of two Russians, Boris Belousov and Anatol Zhabotinsky.92 Their work took place in the early 1950s and was only available at the time in Russian. The Belousov-Zhabotinksy (BZ) reaction occurs with certain combinations of chemicals in a petri dish. Much to everyone’s astonishment, patterns and swirls emerged on the surface of the liquid, intersect-ing and disappearing and re-surfacing over time, and eventually dying away as the chemicals were exhausted. This is an example of “order out of chaos.”93 There seemed no way at the time to explain what caused the patterns and the work was not taken seriously. Prigogine was later able to show, in his work on non-equilibrium thermody-namics, that such order could be sustained if the system were open – in this case, if a continuous inflow of reagents and an outflow of reaction products was provided.

Ilya Prigogine called the Belousov-Zhabotinsky reaction “one of the most important

discoveries of the century”.94



Order can seem like a peculiar word to use here, as the patterns in reactions like the BZ reaction, or the emergence of new species, are not ordered in the sense that the form of the patterns is predictable or easily defined or settles into a fixed stable state. But the patterns that emerge and sustain for a while, have an integrity or order that is distinguishable from chaos, where there would be no features at all.95 Complexity theory, in the tradition of Prigogine, has its roots in the thermodynamics of open systems. Open systems can exchange energy, information and matter with their surroundings. This openness is necessary for patterns to sustain but in addition, there needs to be some sort of relational feedback mechanisms between constituents, which can lead to the emergence and stabilising of self-organising, self-regulating patterns. Section II is devoted to unpacking these ideas.



8 Messiness is next to openness 35



Path-dependency



I wanted to add a further important point. Classical thermodynamics as applied to sim-ple gases and liquids, ignores the importance of the sequence of collisions or the context in which they happen. One molecule of hydrogen is indistinguishable from another molecule. These simplifying assumptions work well for gases and liquids, but these simplifications do not apply in general. When the concepts of thermodynamics are applied to more complex situations, with lots of different types of entities interacting via a range of types of interactions (nuclear, electromagnetic, gravitational), then the path the system happens to follow matters.96

We see this path-dependency in the BZ reaction. The patterns that emerged on the surfaces of the petri dishes change over time and also, if the experiment is run again, are never repeated. It is as if the system has a memory of the stages it has passed through; its current state is shaped by what occurred on the way. What happens in each step shapes and narrows the possibilities of what can happen next and in the future. Stuart Kauffman notes that this tendency, this narrowing of options, called non-ergodicity, also occurs at the level of the universe. He explains that this process is necessary for highly unlikely things, such as life, to have a chance of emerging.

The evolution of life in our biosphere is profoundly … historical. The universe will not

create all possible life forms. Kauffman97



I’ve been very taken with the way William James, understood this point. I quoted his words on determinism in [4]. Regarding indeterminism, he says:

Indeterminism … says that the parts have a certain amount of loose play on one another.

… It admits that possibilities may be in excess of actualities, and that things … may really

in themselves be ambiguous … actualities seem to float in a wider sea of possibilities.

Of two alternative futures which we conceive, both may now really be possible; and

the one becomes impossible only at the very moment when the other excludes it by

becoming real itself.98



In this quote, James captures several aspects of indeterminism:

First: Things are not perfectly defined, there is not just one path forwards; the ‘loose play’, which Peter Allen referred to as “excess diversity,”99 allows for adaptations.

Second: As we cannot pin down which adaptations will come into play, there is ambiguity, to use James’ word, as to which path will manifest.

Third: James understands this leads to historicity or path-dependency – that the future depends on what has happened up to now.

Fourth: He recognises that what was previously possible is no longer available when one path is selected; the range of possible futures is narrowed. This is Kauffman’s non-ergodicity. As Kauffman says: “Non-ergodicity gives us history.”100 36 Section I The Long Walk to Freedom: the Developing Story of the Scientific Worldview



In conclusion



This section, The Long Walk to Freedom, starts with Newton and classical physics; I show the way this science is adopted as a philosophy, as a mechanical worldview that avows prediction, certainty, standardisation, right answers and universal truths [3] [5]. I’ve attempted to illustrate that the universe does not, in fact, behave in that fashion [4] and the mechanical worldview is not very apt as a ‘theory of everything’.

As I read through these pieces, I realise that the three most important reasons why we have to bury the mechanical worldview is because: (a) most situations are open to their wider context, (b) things are messy, and (c) things are interconnected or, to use the Daoist phrase, are ‘mutually entailed’ [28]. This paves the way for the discussion on ‘The conditions for complexity’ [11] in the next section.

In ‘Is there no thing?’ [6], I’ve also detoured into a discussion, building on quantum mechanics, that supports a more processual view of the world – a world not only in process but constituted of processes. This leads into a processual view of complexity.

Prigogine, impacted by Darwin [7], was not the only route into complexity, but the way his understanding emerged from classical science meeting evolution tells a very com-pelling story. His work led him and his colleagues into environmental science and even-tually into the social sciences. His later papers and debates illustrated how he reflected on the bigger picture and saw the importance of mindset and beliefs. I recommend Art meets Science and Spirituality in a changing economy101 and Science, Reason and Passion.102





Section II




Process Complexity





9


Introducing process complexity



What is complexity? What, indeed, is process complexity?



The science of complexity conveys a view of the world as dynamic, richly inter-dependent and full of variety. It is a world – organic and emergent, shaped by history and context – that is naturally patterned, yet always in process. The future, however, is not exhaustively capacious; it is not that anything can happen.

But is that what people imagine when there is talk of complexity? If I asked you to think of images that convey your idea of complexity, you might think of puppies playing with skeins of wool or children emptying the contents of the kitchen drawers into a heap on the floor. You might recall navigating your way through a souk or remember an unbelievably complicated diagram (I remember such a diagram) showing the political and economic forces at play in Afghanistan.1 The word complexity might suggest an impossible, chaotic, ambiguous, useless muddle: a bad thing we should aim to simplify, organise and master.

But what if complexity is the natural way of things, and in its depths lies the source of novelty and creativity and the emergence of the forms and patterns of which we are a part?

Nature is radically complex. Chris Mowles2



The Latin word complexus means that which is woven together, and it neatly captures the essential quality of complexity. What is the nature of this interwoven muddle, this interconnecting, undisciplined connectedness of which we are a part? This is an onto-logical, indeed a metaphysical question that seeks to understand ‘the nature of things’ [2]. And I argue that this quest is a necessary practice for all of us – not merely an amus-ing debate for philosophers, but a central grounding for how to live well. Our beliefs – about the nature of being, about how the world works, about what creates change or stability – drive our methods and behaviours. Without addressing these underlying as-sumptions and beliefs, we can do no more than strive to improve that which we were already doing.

The foundational inquiry underpinning my work is to ask what the science of complexity can reveal. In Section I, I sketched out the gradual complexification of science, starting with Newton’s ‘classical’ view of a universe of ‘things’ that follow laws which ‘lay down’ future behaviour. I brought that section to a close with Ilya Prigogine grappling with the question as to why classical physics and evolutionary biology seem to contradict each other. The word that brought these two sciences together was ‘open’. Situations that are open to their environments display emerging order in the form of patterns.

40 Section II Process Complexity



The emergence of patterns in the chemical experiments that so intrigued Prigogine ran counter to expectations [8]. But, as Prigogine and others realised, it is the possibility to exchange energy, material and information with the wider context that allows such patterns to emerge. For evolutionary processes at every level – from galaxies to amoeba – this ability to suck fuel from their surroundings is the source of complexification.



The emerging ontology of indeterminism, contingency

and process



In Section I, I traced the development of science from Newton to Prigogine. The onto-logical view that emerged by early twentieth century describes a very different uni-verse from the one Newton had envisaged, as described below.



The ontology of the universe by the early twentieth century

A universe that is not deterministic, does not work like a machine, and does not merely

unfold according to plan.

A dynamic universe of forms – forms that emerge, stabilise and eventually disintegrate,

and which are open to and engage with their wider environment.

A universe of interconnection and mutual interaction – sometimes strong, sometimes

tenuous.

A universe of messiness, where the lack of symmetry is a vital ingredient to enable the

emergence and evolution of forms.

A universe contingent on context, where no part is the same as any other.

A universe that is not objectively real in the way we imagine, but, due to the weird be-

haviour at the quantum level, has a sort of intrinsic paradoxical quality that creates a

source of mystery.

A universe in process, always becoming, where ‘what is’ shapes but does not determine

the path for the future to emerge.



As the above summary indicates, by the early twentieth century most of the ingredients for developing complexity theory – a path-dependent, evolving, interconnected uni-verse, shaped by messiness, particular to context and not made of definitive ‘things’ – had emerged through successive scientific developments which built on the founda-tions set by Newton.

One of the reasons I feel complexity science is so important is because it gains its authority from its roots in science, even as it brings into question the ontology of classical science. These roots provide a solid foundation from which to offer a legiti-mate challenge to the adoption of the mechanical worldview as a relevant model for the social and natural world [3]. An aim to be appropriately scientific in the living world is generally better served by ‘embracing complexity’. It is a better fit. Assuming the

9 Introducing process complexity 41



world is controllable and predictable when it is not, does not make it so. Complexity will not just go away if you ignore it.

There can be a fear that if we let go of the machine perspective, we will tumble about with the puppies and the wool, learn nothing and get nowhere. We fear that, if we do face that complexity, we will find it so multifaceted and intertwined that, as we can’t know everything, we may end up knowing nothing. But complexity science says more about the world than ‘it’s all complex’. It provides insights and understandings that allow us to engage with that complexity, make sense of the seeming muddle and be skilful in our responses. Indeed, variation and muddle are vital; they are a source – perhaps even the source – of creativity, novelty, adaptability, and flourishing.



What’s so special about process complexity?



Complexity theory in various forms has been around since at least the 1980s. It has been developed through mathematical modelling in particular, and also through philo-sophical endeavours and experiential work. Section IV, Other Resonant Perspectives, reviews this broader field, as does Embracing Complexity written with Peter Allen and Cliff Bowman.3

In much of the work on complexity there lies an implicit assumption that we can con-ceive of the world as made up of concrete things joined up by forces. Complexity think-ing may allow for these forces to be more complicated, but a sort of realist perspective is still often adopted. Indeed, as I discuss in ‘The raw and the cooked’ [31], complexity thinking can still remain within the paradigms of classical science, and just provide a more sophisticated form of mechanics with which to operate.

But I want to take it further. My aim in this section is to articulate process complexity – a view of complexity that moves us from an image of concretely objective ‘things which interact’ towards an image of entities that are more akin to ripples on a river. It also emphasises the processual nature of the complex world, and in addition includes aspects of the world that are not localised, such as wind or mood.

Ripples provide a great metaphor for a rethink of the nature of ‘things’. Ripples have form and a certain degree of independence, but they indivisibly interact with other ripples, currents, vortices and anything else present. Ripples are paradoxically both separate and not separate; they are identifiable and yet ever-changing.

Process complexity emphasises the processual, systemic, patterned, situational, paradoxical and emergent nature of reality. This is a reality shared by galaxies, ecologies and communities.

The future is under perpetual construction. Ilya Prigogine.4 42 Section II Process Complexity



I don’t wish to claim too much credit for coining the notion of process complexity.5 Prigogine was certainly heading in that direction, and other process thinkers, including Daoists, have also reached a view of the world as essentially constituted by processes in process, as discussed in Sections III and IV. But a processual framing of complexity does, I feel, offer a unique and valuable perspective to process thinking, grounded, as it is, in science. For example, process complexity can allow for an exploration of the way patterns form, stabilise and dissolve [14], or provide a frame through which to distin-guish different types of emergence [19].



In this Section



In this section, I consider the implications not only of recognising a world in process, as did Prigogine, but a world of processes, a world of patterns.

The next piece, ‘A complex view of process’, considers what it means to position complexity as a processual ontology.

In ‘The essential conditions for complexity’, I then discuss the conditions necessary for complexity – openness, diversity and reflexive relationships.

In ‘Characteristically complex’, I briefly introduce the characteristics of a processual complex world and explain how they fit together. These characteristics are:

systemic, patterned, path-dependent, particular, punctuated, paradoxical and

emergent.

In later pieces, I then explore each of these characteristics in turn, considering in more detail what they mean and how they manifest in the social world.



And so, to summarise …



The complexity perspective suggests that the world is constantly shifting. Further-more, it illustrates that the never-ending adjustments at the micro-level support resil-ience of the ecology, community or situation. Paradoxically, such variations also allow for new patterns of relationships to emerge. The complex world is, indeed, composed of such patterns – norms, cultures, ways of doing things, embedded relationally-shaped choices. These patterns emerge, stabilise and ultimately dissolve; they self-organise through the interweaving between their constitutive elements.

The future can be thought of as a dance between patterns and particularities. Current patterns of relationships may potentially be strengthened or disturbed by variations, events, shifts in alliances, modifications in taste, step changes in the wider environment and so on. These patterns do not exist independently of their constitutive elements

9 Introducing process complexity 43



but are maintained by repeating, circling, interdependent behaviours, and through the favouring of certain connections over others. Such patterns develop over time and are shaped by history and context.

Process complexity depicts a world in process, a world that is always becoming. It embraces the paradox that within stability lie the seeds of instability, and within chaos lie the seeds of order. It disavows universal truths and underlines the importance of contextual detail.

Embracing this perspective can seem obvious or subtle, exciting or irritating, rich or overwhelming, depending on your point of view. But in that embracing is offered the promise of an understanding that can lead to discernment and judicious action.

In this section, I focus on unpicking and exploring process complexity as a worldview. Many of the features I describe are not radically different from those you may have already come across if you have some familiarity with complexity thinking – but there is, I suggest, a greater focus on process, pattern and paradox, a re-thinking of the characteristics of a complex world in process, and a drilling down into the nature of emergence, patterning and path dependency.





10


A complex view of process



The emphasis on the processual nature of the complex world. A world in process constituted

of processes.



In this piece, I want to draw out what underlies the integration of a complexity world-view with a process orientation and articulate ‘process complexity’.



A processual worldview



My starting point is that the complex world is necessarily processual in nature. This builds on the direction in which Prigogine was already heading. For example, Erich Jantsch in The Self-organizing Universe, a book dedicated to Prigogine, notes that a “pro-cess orientation”,6 influenced by the ideas of Henri Bergson, underpinned Prigogine’s work. One of Prigogine’s books was titled From Being to Becoming,7 implying that con-stant dynamic change and movement – not stasis – is the norm in the world. Neverthe-less, the substance of a processual view of complexity was not developed.

To put it in context, classical scientists ask what the world is; they start with a stable world of stable things – things that interact, things that can change, but, in the main, tend to stay pretty stable. This is a world of being – a world of stasis that only sometimes changes.

In contrast, a process orientation or process ontology posits that change is the norm and regards stability as a special case. The world according to process thinkers is essentially dynamic, changing and in flow. Process thinkers often tend to focus on verbs and adjectives as opposed to nouns – patterning not pattern, a world of becoming not being.

To say the wind is blowing has already made the wind into a thing; but actually, it is the

blowing that makes it wind. Chris Mowles8



The idea of process complexity not only captures a view of the world as ‘always becoming’ but also leaves behind the idea of a world made of concrete ‘things’. In this sense it goes further than a dynamical view. A dynamical view can offer up a world that changes due to the complex interactions between things. At its core, though, still lurks the image of a world of substance, a world that is real, concrete and objectively there. Process complexity goes further than this and brings an end to ‘things’.



46 Section II Process Complexity



The end of things; the primacy of processes



The shift to process complexity gives up on ‘things’ in a number of ways.

First, process complexity includes ingredients that do not resemble localised entities, although they have agency and existence. Here, I think of the weather, the ripples on the ocean, the atmosphere in a wood or in a church, dust clouds or mycelium net-works beneath the soil. John Dupré describes aspects such as rain, wind, electricity as “commonplace examples of subject-less or ‘unowned’ processes”.9 Timothy Morton describes these as ‘hyper objects’.10

Secondly, process complexity does not exclude the inner world of thoughts and feel-ings, instincts and imaginings.

Thirdly, it suggests that ‘things’ are best thought of as patterns of relationships; they are constituted through the inter-relationships between their constituents. The language of some schools of Buddhism refers to such patterns as ‘empty’ in the sense that they do not have an enduring existence, they are not fixed entities but are ‘held in place’ through the relations between their constitutive elements.

Emptiness: There is no self-defining discrete reality to cause or effect. Forms or feel-

ings are devoid of inherent existence; it is only on the basis of aggregation of subtle

elements that forms exist; forms can only be understood in relational terms to their

constitutive elements. Dalai Lama interpreting the Milarepa text11



Furthermore, patterns are made of inter-relating patterns, which are made of even smaller patterns. Indeed ultimately, from the perspective of quantum physics, any ‘thing-like-ness’ vanishes into interpenetrating fields of energy. The material world is in fact, when it comes down to it, rather immaterial.

There is another point too lurking within quantum mechanics: At the quantum level, properties only manifest through relationships, and qualities that manifest in one relationship may not manifest in another. I discuss this further in ‘Smoke and mirrors’, in the Appendix [73].

Relationships do not merely shape reality, they are reality. Shawn Wilson12



So, is anything a thing? As Rovelli would suggest, even rocks or stars are just temporary constellated patterns; it is all a matter of timescale. And, if I consider an individual – myself – then, as discussed in Section VI, Becoming Ourselves, I am both changing and evolving as time goes by, and I am equally responding to the moment, depending on the circumstances – my breakfast, who I am with, what I am worrying about. The self-organising process that leads to relatively stable patterns, of which I am an exam-ple, is both held together and challenged by the interweaving of all that constitutes me, both inner and outer, both tangible and intangible.

By introducing a processual view, we understand ‘things’ as interpenetrating patterns, patterns which may be fleeting or persistent. In so doing, we uncover a more nuanced and inclusive perspective of the complex world.

10 A complex view of process 47



Furthermore, we do not only leave ‘things’ behind, but the distinction between things and forces also becomes blurred. Instead, we can envisage interactions akin to the way ripples interact on the ocean. Ripples are identifiable entities, albeit temporary and with ill-defined boundaries. They impact each other, may merge, may separate again, and may disappear. We would not describe their interactions and impacts as caused by forces; they interact due to their own characteristics (of speed, amplitude, tempera-ture, density), due to their unique nature, at a particular place and time. So, in replacing ‘things’ with the idea of processes or patterns, ‘forces’ are no longer a separate aspect. This is discussed further in ‘Is there no thing at all?’ [6].



Process complexity – processes all the way down



The term process complexity encapsulates a complexity ontology that takes us away from Modernism’s entrancement with things and sees ‘thing-like-ness’ as temporary and relationally constituted. Accordingly, forces are best thought of as the interpene-tration of entities and their surrounding forcefields.

This interpretation is not unique to complexity; it is a central tenet of most process philosophies [35]. The central concept is that process – be it cohering, stabilising, or dis-solving – is primary. Processes create ‘forms’, which may, for a time, be ‘substantial’ – have substance; that time period may be long or short. But any substantial forms emerge as a result of, and are secondary to, processes.

What is a process?



Defining process is not as straightforward as it sounds, and when digging into various works on process philosophy,13 I observed with interest how difficult it is to pin down. Process signifies the ‘mechanisms’ through which things come together, create forms and then move apart. Process contains a sense of flow and movement. Process is not something separate from substance, but that which causes substance to become sub-stantial. You can’t separate ripple from rippling, or energy from energy fields.

There is a process tradition in biology, explored by Dupré and others in Everything Flows: towards a processual philosophy of biology. They say:

Instead of thinking of processes as belonging to things, we should think of things as

being derived from processes … [it implies that] things cannot be regarded as the basic

building blocks of reality. What we identify as things are no more than transient pat-

terns of stability in the surrounding flux, temporary eddies in the continuous flow of

process.14



Nicholas Rescher would argue that process is the most pervasive, characteristic and crucial feature of reality.15

48 Section II Process Complexity



One point to emphasise: a pattern is itself a self-organising process, a form of process that reflects back on itself, as I discuss further in [14]. The world is processes ‘all the way down’.16

A word of warning – a world in process – what it does and doesn’t mean



I’ve so far emphasised the view that ‘things’ are really relationally interwoven, dynam-ically held patterns. Patterns are located in time and space. However, over time, such patterns may emerge, intersect, change, morph, dissolve and emerge as new patterns. There is flow, there is always becoming. Nothing is fixed forever. As I’ve emphasised, processual worldviews in general take the position that change, rather than stability, is the norm.

But what does that mean? Does this imply that we will experience everything as tem-porary, fleeting, disconnected, blown by the winds of chance? That we have no impact on where we are heading; that all is random, indeterminate, formless, chaotic – just one disconnected step after another? Or maybe it implies we are in a fixed process, a predictable sequence of events inexorably trudging along a pre-determined path to a pre-determined goal? Process complexity is espousing neither a ‘random walk’ nor a fixed path, but something complexly, contextually, in between.

Equally, this idea of flow intrinsic to process complexity does not imply a flow towards, a sense of direction or destination; it does not imply that there is necessarily prog-ress or complexification or increasing sophistication of form. Situations may adapt but they may equally well collapse. Entities may emerge that are more sophisticated than earlier ones (such as, one can argue, the human form compared to a cockroach), but equally there is no reason to suppose that the future is necessarily better than the past. Humankind does not necessarily ‘civilise’; progress is not inevitable.17



In summary



Process complexity can take its place within the broad church of process philosophy, a church which is rich and varied, and includes many flavours and colours. In ‘Process philosophies [35], I provide an overview of this broad field of thought.

What, in particular, does process complexity have to offer compared with other pro-cess philosophies? It gives its own insight into the way patterns pattern, into the way flow flows. Building on its roots in science [8], it is saying more than ‘the world is com-plex, messy, interpenetrating and in constant flow’. Process complexity gives a defini-tive grounding to understanding the processual; it conveys the image of a non-smooth flow with a ‘lumpy’ texture. Think of a mountain stream – shaping and being shaped by its environs; its vortices and rapids, its whirlpools and indents and eddies always changing, yet often maintaining shape. The complex world is more like a mountain stream, full of interest, form and attitude, than like a smoothly flowing river.

10 A complex view of process 49



The processual understanding of the complex world has irreversibility [3] at its root and leads to an understanding that situations are constantly adapting – shaped but not determined by history and context, with the potential for the emergence of qualities never before seen.

The rest of this section is given to developing an overview of the essential conditions and characteristics of this processual complex worldview.





11


The essential conditions for complexity



Inter-relatedness, diversity and openness are the necessary conditions that lead to the

emergence, sustaining and ultimate dissolving of patterns of relationships. This gets to the

heart of what engenders the complex world.



I want to bring to life the conditions under which the complex world is at its richest – what you might call its ‘essential ingredients’. What are the underpinning necessities

that lead to and engender the qualities or characteristics of the complex world – its processual, patterned, responsive, paradoxical, emergent nature? What leads to the emergence and stabilisation of patterns of relationships?



Separating conditions from the characteristic they produce



I recognise that, compared to other complexity thinkers, separating out the conditions from the characteristics of the complex world is an unusual step to take. Rika Preiser et al., for example, present the following list of what they call organising principles.18 These principles suggest that ‘complex adaptive systems’:

1. are constituted relationally

2. have adaptive capacities

3. involve dynamic processes

4. are radically open

5. are contextually determined

6. demonstrate novel qualities that emerge through complex causality

Broadly, I am not in disagreement with Preiser, other than to suggest that things being constituted relationally (1) and radically open to their surroundings (4) are the condi-tions that engender their dynamic (3), emergent (6) and contextual nature (5). In other words (1) and (3) create the conditions for the rest.

In similar vein, Reason and Goodwin, suggest six principles of complexity.19 These are:

1. rich interconnections

2. iterative

3. emergence

4. holism

5. fluctuations

6. edge of chaos

I would argue that 1, 2 and 5 are the conditions that lead to the potential for emergence and holism. The existence or not of the edge of chaos is a whole other topic which I will sidestep here!20

52 Section II Process Complexity



The approaches of Preiser and of Reason and Goodwin are not uncommon, but I be-lieve it is helpful to distinguish conditions from the characteristics they give rise to. Not to do so results in a so-called ‘category error’.21 In other words, we are mixing up fruit with seeds! Paying attention to this distinction can provide a richer and more nuanced understanding of the nature of the complex world.



So, what are the conditions for complexity?



At the end of ‘Messiness is next to openness’ [8], I summarise the conditions for com-plexity that emerged from Prigogine’s work. The three conditions for complexity to fully be realised are:

• open boundaries

• diversity

• reflexive inter-relationships between constituents

In essence, if patterned forms – such as those emerging in chemicals in a petri dish, or in a pond or community – aren’t able to interact with, aren’t open to the wider world, they won’t ultimately be able to sustain themselves. Equally, if ‘ingredients’ are not diverse, but are all identical, like the molecules in hydrogen gas, then nothing substantive can change. Thirdly, if interactions and connections are not reflexive, but fixed – such as those between the rods and cogs of a weaving machine, or indeed those between molecules in a container of gas – then we will not find the features we expect of a complex world.

To bring to life how these three conditions work together, I’d like to consider an exam-ple of the dynamics of a family.



The dynamics of a family

If I told you there was a right way to bring up a family, or a right way to conduct a friend-

ship, you would simply not take me seriously. You would most likely tell me that families

are made up of diverse individuals who are all unique, and who impact each other in

complex, and varying and various ways – ways which are interdependent and some-

times mutually reinforcing. I upset you, and you take it out on our younger sister, who

cries, and Dad is furious, and he blames you, but actually it was my fault, so you are furi-

ous with me, and you take that out on our younger sister, and we find ourselves in that

familiar spiral. At this point Mum arrives home, and the dynamics change in response,

but return when Mum goes out. These are reflexive relationships – A affects B and B

affects A back, but if C is there, the relationship between A and B is different and both

impact C … and so on.



11 The essential conditions for complexity 53



In this way, family patterns emerge. And, despite the comings and goings on a day-

to-day basis illustrated above, it is often the case that certain familial patterns of re-

lationships start to establish themselves and sustain over time. These patterns are

unique to each family. Each family will display particular routines and norms – about

eating together, or around commitment to community activities, or expectations

of what can and cannot be discussed. If you visit another family, you will notice the

differences.

Families are also open to the outside world – they are impacted by friends and school,

or the state of the economy, by break ups, or through getting a dog. The boundary

between a family and the wider world is porous, sometimes ambiguous (where does

Grandma fit?), and is not fixed in stone. These outside influences may reinforce patterns

of behaviour or may disrupt them. The family and the people in it will change over time –

sometimes almost imperceptibly and sometimes more substantially. If one person tries

to change, the rest of the family might re-mould itself to accommodate that change, or it

may do the opposite. If something impinges from the outside, such as a war or a lottery

win, things might change radically and rapidly. The situation is never static, but it does

often settle into sustained patterns of behaviour.



A core condition for complexity is reflexive relationality. In the family, connections between people are not independent of each other, but shape and are shaped by the myriad iterative interactions which tend to form a complex web – a relatively stable, self-regulating pattern of relationships. Such a pattern may shift, form and re-form as people interact, react, impact each other, change alliances and respond to different events and situations. But for such patterns to emerge and sustain, they need reflexive multilateral feedback processes.

The second condition focuses on diversity. Each person is unique and changeable; interactions between people also vary and change over time.

Think of a playground: Each child present is unique, and her behaviour varies with circumstance; furthermore, the links and bonds between children will also shift and change, depending on the nature of the play or the vagaries of friendship. There is diversity between classifications (children, teachers, animals), within classifications (each child is unique) and within interactions (the bonds and interactions between players also shift and change).

Without diversity, there is a limit both to the propensity to adapt and the potential for the emergence of something new. Peter Allen named this the ‘law of excess diversity’.22 If we try to quell that diversity – through, for example, controlling who can engage with whom, or by insisting on conformity – then the group or ecology is constrained and less able to adapt and find new modes and ways of being. Maintaining diversity does not happen automatically and may need intervention as I discuss at the end of this piece.

Order [comes] through fluctuation. Prigogine23

54 Section II Process Complexity



The third essential ingredient is that situations need to be open to their surroundings. In the school playground, for example, children can join and leave, and they can be im-pacted by rain, the arrival of a teacher or another class. There are always comings and goings – and this applies to galaxies, and ecologies as well as to human communities.



A note on boundaries

In the example of the playground, it would be relatively straightforward to define

boundaries, but, in general, the boundaries of a complex situation are themselves

a self-organising property. For example, imagine a woodland starting to establish

itself naturally, through seeds dropped by birds or already buried in the soil. As trees

and other plants grow, the woodland may expand and then retreat a little depending

on conditions. The boundaries of the wood are likely to eventually settle in a place

that balances the synergies between the species within the wood against any impact

from what is happening outside the wood. Boundaries, in the same way as patterns of

relationships, are ‘self-organising’. Furthermore, boundaries are inevitably open. Even

a pendulum, a commonly used example of a closed system, is not entirely isolated from

the atmosphere and, over time, grows dusty and rusts.



So complex situations will naturally be open – and this is a necessary condition. In scien-tific terms, isolated closed situations ultimately run out of energy and die. Interactions take energy and, if no additional energy can get in, patterns cannot sustain. An open engagement with the wider world is vital in order to energise and provide resources. Ecologies are sustained through sunlight and the comings and goings of their visitors; cells in the body take in nutrients; societies, at the very least, need food, water and energy. Openness, as opposed to isolation and closedness, is a fundamental necessity in order that enough resources are fed in so that any emerging patterns can sustain.

A closed system view is extremely problematic when we begin to study living systems,

because no living system can be a closed system. Every living system exists within an

environment and has a relationship with that environment. Alfonso Montuori 24

Essentially, then, these are the three essential ingredients, the necessary conditions for a situation to display the characteristic features of a complex world: (1) a set of diverse and potentially diffuse ‘constituents’, (2) interacting in reflective, diverse and varying ways, and (3) situated in a broader context to which it is open and connected.



But are all situations complex?



One final point to consider: Are there situations in the human and natural world where these salient ingredients – of reflexive connection, diversity and openness – do not ex-ist? Are there any cases where we can claim a situation is not complex? I struggle to think of situations within the human and natural world, and indeed in the wider realms of the universe, where these conditions – of diversity, openness and reflexivity – do not apply. However smooth and ordered things may appear on the surface, it is the

11 The essential conditions for complexity 55



myriad weavings of diverse aspects of its constituents that are busy maintaining its order. That said, there are situations where diversity is suppressed or interactions with the wider world are minimised, as exemplified by the flora and fauna of Australia, discussed below.



The flora and fauna of Australia

In Australia, there are examples where environmental and climate conditions are so

stable, the location is so remote, and resources are so scarce, that specialisation is the

only way to survive.25

In these instances, there are few species and little deviation in the patterns of interaction –

between who or what eats whom. For example, all members of one species may congre-

gate on one lake for one night a year to mate. Or a particular insect lives two-thirds up

the stalk of a particular plant (as a different insect lives below). These situations have

evolved in this way in order to make efficient use of resources; but this creates a limit to

resilience. There is little diversity, little adaptation and minimal impact from the wider

context. This ecosystem, at least on the surface, begins to look more machine-like in its

behaviour – stable, measurable, with clear links, and identifiable, fixed patterns. But if

conditions change and these rigid balancing relationships are destroyed, then there is

everything to play for again – such changing conditions may engender collapse, or they

may draw forth and harness the diversity and the latent potential for adaptation that

was already available.



The point is that even when complexity is minimised, it never goes away and to ignore it is to miss the very factors that ultimately create change.

Enforcing conformity in human systems



The same can be said of human systems. Conformity can be imposed through fear and terror. It can also be engendered through recruitment processes which favour certain types of people over others. Rigidity can be enforced by maintaining strict rules of con-duct, and hierarchical organisations can have a stifling effect too. But underneath this surface conformity there is likely to be a seething diversity, a variety of informal or tacit ways of connecting. These hidden connections can form patterns and features that may be discouraged or rendered invisible but are nevertheless present. I discuss this further, in ‘Who got the power’ [70], in relation to societies under dictatorships.

Always open ...



In summary, no situation can entirely be disconnected from the wider world, and this wider engagement is what feeds and animates. Even the most isolated ecosystem is fed by the sun and watered by the rain and will ultimately be impacted by environmen-tal change; even the most isolated human community interacts outside of itself, if only with migrating animals or flowing rivers.





12


Characteristically complex



An introduction to the characteristics of a complex world and how these characteristics

weave together.



In the last piece [11], I described the conditions that engender complexity. These are: openness, ‘messiness’ or diversity, and reflexive relationships

In this piece, I want to explore how these conditions lead to the characteristics of com-plex situations. These characteristics are:

systemic, patterned, particular, path-dependent, paradoxical, punctuated

and emergent



These factors are fundamental to the essence, the ontological ground, of the complex world. I will describe each of them in detail later in this section. But here I sketch out how they weave together. Please note that in this brief summary I do not develop these terms in detail; that is the focus of the pieces that follow, which explore these concepts one by one. These terms are also defined in the Glossary.



How these characteristics weave together



Central to this taxonomy is the notion of patterns – self-organising patterns, consti-tuted relationally out of their intersecting mutually impacting elements, and sustained through their openness to the wider context. Patterning is the ‘binding thread’ of pro-cess complexity. It offers an image of the world as one of ‘emerging, stabilising and sub-sequently dissolving patterns’.

These patterns themselves intersect, and out of such intersections they become part of bigger patterns. It is patterns all the way up and all the way down! And, as I have mentioned and go on to discuss further in [14], these patterns can be thought of as self-reflecting processes. So, process complexity describes a world in process com-prised of processes.

The form of such patterns is particular to its context and particular to the specific nature of its constituents. Detail matters [16].

The development of a particular pattern is in addition affected by chance events, major shocks or sometimes minor variations within its constituent elements. This patterning process underpins a world that is always becoming. What happens is shaped by the particularities of what happens along the way and is thus path–dependent [15]. And, 58 Section II Process Complexity



as things constitute and reconstitute, new qualities may emerge - qualities that may be radically different from what has gone before [19].

The patterning process can go through stages, as discussed in detail in [14]. In certain conditions, patterns may be able to self-regulate and adapt to changing circumstances. Equally, in their early stages, they may be fragile and ephemeral and, yet over time, may become fixed and locked-in, and may also, ultimately, collapse. So, the path of becoming tends to be punctuated. That is to say, it displays periods of stasis during which we can be beguiled into imagining that the situation is stable, and the present will last forever. However, there are also periods of rapid or radical change, which may present as chaos or collapse or, sometimes, as a smoother transition into something radically new. In other words, the path is punctuated by periods of stability followed by periods of rapid change. I consider this process in more detail in [17].

The next quality is paradox [18]. This is a quality of the complex world that is some-times overlooked.26 Paradox is an ambiguous term in itself. I am signifying that the com-plex world does not reduce down to a description that is unequivocally this or that. Paradox, I am arguing, is an ontological feature of the nature of things, exacerbated by what happens at the quantum level, as I describe in ‘Smoke and mirrors’ [73].

What are the implications of this ontological paradox? A pattern may seem stable, but it is also, inevitably, a little unstable. A particular wobble or fluctuation may helpfully stabilise this particular pattern but also may contain the seeds of the pattern’s demise. There is also paradox concerning the edges of a pattern, which can be viewed as ‘here’ but may also be viewed as ‘there’.

Openness is itself [a paradox] for it enables the conditions for the vulnerability of a

system, but conversely also opens up new potentials for resilience and innovation.

Camaren Peter27



In general, in a complex world, there is no definitively ‘right way’, no definite answer, no unequivocal understanding. Life is full of inescapable paradox, ambiguity and un-certainty. The world is less concretely, unequivocally certain than we may like to hope.

Finally, I underline the systemic nature of a complex world [13]. It is only very rarely that we can reduce our descriptions of the world into separable factors whose impact is additive. The world is deeply woven together through all its aspects. This applies at all levels. For example, the formation of a star depends on a complex balance of nu-clear, magnetic and gravitational forces working on the particularities of the clouds of matter and energy that have come together at that point [4]. We cannot explain what happens by separating out these forces; we cannot ignore how their interweaving may evolve and be affected by new conditions. The world is radically systemic – that is to say, there is interweaving down to the smallest scale.

This, then, is what lies behind the model I present of process complexity, a weaving together of these seven qualities. The approach is not radically different from the view

12 Characteristically complex 59



of complexity that has come before, but there is, arguably, more emphasis on process, paradox and patterns. I have also distinguished between the conditions that lead to complexity and the characteristics of complex situations that result; as I have argued, to mix these two can be considered a ‘category error’.

In the remainder of this section, I will unpack, in turn, each of these characteristics of a process complexity ontology, underpinned by the conditions for complexity – open-ness, diversity and reflexive relationality.

In summary, then, the complex world is:

1. Systemic – pervasive, synergistically interconnected and interpenetrative,

irreducible to its parts, with qualities held systemically, not present in its

individual constituents.

2. Patterned – involving the arising, stabilising and dissolving of patterns – patterns
