that intersect, patterns that self-organise; the dance between patterns and events,

the plasticity of patterns; are events really distinct from patterns?

3. Path-dependent – a world of change and flow, shaped by history and context; ‘the

path is made through walking’.

4. Particular – sensitive to context, to the specificities of the situation – which does

not mean you have to know everything.

5. Punctuated – sometimes in stasis, sometimes with tipping points, runaway change

and collapse.

6. Paradoxical – with no easy answers, no incommensurable ‘truths’ and no aspects

which can be reduced to unambiguous certainty.

7. Emergent – emergence as a systemic quality; and ‘radical emergence’ – when

radically novel qualities and characteristics appear – which have not been seen

before and could not have been predicted.





13


Systemic: aspects of process complexity (1)



Systemic decision-making, systemic context analysis, systemic properties as emergence, and

what’s wrong with talking about systems.



The word systemic can be interpreted in more ways than one: First, systemic applies to the complex reality of the way we make decisions.

Second, it describes how features of a society or ecology result from the complex in-terweaving of many factors.

Thirdly, the word systemic is used to label those qualities that do not reside in individ-ual parts but are characteristic of the whole.

All of these aspects is implicated within a complexity worldview.

Decision-making: I will start by looking at decision-making. Our decision-making is systemic when many different factors – social, historical, economic, ecological – weave together to create the final decision. These factors will include discrete events and chance, as well as being shaped by more established patterns and norms. Consider this example of the decision to buy a car.



The decision as to what car to buy weaves in many factors

The decision to buy a certain car will be influenced by our previous history of car owner-

ship – what we liked, what was reliable. Then there will be our budget, our colour pref-

erences, the sort of image we want to portray, the views of our family and friends, the

extent to which we are driven by our response to climate change, who else will use the

car, and so on. And even then, something that happens on the day, such as a persuasive

salesperson or a seemingly extraordinary deal, may tip the balance.



Systemic interweaving: Secondly, the same interweaving takes place in the way the characteristics of ‘place’ are formed. The fragility of a region, for example, does not just reside in its economic factors, but is also impacted by how ‘the economic’ interacts with and impacts the social, political and environmental factors, and also how the dy-namics of these interactions mutually shift and adjust over time.28 We cannot consider these aspects – social, political, economic, environmental – as separate or treat them as additive. There are synergies and antagonisms between such factors – they may mu-tually reinforce or pull apart.

Ignoring such synergies and antagonisms can negatively impact intended outcomes. Below, I explore the example of an irrigation project in Malawi, aimed at improving socioeconomic factors. In this case, the irrigation water had an unintended impact on health as it expanded the breeding grounds for malaria-carrying mosquitoes.

62 Section II Process Complexity



Irrigation in Malawi29

The World Bank funded an irrigation scheme in Malawi, covering 40,000 hectares.

They focused on its economic and social impact but did not consider the potential im-

pact on health. The broader stretches of shallow water created breeding grounds for

malaria-carrying mosquitoes, and homes for the snail that spreads bilharzia. In 2024,

87 percent of children in this region were found to be infected with bilharzia. An ongo-

ing study is assessing the impact of this project on public health.30



Here is another example of systemic interweaving: The pandemic of 2020 resulted from many diverse factors coming together in complex ways. This had a major impact on the global economy as well as on global health, including child mental health; in developed countries it also contributed to a turn to home working and a consequent migration to more rural areas. The likelihood of a pandemic was well-recognised by epidemiologists but was outside the frame of reference of most economists; the learn-ing here is to think much more widely and systemically about the interplay of diverse factors.



The 2020 pandemic

There are many theories about the source of the virus that resulted in the COVID-19

pandemic. One of the more credible is that the pandemic was started by the transfer of

a virus from a pangolin in a market in China, and for the purposes of illustrating system-

ic thinking, I will focus on this theory.31

How did the animal come to be there? Because pangolin scales are regarded in ancient

Chinese medicine as useful for treating inflammation, there is a strong market for them.

For example, pharmaceutical companies in China are licensed to extract the relevant

substance from their scales.32 There is a conjunction between the economic (people

have money to buy pangolin extracts) and the social (a culturally shaped viewpoint, go-

ing back centuries, as to their medicinal properties). Then there is the impact of tech-

nology and wealth in the form of increased global travel; this accelerates and amplifies

the spread of such a virus, limiting the possibility of containment.

Such a global pandemic has far-reaching implications for the global economy, not to

mention on mental health, the cost of health care as well as on working practices, par-

ticularly working from home. The biggest event to impact the global economy in recent

years came from something not unknown to other disciplines, but outside the area of

interest of most economists.



Systemic emergence: Thirdly, the term systemic is also used to signify that certain qualities of the situation do not manifest in the parts but are attributes of ‘the whole’. These qualities are both everywhere and nowhere. Organisational culture is an exam-ple of such a systemic quality, something that has grown out of people’s behaviours and inter-relationships over time and has sometimes also been nudged and shaped by events. The behaviour of slime moulds, of mycelium networks, or the so-called wisdom of crowds are examples of distributed forms of intelligence – an intelligence not held

13 Systemic: aspects of process complexity (1) 63



in any one place by any single component. However, having said these qualities are attributes of ‘the whole’, that does not necessarily mean they are uniform. The culture of customer-service orientation, for example, might be stronger in some parts of the organisation than in others.

These distributed dispersed systemic qualities are often labelled as emergent. They become established only as a result of the complex interwoven interactions and inter-penetrations of the constituents. I discuss emergence and present a more comprehen-sive overview of its meaning in a later piece [19].

The last point I want to make here is that it is through the reflexive, systemic inter-weaving of factors that patterns of relationships emerge and stabilise. I will explore this process of patterning in more detail in the next piece [14].



What’s wrong with talking about systems as opposed to talking

about systemic patterns?



Finally, I want to explore why it can be unhelpful to talk about systems and why it may be preferable to talk about situations being systemic.

Bickering in Budapest



The first time I really considered the implications of the reification of systems was in 2001, at a meeting in Budapest that Peter Allen and others had arranged to discuss all things complex. This meeting was attended by Paul Cilliers, Ralph Stacey and Doug Griffin, amongst many others, and the theme was ‘The Limits of Scientific Knowledge’. My most enduring memory of the meeting was a heated exchange about the impli-cations of speaking about systems. ‘To start there,’ Stacey and Griffin emphasised, ‘is to assume a priori that the world manifests itself as systems – groups of things con-nected, with definable boundaries separating them from other systems and from their environment.’ Their point was that if we start with the idea that systems incon-trovertibly exist, then it can prejudice what we observe or experience – as we may in-advertently wish to confirm our expectations. To assume that systems exist is already to delineate the lens through which we might view what is happening and how to in-tervene. Others in the meeting, more used to modelling or designing systems, did not really see the problem.

Improving health systems



I was reminded of that moment more recently, coming across a project focused on ‘im-proving health systems’. It really struck me that a great deal was potentially wrapped up in defining the issue in that way. For example, did ‘improving health systems’ mean improving the ways a particular network of hospitals and doctors’ surgeries operate? Or did it mean improving the IT systems that allowed information to be shared? Will it 64 Section II Process Complexity



include the determinants of health – the interplay between poverty, housing, isolation, stress, addictions, mental health and so on? Will it consider improving health system-ically through looking at the interplay between public health initiatives and primary health care? Was the health system an attribute of society or of the interventions into that society? My point is that, through defining the task as ‘improving health systems’, the broader consideration of, say, health determinants is potentially exported to the wider context, even treated as ‘exogenous’, rather than seen as either central or inte-gral to the problem.



Coupling between systems?



If a society or community is sufficiently in balance that its qualities become stable and measurable, it is tempting to regard such societies or communities as ‘systems’. This legitimates, for example, talk of ‘acting on the system’ or looking to work with the couplings between systems – rather than remembering that interactions happen be-tween the constituents of the society or community – in particular, between people. While systemic qualities do emerge and may become stable, these qualities cannot be cut away from, or disentangled from, the relating, diverse, interconnected messiness out of which they emerged, and which causes them to sustain. You cannot really act on ‘the system’; you can only, in the main, impact individuals and the connections between them.

As you can see, I am wary of the word system. It suggests something that ‘exists’, some-thing that can be designed and managed, acted upon or changed.



Distinguishing between designing ways of working and describing

the ‘nature of things’



The point I am making about systems is an ontological one. I am asking whether the world tends to show up as ‘systems’, whether ‘systems’ constitute the nature of things. This is not to deny another use of the word ‘system’, to refer to the designed rules and processes of management or control. The claiming of state benefits, for example, re-quires a set of processes and rules (the management system) which clarify the required behaviours of a ‘system’ of professionals, often supported by a computer ‘system’.

It is important, therefore, to clarify whether we are discussing something designed or something innate. Designed systems will work best if they mirror the dynamics of the situation to which they will be applied. I note that management systems, often being rather mechanical in design, do not always work in the ‘real world’ in the way they were intended. This is discussed in ‘Bending the rules’ [52].



13 Systemic: aspects of process complexity (1) 65



This design language can creep across into other spheres. I feel concerned, for example, when we start to talk about ‘ecosystem services’ or even ‘economic systems’ as we run the risk of reifying concepts about the real world. For example, to call aspects of nature, ‘ecosystems services’ raises both ontological and ethical concerns. It can limit the ways we choose to explore the natural world, how we interpret what we find and what we choose to do as a result.

So, when at all possible, without tying myself in linguistic knots, I prefer to talk of the real world in terms of systemic patterns, since this feels much more tentative and ‘allowing’ than describing aspects of the social and natural world in terms of ‘systems’.





14


Patterned: aspects of process complexity (2)



The emergence, stabilising and dissolving of patterns of relationships and the role of

variation in this.



The word pattern has more than one meaning. It can signify something fixed and definite, like the repeating motifs in a knitted cardigan, or the fractal patterns in the florets of Romanesco cauliflowers. It may refer to hidden or hard-to-find patterns – such as those patterns that AI systems can extract from big data sets, that are virtually impossible to find any other way [51]. And it can signify how history – such as the rise and fall of nations or the rise and fall of dictators – often seems (almost) to repeat itself.

History never repeats itself, but it often rhymes. Mark Twain

The term pattern in the context of complexity refers to:

the systemic outcome of mutually reinforcing relationships that are temporally

or spatially situated

In many ways, I would prefer to use the word patterning to pattern.33 It conveys to an even greater degree that sense of something temporary, tentative and subjective – subject to wobblings and variation, less than definite. Qualities of patterns are depen-dent on the particularity of the way things interact and re-interact – strengthening some pathways and weakening others; these qualities are rarely entirely static. But, in order to avoid overburdening the text, I will stick in the main to the word pattern.

As I discussed in [11], for such patterns to emerge and stabilise in the complex world requires three conditions – openness to the wider environment; reflexive, reflective interactions between micro-ingredients; and diversity. Patterns may emerge out of ‘chaos’.34 They may become fairly stable and may persist, sometimes, over very long timescales. However, the reality is that they are constantly shifting and changing, due to variation, shocks, shifts in the wider environments, changes within individuals and changes in relationships. Indeed, it is these variations to the norm, these ‘micro-events’, that enable resilience and self-regulation, and lead to a relationally sustained sort of stability. And, paradoxically, this very same mechanism of variation can also destabilise patterns and cause them either to collapse or to shift into new forms with new emer-gent qualities. The very process that leads to stability ultimately leads to its collapse. This is a very interesting aspect of the processes of patterning that complexity theory brings to light.



68 Section II Process Complexity



The dynamics of pattern-forming and pattern-dissolving



In this piece, I want to bring alive the way patterns form, stabilise and self-regulate, then dissolve. This dynamic perspective on patterning is central to a processual view of complexity. The work that, in my view, best illustrates the process of pattern- forming and pattern-dissolving is that of C. S. Holling describing the life cycle of forests, as explored in Panarchy.35 I will briefly summarise this work.



The ecological life cycle: chaos – self-organisation – self-regulation – maturity –

lock-in – collapse36

Self-organisation out of chaos: Holling begins by describing a nascent forest, as it

starts to get established.

Initially, there is chaos, or randomness – no repeating connections, lots of diversity, no

sustaining forms. One part will look very much like another; there is much potential.

The nascent forest has to be open to its wider context for ‘new ingredients’ to arrive. In-

teractions, engendering the beginnings of mutual interdependency, lead to patterning.

Some of these nascent interconnecting patterns will survive and thrive, and some will

be less successful and disappear.

As the forest growth continues, its components become increasingly linked together,

and through this process, the forest evolves more ways of regulating itself and main-

taining its stability. This is a description of the self-organising process. Self-organisa-

tion is driven by chance beginnings (whatever happens to turn up in the growing forest,

what seeds birds drop, which animals wander in) and haphazard connections (shall I

eat this or eat that, can I find a place to mate here or there). Which of these new con-

nections sustains depends on how well they work (that was a good place to bring up my

young) and on what competition there is (I too want to eat that; I too want to live in that

hollow).

Self-regulation describes the process of maintaining the same basic pattern through

wobbling and shifting, that is through micro-adjustments. It is the variation between and

within the ingredients that causes the pattern to find resilience and dynamic stability

– in the face of competition and in the face of any threatening external factors. In this

way, nascent patterns start to stabilise.

Maturity and pruning: As one pattern between particular species (say the link between

soil composition, bluebells, conifers and particular insects) gets established and is suc-

cessful, other competing patterns (the wood anemone and wood sorrel) “are pruned

away”. Any new possibilities may be locked out if they cannot compete with what is al-

ready established. The diversity of species and organisms slows down and may even

decline.

Lock-in, loss of resilience and collapse: But, for the forest to be able to find resilience to

the inevitable variations that happen – due to shocks, changes in climate, availability of

nutrients, and competition for resources – it needs to find a balance between efficien-

cy and the ability to experiment with alternative pathways and patterns. This requires

‘excess diversity’, creating some ‘slack’.37 Otherwise the forest’s ever-greater efficiency

14 Patterned: aspects of process complexity (2) 69



eventually reduces its capacity to cope with severe outside shocks and it becomes “rigid

and brittle”.38 It may, indeed, reach a tipping point at which just one more small nudge

may cause collapse of form. This collapse may result in a return to chaos; or it may result

in the runaway success of one rogue species. I think of Himalayan balsam ransacking

the banks of some British rivers, wiping out all native species and then, because it dies

down to nothing in the winter, leading to their collapse – and in effect destroying the

very territory it conquered.

However, collapse is not all bad. If the ecosystem collapses, it can liberate an “enormous

potential for creativity and allow for novel and unpredictable recombination of its ele-

ments”.39 We are back to the beginning. The forest may regenerate in a new form.



But are forests identical to the social world?



I came across the above description of the life cycle of a forest a long time ago, and it was a game changer in terms of my understanding. It really brought home to me the role of variation in the establishing of reflexive and dynamically stable patterns. Varia-tion – which could be due to new ‘invaders’, new choices made by existing incumbents, or variations in the size or age of the flora or fauna – creates the wherewithal for the stabilising of mutually beneficial patterns of relationships. But variation also provides the source of its destruction.

This description of the forest captures the tension between – the dance between – di-versity and efficiency, structure and change, stability and creativity. And it emphasises the way that the ‘unit’ of interest, the unit with agency, is not the species or individual animal or plant, but the ecological pattern.

The social world is not necessarily as tidy as a forest that has been largely left to its own devices. The social world does not trot so inexorably from birth to death. If we look at social systems, differing parts of the system may be at different stages in the lifecycle; there may be certain aspects that are very locked in, such as long-standing ethnic distinctions. At the same time, there may be other aspects that are maturing and effective, such as functioning laws and systems of governance. There may be yet further aspects that are emerging and forming, like new civil society or new entrants to certain markets fuelled by opportunities created by new technology. Thus, the differ-ing lifecycle stages, undertaken in a more stepwise fashion in an isolated forest, may, in social settings, overlap, intertwine and mutually impact each other.

The self-organising propensity of societies was remarked upon by Friedrich Hayek, who said:

Purposive structures might grow up which owed little or nothing to design, which were

not invented by a contriving mind but arose from the separate actions of many men

who did not know what they were doing.40

70 Section II Process Complexity



Do we always find patterns?



It is important to recognise, however, that the world does not always show up in the form of dynamically stable patterns. Great social and environmental upheavals can also result in chaos. Equally, very controlling regimes sitting on top of ethnic dissents may display a seeming order that hides both a significant potential for disorder as well as masking some hidden patterns of resistance. Patterns can balance adaptability and efficiency – but in situations of instability or, paradoxically, in situations of tight con-trol, they will struggle to become established. The world is always complex and has a tendency to patterning – but we have to start by exploring the dynamics and norms of what is happening and not make a priori assumptions about what we might find. One can argue, indeed, that a tendency towards chaos is a not uncommon social pattern!



And are patterns really distinct from events?



One of the phrases that attracted attention from Embracing Complexity was the idea of a ‘dance between patterns and events’.41 We were using the word events to include big events – like a tsunami, and smaller events – like a new manager. There are also micro-events – such as a chance encounter, or a seemingly innocent or minor choice. Events big or small can contribute to stability and resilience. But they can also invade, as Peter Allen described it, and destabilise. There is an irresolvable paradoxical dance: too little variation or too stable a context, and we lose resilience even as we gain effi-ciency; too much variation and it may be too costly to maintain all that diversity. Equal-ly, some particular event or some large fluctuation may tip the balance and catalyse a shift into a new state.

Process complexity causes us to question whether patterns and events are really distinct. Sometimes what will destabilise the status quo is the rising of a new pattern which starts to suck energy and resources into it. This might be a new way of shop-ping or a new emerging galaxy which devours matter from nearby constellations. The ‘event’ of a new pattern can shift the existing pattern.

Maybe it is more accurate to think about large patterns and small patterns – or long-lived or more fleeting patterns – than to speak about patterns and events as if they are necessarily different in kind.



A new CEO – pattern or event?

The arrival of a new CEO will constitute an event to an organisation. But in truth, the

new boss does not come unencumbered. Maybe she is part of a network known for new

ideas and ways of working, and this is why she was chosen? The CEO will inevitably

be part of other social patternings – family, community and peer group – and will

carry these wider connections with her when she is at work. Should she be construed

as an ‘event’ acting on the organisation, or is the new boss better thought of as an

aspect of pre-existing, intersecting social patterns that engage with the patterning of the

organisation she is joining in a way that changes and shifts and re-shifts all of them?

14 Patterned: aspects of process complexity (2) 71



So, the description of the shaping of the future as a ‘dance between patterns and events’ can be extended and reframed, in a processual view, as a dance between long patterns and short patterns, or indeed between long events and short events. Not as catchy though! It is intriguing to find that Rovelli, in the field of quantum gravity [6], and Ames and Hall describing the cosmology of Daoism [23], make very similar statements.

In similar vein, Dupré, discussing the nature of organisms in Everything Flows: towards a processual theory of biology, states that: “An organism, whatever else it may be, is an event – something happening.”42



In summary



I have covered rather a lot of ground in this piece. First, I discussed the primacy of dynamically stable patterns of relationships, which draw their stability from a level of variation and a degree of natural selection in the reflexive relationships that become established. Complex, mutual, reflexive interactions at the granular micro-level can create relatively stable forms or patterns at the macro-level.

Then, through working with the life cycle of the forest, I explored how patterns may change over time and may, under certain conditions, become rigid and less resilient to shocks.

Finally, I considered, in line with a processual view of complexity, that there is no real categorizable distinction between patterns and events.

Prigogine was intrigued by the emerging patterns in particular chemical mixes. I devel-op, in [11], the example of how the patterns of relationships and norms of behaviour derive in families. We find similar patterns of interaction and dependence in ecologies – at the level of forests, oceans or organisms. The universe of galaxies is patterned. The brain is patterned in its repeating neural networks, leading to sustained patterns of thinking [44]. We find patterning in societal and organisational cultural norms, in pow-er relations, and in beliefs.

Patterns, I am arguing, constitute our world, and articulating the way in which patterns form and dissolve provides a powerful narrative of change.





15


Path-dependent: aspects of process complexity (3)



Exploring the dynamics of past, present and future and considering the meaning behind ‘the

path is made through walking’.

We need to explore how the past shaped the present and how the present is

co-responsible for the future. Kurt Richardson and Paul Cilliers43



The future is dependent on what happens on the way; that hardly seems like a con-troversial statement. The future does not follow smoothly from the present, can-not be predicted, and is shaped but not determined by the particularities of history and context. The future is also impacted by chance events. Thorstein Veblen, in Why isn’t Economics an Evolutionary Science, written in 1898, coined the phrase “dispassionate cumulative causation” to express the nature of this process.44

In this piece, I want to unpick various aspects of path dependency: the role of expecta-tions, the impact of chance, the weaving together of history, context, events, choices and values, and the way the ‘path is made through walking’.45 If the future is co-created, then, as Paul Cilliers argued, ethics imbues everything.



Envisaging path-dependency

Sometimes, in teaching these ideas, I share a picture of a large wave breaking on the

shore. I ask: ‘What creates the shape of that particular wave, and determines the way it

breaks at that particular time?’ Students find it easy to recall many of the features that

have an impact – the phase of the moon, water temperature, the slope of the beach, the

wind, the time of the year. But they often fail to include two other important factors:

one is history and the second is the role of chance. Regarding history, maybe there was

a tsunami two days earlier across the ocean, or an iceberg collapsed hundreds of miles

away? Such historical events will have an impact on the shape of that wave that combine

with the impact of the local conditions and the gravitational pull of the moon.

With respect to chance, maybe two seconds before the wave was about to break, a

whale surfaced, or a submarine?

The outcome depends on the complex weave of all these factors coming together. So,

path-dependency is not as simple as a series of logical causal steps as encapsulated in

the saying “for want of a nail, the shoe was lost, for want of a shoe the horse was lost”. It

is multi-factorial, intertwined and complex!



74 Section II Process Complexity



The role of the imagination



It is interesting to consider that path-dependency is also impacted by what we imagine about the future. I like the following quote, by Prigogine.

In living systems, the behaviour at a given time is partly determined by memory and

partly by the anticipation of the future. … In this sense, the future contributes to the

present.46



However, sometimes the need to respond rapidly to situations can make it very dif-ficult to pursue intended goals and intentions; decisions, in some circumstances, are primarily determined by immediate concerns, and the vision of the desired future holds little sway. The following example – considering the actual journeys taken by asylum seekers, and how these differ from the expectations of both themselves and policy makers – illustrates the point.



The path-dependent process of asylum seekers47

The decision-making of asylum seekers starts in their imagination some time before

their departure – the hoped-for new life as seen on the movies or infused with the de-

scriptions of those already in their new country (who might be exaggerating to cover

up their shame of not living a successful life). Would-be asylum seekers are also af-

fected by the motivations of their families, wanting someone – often a young male –

to pioneer the way, and be able to send for others.

As they travel, their decision-making is constrained by their evolving situation, and is

often adaptive, impacted by ‘rapidly evolving circumstances’ requiring ‘rapid evidence

assessment’. Their emerging trajectory is a complex weave of factors – chance encoun-

ters with smugglers or with other migrants, weather, sudden opportunities, health and

money. Evidence suggests that their choice of destination will change many times on

the way; evidence also shows that the policies of the country to which they are headed

appear to have little impact. What has greatest impact is the hearsay swapped between

travelling migrants and the sometimes ‘talked up’ stories of those who have arrived at

their destination.

This has an interesting implication for government policies in relation to migrants. Such

policies can often seem based on stereotypical, dehumanising views of asylum seekers,

seeing them as ‘undesirable’, or as selfish in that they are trying to ‘jump the queue’. Re-

search suggests that government policy and the behaviour of migrants is largely decou-

pled, and governments seem to base policies on inaccurate notions of how policy might

deter them.



The impact of events



The story of asylum seekers illustrates the impact of chance events.

15 Path-dependent: aspects of process complexity (3) 75



Chance events can be hugely influential in shaping the future. For example, in 1348, the plague in the UK killed off so many serfs that, as a result, farm workers were able to demand better conditions and be released from serfdom. This catalysed a huge shift in social and economic patterns. History is conditioned in this way; certain events or fac-tors shift the conditions, which can allow something not previously possible to happen.

Adam Moore explores this theme. He explains, in The Eventfulness of Social Reproduc-tion, that some social scientists theorise that there are periods of structural stabili-ty followed by significant events that may radically change things. He, in resonance with the complexity perspective, sees structure and events not as sequential but as co-evolving. Moore says:

Casting structure and events as irreconcilable opposites does not lead to an explana-

tion of how events can effect change in social structures, or, in turn, how the outcome

of events is impacted by the structures they transform. Moore48



This ‘dance between patterns/structures and events’ is discussed further in [14].



There is more than one future, but not an infinite number; the

‘path is made through walking’



Peter Allen used to unsettle people back in the day by asserting that there is more than one future. I am not quite sure why people found this so upsetting other than it is another recognition of the limits to determinism. He was suggesting that, whilst the past constrains and shapes the future, the future is nevertheless not fixed. There is another way to say this: When things happen, when “possibilities become actualities”49 then some future possibilities become unavailable. Stuart Kauffman refers to this nar-rowing of options as non-ergodicity, discussed in [8]. In choosing to live in the south, or study this subject not that, I shape and constrain my future options. The future is not an open-ended flat terrain where anything is possible, but a terrain that is shaped along the way by what we and others choose.

To put it another way, the future is co-created. The path both shapes and is shaped by actions, intentions, chances, choices and everything that happens along the way. The Buddhist version of this is the idea of karma.50 Daoists say that ‘the path is made through walking’.51

I particularly like the notion that ‘the path is made through walking’. I think it contains the heart of what path-dependency is about. First, I join a path that has been made through the walking others have done before me. Second, I don’t make the path alone – I am affected by the ‘footsteps’ of others around me. Third, the future path will follow on from where we have got to, and the future trajectory will be constrained by the path already taken, and yet is still open to possibilities. Fourth, something unexpected might happen – a boulder arrives out of the blue which destroys part of the path and chang-es the game. The point is, that there is no certain path already laid down that we can merely follow.

76 Section II Process Complexity



We experience what Catherine Keller, in On the Mystery,52 calls ‘creative freedom’ – but our freedom is constrained by the current conditions.

If the world is an open-ended process of interactions, it is because we may exercise

choice in the way we influence each other’s becomings and the way we shape our own

becoming out of the manifold of influences. We are indelibly marked by our past. We

cannot escape the process of being influenced and of influencing. But we may exercise

creative freedom within it. Keller53



The implications of path dependency: seed the system with good

ingredients?



The end cannot justify the means for the simple and obvious reason that the means

employed determine the nature of the ends produced. Aldous Huxley

If our collective actions shape the future for us all, what does that mean for personal practice? Paul Cilliers, in particular, thought deeply about the implications of path de-pendence; for him, it brought attention to the issue of ethical choice [32]. Building on Huxley’s famous quote, we cannot just say that the end justifies the means because what we do on the way – the means – determines the end. If we make choices – say to lie – in order to achieve an end, the lie enters the system. Was it worth it? We still can’t be sure if ‘the lie’ will achieve what we set out to achieve, but the lie plays a role in co-creating the future norms of those it impacts. For Cilliers, path-dependency and the impossibility of determining the future highlighted the importance of acting ethi-cally. Every choice, in his view, is a moral choice. Regimes that condone the murder of civilians tend to become regimes that lock-in the condoning of the murder of civilians. Murdering civilians will have an impact on the culture whether or not the desired out-come, the reason for murdering civilians, is achieved.



Lock-in



There is one final and very important issue to raise and that is lock-in. This is a theme I explore in some detail in ‘Who got the power?’ [70]. History shows us how the accumu-lation of power or wealth can shape society in such a way that things become very hard to change, as discussed in ‘The dynamics of pattern-forming and pattern-dissolving’ in [14]. This is called historical institutionalism.54 However, no matter how locked-in situations become, history also shows us that things do change, even if that change is, initially, into collapse. Path dependency from a complexity perspective is always a weaving together of the impacts of the past, together with the impact of changes in the wider environment, together with chance, fluctuations and choice. We may look back and gain a sense of the path that has emerged, but the future is less easy to determine.

15 Path-dependent: aspects of process complexity (3) 77



In conclusion



The term path-dependency signifies the way the past shapes but does not determine the present, and the way the present limits but does not determine the future. It allows for the way events or changes may ‘invade’ existing patterns and norms and so cause them to shift or collapse. It emphasises that change and stasis do not follow sequentially, one after the other, but are intertwined, woven together. As Prigogine pointed out, it is easy to want stability to last and focus on its attributes in a ‘scientific’ manner, while banishing instability to the historical study of key events and key ‘men’.55

We may want to “cast structure and events as irreconcilable opposites”56 – but in real-ity, they are not.





16


Particular: aspects of process complexity (4)



The particularity of detail has an impact on what happens. But when patterns stabilise,

we do not need to know all the detail to get to grips with what is there. However, it is also

important to pay attention to any minute signs of emerging change.



One aspect of the processual worldview that is implicit in what has been covered so far is the importance of the particularity of history and context. The particular ingredients and the particular sequence of events and relationships that have led the local present is material to which ‘present’ has emerged. There is sensitivity to context, to the availability or not of diverse ingredients, to chance and to subtle variations. If this particularity is averaged out statistically, as discussed in ‘The raw and the cooked’ [31], we can become blind to the very mechanisms that have co-created what is here, and which shape how it might change. The process of change is fundamentally deter-mined by this very detail, the ‘minute particulars’; what is there, how did it get there, how do and did things interact, what patterns of relationships have emerged, how stable are they, and how might they be destabilised?

He who would do good to another must do it in minute particulars: general Good is the

plea of the scoundrel, hypocrite, and flatterer, for art and science cannot exist but in

minutely organised particulars. William Blake

Attention to particularity can sound menacing, as it can seem to suggest that we need to know everything about everything before we can act. But in practice, what tends to emerge from the process of reflexive interactions between things, are patterns and forms. These patterns and forms can be relatively stable and require relatively few pa-rameters (compared to the number of the micro-elements out of which they are con-stituted) to capture their qualities. For example, if I want to describe the culture of an organisation – considered as a patterning of relationships – then I can probably do that well enough in half a dozen words. I don’t need to know what everyone is doing or thinking at all times. This accords with our experience of the world, a world which we often find relatively stable and predictable. Indeed, sometimes the world seems so fixed that we despair of anything ever changing. For some of us, at least, life is not in general chaotic or random.

Having said that, there are, of course, many places where chaos is the norm; my news-paper alerts me to that every day. Engaging with any situation requires an understand-ing of the particular dynamics at play. It is worth noting that even where there is chaos, it is likely that there will remain some familial and social patterns that provide some coherence.

80 Section II Process Complexity



Things start here and now



One of the core tenets of complexity theory is that what emerges has its roots in the ‘here and now’. This was at the heart of Darwin’s insights – that evolution is initiated by a particular situation. To give an example: It was on a particular pond, on a particular Tuesday afternoon that Fred the frog was born with longer legs than his siblings. If this gives Fred an advantage over his rivals and he ‘gets the girls’, then this may trigger the beginnings of a change to the frog population. If these long-legged frogs also have an advantage in other places too, places where conditions are not necessarily the same, then such a change might spread and become established.

The point is that what emerges more globally starts with the local. Darwin catalysed a change to the way science was understood; science, prior to Darwin, tended to focus on universal laws and predictability. This recognition of the power of the particular changed the game; this is discussed in more detail in ‘Unity and uniqueness’ [5] and in ‘Darwin and evolution’ [7].



Systems versus complexity perspectives



One of the imputations that might be levelled at systems perspectives, rather than complexity perspectives, is that systems perspectives tend to preference the getting to grips with current patterns of relationships and place emphasis on methods to achieve that. This can lead us to infer that if we understand present patterns, this will provide a good grasp on what might unfold in the future.

Those working with complexity perspectives do also focus on patterns of relationships but are just as interested in spotting any signs of instability or signs of newness. Com-plexity theory does not, as a general rule, indicate that the present is a good indicator of the future. The complexity practitioner is looking out for relatively stable patterns, which can often be described using relatively few terms, but at the same time is look-ing for signs of impending instability and nascent change. Attuning to early signs of change is a subtle process compared with recognising, describing and defining current relatively stable patterns [27]. This movement in attention – between what is stable and what is unstable, between what is established and what is new – is the stuff of complexity.

Much of the practice implied by embracing complexity – be it research, strategy development, change, impact assessment or ‘living well’ – requires an approach contingent on circumstances. The future depends on the particularities of the situation. This is explored in detail in Section VII(c).





17


Punctuated: aspects of process complexity (5)



The nonlinear ‘lumpiness’ of change, change can happen in ‘fits and starts’.



Process complexity points to a certain non-uniform ‘lumpiness’ or texturing in the processual evolving of social and natural systems. There are times of relative sta-

sis, and there are times of incremental drift, when not much happens. There are other times when there is a shift into something radically, and irreversibly new. This accords with the idea of punctuated equilibrium, proposed by palaeontologists Stephen Jay Gould and Niles Eldredge.57 Punctuated equilibrium describes the way that that evo-lution occurs in spurts – in ‘fits and starts’.58 There are long periods of relative stability with little activity in terms of extinctions or the emergence of new species, interrupted by intermittent bursts of activity.

This idea fits very well with complexity thinking, which emphasises that change is nat-urally episodic,59 and occurs at irregular intervals. A ‘tipping point’ is the name given to the juncture at which there is the start of significant change, one that results, ultimate-ly, in a new era. It often signifies a so-called ‘regime shift’,60 whereby the qualities and characteristics after the tip are substantially different from those before; new prop-erties have emerged [19]. The regime shift is not always contained or smooth; there may be runaway change, as in the take-off of a pandemic, or there may be collapse. These are also likely to be nonlinear effects – small changes can lead to exponential outcomes, as discussed further in [67].

There are a few warnings to make here: Talking of tipping points can place all our at-tention on the ‘straw that broke the camel’s back’, to use a rather unattractive image. A sandpile seems stable until the point when one last grain of sand tips the balance and results in a cascade. There is a danger, though, in focusing on the ‘final straw’ or ‘last grain’ rather than on the conditions that led up to it. If we want to engender stability, we need to pay attention much earlier as to whether the system as a whole is becoming less diverse, more rigid, and less resilient. We also need to notice whether there are new factors appearing which might take hold.

Climate scientists, for example, have been warning of the effects of greenhouse gases since at least the 1960s. Scientists such as Rachel Carson61 recognised that the eco-logical picture as a whole, the systemic interweaving of many factors, was pointing towards irreversible climate change. She and others were advising that early interven-tion could prevent the arrival of that point of no return, when it becomes too late to reverse warming – a point which many scientists now fear we are already past.

One of the problems with nonlinear, episodic, punctuated change – and I discuss this in more detail in ‘Run, run, runaway’ [67] – is that we Western-educated citizens have a tendency to cling to the notion of an incremental, linear world. We do not, on some level, really believe that the status quo won’t go on forever. We behave as if we believe 82 Section II Process Complexity



that, if there is a little bit too much climate change, we can just reverse it by switching off the oil for a while or doing a bit more recycling. However, attuning ourselves to the idea of radical, irreversible change may be fundamental to our survival.



Nonlinear change may signify that a new ingredient is entering

the mix



There is a telling paragraph in Embracing Complexity couched in the language of non- linearity.62 Here we point out that if an existing balance between two factors in connection starts to change, this will be viewed as a nonlinear relationship emerging between the two things. But – and this is the key point – what it often signifies is not that there is a change in relationship between the things we knew were there, but that some new ingredient is entering into the mix.



Tipping points versus fluctuations



Another issue to consider is that we cannot be sure, until later, that we have really gone through a tipping point and entered a new regime. We cannot always tell the dif-ference between a fluctuation that, in the end, did not catch on, and one that may be-come more permanent. Sometimes only time will tell.

It is like waking up with a sore throat. Is this evidence of the beginning stages of a cold, or will it resolve over the course of the day? This argument has been used in question-ing climate change. Is what we are experiencing a real shift, or just a bit of a fluctuation in the climate metrics? Unfortunately, as I write this in 2024, the evidence of unprec-edented shifts in temperature, ice cover, salination, and extreme weather events con-veys such a compelling picture that only the very determined would now be able to refute it.

Punctuated equilibrium reminds us that while complex interweaving is always occur-ring, there remain periods when everything appears fixed and stable. We can, during these stable periods, be prone to taking them for granted. During periods of stasis, we find that we are able to identify the stable and often almost linear causal links through using standard analytical tools. We gain confidence as we successfully predict the out-come of a new intervention or investment strategy. Classical economics was built on the idea that economies only shift incrementally from stability, and hence modelling them in times of stability, as with stable systems in classical physics, can be effective. Models, it is true, can work well during periods of locked in stability.

So, what is the problem? The problem, in part, is that we appear to be living in an age of turbulence, where change in very many aspects of planetary life – economic, polit-ical, health, climate – is fast and furious. So, any appearance of stability is likely to be short-lived. Secondly, if stability has been artificially held in place through hierarchy or

17 Punctuated: aspects of process complexity (5) 83



fear or minimising diversity and adaptability, conditions can be particularly prone to sudden change.

It is also easy to be lulled into complacency when models seem to be working well and are providing seemingly reliable predictions. This may cause us to take our eye off the ball, and hence we may miss any signs of emerging instability or fail to notice new fac-tors starting to take hold. We may become blind to those very conditions and factors that lead towards the tipping into new regimes.

The strategist who merely optimises the present and is taken by surprise at unexpect-ed radical shifts, is never going to change the world. Conversely, those strategists who spot gaps and opportunities, nascent shifts in current behaviours, and emerging signs that there are untapped niches – these are the people who ride with the waves, and who identify and amplify any favourable conditions that are starting to reveal them-selves [56].





18


Paradoxical: aspects of process complexity (6)



Exploring why paradox is such a central feature of a complex world.



A paradox, according to Wikipedia, is a logically self-contradictory statement, one that runs contrary to one’s expectation. Para doxa is translated as ‘against common sense’. It usually signifies that there are conflicting-yet-interrelated aspects of a situa-tion that exist simultaneously and persist over time.

Carlo Rovelli and Chris Clarke bring the idea of paradox right back to the level of the quantum. The thought experiment, Schrödinger’s Cat63 – which describes the idea that the mythical ‘quantum cat’ can be both alive and dead – is one expression that brings paradox to life and shows that the physical world incorporates paradox at its core [73].

In the complex world, there are many situations which display a quality of paradoxical incommensurability, whereby two or more opposing factors may be existent and per-tinent at the same time. So, in a process complexity perspective, we are often handling the co-existence of incompatible opposites. And this is at the level of ontology; para-dox is an aspect of ‘the way things are’ – an aspect of existence, not just a feature of the difficulties of knowing or engaging with the world. The distinction between ontology and epistemology is discussed in ‘Metaphysics and chocolate’ [2].

Ecologies, organisations, communities and indeed galaxies contain paradoxical quali-ties in many ways. They display both structure and variation, are both stable and un-stable, both fixed and flowing. Their future is neither random nor certain. Boundaries are not merely a matter of opinion, nor are they objectively existent. Patterns of rela-tionships meld into the wider world and yet have a degree of definition. There needs to be variation in order to create stability, but that same variation may catalyse instabili-ty. These are examples of the paradoxical stuff of the processual complex world.

Paradox implies there are no easy answers. Indeed, it implies there are no right answers that can be found through logical analysis of definitive data, in part as data itself – in-deed the world itself – is ontologically paradoxical. Many situations are a conundrum; it all depends.

Many of the pieces I include further on in this book look at the implications for practice of the paradox that is at the heart of the complexity ontology. How does this impact ‘ways of knowing’ [Section V]? Should we, for example, use what Goethe called ‘con-templative knowing’ [40]? What does embracing paradox mean for how to engender change [57] or how to approach politics or governance [70]? How, for example, do we find ways to trade off freedom and responsibility or – even more complex – liberty, equality and fraternity? Can we find ways of going beyond the logical mind, allowing a more integrative, subjective engagement with the situation [37]? Working ‘critically’ 86 Section II Process Complexity



with others, grappling with many perspectives can facilitate fresh insights, and novel ‘out of the box’ solutions [36].

There is a fundamental irreducibility to reality that we can never dispel … reality is lost

by being abstracted. Camaren Peter64



I describe in ‘Polarities, paradox and the middle ground’ [60] an approach to working with polar opposites and encouraging a ‘third way’ to emerge – a sort of transcend-ing, both-and integrative insight or outcome. This contemplative process is where ‘mystery’ can enter, the crack in the logic which allows the light in, to misquote Leonard Cohen. This is not a compromise, not merely a question of finding a mid-point between opposites; it is the surfacing of something excitingly, radically ‘new’. This is known as Hegel’s dialectic: “An interpretive method in which the contradiction between a prop-osition and its antithesis is resolved at a higher level of truth”.65

This process of transcendence is based on what Plato called the “logic of the included middle”.66 As Camaren Peter puts it, “there will be fundamentally irresolvable, ‘unde-cidable’ factors that occupy the territory of the middle”.67 In other words, you can’t find a way to resolve irresolvable differences through ‘absolute’ logic and analysis.

The present knows no absolutes, only uniqueness. Peter68



Peter continues: “[The solution] requires learning, participation, integration and negotiation, i.e., strategies that engage directly with the contextual specificities … and which can possibly lead to their resolution … under certain context-specific condi-tions.”69 Clarke, referring to quantum physics, calls this context-specific logic [73].

The point is that we have to tussle with these paradoxes and not rescue ourselves by flipping to one pole or another. Anyone can build an efficient organisation that works like a machine; anyone can build a flexible, fluid, adaptable organisation ready for anything. But to find a way both to be able to survive now and have the capacity and breadth to succeed into the future is much harder [59]. The aim is not to find a compro-mise, but to allow the paradox to act as ‘grit in the oyster’ so that a new kind of solution can surface.

Much more could be said about paradox. I refer you to Chris Mowles’ book Managing in Uncertainty: Complexity and the Paradoxes of Everyday Organisational Life.70

One must not think ill of paradox, for paradox is the passion of thought, and the thinking

without the paradox is like the lover without passion … the ultimate paradox of thought

[is] to want to discover something that thought itself cannot think. Søren Kierkegaard





19


Emergent: aspects of process complexity (7)



Exploring different aspect of the meaning of emergence.



Finally, there is emergence, and I’ve left the trickiest until last. Emergence is a term deployed in many different ways. It is used colloquially and

loosely to mean that something new has appeared – as in ‘light emerged out of dark-ness’. It can also signify qualities that are systemic – not present in individual parts [13]. The classic example is the wetness of water. Wetness is a systemic property that is not characteristic of the hydrogen and oxygen atoms of which it is constituted; wetness is often described as an emergent property.

In this piece, I want to deconstruct the idea of emergence. I consider the way systemic properties are sometimes reversible and sometimes not; I also suggest that emergence is not necessarily systemic. And I describe the notion of radical emergence. Emergence is complex!



Emergence equated with systemic properties



Many self-organised patterns that have become stabilised and in balance will have sys-temic qualities; the particular qualities depend on the particular way the ingredients which make them have combined. Another arrangement of the same ingredients could lead to different systemic qualities.

Let me give an example: Imagine a group coming together for the first time to trek across Greenland. Over a few days, friendships will be made, and routines, habits and a sort of ‘culture’ will be established. If weather conditions change rapidly and there is a crisis, things may change as a result. After the crisis, will the ‘old’ culture be re-estab-lished, or will the crisis have irreversibly changed the norms?

The shifts in relationships between the trekkers as a response to the weather allowed a different culture (a systemic property) to emerge in the trekking group. There are no new ‘ingredients’ – but one culture emerged, then another took over, and maybe a third might result at a later stage.

The culture of a group, as Gregory Bateson points out, is likely to have a normalis-ing effect on individuals and impact future behaviour. The ‘manners’ or norms of the group are sensed by individuals and play a role in shaping their and the group’s future behaviour.71

88 Section II Process Complexity



There are many examples of such systemic behaviour in the natural world too. The flocking behaviour seen in murmurations, the way slime moulds navigate a maze in search of food, the behaviour of ants and bees – all these are examples of collective behaviour whereby ‘intelligence’ emerges in the aggregate.

There are a couple of points that I want to raise about equating emergence with systemic qualities.

Let me use murmurations to make the first point; the emergent qualities of these mur-murations do not substantially change as the birds swirl around the sky, and there are no radical differences between murmurations. The nature of the movement is constrained and is recognisable; there are no surprises. This is similar to water; once you’ve experi-enced that water is wet, it is no longer a revelation.

The second point is exemplified by ants. Ants can display different modes of behaviour; if they are responding to a threat rather than to a new food source, for example, the pattern of behaviour is markedly different. However, these changes are reversible; the ants will return to ‘feeding mode’ or ‘reproducing mode’ when the threat is passed.

In my mind, the systemic qualities displayed by ants and murmuring birds – qualities that exist only in the collective – are a weak form of emergence in that they remain within expected limits.

However, things are not always like this. There are also situations when things tip irreversibly into a new regime and radical new systemic properties emerge. For example, there is a substantial difference between the qualities of snow when it first falls, and when it has partially melted and refrozen. These are irreversible shifts in the systemic qualities of the snow; the snow cannot return to how it was.



Emergence that is not systemic



The examples described thus far are all examples – some reversible, some not revers-ible – of emergence as a description of systemic properties held by the whole. However, to muddy the waters, emergent properties, in my view, are not always systemic.

For example, in a society where power is in the hands of the few, there may emerge dis-tinct groups who lead very different lives. A small group – let’s say of the fabulously rich and powerful – may emerge. But in this example, emergence is not, I would argue, a col-lective, systemic feature of the whole. However, the emergence of a new group, or new technology or new habit may ultimately lead to systemic change as discussed below.



Radical emergence



I’m now going to make things even more complicated and introduce what Stuart Kauffman calls radical emergence.72 Radical emergence can occur in situations when

19 Emergent: aspects of process complexity (7) 89



there is an addition or subtraction to the ‘dimensions’ of the problem – such as a new technology, or the extinction or exodus of some of the ‘ingredients’. This change to the ‘dimensions’ can re-engineer the whole landscape, and out of this a novel future with novel features can emerge. What this future state looks like cannot be known in ad-vance – and could not have been imagined or predicted from the perspective of the previous state. Clayton Christensen describes this as the outcome of disruptive inno-vation; we enter into a world of ‘unknown unknowns’.73

Kauffman and Brian Arthur74 point out that sometimes something is created for one reason – such as the internet – and yet catalyses radical shifts in many other ways – global communication, home working, education, parenting, friendship and so on. The same can be said of the impact on longer-term societal norms of the pandemic of 2020.

In the complex world, the possibility that the future may emerge as novel, surprising, and unknowable is a central factor. New ‘dimensions’ in situations open to the wider world are always a possibility. Think of a sea in which nutrients arrive through a shift in ocean currents, or ships bring pollution, or sharks move further north due to climate change. These new factors facilitate the ‘radical emergence’ of new behaviours never before seen.

Initially, such features may not be systemic, as with the social impact of powerful in-dividuals. But over time, such emergent features may become features of the whole.



A taxonomy of emergence



So, to summarise:

First, the term emergence is often used to refer to the properties of systems or groups that are held systemically and not displayed at the level of the individual components. The particular nature of the systemic properties may shift reversibly with conditions (like the trekkers in Greenland) and sometimes shift irreversibly (like snow).

Second, there are situations in which a distorting influence, like the power of key lead-ers or the behaviour of a dominant firm, leads to new factors that, at least initially, are not overarching or systemic.

Third, new factors entering the situation, such as a new technology, may ‘change the game’ and lead to radically new systemic properties never before seen or imagined, and impossible to predict.



End note: processual emergence



After I thought I had finished this piece, I woke up the next morning with an addition-al thought. What about those situations when something collective grips a crowd, something that seems suddenly to appear out of nowhere? Isn’t that an example of 90 Section II Process Complexity



emergence? I am imagining the point at which a crowd turns violent, or when a flock of animals on the African savanna smells danger, stiffens and then bolts as one. I remem-ber a not dissimilar experience on a management course at 3 a.m., when everyone had stayed up all night to design and build a factory as part of a competitive simulation. Suddenly, after bickering most of the night, the group pulled together and started to act as one unit. Musicians or singers who jam together have this experience too.



Community building

Morgan Scott Peck developed an approach to community building.75 About fifty people

gather together for a weekend to learn about how communities cohere; there are no

particular instructions. Scott Peck notes that such a group goes through distinct stages:

At first, the more extrovert makes speeches, and the quieter ones sit impassive. Then

people may become annoyed and talk over each other and disagree. Next there can be

a sort of empty silence that descends. Finally, it is as if something sparks the group and

a new sense of connection may engulf the participants – a collective resolve, a shared

connection, an ease.

I remember a particular weekend when I was party to this. It was very profound, and

that moment has remained with me, decades later.



Both ‘jamming’ and this kind of community building result in a form of systemic emer-gence – but one enabled and extended by the interacting of people who have loosened their ego boundaries and defensive separation; they are allowing the possibility of a shared connection to be woven more deeply. This ‘allowing’ goes beyond deliberate intent; it is more unconscious.

As some political writers have noted [70], this sort of shared deep connection between people is often brought to the fore when resisting repressive regimes or campaigning together for a shared belief. So, what emerges when we connect at a deeper level – the nature of the new behaviour – is, arguably, different in kind from that which emerg-es when people in a group remain distant and polite. I am going to call this processual emergence as it occurs when we humans act more like porous interwoven ‘processes’ – more like ripples – than individual units. The inference is that something more pro-found and transformative can emerge if we release our tight hold on our sense of self, allow boundaries to soften, and connect more deeply with each other and with the wider world.

Recently, a colleague sent me a paper by Otto Scharmer and Eva Pomeroy, and I re-alised that my musings about processual emergence chime with their description of “trans-subjective knowing”. This is a knowing that we experience, via the “social field” as “coming through us but is not of us”.76 I am enticed to dig further [36].



19 Emergent: aspects of process complexity (7) 91



And so …



There is much to say about emergence, and I hope, in a sense, you are a little confused. Emergence is one of those concepts that it is easy to imagine you’ve ‘got’ when, in fact, it is layered with meaning and evades capture. I never feel quite satisfied with what I read or write about emergence, and I welcome that, as it keeps me observing and re-flecting on experience and stops me putting emergence in a box of ‘mastered concepts’ to take out every so often to polish.





Section III




Daoist Cosmology: a Better Way?





20


Introducing Daoist cosmology: a better way?



Introducing Daoism as a process philosophy, the background to the Dao de Jing and its

purpose in exploring leadership and the ‘cultivation of character’.



Daoism (or Taoism) emerged in China in the fourth or fifth century B.C.E. and, simply put, adopts a cosmology that is very resonant with that of process complexity. I

cannot really point to any substantive differences between the two except that Dao-ism goes a bit further and embraces some concepts better developed in quantum phys-ics than in complexity. I continue to find this astounding.

The Dao de Jing, the core book of Daoism, is attributed to Lao Tzu, who was an elder contemporary of Confucius, although little is known about him, and there is a strong possibility that he never really existed.1 The text was most likely derived from a much earlier oral tradition and may have been assembled from the work of many authors.2

I first came across the Dao de Jing in my teens, but I re-discovered it in 2003, as I explain in the Introduction, when Peter Allen returned from London to Cranfield clutching a new translation and commentary by Roger Ames and David Hall.3 Peter was amazed at how closely Daoist cosmology resembled that of complexity. That was, for me, the starting point of what has become a twenty-year dialogue with this text. Its evocative and poetic [39] nature, its paradoxes and obscurities, its exhortation to become my best self has led me along a quest for meaning that is as much contemplative as cog-nitive. It requires a right-brained, imaginative sort of engagement [45]. My occasional flurries of insight accord me great pleasure.

The Dao de Jing is grounded in a naturalistic metaphysics [2], derived from lived ex-perience – from being immersed in the world and being a part of that world. The Daoists developed their cosmology by being present to their lives and surroundings, by attending to the consequences of actions and inactions. This is a phenomenologi-cal4 approach. It is not a view created from constructed stories or theories concern-ing the heavens or the gods, or a creation out of chaos. Nor does it imagine a world made of building blocks, or a world unfolding on its allotted path towards its intended destination.

The Dao de Jing, however, does not merely give voice to Daoist metaphysics. Its meta-physical position also provides the basis of an exploration of leadership, ethics, strat-egy, harmony, and skilful living. The aim is to help us cultivate ‘character’, a “quality of character that makes this world itself a better place”.5

In this section, I want to bring alive the Daoist cosmology. Then, I want to explore what this might mean for practice. My reasons for doing this are threefold.

96 Section III Daoist Cosmology: a Better Way?



First, the way certain scholars have written about the Dao de Jing brings great depth, colour and nuance to its core tenets. These core tenets are not alien to complexity thinkers, but the Dao de Jing has a way of expressing them that engages me at a deeper level. Any shifts in perspective I make seem more embodied. Compare ‘be authentic’ with “Display a genuineness like raw silk”;6 or ‘things only manifest in relationships’ with “There is no view from nowhere, no external perspective, no separate vantage point; we are all in the soup.”7

Secondly, if the Daoists, nearly two and a half thousand years ago, reached similar con-clusions to modern complexity thinkers about ‘the way the world works’, I feel vali-dated in my conviction to ‘embrace complexity’. It cements my view that complexity thinking has something vital to offer the world.

Thirdly, there is much expressed in the Dao de Jing about how to live well in this flowing, changing, contextually sensitive world. These insights had been honed over centuries and provide a rich source of encouragement. They are worth contemplating. I have also been intrigued that very similar ideas turn up in contemporary texts on politics or so-cial change, from people who have lived through complex times and experienced how change actually happens [70].

With regard to Daoism, I have studied primarily the work of Ames and Hall. In my view, they explain convincingly why Daoism takes this very engaged, contextual, relational, subjective and path-dependent view of change – a processual worldview. Although I preference their work, I do also consult that of other scholars, for example philosopher and sinologist François Jullien,8 Chinese poet and translator David Hinton9 and sinolo-gist Michael Puett.10 There are other Daoists texts too, specifically The Zhuangzi11 and The Art of War.12 Both of these books further illuminates and contextualises the key philosophical concepts and offer suggestions for practice.

In this focus on Daoist cosmology, I want to bring to the fore the actual language used both in the Dao de Jing and its associated commentaries. I would like to enable you, the reader, to meet the work head on, not overly sanitised and mediated by me. So, I have included many quotes and referrals to commentaries, more than in any other part of this book. Ames and Hall do use quite intricate language, and I feel I have gained a great deal by grappling with it, by reading and cogitating and re-reading. It demands effort, but I have resisted the offer of a sound-bite version, and I hope you enjoy the tussle. Roger Ames was, at the time of writing, still lecturing, and I found a recording13 which I really enjoyed, so I recommend that too.

Ames and Hall explain that the Dao de Jing itself was written more as a series of par-able, as devices to make the reader think, than as an explanation or compendium of answers. As they explain, the relationship between the text and its student is one of “non-coercive collaboration”.14 Students, through many readings of the text, ac-quire their own unique understanding of its insights, mediated by their own life experiences.15

20 Introducing Daoist cosmology: a better way? 97



There is an important point lurking here. This approach to engaging with the text mir-rors the Daoist notion that ‘truth’ emerges within relationship [5]. There is no one truth, and the text provides one side of a dialogue with us, the readers. Each of us is likely to have different insights and understandings, and these insights are likely to change as we meet the text on different occasions over time. Ames and Hall equally suggest that even if we find, in our reading, confusions and seeming contradictions, we should trust that there is a “kind of unquestioned veracity”16 that reflects the culture of the times in which it was written. In other words, let it seep in and approach it openly and generously, rather than with a critical Western mindset.

With regard to the historical context and culture in which it emerged as a text, it is worth noting that the Dao de Jing came together during the Warring States period (403-221 BCE), “one of the most virulent and chaotic periods in Chinese history”.17 The Dao de Jing was a response to this chaos, to those “darkest of days”.18 It provided an alternative vision, a more benign vision of what the human experience could be like.19 Daoism was not the only game in town, and Confucianism, which has a much more hi-erarchical, top-down feel, was its contemporary.

Two final things to say by way of introduction:

First, I recognise that to dare to write about the Dao de Jing, the fifth most translated book,20 is a tall order. I am not holding myself up as an expert in Chinese philosophy. There are very many suggestions and examples embedded in its pages, and I am not aiming to address all of these. My project is to show how the core Daoist cosmology, which I have hoped to encapsulate, expresses the nature of the complex processual world. Furthermore, I want to share the insights for practice this uncovers. I hope it opens up the exploration for you and serves as a catalyst for further research and con-sideration on your part.

Secondly, Daoism is still practiced in today’s China and elsewhere. Indeed, the World Federation of Taoism was initiated21 at the site of one of the temples of Maoshan, a principal sacred site of Daoism, on September 24, 2023.22 But, as reported by Tim Gar-dam,23 there is a big difference between Daoist ‘religious’ practices and beliefs, and Daoist philosophy. I am not venturing in the religious direction. Neither have I much to say about Chinese medicine nor the martial arts, even though these practices reflect many of the tenets of Daoist philosophy, although I address this a little in [29]. I am concentrating primarily on Daoist philosophy, as revealed in the Dao de Jing and other related texts, specifically The Zhuangzi and The Art of War.

Please note in this section, that quotations in italics are directly from the Dao de Jing and the quotations in standard typeface are from commentaries.





21


The missionary position



Explaining the nature of language in the Dao de Jing and the dangers of viewing it through

Western modes of thinking inferred by early translators.



I hope the title has got you intrigued. Read on. This piece makes important points con-cerning the nature of language and the pitfalls of seeing things through one’s own

cultural lens.

As I have explained, my way into Daoism is informed in particular by the work of Roger Ames and David Hall. These philosophers have written many books on ancient Chi-nese philosophy, and I find the depth, nuance and scholarship they bring to this task both illuminating and, on occasion, thrilling.24 Their work is well referenced and seeks to position their understanding of the Dao in its historical and social context by dint of working with a variety of Chinese texts from that period. In their 2003 translation of the Dao de Jing, they go to great pains to portray the stanzas with precision and supply copious footnotes and explanations of how they have translated key Chinese characters.

One point they make fiercely and repeatedly is that we must be careful not to read and interpret the underlying Chinese cosmology through a Western lens and poten-tially grossly distort its meaning. They explain how the first Chinese-English dictionar-ies were assembled. This was undertaken in the late nineteenth and early twentieth century by Christian missionaries, whose main purpose was to translate the Bible into Chinese. These missionaries found Chinese characters which seemed to best fit the concepts they wanted to portray, and subsequently “insinuated” these into the dic-tionary itself.25 Hall and Ames explain that the missionaries introduced terms such as Heaven and Truth and Self as translations for Chinese characters which “bear little resemblance to these concepts”.26 Tian, for example, is often translated as Heaven or God. But, if you look up the character tian in a Chinese Chinese dictionary, that mean-ing (heaven) is absent.27

Concepts of Heaven, Truth and Self are overlaid in the classical Western creed by as-sumptions of objectivity, universality, and transcendentalism (as explored in [5]). If such a dictionary is then used to translate the Dao de Jing, it becomes easy to infer that such concepts belong to the Daoist cosmology, whether they do or not.



Beginnings

Let me give an example relating to the idea of beginnings. Chapter 52 of the Dao de Jing

starts with the words, “The world has its fetal beginning that can be considered the mother

of the earth.”28 The Dao de Jing often uses the idea of the mother or the womb or the

bottomless goblet to mean that spontaneity and novelty are without end.

100 Section III Daoist Cosmology: a Better Way?



Ames and Hall explain that the ‘fetal beginning’ is a claim about the continuing present

rather than about some ultimate origin or initial beginning.29 Reading it through a West-

ern lens, it would be easy to conclude that the Dao de Jing is describing a Beginning. Even

more contentious, Chapter 25 states: “There was some process that formed spontaneously

emerging before the heavens and the earth, silent and empty.”30 Ames and Hall explain that

concepts such as heavens and earth, just like the idea of ‘the person’ or ‘the community’,

are not seen as fixed things but as emerging and sustaining patterns. For these patterns

to emerge and sustain, there needs to be a continuous source of novelty and diversity,
