a continuous beginning in the continuing present. It is a swinging gateway that allows

novelty to emerge, not a one-off downpour or a gushing spring [5].



Furthermore, Chinese words often correspond to a range of meanings, some of which can seem contradictory. Even when this is accepted, Hall and Ames explain, the ap-proach is not to choose the word that seems best to fit the context, but to understand that the meanings are to be taken as paradoxical, as all relevant at the same time. 31 They say, “many, if not most, of the characters have multiple alternative meanings” and it can appear that “the translator, informed by the context, is [supposed] to select the most appropriate one”.32 Hall and Ames are clear that the translator should not be se-lecting the word that ‘best fits’; a Chinese character does not sometimes mean one thing and sometimes another, but as they explain, it “always means both”.

Shen does not sometimes mean human spirituality and sometimes divinity. It always

means both of these and, moreover, it is our business to try and understand philosoph-

ically how it can mean both.33

This is a very different use of language from that which is common in the West. One further difference to note: there is no distinction between nouns and verbs. Thus, Ames and Hall often translate dao as way-making – a process – rather than way – a noun. Presenting dao as dynamic has a very different significance in that it doesn’t infer ‘the way’ is a ‘thing’.

With all these aspects in mind, Hall and Ames conclude that we need to “maintain two systems of thinking in our minds (the Western and the Daoist) without recipro-cal infection and yet in some way mediate between them”.34 In other words, we have to keep alert to the differences between Western and Daoist modes of thought and guard against reading either of them through the lens of the other. I have to say I find this exhilarating. The assumption that the underlying philosophy of another culture will fit into the schemes and assumptions that are common in a Western culture – a form of cultural reductionism,35 or “an expression of our provincialism”36 – is a hugely important point to acknowledge if we are really to unravel the cosmology underpinning Daoism. Ames and Hall also acknowledge that they cannot claim to have found a literal translation either and must inevitably be affected by both their own “prejudices”37 and lacunae, and by the huge differences in the structure of language between English and Chinese.





22


Yes, we have no cosmos:38 uniqueness not unity



Introducing the fact that Daoism does not embrace universal, overarching mechanisms of

control but views everything as shaped by history and context.



With this dialogue concerning missionaries and paradoxical meanings ringing in my ears, what then, can be said about Daoist cosmology [2]?

The key aspects of a Daoist cosmology, building on Ames and Hall, are:

1. Daoist cosmology does not embrace the notion of an overarching cosmos, a driv-

ing force or ‘permanent reality behind appearances’. It is acosmotic.39

2. Daoist cosmology is processual.

3. Daoist cosmology is relational, contextual and paradoxical.

In this piece, I will start by exploring the statement that Daoist cosmology denies there is an overarching cosmos at all.

The Daoist does not posit the existence of some permanent reality behind appearanc-

es, some unchanging substratum, some essential defining aspects behind the accidents

of change. Rather, there is just the ceaseless and usually cadenced flow of experience.40



Ames and Hall emphasise in bold capital letters that the Daoists do not embrace the existence of an overarching anything. There is no God for them. There is no Divine purpose. There is no Way. There is no coherent Oneness. There are no universal Laws of Nature. There is no Beginning. There are no transcendent ‘Oneness-type’ concepts, nor any appeal to, or recognition of, universality.

The Zhuangzi [assumes] that the process [of Dao] has no initial beginning.41

As they say: “The Daoists’ understanding of cosmos as ‘the ten thousand things’42 means that, in effect, the Daoists have no concept of cosmos at all, in so far as this notion entails a coherent, single-ordered world which is in any sense enclosed or defined”.43

I have discussed the disavowal of a unified cosmos in ‘Unity and uniqueness in’ [5]. In brief, the point is that if there were Unity, a unified cosmos, then everything would be fixed, ordered and pre-determined. There would be no leeway, no individual, indepen-dent agency for anything. It is reminiscent of the discussion that happens in some cir-cles about free will. If the universe is a deterministic machine, then we can say that there is no freedom to determine our future. If we are merely cogs in that machine, we are all, whether we think we are or not, just unfolding along our allotted path, doing what we were programmed to do from the beginning of time. But the Daoists saw uniqueness everywhere. They saw how situations were created out of the unique 102 Section III Daoist Cosmology: a Better Way?



elements that were present and affected by the unique circumstances in attendance. Their whole focus was on uniqueness. They ask: How can we attune to the flow of cir-cumstances of which we are a part? How can we take note of minute changes that may give opportunities for change? How can we become our best selves, so our particular uniqueness plays its part in generating a future that is fruitful for the [local] whole?

This focus on the local and unique – the acosmotic – was where their attention lay, and, according to Ames and Hall, it simply did not occur to Daoists to consider overarching laws or principles. They start with difference; difference together with interconnection can lead to (temporary) order, but the primary ontological quality where everything starts is difference. This is resonant with Darwin – that difference drives evolution, ad-aptation and change; it is also resonant with the complexity perspective in that the sort of order that emerges is self-organising order, shaped by the particularities of con-text [14]. And Gregory Bateson encapsulated the idea in his phrase “a difference which makes a difference”.44

[The Daoist] mode of thinking accepts the priority of change or process over rest and

permanence, presumes no ultimate agency responsible for the general order of things,

and seeks to account for states of affairs by appeal to correlative procedures rather

than by determining agencies or principles.45



I include this snippet from The Zhuangzi which expresses poetically and allegorically this quality of uniqueness:

The wind blowing through myriad different things causes these sounds to die out of their own

accord. Together things each take on their own sounds, but who is behind it all? 46

The snippet is illustrating that there is a relationship between the wind as it shows up in the ‘here and now’ with the particular shape of the things it blows through. Each par-ticular combination of the wind as it blows here, coupled with the particular shape of the thing through which it blows, creates a sound unique for each thing at each mo-ment. And there is nothing and no one behind these sounds.

This acosmotic world has the advantage over its cosmotic rival in that it is brimming with possibility and novelty. Ames and Hall say: “The insistent particularity possessed by each item … expresses itself in processes of self-creative events.”47 This means that a degree of chaos with lots of ‘difference’ going on is a necessary source of creativity and change. In the Anglo-European tradition, chaos is to be overcome, as Plato insisted [5]. In Daoism, ‘difference’, the chaotic element, far from inhibiting order, if left alone to contribute spontaneity, allows for creative transformation and re-ordering.48 Differ-ence is precious, it is the source of potential. Darwin would agree [7] [16].





23


Everything flows



Exploring the processual, emergent nature of the Dao

Dao is the process of the world itself49



Dao is the central foundation of Daoism.50 It is an ever-emerging way or ways, but not The Way. It is neither the Way of God nor the Way of Nature, in the sense that something pre-exists that we have to try and follow or get in tune with. There is no “single pattern or telos that could be said to characterise its processes”.51 Dao is not a whole, but many such wholes.52 The way is “constituted by a myriad of unique particu-lars”,53 co-created by all that is there.

Moreover, the Dao signifies both the way and way-making; it “entails both the pro-cess [of way-making] and the created product [the way]”.54 And the way is never fin-ished, never becomes ‘the right way’ or ‘this way’. It is always “under construction”.55 Prigogine used a similar phrase:

The future is under perpetual construction.56



In the following, I want to deconstruct various aspects of way-making that Daoism em-braces. This is not a straightforward piece; it introduces several metaphysically chal-lenging concepts. I find it fascinating stuff, but certainly didn’t take on all these ideas at first reading (or writing)!



1. Everything flows: change is the norm and is irreversible

Only becomings are. Prigogine 57

First is the sense of flow and change as the norm. Things are always becoming. This is a central tenet of all process philosophies, not only Daoism.

For the Daoist, the flow of experience has no beginning and no end.58



Furthermore, in this complex weaving of the process of becoming, there is no going back. The process is path-dependent and irreversible, shaped by “a seamless combina-tion of natural and human forces”.59



104 Section III Daoist Cosmology: a Better Way?



2. The dance between what is established and what is

appearing [14]

Refined notes and raw sounds harmonise with each other.60

Next, there is this sense of co-evolution between the flow that is already established, and the events and happenings, choices, actions and inactions that are now happening. These ‘now happenings’ can shape and impact that which is already flowing.

The phrase “the path is made through walking”,61 aims to encapsulate this. I share the whole quote by Machado in the Introduction. To mix metaphors, we step into the flow-ing river, and it already has a great force within it.62 And yet this tree or this rainstorm or this boulder may change its course. The mountains shape the river, and the river shapes the mountains. Everything that is there, seemingly insignificant or not, com-bines to create the future. The path shapes us, and we – and everything else – shape the path. There is not just One Path, but a myriad of paths, like tributaries and feeder springs – they may come together or split apart.

The way is both made and followed.63

As a result of this co-evolutionary process, there is always a dance between the rel-atively stable context and the particularity of happenings here and now. As Ames and Hall say, “A process worldview is one of radical contextuality, where the particular and its context are at once continuous and distinct.”64 This is an intrinsic paradox that the Dao de Jing embraces. Things are “in some degree coherent and determinate” and “in some degree novel and unpredictable”.65

In this evolving order, there is at once a familiar rhythm to life and the newness of each

moment.66



3. Eating your own tail – the mutual entailing of opposites and

the swinging gateway

It is upon misfortune that good fortune leans,

It is within good fortune itself that misfortune crouches in ambush,

And where does it all end?67

Ames and Hall continue: Things are always giving way to other things. Whatever is manifesting, there is always some inkling of its opposite. Death is present from the moment of birth. The masculine element (yang) is embedded in the feminine (yin) and vice versa. However permanent things seem, they already contain the seeds of their demise. Things rise and fall, engage and disengage, live and die. Nothing is ever entirely whole nor entirely distinct and discrete.68 This stands in contrast to the Western myth of progress. It brings into mind the place of collapse, death, decline and life cycles, and their necessity within the stream of life.

23 Everything flows 105



Ames and Hall describe the way forms emerge and are sustained by the weaving to-gether of those ‘ingredients’ that constitute them. They note there are times of rela-tive stasis and times of change.

When this interweaving results in relatively stable ‘wholes’, their qualities are more open to definition and description and are relatively “determinate”.69

Equally, there are times of indeterminacy, when there is “spontaneous emergence of novelty” which can “only be immediately felt”.70 In other words, the early signs of these novel ‘shoots of newness’ are not analysable, and their features are not definable be-cause they have not yet fully come into being; they “resist rationalisation”.71 We have to approach the spotting of new shoots tangentially, it requires a subtle and light touch. This apprehension of the early signs of newness is central to skilful practice for the Daoist, as I will discuss later in this section [27] and is also discussed in Section V on Experiential Ways of Knowing [41].

Ames and Hall make the point that the gateway for novelty is not always open. It is episodic or punctuated [17]. When it swings open, then there is the potential for “un-caused and therefore inexplicable novelty” to arise. When that process is less potent, when the gateway is swinging closed, things may stabilise and persist for a while.

Within the rhythms of life, the swinging gateway opens, and novelty emerges sponta-

neously to revitalise the world … whatever is most enduring is ultimately overtaken in

the ceaseless transformation of things.72



4. Getting it together – ‘consummate integrity’

Able travellers leave no ruts or tracks along the way.73

There are two different but related aspects to integrity. Patterns with a felt sense of harmony or ‘rightness’ are deemed to have integrity, and a person is deemed to act with integrity if he or she is able to skilfully attune to the current flow. This is a phe-nomenon well known to jazz bands who seek to find an integrity of style, a just right-ness in their jamming [19]. Equally, how a player joins the current play requires skilful attunement to what is already in play. This process is also familiar to visual artists [39]. To give an example: In working with artists to develop illustrations for this book, I have been really struck by the fact that there is a point in the composition when it feels the design has arrived – and any additions beyond that can actually take away from that sense of composition. Equally, there are times when a new white blob in the picture just sets it off and makes all the difference!

Another example of attunement in action: Imagine you join a group that has been meeting regularly for some time. Can you attune yourself to the group’s norms – is there an established way of doing things, a slowness or crispness? Are there power relations or hierarchies that may not be overt? How are decisions made? If you can feel into and respond to these often-subtle signals, you may be skilful in deciding how or 106 Section III Daoist Cosmology: a Better Way?



when to make a suggestion, whether or not to rise above the parapet. If judged well, this can engender a feeling of appropriateness and is more likely to be effective than just ‘barging in’ [42].

Integrity is something becoming whole in its co-creative relationships with other

things.74



Ames and Hall say that “integrity is consummate relatedness”.75 I like this. It evokes the sense for me that coming together is more than connection or interconnection; the word consummate captures, for me, that ‘poetic’ sense of wholeness that can emerge and gives something meaning or feels meaningful. It is elegant, poised, and has a sense of exquisite rightness.



5. There are no things: things are porous, processual events

Way-making blunts the sharp edges and untangles the knots; it softens the glare and brings

things together on the same track.76

Ames and Hall position Daoism very much as a process philosophy [35]. In similar vein to the discussion on process complexity [10], they say that ‘things’ are not really things at all, but “are in fact processual events” [6].77 Some constitutive interacting elements may knit together for a while, leading to a fairly stable pattern, but nevertheless, these patterns are always changing and shifting into something else. The word ‘event’ might create a sense of a short-lived process; ‘pattern’ might sound more stable; and a ‘thing’ like a rock or a mountain may evoke the idea of something even more long-lasting. But, as is common with a process view, there is no ontological distinction between events, patterns and things. It all depends on your point of view, and on the spatial and tempo-ral scales that pertain to your domain – be you an ant, a galaxy or a tree.

Ames and Hall take the argument further: “These processual events are porous, flow-ing into each other.”78 This is to recognise that these things/patterns/events are intrin-sically related to those other things/patterns/events that provide them context. I use a description in ‘Introducing process complexity’ [9] of the way ripples on a pond merge and flow into each other, and this is very much the image that Ames and Hall are allud-ing to. Ripples exist, but they are not ‘things’ and are constituted by their interactions and by the nature of the context.

This is quite a leap for the Western mind and cannot just be accepted easily. If I con-sider myself as an individual, then in one sense, given all my cells are replaced over ten years, it is hard to argue with the idea that I am in process. I am also in the process of ageing, and I also change from day to day depending on what I am doing and who I am with. The form I hold shifts, sometimes slowly and sometimes rapidly. It sometimes

23 Everything flows 107



shifts backwards and forwards depending on the situation [19]. The same applies to stars and galaxies and rocks, which, equally, shift, age and morph into other forms. The emphasis made here is a reminder that those things we call ‘things’ are, in general, constituted by the relationships between the elements of which they are made and by their relationships with the wider context. It is a very different view from the Western tradition, which places emphasis on being rather than becoming, and on the primacy of the individual. The focus on becoming is a central tenet of this book, and one to which I return many times.



6. Time and space do not exist separate from events but are

constituted by them



There is one final point to make in this exploration of the Dao as processual, that harks back to the discussion on the absence of a unified idea of cosmos and is also a point made by quantum gravity expert, Carlo Rovelli [6]. Daoists see time and indeed space as constructed by events. They do not think of Absolute Space or Absolute Time. They say:

The Chinese tradition does not have the separation between time and entities that

would allow for either time without entities or entities without time – there is no pos-

sibility either for an empty temporal corridor or an eternal anything (in the sense of

being timeless).79



In other words, things are neither eternally always there (as Newton imagined [5]), nor is there a ticking clock in the universe counting time from the beginning [3], which implies that the universe can then be populated by events (in time’s empty corridor). In similar vein, space is not a pre-existent empty box in which things can happen [6]. Space only has meaning in respect to the relation between ‘things’. Prigogine, intrigued by the nature of time, would have applauded this next statement:

It is the pervasive and collective capacity of the events of the world to transform con-

tinuously that is the actual meaning of time.80



This idea of no absolute time or space is tricky stuff, to say the least. I have returned to these sentences describing the Daoist view of time and space very many times. Discus-sions on quantum physics and quantum gravity [6] [73] describe time and space in ex-actly the same way. But without doubt, this is a mindbender, which needs to percolate. It cannot, I find, be fully absorbed by the analytical mind alone.



108 Section III Daoist Cosmology: a Better Way?



To summarise …



This piece has gone into some depth on the essence of the processual view embedded in Daoism. This is a ‘strong’ processual worldview [43], a view that goes beneath the idea of things, and takes change and ‘always becoming’ as the normal state of affairs. There is the idea of the end being present in the beginning, and the consideration of when we know a pattern is a pattern – the concept of ‘consummate integrity’. The final two sub-sections of this piece morph into the same territory as quantum physics and deconstruct our understandings of time and space. The idea that the Daoists discov-ered the same territory as the quantum physicists, just through observation and expe-rience, seems almost fantastical!





24


We are all in the soup



Daoist cosmology is relational.



There is no view from nowhere, no external perspective, no separate vantage point; we

are all in the soup.81



I am constantly amazed when I read the interpretations of Daoist cosmology, how closely it resembles the tenets of that most modern of sciences, quantum physics.

Physicists Carlo Rovelli, Chris Clarke and others have been pointing out the implica-tions of quantum physics for our ideas about ‘reality’ [73]. Things only really manifest in relationships, and the objective existence of the ‘things themselves’ is contentious. This makes reality paradoxical, as it depends on what is relating to what. Daoism, in similar vein, embraces a non-dualist view; we cannot think of an observer as being sep-arate from what is observed. The viewer is always embedded within it, ‘in the soup’.

Subjects are always reflexively implicated in the way in which they organise the world.82



This same concept underpins the way the Daoists conceive of the person. The word xin, for example, a concept that turns up regularly in the Dao de Jing, is usually translated as ‘heart-mind’. Thinking and feeling are not separated. The idea that we would know something or explain something through ‘just thinking’83 is an alien concept. Recent work in neurology and psychology reaches the same conclusion, as discussed in Sec-tion VI, On Becoming Ourselves.

Ames and Hall also make the point that the Chinese do not think about the self as primarily an inner process and do not distinguish between inner and outer worlds. As they say, “the Daoist self is a function of its relations with its world”.84 A further consequence of this is that “saying, even thinking something about the world is doing something to the world”.85 Prigogine makes a similar point, arguing that the ‘action’ of anticipating the future makes a contribution to now and can impact our choices in the present86 [15].

Furthermore, the Dao de Jing does not differentiate between the human individual and nature. Daoism avoids anthropocentrism. Hall and Ames include the following analogy from The Zhangzi:

If people sleep in damp places, they ache at the waist, and end up half paralysed. But what of

the mud loach? 87





25


Making this life significant



Lessons from the Dao de Jing on how to cultivate your personhood in order to play your role

for the community.

Display a genuineness like raw silk and embrace a simplicity like unworked wood.88



Ames and Hall state that the defining teaching of the Dao de Jing is how to “make this life significant”. We do this by “getting the most out of what each of us is”. 89

A person is … a dynamic pattern of personal, social, and natural relationships.90

The deepening of those relationships that in sum constitute us as a person is a pro-

foundly co-creative process of ‘doing and undoing’, of shaping and being shaped.91



What does this mean, and how do we do it? The Dao de Jing admonishes us to pay at-tention to self-cultivation and self-creation, but this self-cultivation is always seen as a contribution to the strengthening of the community.92 Given each of us has some agen-cy both to cultivate ourselves and to make choices, then to make our lives significant, we have to strive to become our personal ‘best selves’. This enables us to play a part in contributing to the lives of others, engaging with the wider community of nature, and co-creating a harmonious and equitable future. As Ames and Hall say, we each have the potential to become effective and influential in transforming the worlds in which we live.93

Cultivate it in your person, and the character you develop will be enduring.

Cultivate it in the world and its character will be all-pervading.94

Those who are committed to way-making in what they do are on their way.

Way-making is enhanced by those who express character, just as it is diminished by those

who have lost it.95

Endeavouring to become our best selves does not suggest working towards any abso-lute measure or criterion related to ethical codes or established rules of behaviour. It is an invitation for each person to develop his or her particular talents and character, honed by experience and adapted to the current flow of circumstances and needs of the community. Each person needs to recognise the continuity between herself and the other, and then respond in such a way that her own actions promote the interests and well-being both of herself and the other.96

How we make meaning of the world of which we are a part, what we perceive is need-ed, and what we feel we have to offer is always from our own unique perspective. As Ames and Hall explain, “to say something about the world is to say something about 112 Section III Daoist Cosmology: a Better Way?



oneself, one’s disposition, and one’s values”.97 We can thus embrace our own “intrinsic excellence and integrity”.98 We have to work at becoming human.99 Chan Buddhism is strongly associated with Daoism and the Chan meditator is urged to see meditation not merely as focused on calming the mind, but as a practice aimed at self-refinement in the context of what is needed by our community [29].

The practices of Chan are intentional. It is a path going somewhere, not a seat under an

apple tree where the fruit can be savoured. Sheng-yen100



Awareness is essential: preparation is crucial, and practice is indispensable.101



How do we judge what to do? The Dao de Jing does not provide absolute guidance. In-stead, it offers “a vocabulary of images that enable us to think through and articulate an appropriate response to the changing conditions of our lives”.102 Its approach is to nudge, provoke, and catalyse reflection and inquiry – but never to tell.





26


Trust natural harmony



The Daoist is view that leaders need to trust that people will find their own way and that it

is rarely helpful to ‘lead from the front’.

The world is a sacred vessel and is not something that can be ruled. Those who rule it ruin it,

those who control it lose it.103



One of the leadership themes explored within the Dao de Jing is that you should not ever tell people what to do or make them do it, even if you feel your wisdom and maturity means that you see things clearly. The view is that this never works in the end, and “sagacious rulers” need to “trust that the world can be relied on to or-der itself” and “will flourish if left to its own impulses”.104 Indeed, “enforcing order pro-duces disorder”.105 Even if coercion or enforcement is temporarily effective, it can, in the long-term and from a wider perspective, become a source of destabilisation and impoverishment. 106

Cut off cleverness and get rid of personal profit and there will be no more brigands and

thieves.107

Bottom-up, unmediated morality is particularistic and alive; an ethic imposed is at best

an ossified remnant of what was once real morality.108



Part of the reasoning centres on the recognition of our diversity. Because everyone is different, trying to make people accord with one view or one way of behaving strangles that diversity and pushes situations to an extreme, upsetting the rhythm of life.109

It is the diversity in social and natural systems that allows them to find a natural balance.

Some move ahead while others follow behind

Some breathe to warm themselves while others breathe to cool themselves down

Some are strong while others are disadvantaged.110

This confronts me. I am clear that the dictatorial parent may crush individuality, create anxiety, and diminish the growth of a sense of self in their children. Parenting, which aims to hold the space for individuality and maturing, requires much more attention and never has the luxury of easy answers, but it can, arguably, lead to wiser, more poised young adults able to become aware of their own natures.

But never to intervene, never to use power? What should I do if someone beats a dog – or worse, a child – in front of me? What about when societies have become so corrupt or imbalanced that people have lost faith and cannot access their natural proclivity (if natural it is) towards fairness and harmony?

114 Section III Daoist Cosmology: a Better Way?



This is part of what draws me to the Dao de Jing, the wrestle for meaning, the questions it raises, the seeking of the particular interpretation that is appropriate to context. Ames and Hall suggest we should think of “life as art”.111





27


To find the cracks, take in the whole



The idea that by taking in the whole perspective we are best able to spot signs of change ‘at

the beginning of the beginning’.

The slightest fissure produces indications that enable an attentive eye to detect it.112



One of the ideas I am particularly fond of in Daoism is the idea of sensing into the beginning of the beginning of a change. Franc¸ois Jullien, in the Treatise of Efficacy, has this to say:

All his skill lies in the earliest possible detection of the slightest tendencies that may

develop. By spotting these almost before they have begun, before they have had time

to emerge and manifest their effects, he will be in a position to foresee where they will

lead.113

It is only way-making that is as efficacious in the beginnings of things as in their completion.114

This ‘detection of the beginning of the beginning’ is what complexity afficionados refer to as spotting ‘new shoots’ – the nascent emerging of something different, the glim-mers of shifts in the status quo. It creates difficulties for our Western mindset. When do you decide that a drought really is a drought, for example? There is a reluctance for an aid agency to move too quickly, when information is anecdotal and relies on local people who have lived through such times before. But if you leave it until the facts are indisputable, people and animals are already dead or dying; it is too late. Do we value judgement, insight, experience and its resultant wisdom or are we so keen to wait for hard indisputable evidence that we miss the moment? Entrepreneurs know about this. First, you patiently scan for new opportunities, then you act quickly and decisively.

At first be like a modest maiden

And the enemy will open his door

Afterwards be as swift as a scurrying rabbit

And the enemy will be too late to resist you.115



How do you spot tiny changes and quirky opportunities that are particular to circum-stances? The Art of War is Daoist in origin, but, unlike the Dao de Jing, is focused on win-ning battles rather than cultivating your nature. However, it gives interesting insights as to how to spot any signs of newness, and notice ‘cracks’ that offer new opportunities.

It suggests that we need to gather as much information as we can and consider “all the details that produce the totality of the present”.116 In that way, we can glean a compre-hensive understanding of the actual circumstances. This “empowers the imagination” 116 Section III Daoist Cosmology: a Better Way?



to register and take advantage of the “full range of inherent possibilities” in the situ-ation.117 So by widening the field of inquiry as much as possible, we are most likely to spot all the options for action. The Dao de Jing agrees.

The Dao de Jing encourages a comprehensive, processual view of experience that

requires a full understanding of the larger picture and the ability to locate and appre-

ciate the particular event within it. This broad view allows one to contextualise partic-

ular events and provides the peripheral vision to stay focused while at the same time

anticipating future turns.118

In other words, it is through giving full attention to the broader picture that the partic-ularity of opportunities can be visible. These may be nascent signs of newness, or they may be already existing gaps or cracks that we spot more clearly through viewing the whole.

Ames says this method is “radically empirical” in that it implicitly assumes that the details and context governing a particular situation are always unique. We have to approach a situation by investigating its particularities; what we did here or there, or what we have learnt on average from analysing similar situations, will not do. There is a need to gather information in a particular situation widely and, at the same time, be alert to any signs of change. Like birdwatching it is through continuously taking in the wide expanse of terrain that the birdwatcher spots the slight movement that signifies that a bird, notable through its actions and form, has come into the scene.

This bird-watching process results from ‘field awareness’ – taking note of the “rela-tional character of the web of events” and the resultant patterns or “symbiotic conti-nuities” that sustain.119 I discuss this further in ‘Strategy in context’ [56].

This focus – on viewing widely and thus spotting opportunities – is what leads to in-sight.120 This is the source of wisdom for the Daoist – a wisdom emerging from insights in the present moment pertinent to the unique circumstances at play.121 This is not Wisdom that can be transferred from situation to situation, but wisdom that comes from immersion in the particular.

I find this fascinating, perhaps because it accords with the approach I outline in [56] in relation to strategy development. In that piece, I share a method to explore the complex context as widely as possible, thus allowing opportunities, threats and new emerging features to become more visible. Had I internalised this aspect of the Daoist philosophy before I developed my own approach, I wonder? Not consciously, but may-be I had. This idea of homing in on potentially useful actions through staying with the wider field is also an example of what has been called ‘finding the simplicity the other side of complexity’ as discussed in [55].





28


Mutual entailing: me for you, and you for me122



The impossibility of separating things out and the idea that ‘for each of us to flourish, we all

have to flourish’.



The vocabulary of Ames and Hall challenges me to worry away at it and dig for meaning. So … we arrive at mutual entailing! I introduced this topic in ‘Everything flows’ [23] and here I see what else it might err entail.

The point is, in this mutual entailing, there is an impossibility to separate me from you or me from the context. What I do necessarily affects you, and what you do neces-sarily affects me. Thus, if I have better information than you, or have better analysed that information, or been more insightful and strategic in what I have gleaned from the information, then I can make a move. This inevitably will change the ground and change your options. As I understand it, this is a central aspect of the Chinese martial arts. Even doing nothing has an impact. With respect to the power of doing nothing, I think of the ‘broken record technique’.123 If I keep calm and keep merely repeating my position despite anything you may say, then in the end, you may find it impossible to maintain your energy of resistance, and your opposition may lose energy and collapse.

There is another important aspect to the implications of mutual entailing, and that is to do with moving quickly, as in the martial arts. If you spot an advantage, it is of-ten through moving quickly that you can outmanoeuvre the other. Think of those quick-witted ones in business meetings who seize an opportunity to make a point and sway the group, whilst you, still floundering for words, miss the moment.

Note that the Daoist is talking about apprehension and then action – about knowing through using the senses and then moving into action. He stays alert to the myriad of signals emanating from the ‘other’ and the context. He then seeks to see patterns in that information and also spot signs of emerging change from which he can profit. This is predominantly a right-brained, intuitive process informed by information; it is not predominantly a left-brained analytical process [45].

In contrast, in traditional approaches to strategy development, it can seem that we are taught to analyse the situation and decide on the best action as if nothing were changing, as if no one else were acting. In reality, our strategic actions, in the process of enacting them, will limit or open up possibilities for other actors, who will both respond to us and make their own play. Strategy implementation is more like playing rugby than plotting a campaign to cross the Andes [56]. Strategy needs to be, at least in part, strat-egy in action, strategy reacting to the actual unfolding of situations as we progress.

Self and enemy are coterminous and mutually entailing … we are able to redefine our-

selves and the enemy by coordinating shared relations to precipitate an advantage.124





29


It’s experience that counts



Daoism is a naturalistic metaphysics, developed experientially.



I want to end this section by really underlining that Daoism is a philosophy that emerged through engaging with experience, through paying attention to what is hap-

pening around and within. This is an example of what John Dewey called a natural-istic metaphysics, a metaphysics that does not come from the “study of an ultimate, abstract or cognitive reality, but a reality that is forever immediate and emergent”125 [2] [34]. This is ‘radical empiricism’.126

In order to be in a position to attune to the world around you and to your own felt expe-rience, practices such as Tai Chi and Qigong were developed to “root people in natural process”.127 The focus is on “the immediate experience of things”128 not on explanations emerging from theories.

Chan Buddhism was strongly influenced by Daoism,129 and developed various medi-tation practices to bring the practitioner into quiet awareness in order more fully to experience herself, the nature of her surroundings and her connection with it. Practi-tioner’s insights and instincts, then, are kickstarted and honed by this experiential im-mersion rather than relying primarily, as is valued in the West, on the analytical mind.

[Chan] is empirically based, insights coming not from belief or abstract speculation, but

from close attention to the deep nature of cosmological process and our everyday ex-

perience.130 David Hinton131

[Meditation] is “sitting still in Dao’s company”. Hinton132

John Crook describes the aim of Chan practice as “seeing the nature” of “the flowing aspect of everything co-arising”.133 This co-creation or co-arising implies that we can never be entirely separate from our environment and hence from ‘nature’.

[Chan] is what we might now call, ‘deep ecology,’ meaning it weaves human conscious-

ness into the ‘natural world’ at the most fundamental levels. Hinton134



Hinton further makes the point that this grounding in experience “comports with mod-ern scientific understanding.”135 It is ‘scientific’ when the world is complex, contextual and emergent. Given the thesis, the starting point for this section on Daoism, is that complexity science and Daoism are almost indistinguishable in their philosophical stance, I can only agree.

120 Section III Daoist Cosmology: a Better Way?



Concluding …



I hope this section has proven interesting and illuminating. I never seem to get tired of returning to these ideas; they inspire me. They inspire me in part because of the shared cosmology with complexity. But, further than that, I find the Dao de Jing inspiring be-cause it exhorts me to ‘make this life significant’ and to ‘cultivate’ myself in service of the community.





Section IV




Other Resonant Perspectives





30


Introducing other resonant perspectives



Taking a wider view of complexity and exploring other processual traditions.



There are many approaches to complexity and, equally, many processual bodies of knowledge. In Section II, I introduce Process Complexity. In this section, I take a wider view of complexity theories, and also explore other processual traditions – pro-cess philosophies, Pragmatism and process sociology. The process approach embodied within Daoism is considered in Section III.

The first piece, ‘The raw and the cooked’ [31] differentiates, to use the categories in-troduced by Edgar Morin, between ‘restricted’ and ‘general’ complexity.1 In a nutshell, the focus of restricted complexity is to find useful ways to represent, to map the com-plexity of the ‘real world’. General complexity is more concerned with the nature of the ‘territory’, rather than the map. Process complexity [10] sits within general complexity, and the work of Ralph Stacey and Paul Cilliers – two foundational complexity thinkers – increasingly moved there too. In ‘Different flavours of complexity’ [32], I describe their work and also comment on approaches that emerged from the biological rather than the physical sciences.

Following this, I explore the territory of process sociology [33] and take an overview of process philosophy – from Heraclitus to Whitehead and process theology [35]. I discuss Pragmatism [34], focusing in particular on the work of Mary Parker Follett. I am engaged by the way she describes and considers complex situations. I reflect, in all these pieces, on similarities and differences with process complexity.

I find it reassuring that this processual view of the ‘nature of the things’ has emerged in so many fields. Daoists and Buddhists, early modern process philosophers, Pragma-tists, process sociologists and biologists – along with other complexity thinkers coming from different starting points – describe the world in very similar ways. It is significant that these are fields of knowledge in which people are primarily paying attention to ex-perience, immersing themselves in the complexity of the world around them. This leads to naturalistic forms of metaphysics, derived empirically, not from abstract conceptual thinking or mathematics.



What I don’t cover in depth



My focus is to reveal the nature of the complex world. This is predominantly an onto-logical venture. My belief is that, out of such grappling with the nature of the world as it is, out of this ‘making sense’, we may change our views as to how to ‘make waves’, live skilfully and make a difference. So, in my explorations of approaches to complexity, I have preferenced those which derive their ontological perspectives from the dirt of experience.

124 Section IV Other Resonant Perspectives



I pay less attention to approaches that are more epistemological or methodological in their quest, ones that fall more within Morin’s classification of restricted complexity. I have little to say about the field of systems thinking,2 nor do I focus on computational approaches. I don’t cover the contribution of the Santa Fe Institute, other than through the work of W. Brian Arthur, Stuart Kauffman and Brian Goodwin. I don’t tend to use the phrase ‘complex adaptive systems’3 because, as I go on to discuss, I share Stacey’s concern about the reification of ‘systems’, discussed in [31]. I am also concerned about the reification of the notion of ‘adaptation’; it seems to me that sometimes thing adapt to changing circumstances, and often they do not.





31


The raw and the cooked4 of complexity



A comparison of restricted and general complexity; considering the difference between

working with the map and engaging with the territory.



To talk of complexity theory or complexity science is a complex thing in itself. Not everyone is coming from the same place, and this can be confusing to navigate. 5 To aid this navigation, I build on the categorisation introduced by Edgar Morin in Re-stricted Complexity, General Complexity.6 Morin makes a distinction between a framing of complexity that sits within the ontology of classical science, which he calls ‘restricted complexity’; he contrasts this with the raw ‘general complexity’ of the ‘real world’. Gen-eral complexity is more paradoxical, more integrating, more challenging, ambiguous and uncertain – but also ripe with potential. The basis of Ilya Prigogine’s ideas, intro-duced in ‘Messiness is next to openness’ [8], sits within general complexity, and general complexity forms the main orientation of this book. However, in this piece, I want to explore the difference between these two ‘schools’.

Restricted complexity emanates from the world of models, maps and mathematics. The aim is to find ways to represent the complexity of the real world. The focus is on finding a good map. General complexity, by contrast, starts further back into the primordial mud, and champions the attainment of knowledge through wandering the ‘territory’. Alfred Korzybski’s phrase, “the map is not the territory” seems pertinent here!7

General complexity is complexity that is beyond (or before) mathematics – complexity that cannot entirely be captured by models, flowcharts or causal loop diagrams. In the main, the physics and biology of open systems sit within the ‘general’, and present a picture that is more messy, more concerned with engaging with the rich nature of the terrain than a representation of that terrain. The work of process sociologists tends to be served up ‘in the raw’, and complexity thinkers Ralph Stacey and Paul Cilliers moved increasingly in that direction. General complexity often starts with experiment and observation – of forests, cells, swirls in chemical systems, galaxies, social groups or so-cieties – rather than with conceptual abstractions. Both approaches to complexity are designed to help us determine how to act and live in the complex world; both see a role for models and maps, but they are situated within distinct ontologies.



Restricted complexity



Morin reminds us that “the mission of [classical] science is to search for … the hidden order that is the authentic reality of the universe.”8 Approaches that fit the label of restricted complexity are focused, he says, on revealing such hidden order. The mod-els and mathematics required to achieve this are far from simple, but there is an 126 Section IV Other Resonant Perspectives



underlying assumption that there is something objective and definite lurking beneath. The inference is that there is order to be uncovered, and a presumption of stability rather than instability. These assumptions are brought into question within general complexity.

Complex systems, from this restricted perspective, are comprised of “a very large num-ber of interactions and feedback, inside of which processes [are] difficult to predict and control”.9 Nevertheless, as Morin says “one still remains within the [framework] of classical science”.10 In other words, the implication is that, with clever mathematics and computational techniques we can represent and understand ‘real-life’ pretty well. Kurt Richardson and Paul Cilliers11 call this hard complexity, and David Byrne describes it as “social physics”, which, he says, “remains in important respects merely reductionist”.12



Cybernetics

One of the early developments that led into complexity thinking was cybernetics, as-

sociated with Norbert Wiener.13 The emphasis in cybernetics is on how to control and

stabilise ‘systems’14 using negative feedback loops, which tend to bring forces into bal-

ance. Imagine you are sailing a boat; there is wind and current, and you have a rudder,

a sail and your body weight. You are trying to cross an estuary, using the tools under

your control (weight, rudder, sail) to work with the external forces of current, wind, and

tides; you are using feedback in real-time. The number of variables – wind, tide, rudder,

sail – is small, although the feedback processes will subtly change with the conditions

and will require fine-tuning.15

Cybernetics uses this kind of thinking to explore problems in other spheres. It garnered

interest, for example, in exploring the self-regulation of biological systems.16 Living

systems tend to self-regulate – to balance themselves – or else they die. Focusing on

negative feedback loops is a useful way to conceptualise the processes adopted by liv-

ing organisms. However, cybernetics does not look at the detailed micro-processes, but

conceives of them in aggregate, as nutrient flows or cell densities, for example.



Byrne takes the strong position that “the development of systems of equations, how-ever sophisticated, may well have very little to tell us about the social world”.17 Cilliers takes an equally strong position, arguing that restricted complexity “can only deal with technical issues in an instrumental way”.18

Max French et al. find that many public health studies follow the model of ‘complex adaptive systems’ which, they explain, fits within a restricted complexity approach. They comment:

The methods that predominate in the policy literature to cope with complexity … hold

as their core purpose the attempt to wrestle complexity into a manageable format,

with complexity functioning, as Morin described, as a ‘kind of wagon behind the truth

locomotive’.19



These are challenging statements. Modelling, of course, does have much to offer when exploring complex situations. However, like Morin, my main preoccupation is to ask

31 The raw and the cooked of complexity 127



what exists in the world that is not easily captured by mathematics or modelling, how-ever sophisticated those technique may be.



General complexity



General complexity aims to focus on the real-life situation itself, rather than a simpli-fied representation of it. Prigogine mused about the behaviour of swirls and patterns on the surface of dishes of chemicals in the Belousov-Zhabatinksy experiments [8]. Bi-ologists such as Umberto Maturana and Francisco Varela considered the ways cells and biological systems retain a degree of form or order through wobbling and self-reg-ulating around that.20 Stacey and Cilliers, in their different ways, focused on the so-cial world. There was recognition that, for patterns to emerge, situations need to be open to their wider surroundings, and there must exist a level of diversity and variation amongst the constituents. There is also an irreducible, entwined inter-relationship be-tween the micro and the macro.

The knowledge of the parts is not enough, [but] the knowledge of the whole is not

enough if one ignores the parts. Morin21



Morin raises the issue of the “relation of whole-part mutual implication”, which is to say that we can never entirely tease apart any emerging forms or ‘wholes’ from their constituents. The forms that emerge cannot be reduced to their parts, or straightfor-wardly be built up from their parts. And ‘the whole’ is never entirely ‘whole’. There is a degree of order or form, but it never entirely stabilises, and there is always a degree of disorder. Situations are inherently paradoxical, not entirely definable, and liable to change over time.

Prigogine, Allen and colleagues describe how patterns – forms – stabilise through the interplay of reflexive yet varying relationships; but these very same processes also cause patterns to lose integrity, shift or dissolve. The same process that creates sta-bility also dissolves it. For example, the chemical systems in the Belousov Zhabotinsky reaction that started Prigogine on his exploration of open systems, display emergent patterns.22 But these patterns are always subtly changing, and it is impossible to pre-dict where they might emerge and for how long they might sustain.

This paradoxical, elusive dance – between order and disorder, between stability and chaos, between form and formlessness, between uncertainty and certainty – is the stuff of general complexity. There is always an irreconcilable, intrinsic tension that must be embraced. There is no assumption that there is an objective ‘order behind appearances’. Features that are paradoxical, subjective, sometimes emerging and sometimes stabilising cannot be reduced to objective and definable parameters and dimensions, as in classical science. Moreover, this ‘messiness’, a messiness that cannot be captured by analytical means, is both inevitable and a largely positive quality of the world. Indeed, it is the source of novelty, emergence, resilience and change.

128 Section IV Other Resonant Perspectives



A note on modelling



The focus of this book is to stay with the territory of the complex world and explore what that means for practice and action. I am also intrigued as to how the complexity worldview, at its richest, resonates with other bodies of scholarship including neuro-science, process philosophy and certain political perspectives.

I am therefore not placing attention on maps and modelling; rather, I am exploring as-pects of the complex world that are not easily capturable through these methods. But this is not to dismiss their role. Climate change models, for example, which use highly sophisticated mathematics, are of vital importance. Thomas Schelling, to give another example, modelled segregation in cities.23 His work illustrates how the emergence of patterns of extreme segregation requires only a very slight, not strong, preference to live in areas along with people ‘like you’.

It is also the case that every sort of research methodology necessarily closes off some information, bounds the problem in some way, and ignores certain aspects of the situ-ation. Even language itself is a way of modelling the world, and itself limits to what we pay attention, and how we frame what we see.

Mathematics, models and maps can have great value in surfacing hidden patterns. But the complex world can have many aspects and features that cannot easily be captured objectively. Models may be very helpful or may be very misleading, as Peter Allen demonstrated in his example, below, of Chesapeake Bay.24



Chesapeake Bay

Allen’s model of Chesapeake Bay was built on actual data as to which species existed

in the bay, their population densities, average sizes and who ate what. But when the

model was used to predict the future, it did not display the resilience found in real-life;

in the model, the species populations collapsed. Why was this? Because, if something

changed – maybe a reduction in a certain type of food – then what happens in real-life

is that the weakest and oldest members of a particular species would perish first, thus

changing the average age and weight of the population. Then maybe that species would

survive less well and create the space for a rival species, able to eat a wider variety of

food types, to gain prominence.

The balance in the ecology was maintained by adaptations at the micro level, thus ren-

dering obsolete the aggregate parameters held in the model. This flexing and shifting

at the micro level is the way patterns are held together, and it can lead to balance. This

micro-shifting is very specific to the particular conditions and chance events that occur

and is thus very hard to capture in a model. If we, in effect, assume that the measured

inter-relationships between the different fish systems and their food systems are set

in stone, and can be represented at the macro level, then we are letting the actuality as

to how resilience or change happen be rendered invisible, and slip through our fingers.

31 The raw and the cooked of complexity 129



Where models are used to for the purpose of prediction, it may be possible to compare their outcomes with ‘real-life’ and then consider in what ways the model is deficient, and what has been omitted that seems to be relevant. But there will always be a gap.

I list below some of the limitations of mathematical models.

Boundaries: Models generally need defined boundaries. But what if the absence or permeability of boundaries – letting in new aspects, or providing resources or energy – is significant in real-world situations? Models tend to be better suited to exploring closed problems rather than open processual situations.

Paradox: Models cannot easily handle paradox, an important aspect of the complex world.

Human complexity: Humans in interaction are supremely difficult to model. Models of the social world struggle to do much more than assume people can be represented as ‘units’ connected by forces or following rules. A degree of learning may be incorpo-rated into some of these units, but the sheer complexity of unique humans, and their complex histories as they engage in interactions with other unique humans, cannot ad-equately be captured. This may not be of concern when developing simple models look-ing at specific issues, such as queueing or crowd behaviour. But in general, the complex interplay of inner and outer worlds, of history and context, of reason and emotion is beyond the limits of what modelling can achieve. For example, trying to include, in cli-mate change models, how the public might respond to predictive information, or how they are reacting to the experience of a changing climate, is very tricky; it is hard to anticipate how people will respond, and how their responses will impact others.

Helpful in exploring the now: Mathematical modelling and process mapping can help a great deal in uncovering the complexity of ‘now’ and can provide a ‘systems perspec-tive’ that brings to light current systemic patterns. For example, I advocate a particular mapping approach to analysing complex contexts in [56].

Not so good at handling an emerging future: What is arguably much harder to surface through modelling is what the future might look like – when that future, as is the case in most situations with open boundaries involving humans, is not just an extrapolation from the past. There may be something new entering the field, such as changing global conditions or a technological innovation; there may be aspects of the system which, whilst currently inconsequential, gradually gain in importance and can tip the system into a new form. It is hard to model open systems and trace the processual, flowing, changing aspects of situations. Models and maps struggle to illuminate what might emerge, what might invade the system, and what new forms could possibly evolve. To put it another way, if the detail that is left out of the models is the very thing that catalyses the emergence of different futures, then the outcomes of the models must, at the very least, be viewed with caution, and may indeed be very misleading as Allen showed with Chesapeake Bay.

One thing a person cannot do, no matter how rigorous his analysis or heroic his imagi-

nation, is to draw up a list of things that would never occur to him. Schelling25 130 Section IV Other Resonant Perspectives



Most modellers tend to be well aware of these limitations, but those policy makers who are the recipients of models are often looking for certainty to back up their poli-cies. So, it is tempting for policymakers to underplay the limitations of the models, and to present conclusions as more definitive than they are. Then, if predictions turn out to be inaccurate, the public may start to distrust modelling, or distrust policy makers – or even, as is often said, come to distrust ‘the science’.

One final point to emphasise: In these comments, I am focused on the limitations of us-ing models to explore open situations in the complex social and natural world. I am not refuting the power of mathematical models when designing bridges, or spaceships, or getting to the moon. The problem arises when we hope the social world will mimic the behaviour of complicated, but essentially definable, mechanical situations.



Complexity as metaphor

A man’s reach should exceed his grasp Or what’s a meta for? Gregory Bateson26



I want to turn my attention to the use of complexity concepts as metaphor.27 Mathe-matical models adopt particular types of mathematics and make a variety of simplify-ing assumptions. The models themselves sometimes display certain exciting-sounding characteristics such as strange attractors, the edge of chaos, small things have big ef-fects, equifinality or power laws.28 The point is that many of these features are artefacts of the models, rather than a representation of actualities of the real world.

It is easy to become entranced by these concepts and infer that they encapsulate com-plexity theory. The problem is that some of them are incompatible; they come from different classes of model. For example, some situations are strongly impacted by ini-tial conditions, expressed by ‘the butterfly effect’;29 other situations are stubbornly in-dependent of initial conditions and end up at the same end point whatever, seemingly, you do. This is equifinality. Concepts can become reified – treated as if they are real things that exist in the real world. I can see that, if these concepts and used metaphor-ically, they can play a useful role in catalysing the imagination. Metaphor can shake us out of mindsets and offer partial perspectives and useful challenges. But some meta-phors are more appropriate than others, some metaphors are misleading and even the most apt remain … metaphors.



And things are never that simple …



Returning to ‘raw’ general complexity and ‘cooked’ restricted complexity, Morin’s point is that, for some people, constructing rule-based models of ‘things’ connecting togeth-er, seems self-evidently a helpful approach to shed light on what is happening. For oth-ers there is a deep wariness about an over-reliance on mathematics and computation.

31 The raw and the cooked of complexity 131



In practice, both have value. And the truth is that, even if we try, we can never work with all the information that is there, so in some sense or another we are always mak-ing choices about what to include and what to ignore, whether computational models are involved or not. I have always been amused by Jean-François Lyotard’s take on this, captured in the following quotation. If we were to try to know everything before we decide anything, it would take us all our time, and the situation, in the meantime, would have been changed by the process of looking!

A complete definition of the initial state of the system would require an expenditure of

energy at least equivalent to that consumed by the system to be defined. … A layman’s

version: An emperor wishes to have a perfectly accurate map of the empire made. The

project leads the country to ruin – the entire population devotes all its energy to car-

tography. Lyotard30

Sometimes we like our complexity raw, and sometimes we know it has to have a degree of cooking for it to be palatable, depending on the context and our aims. We all adopt some form of framing, some form of modelling, if only through the way we talk about something. We all ‘abstract’ to a degree and go beyond merely immersing ourselves in the world’s complexities. For example, Carl Weick discusses how even hunches can be thought of as a type of internalised model.31

Without an ability to abstract and consider the implications of a complex world, we would struggle to make sense, make decisions and take action. But we need to take care to not become blind to our simplifying assumptions and wedded to our preferred methods. We should continue to remain alert to what is missing and what is shifting and keep an eye on the skittishness and ‘wilfulness’ of the territory, and its ability to confound us.





32


Different flavours of complexity



Discussing the work of Stacey, Cilliers and the approaches to complexity that emerged from

the biological sciences.



There is inevitably a difference in emphasis, or a difference in basic assumptions, that underpin independent fields of scholarship. The understandings of the com-plex world, emerging from the fields of physics, biology and social science, are not iden-tical; each contains different slants and emphasis.



Complex responsive processes and ‘différance’



Some complexity scholars, although they started their journeys into complexity with the science of physics or biology, increasingly took their inspiration from the study of social life.

For example, Ralph Stacey’s first book, The Chaos Frontier,32 built on chaos theory; later he introduced influences from group analysis and process sociology into complexity science, and developed ‘complex responsive processes’.33 He and his colleagues, in-cluding Patricia Shaw34 and Douglas Griffin,35 explored the micro-practice of conver-sations, and showed how complex patterns of relationships, and patterns of meaning and identity, emerge one sentence and one gesture at a time. Stacey and his colleagues “place social interaction at the heart” and explore how this social interaction “creates patterns of relating and then more patterns … patterning and then further patterning of experience”.36 Their work provides an essential contribution to the development of a process complexity ontology and brings to life the way patterns emerge and sustain in organisational life. And, through their focus on the person, they explore how qualities such as intention, memory and power amplify and enrich the patterning process.

Paul Cilliers moved from engineering to philosophy and is renowned for his book Complexity and Postmodernism.37 He, like Stacey, turned his gaze to human behaviour, specifically the reflexivity of micro-variations in interactions between people. He be-came intrigued by différance, having been attracted to the work of Jacques Derrida.38 He paid particular attention to ethics, perhaps not surprising as he was writing in South Africa towards the end of apartheid.39 Cilliers’ ideas have further evolved, in particular through the work of Minka Woermann and Rika Preiser.40

It is not possible to tell a single or exclusive story about something that is really

complex. Cilliers 41



Cilliers made the point that, because we cannot deal with complexity “in all its com-plexity”, we inevitably have to make judgements about how to reduce that complexity 134 Section IV Other Resonant Perspectives



as we try to understand it. As he says, there is no objective way to do this. There is a sense in which what we choose to focus on, what we emphasise, and what we leave out, are inevitably choices with ethical implications.42

The practices of Stacey and Cilliers differ from each other, but one common factor is their recognition of the importance of difference. As discussed in ‘The essential condi-tions for complexity’ [11], a core ingredient of rich complex situations resides in differ-ence or diversity – the variety and non-conformity that exists in any social grouping or natural ecology. Stacey and Cilliers were both interested in how differences – in a ges-ture, in an opinion – are reflexive, and how they fuel the patterning of the social world. Gregory Bateson remarked that the bridge between reality ‘out there’ and perception ‘in here’ “must always be in the form of difference”.43 Through being open to the nu-ances in what is occurring in the outer world, we may rattle the certainty of our inner world of beliefs.

Our identity as individuals or groups of individuals, is not an essential characteristic,

it is a dynamic property which is constituted relationally. It emerges through the in-

teraction of difference. Difference and asymmetry are necessary for the richness of a

complex system, it is the most important resource of a complex system, not a problem

to be solved. Cilliers and Preiser44



Complexity and the biological sciences



Ludwig Von Bertalanffy was one of the first people to equate systems thinking with the behaviour of biological organisms. In General Systems Theory, he advocated “an organis-mic conception in biology which emphasises consideration of the organism as a whole and sees the main objective of biology as the discovery of the principles of organisa-tion at its various levels”.45 As with any perspective, taking learning from the biological might lead us to make certain assumptions. I want to consider two of these: one is the tendency for self-regulation and the other is the idea of the life cycle.

Self-regulation



Biological organisms try to stay alive. Autopoiesis is one expression of this tendency to self-regulate in order to survive.46 This resonates with the idea of natural law, which, as Thorstein Veblen explains, suggests that it is ‘natural’ for things to find balance.47

When looking to apply these ideas to wider situations, we have to ask whether it is appropriate to assume the success of self-regulation. Clearly those biological systems that survive are ones that found a way towards a dynamic balance with their environ-ments – they extracted their resources, but not to such a degree as to destroy them. But, as I discuss in ‘Patterned’ [14], ecological systems do not always survive, they sometimes collapse. And, to state the obvious, only those biological systems that sur-vive have survived! Many possible biological options, some perfectly viable, do not, and, indeed, extinction is much more common than survival. Estimates suggest that

32 Different flavours of complexity 135



99.99 percent of all species that have ever lived are now extinct.48 Humankind, you might argue, is set on a mission of destruction of habitat through climate change, pol-lution, digging up forests and planting monocultures dependent on artificial fertiliser, and so on. We can’t, evidence would suggest, rely on self-regulation.

So, whilst there is a tendency towards patterning, there is also a warning against the assumption that balance is natural, that patterns will somehow look after themselves, and will necessarily self-regulate and stabilise.

The life cycle



Another idea particular to a biological take on complexity is the life cycle. Again, this is a very potent idea. An animal is born, matures and dies. Death beckons, ageing is a one-way, irreversible process. In [14], I consider the life cycle of a forest.

But when we are thinking about the behaviour of complex situations in general, the path is not so smooth; there can be detours and reversals. Societies, for example, might emerge, conquer territories, become great, get defeated and rise again. To give another example, I remember the popularity of Hush Puppy shoes in the 1970s. But, after decades of success, they faded from view, only to be revived again, in the 1990s, through the interest of a designer who got his models to wear them on the catwalk. They became ‘cool’ again, for a while and sales rocketed.49

My point is that we cannot assume that situations in the social world smoothly follow a life cycle; even the pull towards ‘death’ can be circumvented in the short term as new resources and new opportunities arise. The focus on path dependency requires us to stay with the actual journey and pay attention to what is happening. We must not as-sume there is an inevitable smooth pathway towards an expected end point. The ‘path is made through walking’, but the walk does not follow the beat of a particular drum, nor unfold uniformly towards a destination, even that of inevitable death.



Comparisons with process complexity; and the value of

transdisciplinarity



Complexity thinking that evolved through the study of open systems aims to incor-porate no a priori constraints on outcomes.50 There is no assumed teleological pull towards balance and self-regulation, nor an expected stepwise movement towards death. Indeed, locally, the emergence of order through an influx of energy from ‘out-side’ is what allows the birth and the emergence of new forms and features, in opposi-tion to the general trend ‘downwards’.

The work on open systems pays attention to the external environment and to the past. This focus the environment was, perhaps, less prominent in the early work of complex responsive processes, which, in capturing the nuance of human interaction, had less 136 Section IV Other Resonant Perspectives



to say about the wider field, including non-human elements such as technology and geography, and factors such as laws, rules and established cultures.51

Human interaction also forms the substance of process sociology [33]. Equally, there are many examples of resonant thinking in the work of neurologists and psychologists, as explored in Section VI. The mutual enrichment between all these differing fields of-fers a fruitful way forwards and each approach adds nuance and merit.

It is, however, important to recognise the ways in which each field imports its own lens-es, and to consider what hidden assumptions may lurk in the shadows. Grappling with the complexities of the social and natural world needs at least a multidisciplinary, if not a transdisciplinary, approach.52

We can only approximate an understanding of complexity by employing a plurality of

descriptions. Transdisciplinary activities are our only option. Cilliers and Preiser53





33


Process sociology



An overview of process sociology and its connection to complexity thinking.



We must not begin by talking about pure ideas – vagabond thoughts that tramp the

public highways without any human habitation – but must begin with ‘people’ and their

conversation. Charles Peirce54



Process sociology is very in tune with a processual view of complexity. This brief ex-position only touches on a vast and rich body of knowledge.

Process sociologists do not necessarily address the similarities between their work and complexity theory (why should they), but they have often taken their inspiration from similar roots. For example, in On Organizational Becoming: Rethinking Organization-al Change, Haridimos Tsoukas and Robert Chia begin with quotes from Henri Bergson, William James and Ilya Prigogine.55

Process sociologists focus primarily on interactions between people. In general, they do not consider interactions with the non-human world, or interactions with collec-tive aspects of the world such as ecologies or markets. Their interest is to understand the dynamics of organisations and other social groups. Many explore the impact of language – of conversation and inner thoughts – and how language forms the basis of ‘making sense’ and deciding on actions.

The coexistence of people, the intertwining of their intentions and plans, the bonds

they place on each other, all these, far from destroying individuality, provide the medi-

um for it to develop. … The social fabric, in this sense forms the substratum from which

and into which the individual constantly spins and weaves his purposes. But this fabric

and its actual course of historical change as a whole, is intended and planned by no one.

Norbert Elias56



Elias wrote The Civilizing Process in 1939 and developed a very similar processual worldview to that which underpins process complexity.57 For example, he described the self-organising social process as the “perpetual movement of relations between people as they enable and constrain each other”.58 Stacey tells us that Elias coined the expression “centrifugal spurt” to describe the concentrating of political power.59 This mirrors the discussion in ‘Who got the power’ [70] as to how dictatorially inclined po-litical leaders can enhance and lock-in such power. Equally, Elias saw power as an ev-eryday matter, an inevitable part of interacting.

For Elias, power is a characteristic of all human relating … to form and stay in a relation-

ship with someone else, one cannot do whatever one wants.60 138 Section IV Other Resonant Perspectives



Elias goes on to describe the systemic nature of patterning, emphasising how patterns develop qualities that are neither planned nor intended. He says:

This basic issue resulting from many single plans and actions of people can give rise

to changes and patterns that no individual person has planned or created. From this

interdependence of people arises an order sui generis, an order more compelling and

stronger, than the will and reason of the individual people composing it.61



Mowles also notes Elias’ attunement to paradox, defined as “processes of inclusion and exclusion, cooperation and competition, stability and change”.62 Elias did not see process as an abstraction, but as something weaving and interweaving in the here and now, between people. Nor, says Mowles, did he see process as producing social wholes.63 Elias’ view is holistic and systemic, but never complete, never ‘neat and tidy’. This is reminiscent of the description of process complexity as the emerging, stabilising and dissolving of patterns of relationships – dynamic, imperfect, temporal [10].

Elias’ interest was in the dynamics of all types of social groups. There are other schol-ars who are primarily focused on developing a processual view of organisations, for ex-ample Chia and Tsoukas. They explore, amongst many other things, the dance between top-down organisation design and the bottom-up impact of people pragmatically try-ing to ‘get things done’. As they say, “organization is an emergent property of change” [59].64

Then there is the issue of sense-making: People in organisations need to grapple with huge amounts of information in order to decide what to do. Sense-making can be seen as a form of pattern recognition. Varney65 describes it as the emergent “patterning of attention” which “involves isolating and communicating … what is given attention from an endless stream of events, actions and outcomes”.66 This process of sense-making inevitably has a ‘political’ aspect, in that it has great power to shape the future.

Carl Weick is a process-oriented organisational scholar renowned for his work on sense-making. He wrote a thought-provoking paper in which he considers “recon-necting flux and hunches” as a way of understanding change management. He regards hunches as intuitions “drawing an inference from slight evidence”.67 Flux, in contrast, is more concrete and closer to first-hand experience. His point is that if change agents become too enamoured with a ‘misplaced concreteness’68 of what is happening, they restrict their ability to re-imagine things from a new angle. Equally, if they get too caught up with their intuitive flurries of insight, they may lose touch with ‘what is’. We-ick’s advice is to ensure reciprocal movement between the two. He also emphasises that sometimes it is the subtle, seemingly insignificant, events or features that hold the key to the change. This is very resonant with Daoism.
