Weick’s approach to sense-making,69 according to Tore Bakken and Tor Hernes, is part of a ‘linguistic turn’ in organisational studies. Sense-making is seen as an issue of lan-guage and communication whereby “situations, organizations and environments are talked into existence”.70

33 Process sociology 139



I am not going to go further into this very engaging topic of process sociology. The ex-citing point for me is to see the extent to which the same concepts and understand-ings that emerge through the route of complexity science – flow, paradox, patterning, change triggered sometimes by small seemingly insignificant events, the dangers of reification or ‘misplaced concreteness’, and so on – equally arise when viewed through the lens of social process. And, within an organisational setting, these social interac-tions both shape and are shaped by the rules, structures, formalities, and indeed the informalities, of institutional settings. It is intriguing to consider the powerful role of language both to change and constrain, and I wonder how the power of language weaves together with the power of budgets, technology, and government policy – not to mention the impact of social myths, cultural norms and other external factors – to shape the future.





34


Pragmatism



Exploring in particular the work of Mary Parker Follett.



Pragmatism emerged in the late nineteenth century, provoked by Darwin’s signifi-cant impact on philosophical thought. Pragmatism also influenced the develop-

ment of complexity thinking. Darwin’s work showed that the particularity of situations mattered, and how evolution is shaped by the details of any variations impacting the local environment [7]. So, as nothing is certain, a degree of pragmatism in required to assessing situations and decide on action.

I have chosen to focus on the work of John Dewey71 and Mary Parker Follett.72 I have only relatively recently been introduced to Follett, and I like the nuance with which she writes, and the way she brings certain ideas to life.

Pragmatism acquired its name, so it is said, because it sits, pragmatically, between the two opposing traditions of idealism and realism. In a nutshell, idealism says that the world is subjective because we approach everything from the depths of our own indi-vidual minds; everything is an idea, so to speak, even the ‘outer’ world. Realism, by con-trast, is taking the position that there is a ‘real’ world out there.73 Pragmatists accept that our minds colour what we experience, and how we interpret our experience, but nevertheless they believe that what exists is more than a matter of opinion. They view the world as ‘real’, even if impacted through our perceptions and our engagement with it. This position resonates with Daoism (Section III) and with a processual view of com-plexity (Section II); I explore the implications for ‘knowing’ in Section V.

Pragmatists also take the view that theorising for its own sake is not worthwhile and, indeed, should be approached with suspicion. Theories, they argue, ideally emerge out of experience – out of active, involved engagement with the world – rather than through ‘just thinking’ about it. Knowledge and theories that help us to make sense of our experience and inform how best to act, are, through the lens of Pragmatism, useful because they are authentic – authentic in the sense that knowledge has had to be grappled for, scrutinised and challenged sceptically. In other words, Pragmatists advocate gaining understanding and developing useful practice through acting in the world. They distrust any idealised, generic, abstracted perspective, and are wary of knowledge that seems disconnected from living; grappling with life is how knowledge is socially produced.

The leading proponents of Pragmatism were Charles Peirce, William James, John Dew-ey, and George Herbert Mead. Dewey, in particular, emphasised that engaging deeply with what is happening to us can demonstrate the extent to which we are entwined 142 Section IV Other Resonant Perspectives



with, and a part of, the wider world. Dewey says:

Experience is the way we interact with and are radically related to our social and nat-

ural environment; through the concept of experience, we understand the individual as

continuous with one’s environment.74

Dewey is adopting a relational worldview. In other words, we are constantly affected by our relationships and interactions, to the extent that we cannot really assert defin-itively that ‘I am this’ or ‘you are that’. What we become and how we experience each other are functions of the reciprocal iterative interactions between us. Dewey contin-ues: “Both subject and object become integrated in the active and relational process of experiencing.”75

The self is something which has a development; it is not initially there, at birth, but aris-

es in the process of social experience and activity, that is, develops in the given individ-

ual as a result of his relations to that process as a whole and to other individuals in that

process. Mead76

Follett was a political scientist, and “developed [her philosophy] out of her own en-gagement with community issues”.77 She believed that experience is the starting point for theorising. She said “I am giving my experience; I am not giving philosophy out of a book.” She explained that her ideas arose through watching the ways people were “interacting, unifying and emerging”.78

The following gives a flavour of how she considered the reflexive details of interaction that lead to the emerging self:

I never react to you but to you-plus-me or, to be more accurate, it is I-plus-you reacting

to you-plus-me … that is in the very process of meeting, by the very process of meeting,

we both become something different. It begins even before we meet, in the anticipa-

tion of meeting.79



Follett is emphasising that this iterative reciprocal interweaving changes us; that “the self is always in flux, weaving itself out of its relations”.80 She notices that the process starts even “in the anticipation of the meeting”; the inner world of our ideas meets the outer world that we engage with, and idealism and realism are intertwined. Further-more, she does not separate the knower from the known, or the subject from the ob-ject; she transcends ‘subject-object dualism’, and the ‘knower-known dichotomy’.

Follett explores the wide implications of this emergent, relational, contextually shaped worldview that is so resonant with process complexity.

First, there is a focus on the reciprocal shaping of individuals, organisations and societ-ies. The following quote suggests how reflexive interactions lead to the emergence and stabilising of patterns of relationships and resonates with the way I have described the ‘ingredients for complexity’ in [11]. Follett describes how home, school, parents and pupils all mutually shape and influence each other.

34 Pragmatism 143



Think of the boy going to school. He is not responding to the school merely, but also to

his own response to school … going to school may so stimulate him that he works much

better at home with his mother. … And the school too is affected by [the interaction be-

tween home and school]; through either his or his parents demands … it may improve

its methods. And so the interweaving goes on; the more the school alters the boy, the

more chance there is of the boy altering the school.81

Second, Follett explores the way small events have the potential to impact the wider world. This, as she says, gives a responsibility and an ethical dimension to every act, as considered by Paul Cilliers [32]. She says:

I contribute to society my mite, and then … my contribution by means of all the cross-

currents of life always has so much more than itself to offer … when I fail someone or

some cause, I have not failed just that person or that cause, but the whole world is

thereby crippled.82

Third, she explores the idea that difference, including conflictual difference, opens the way for new norms to emerge. I particularly like the emphasis she places on embracing conflict and how she understands how the positive role for conflict requires not seeing the self as fixed.83 She says:

If the self with its purpose and its will is even for the moment a finished product, then of

course the only way to get a common will is through compromise.84

Conflict is generative, she feels, and new ways forward can emerge as a result. In con-trast, compromise, whilst it may engender a safe outcome, does not take us into new territory, and does not invite novelty.

This strikes me as a very interesting insight. If we are fixed, then the best we can do is to find a compromise – as I cannot change, and neither can you. If we are not fixed but always changing, then we can engage with our own inner conflicts, at the same time as responding to any mutual differences or disagreements between us. In similar vein, political scientist Chantal Mouffe emphasises the value of ‘agonistic dissent’ [70].

Follett explores how embracing diversity allows purpose, will and power to emerge through dialogue and mutual struggle. In relation to power, she says:

Genuine power can only be grown, it will slip from every arbitrary hand that grasps

it; for genuine power is not coercive control, but coactive control. Coercive power is

the curse of the universe; coactive85 power, the enrichment and advancement of every

human soul.86

She warns against the ‘herd dream’, the pull to conform and belong:

We are capable of creating a collective will, and at the same time developing an individ-

ual spontaneity and freedom hardly conceived of yet, lost as we have been in the herd

dream … this is the reality for man: the unifying of differings; [but we must] sweat and

suffer to make a whole.87

144 Section IV Other Resonant Perspectives



In her consideration of creating a collective will, she is touching on the idea of ‘wholes’ emerging through honest tussles with difference. But she sees these emerging, uni-fying themes of purpose, will and power as locally and contextually created, as local wholes. This rejection of the universal in preference of the local is central to Daoism, complexity and evolutionary thinking, as discussed in [5].

Pragmatism reaches very similar conclusions to the processual tradition in complexity thinking. I find it both exhilarating and reassuring that process complexity is reinforced and underpinned by these earlier thinkers, to whom we can turn for insights into how to live and work in a complex world.





35


Process philosophies



A brief overview of process philosophies and a comparison with process complexity.



In this piece, I want to position process complexity in relation to the more general cat-egory of process philosophy. I overview the history of process philosophy and point

to some of the differences between the two.

As discussed in ‘A complexity view of process’ [10], a processual ontology contains, in general, three related themes:

• The world is constantly in a process of flow, of becoming. Change is the norm and

stasis a special case, rather than the other way around.

• This flow is irreversible. What has happened shapes what happens next, and there

is no going back.

• ‘Things’ in the world are better thought of as relationally constituted, inter-

penetrating patterns – more like ripples than ‘things’. The world is not built up from

building blocks and cannot be deconstructed into irrefutably existent lumps of stuff.

Process complexity emerged from a scientific perspective, but there is a parallel path to this scientific derivation of a process orientation, and that is via the field of phi-losophy. Crudely, philosophers are allowed more freedom than scientists to conjure with ideas; arguably, scientists are more restricted by evidence and proof. There are, of course, overlaps – with scientists becoming more philosophical, and philosophers looking to science to support their musings. And there has always been a dialogue be-tween the two.

In this piece, I want to explore strands and themes within process philosophy. This is not a comprehensive review but aims to draw together similarities and differences of emphasis between process complexity and this more general category of process philosophy.

The first point to make is that many of the features I describe as aspects of process complexity – flow, change, primacy of patterns, irreversibility – will equally be found in the work of process philosophers. I am not trying to claim intellectual primacy for these ideas; more, my project is to show how science gets you to this point, and how the science of process complexity in particular, offers a certain type of illumination. The science route, for example, can provide insight into how patterns of relationships emerge, interact and dissolve.



146 Section IV Other Resonant Perspectives



The fifth and fourth centuries B.C.E.



I start with the pre-Socratic philosophers, notably Heraclitus. Heraclitus and others were working in the West in the same timeframe as the Daoists, based in the East. I discuss the work of the Daoists in some detail in Section III.

Very little is known about Heraclitus, except that “he spent time in Ephesus, he came of an old aristocratic family, and he was on bad terms with his fellow-citizens”.88 Geoffrey Kirk et al., in The pre-Socratic Philosophers, tell us that “all pre-Socratic thinkers were struck by the dominance of change in the world of our experience”, and that, for Hera-clitus, “it was the complementary idea of the measure [or form] inhering in change, the stability that persists through it … that was of vital importance”.89 Heraclitus observed the incommensurability between stability and change, and he noted the way form in-heres in change and change is present in form. This entirely accords with complexity thinking and Daoism, and may seem familiar by now. He said:

Things taken together are wholes and not wholes, something which is being brought

together and brought apart, which is in tune and out of tune; out of all things there

comes a unity, and out of a unity all things.90



Heraclitus is most quoted for his words on flowing rivers. Plato summed it up nicely: “Heraclitus says that all things are in process and nothing stays still; and likening ex-isting things to the stream of a river, he says that you would not step twice into the same river.”91 This signifies that neither you, nor the water you step in, nor the bank, nor the creatures in the water, nor the wind that causes its ripples, nor the rainstorm upstream, are ever quite the same, from moment to moment, and are never exactly repeated. Everything is in perpetual flux like a river.92

According to Kirk et al., a more accurate translation is:

Upon those that step into the same rivers different and different waters flow – they

scatter and gather, come together and flow away, approach and depart.93



Buddhism, also contemporary with Daoism, similarly contains many aspects that are processual in nature, and indeed Chan Buddhism [29] marries the two. Joanna Macy investigated the ideas embedded in early Buddhist teachings.94 She focused on the notion of ‘dependent co-arising’, which presents causality “not as a function of power inherent in an agent, but as a function of relationship”. This dependent co-arising oc-curs because “of the interaction of multiple factors where cause and effect cannot be categorically isolated or traced unidirectionally”.95 This mutual reflexive process, this multi-directional interweaving, is how processes can feed back on themselves and re-sult in relatively stable, self-organising patterns [14]. As Macy says, it demonstrates there is “novelty as well as order”.96

Buddhists, Daoists and the pre-Socratics have in common that they inform their views experientially, rather than tussle with abstract ideas. Their philosophies emerged

35 Process philosophies 147



during the Axial Age when the hierarchies of the late Bronze Age were breaking down across the world and causing people to think afresh.97

Moving on from the fourth century B.C.E., Adrian Ivakhiv usefully lists many others who, to varying degrees, held a process orientation.98 He includes Baruch Spinoza, Gottfreid Willhelm Leibniz and many of the Romantics – with their opposition to En-lightenment’s rationalism – including Goethe and Friedrich Schiller [40]. I am not going to delve further into this broad church, and I jump now to the late nineteenth century.



Process thinkers post Darwin: Alfred North Whitehead and

process theology



The early modern process philosophers, notably William James, Charles Peirce, John Dewey and, a little later, Henri Bergson, were catalysed by the work of Charles Darwin. Darwin introduced the idea that variation is necessary for newness and emergence [7]. These process philosophers embraced the notions of indeterminism, the importance of context, and the primacy of flow.

Then there was Alfred North Whitehead who, in 1929, wrote Process and Reality.99 Whitehead was a mathematician, and was intrigued by the ideas emerging in relativity and quantum mechanics in the early 1900s. Indeed, he developed his own version of relativity.100 He wanted to formulate an integrative worldview, a ‘theory of everything’. Whitehead scholar Michael Halewood comments:

Whitehead aims for nothing less than the construction of an organic system of the uni-

verse that not only brings quantum and relativity theories into coherence, but gath-

ers up scientific truths, aesthetic feelings, and religious values into an integral vision

of reality.101



Whitehead gave birth to the philosophy of organic realism which “rejects the bifur-cation of nature into mind and matter” through a “reformulation of perception”.102 He says “science has taken on a new aspect that is not purely physical or biological. It be-comes the study of organisms”.103 I note that biologist Ludwig von Bertalanffy, in Gen-eral Systems Theory, rather grumpily comments that he, von Bertalanffy “advocated an organismic conception of biology in the early 1920s”, i.e. before Whitehead’s work on this topic was published in 1925.104

In Whitehead’s words: “The philosophy of organism is mainly devoted to the task of making clear the notion of ‘being present in another entity’.”105 He is capturing the re-flexive ‘I affect you and you affect me’ or ‘I affect the whole and the whole affects me’ mutual interweaving or ‘mutual entailing’, as the Daoists call it [28], that refutes the separation into ‘things’.

We are not only made of stardust but also of starlight. Peter Sjostedt-Hughes106

Actual entities are drops of experience, complex and interdependent. Whitehead107 148 Section IV Other Resonant Perspectives



Whitehead made a huge contribution to process thinking. For example, he talks about the fallacy of misplaced concreteness, by which he means that there are no objective, fixed, enduring ‘things’.108 He also recognised that change and stability are both insep-arably present.

There are two principles inherent in the nature of things … the spirit of change and the

spirit of conservation. There can be nothing real without them [both]. Whitehead109



He focused on events, seeing these as the coming together of many diverse factors.110 This is reminiscent of the concept of critical junctures or tipping points in complexity theory – those times and places when many intersecting influences create the poten-tial for novelty, and can lead to ‘radical emergence’ [19]. His views also chime with Rov-elli’s comment that events are no more than very short processes; or, conversely, that processes can be considered as long events [6].

In actuality, there are no static substances, but only events, occasions, processes.

Sjorstedt-Hughes111



Whitehead was also of the view that all entities, seemingly alive or not, have a degree of sentience; he saw this a natural next step, having removed the distinction between mind and matter. He was an early panpsychist thinker.112



Comparison with process complexity



Much of what Whitehead and his contemporaries described resonates with process complexity – flow, an organic perspective, and a focus on processes and events. Pro-cess complexity, if it could engage in the debate, would concur with the rejection of a dualism between the inner and the outer world of mind and matter. In Section V, I ex-plore the implications for ‘ways of knowing’ about a complex world in which we think-ing and sensing humans are not separate observers, but are embedded.

However, there are differences. Process complexity has not moved far into theories of perception and mind, nor indeed into panpsychic perspectives that were of such inter-est to Whitehead, Bergson and others.

Metaphysical speculation



Whitehead, Bergson, and Teilhard de Chardin113 were keen to consider how science and spirituality could be re-integrated; Whitehead and Teilhard de Chardin brought God into the picture. This “metaphysical speculation”, as Richard Tarnas calls it, did not go down well with everyone. It did not “possess a sufficiently demonstrable empirical basis”.114 Tarnas continues:

35 Process philosophies 149



There seemed to be no decisive means for verifying such concepts as Bergson’s cre-

ative élan vital operating in the evolutionary process, Whitehead’s evolving God who

was interdependent with nature and its processes of becoming, or Teilhard’s ‘cosmo-

genesis’ in which human and world evolution would be fulfilled in an ‘Omega Point’ of

unitive Christ-consciousness.115



Tarnas is suggesting that there is a desire to combine teleological considerations with the more grounded, experiential development of a processual view – to consider that we might be nudged by, or pulled towards, something that imbues us with ‘godliness’. Whitehead, for example, although he rejected the traditional Christian idea of God, introduced God as the “original potentiality of novelty”, and “the main guarantor of rationality and logic”.116

Whitehead considers God the ground of order and novelty, offering an ‘initial aim’ or

‘lure’ to each emerging occasion. Catherine Keller117

Process philosophy, some argue, really took off through the development of process theo- logy, initially through the work of David Ray Griffin118 and Charles Hartshorne.119 Keller, in On the Mystery, builds on Whitehead’s ideas, and was also influenced by Ilya Prigogine and Stuart Kauffman. She asks, “what does it mean to do theology as an open sys-tem”,120 and states that “a theology of becoming discerns its divinity in and as a living process”.121

Process signifies the intuition that the universe is not a static being or the product of a

static Being – but an immeasurable becoming. Keller122



What tends to underpin process theology is the instinct that there is ‘something’ that calls us to self-actualise and gives us a “cosmic appetite for becoming, for beauty and intensity of experience”.123 Keller describes a God that is “at once eternal and be-coming”.124 She continues: “Creative Wisdom … does not impose order but calls forth self-organizing complexity.”125 This sentiment is captured, I feel, in the following poem by D. H. Lawrence.



Red Geranium and Godly Mignonette, D. H. Lawrence

Imagine that any mind ever thought a red geranium!

As if the redness of a red geranium could be anything but a sensual experience

and as if a sensual experience could take place before there were any senses.

We know that even God could not imagine the redness of a red geranium

nor the smell of mignonette

when the geraniums were not, nor the mignonette neither …

But imagine, among the mud and the mastodons

God sighing and yearning with tremendous creative yearning, in that

dark green mess,

oh, for some other beauty, some other beauty

that blossomed at last, red geranium, and mignonette.

150 Section IV Other Resonant Perspectives



Process complexity, starting more prosaically with a more experiential inquiry, an ap-proach that fits within naturalistic metaphysics, is not driven by a particular desire or aim to marry science and religion. Quantum mechanics (as discussed in [73]) does, however, open the door to mystery and – to borrow a phrase from David Ray Griffin – opens the door to the “re-enchantment of science”.126 There is certainly a recognition within process complexity that the separation of science and spirituality is ontolog-ically artificial, and I share with Kauffman, the author of Reinventing the Sacred,127 an openness to such re-integration. But the idea, animated in Lawrence’s poem, of God and the impact on us of his yearning, is neither inherent within process complexity, nor intrinsic to it. This is a distinction from those process philosophers who are seeking explicitly to integrate God, or a desired or expected endpoint, or a disposition towards goodness, into their processual philosophy.

Ethics



However, the issues of goodness and ethics, and the importance of values and beliefs does emerge as of key importance in process complexity – but it is not seen as some-thing we are nudged towards by God. Its provenance arises from the idea that ‘the path is made through walking’ [15]. We create the future through our actions, and our ac-tions are shaped, in part, by our values and beliefs. In that way, ‘being good’ or other-wise shapes the future. Paul Cilliers and Francisco Varela emphasise this point. Varela comments that if we are laying down the world – not “mirroring” or responding to the world as if it is a certain thing, then this has “an obvious ethics associated with it”. “The ethic”, he says, “is based on permanently giving up certainty”.128

One can argue that there is an advantage in acting ethically, as it can engender trust and enable groups to function; some contend that it confers an evolutionary advan-tage.129 Arguably, behaviour that values others together with the natural world, is more likely to engender a sustainable and resilient future. ‘Good’ behaviour, though, is positioned as a personal choice, albeit one shaped by culture and history. Goodness lured by God, who predisposes us towards it, or Goodness as an absolute concept, are not inherent in a processual view of complexity.

As Kauffman says:

This emerging view finds a natural scientific place for value and ethics, and places us

as co-creators of the enormous web of emerging complexity. … In this scientific worl-

dview, we can ask: Is it more astonishing that a God created all that exists in six days,

or that the natural processes of the creative universe have yielded galaxies, chemistry,

life, agency, meaning, value, consciousness, culture without a Creator? In my mind and

heart, the overwhelming answer is that the truth as best we know it, that all arose with

no Creator agent all on its wondrous own, is so awesome and stunning that it is God

enough for me and I hope much of humankind. Kauffman130





Section V





On Experiential Knowing in a Complex World





36


Introducing experiential knowing



Why experiential knowing is vital in a complex world, and how to go about it.



This section is concerned with how to approach ‘knowing’ in a complex world. In Section II, Process Complexity, I have describe the complex world as patterned, processual and emergent, and discussed how the particularities of context and histo-ry shape what happens. These are ontological statements that describe the nature of things. This then begs the epistemological question as to what knowing means and how to go about it. How to ‘know about’ the complex world forms the subject of this section.

Any methods need to reflect the nature of this complex world – flowing, changing, pat-terned, systemic, and subject to the emergence of new features that could not have been predicted. If things flow and change, we may want to trace the path of this flow over time. If things are patterned, then it will be necessary to have methods that can identify such patterns and distinguish their systemic features. If change emerges in ways that are imprecise and sometimes subtle, then we need to be able to spot early signs of novelty.

Many of these characteristics are qualitative. What are the edges of a pattern? Is that blip a burgeoning sign of change or a mere fluctuation? What really did happen on the path from then to now? Even when quantitative information is available, it generally won’t be sufficient to capture the whole picture. We are often interested in what lies beneath or out of reach of an objective, perhaps smoothed-out, or statistical view. We are asking what makes this situation significant, what are the features and qualities and events that matter here.

Methods of knowing work best when they echo the characteristics of what they are seeking to know. Using methods that infer that the world is made of ‘things’ working together in certain ways (the machine perspective) will not be effective if this is not so. Indeed, if we take such an approach, we may iron out from the start the very complex-ities we are trying to find.

Traditionally, science has privileged knowing through thinking over knowing through

doing. Peter Reason and Hilary Bradbury1

In this section I focus on experiential ways of knowing. Such an approach reflects the contextual, particular, qualitative, emerging nature of that which we are seeking to ex-plore. This experiential approach to knowing does not lead to certainty, because, apart from any other reason, in a complex world there is not certainty to be had. This experi-ential knowing tends to be incomplete and is often paradoxical, driven by the fact that the complex world by nature tends to be paradoxical and indefinite [18].

154 Section V On Experiential Knowing in a Complex World



No separation between the knower and the known



One very important point to make is that we cannot really separate ourselves from the situations we are trying to observe. We cannot separate the ‘knower from the known’.2 This is for many reasons:

First, even the very fact of observing or undertaking a diagnosis will have an impact on the observed. For example, asking people questions in an organisation alerts them to the way the wind is blowing, to what is being valued. This will condition both what they say and what they do.

The nature of the attention that we bring to bear on the world, and the values which

we bring to the encounter, change what we find; and in some absolutely non-trivial

sense, change what it is. At the same time, the encounter … changes who we are.

Iain McGilchrist3



Secondly, we inevitably bring our own mindsets, biases and values to what we investi-gate. This will shape what we notice and what we ignore and affect the conclusions we reach. Our reason and emotions, the effect of our past, and the impact of something happening in the present will all work together to shape our perspectives. We need to engage attentively, and, as far as possible, without preconceptions – but we will never entirely be able to ‘see’ our own lacunae!

Finally, our perceptual apparatus – what the human instrument can hear and see and how our brains link to our sensory tools – limits what we are even capable of experi-encing.

A human being experiences himself, his thoughts and feelings as something separate

from the rest, a kind of optical delusion of consciousness. This delusion is a kind of

prison for us, restricting us to our personal desires and to affection for a few persons

nearest to us. Our task must be to free ourselves from this prison by widening our cir-

cle of compassion to embrace all living creatures and the whole of nature in its beauty.

Albert Einstein



What sort of things are we looking for in exploring

a complex world?



The complexity ontology emphasises the systemic, patterned, path-dependent, par-ticular and emergent nature of things – so our methods of knowing need to be able to engage with such features.

Exploring the systemic, the interwoven: We need to explore things systemically, in their interwoven state. We need a systemic practice and an ability to scan both wide and deep.

36 Introducing experiential knowing 155



Pattern-spotting: We require ways to engage with imprecise patterns – of relationally held, dynamically-stable norms. What does it take to sense into and attune to features that are relatively stable, but not fixed or objectively existent?

Instead of trying to analyse complex phenomena in terms of single or essential princi-

ples, [complexity] approaches acknowledge it is not possible to tell a single or exclusive

story about something that is really complex. The acknowledgement of complexity,

however, certainly does not lead to the conclusion that anything goes. Paul Cilliers4

Recognising patterns … cannot be put into a definite procedure. Richard Feynman5

Emerging signs of change: As well as tuning into patterns, there is the question of how we explore the way these patterns emerge, intersect and dissolve. As part of this, how can we notice any early signs of the dissolving of such patterns, and get a sense of what is contributing to this dissolution? And how can we spot its opposite, when the glim-merings of new features are starting to show themselves? Can we find ways to pay attention to the minutiae of happenings?

Tracing the path that has been followed: As things tend to be in a process of becoming, to attune to this, we will need to pay attention to the ‘steps on the path’ as the future emerges. What methods allow for tracking this dynamic over time, and can also help us to notice the sometimes-tiny steps and happenings that shaped this journey as it unfolds?



Critical subjectivity and multiple perspectives



In these explorations, we are inevitably dealing with subjective perspectives on what we perceive in the world [37]. And we are also dealing with concepts – such as that of a pattern or whole, or of a path, or an emerging sign of newness – when such concepts or notions are not themselves easy to pin down, and do not lead to unambiguous defi-nitions. For example, what, really, is a pattern and how do we know if this is a pattern?

To make sense of this ambiguity, it is often advisable to work with multiple perspec-tives and to work in a way that is ‘critically subjective’.6 In other words, this complex world is not approached with the view that what is there is just relative to each person’s opinion. There is no assumption that any opinion goes, or that things are just social constructions.7

In a foundational view, there is a stable world out there and we just need to find facts

about it. Anti-foundationalism is a relativist position claiming that there are many

worlds equally valid. Post-foundationalism suggests we should find a good enough un-

derstanding of the world for now to take the next step. Chris Mowles8



The advice is to work critically and painstakingly with many diverse views, and with both qualitative and quantitative information; the aim is to ‘home in’ on a sense of what is there, and what is salient. To work critically means to legitimate questions such as: ‘Is 156 Section V On Experiential Knowing in a Complex World



there any information which points to its opposite’, ‘how is that view biased by previous experience or beliefs’, ‘are there any counter views’ and ‘what is missing’.

We are not looking for overlap or consensus … but for the emergence of new insights.9

Working critically requires more than weighing up views or contributing new informa-tion; it is a transformational process in itself that can bring clarity and often leads to fresh perspectives. A lovely example of teachers working in this way through discuss-ing fictional scenes taking place in the classroom, is included in ‘Artful art’ [39].



Knowing is not generally distinct from transforming



In practice, exploring complex situations is in itself a change process, one that is like-ly to impact both the explorer and the explored. In the process of gaining knowledge, we change our beliefs, our behaviours, and our relationships. Through simply, quietly, wholeheartedly being there, we absorb a deeper, more embodied form of knowledge; we learn afresh and re-conceptualise. And, through seeking to know, we inevitably im-pact the very situations that we are exploring.

So, whilst this section focuses on how to explore the complex world more than other sections do, seeking knowledge is not a practice that can be entirely separated from decision-making or action. These are not distinct steps or processes – know, decide, act. For this reason, methods to gain knowledge are not just considered in this section but are scattered throughout the book.



What this section includes and when to go elsewhere?



In the next piece, ‘On the subjective’, I take a look at what is meant when talking about subjectivity.

In ‘The sounds of silence’, I explore how mindfulness and solitude can engage our deeper senses and change what we experience and what we come to know.

‘Artful art’ explores the role of art and poetry as ways to know, subjectively and holis-tically.

‘Wholesome fare’ focuses on holistic ways of engaging with patterns.

In ‘From morning twilight comes the dawn’, I consider ways to spot the ‘glimmerings of new beginnings’.

Finally, in ‘Staying with the trouble’, I explore how to ‘stay with the path’ and how to notice what emerges on the way.

In this section, I include personal examples from my own experiments in knowing, in the hope that this will brings alive some of the subtleties of following the detail as things

36 Introducing experiential knowing 157



unfold, finding ways to apprehend patterns and wholes, and spotting newly emerging shifts and changes.

In addition to the pieces in this section, some of the pieces in Section VII, Perspectives on Organisational Practice, also include approaches to ways of knowing. ‘Simplicity the other side of complexity’ [55] describes the importance of not overly constraining the information to be considered and suggests boundaries are kept wide so that any ‘simplicity’ becomes apparent. ‘Strategy in context’ [56] explores how those focused on strategy development can scan the context systemically, get to grips with current patterns in the market or environment more generally, and also be skilled at spotting opportunities or nascent change. ‘Paradox, polarities and the middle ground’ [60] con-siders how to work with paradox.

For reasons discussed in the ‘The raw and the cooked’, I don’t, in this section, discuss the role of mapping and modelling, but I do give an overview in that piece [31].



An afterthought:



I recently came across a paper by Otto Scharmer and Eva Pomeroy, Fourth Person: The Knowing in the Field, in which they consider how we respond to the deeply troubling polycrisis of our times.10 I address this topic of polycrisis in [71]. Their view is that we need to “sense our own agency” as to what each of us can and should do to engage. As “mind and the world are not separate, but rather are entwined in a co-shaping relation-ship”, we can hone “trans-subjective knowing” – a knowing that has a collective quality, reminiscent of Jung’s collective unconscious.11 Out of such trans-subjective knowing, as we engage with the “social field” (similar to the gestalt field [47]) we may sense into “what isn’t yet, what is about to emerge”.12

I include this brief reference to their work because it resonates so strongly with many of the themes in this section: the idea of knowing being subjective [37], the need for stillness to connect with this interiority [38] in order to sense into ‘wholes’ [40] as well as intuit that which is becoming [41]. Furthermore, Scharmer and Pomeroy see this practice as a vital pre-cursor to action. They view such deeper, collective knowing as the source of intuiting how to be and what to do, as we engage with the challenges of our times. They emphasise that experiential knowing is not a self-indulgence or a side line but central to creating wisdom and confidence to find our personal contribution to the future.





37


On the subject of subjectivity



Unpacking the meaning of ‘subjectivity’.



One particular term hovers around considerations of knowing, and that is the con-cept of subjectivity.

In a complex world, as discussed in Section II, the world does not present itself as made of objectively existent entities interacting in measurable ways. There is a subjectivity as to what we perceive and how we interpret what we perceive. There is a lack of cer-tainty of perception because the nature of what we are perceiving has a fuzziness to it. Moreover, the meaning of subjectivity is itself multi-layered and ambiguous.

I want to suggest that there are three aspects to subjectivity: one centred in the person who is perceiving, one centred in the nature of what is perceived and the third em-bedded in a rather different view of the nature of ‘reality’ that emerges from quantum physics.

First, to call my perceptions subjective implies, on the one hand, that what I perceive is influenced by my own personal beliefs, sensory apparatus, or experience. For example, although we can define the colour red in an objective way as corresponding to a par-ticular light frequency, I cannot know what you experience when you see red, and you cannot know what I experience.

[It would be an error] to believe that the images that I see are in fact that which I am

looking at, that my mental map is the external territory. Gregory Bateson13

We experience more than quantities; we also experience qualities such as colour, tex-

ture, pain, health, beauty, coherence. Science tends to dismiss these as ‘subjective’. ...

Subjectivity is getting squeezed out by science. ... I believe there is a whole scientific

methodology that needs to be developed on the basis of what is called the intuitive way

of knowing’. Brian Goodwin14



Second, there is another source of subjectivity which is concerned with the nature of the complex world. Consider for example, the idea that the complex world presents itself in patterns [14]. Patterns are not definite things. Where are the edges of a wood, what is core to an organisation, what constitutes my family? And, as things change, what is actually starting to emerge afresh? These are questions that do not have defin-itive, objective answers. And yet they are more than just a matter of opinion.

Third, quantum physics emphasises that the universe is not entirely concretely there, not a fixed, objective entity. It manifests, so modern physics suggests, in the relationship between aspects of it. This is a very big topic and one I explore in ‘Smoke and mirrors’ [73]. One can argue that the universe is ‘subjective to itself’ [73]. In other words, the 160 Section V On Experiential Knowing in a Complex World



reality shared by this quantum fluctuation relating to that gravitational fluctuation is different from the reality it shares with this other fluctuation. Quantum physics pres-ents us with an ontological sort of subjectivity, which brings strongly into question whether the world, at its heart, is ‘real’ in the way we tend to imagine.

So not only do we perceive subjectively – but what we perceive is itself fuzzy; and – even stranger – is ambiguous, paradoxical and ‘subjective to itself’.

These three aspects of subjectivity are not distinct in practice. The mysterious, rela-tionally impacted fuzziness of the nature of everything, implied by modern physics, un-derpins the more day-to-day imprecision of the patterns and emergences in the social and natural world. These two aspects are then complexified by our own subjectivity; we can never just be observers, as our own unique senses and conceptual frameworks impact what we perceive. And of course, in many respects, we too are part of the pat-tern.

Mind and the given cosmos are engaged in a co-creative dance, so that what emerges

as reality is the fruit of an interaction of the given cosmos and the way the mind engag-

es with it. ... Reality is subjective-objective, always called into being and shaped by the

participation of the knower in what is known. Peter Reason15



And, just to confuse things further, this is not to say that nothing is objective. There is a car outside my window and the chances are that you would see it too. The moon in the sky and the tides follow inexorably their predictable paths. Statistical analysis really did uncover that smoking causes cancer.

But, in general, in order to engage fully with the complex, open, inter-relational social and natural world, we need to ensure we do not exclude that which is indefinite, quali-tative, contextual, local and emerging. There is a difference between knowing smoking causes cancer and exploring what are the wider determinants that cause a particular group of people to continue to smoke nevertheless, or that makes them more vulnera-ble to the disease. We need to resist the desire only to give phenomena salience when they become more settled, fixed and statistically significant. It is through engaging with things in flux, with things in patterns, that we have a chance to explore the na-ture of change and becoming, and indeed the nature of relative stability. We need to be curious and open both in our perceiving, and in our thought processes and in our sense-making; we need to respond to both what seems concrete and not in dispute and pay attention to that which is more imprecise.





38


The sounds of silence



On the merits of mindful stillness to experience the world and find meaning.



Knowing in a complex world, as I have argued in the introduction to this section, is not achievable merely through rational data-gathering and analysis – practices

that constitute the traditional scientific method. There is a need for finding ways to sense into situations in a more holistic fashion. But ‘being present’ in this fashion is often limited by the inability to escape from the thinking mind.

Jungian Robert Johnson, in Ecstasy, talks about knowing “irrationally”, through the senses, in order to “see the world instinctively, on a sensuous, intuitive level rather than in an abstract, logical, once-removed way”.16

Meditation and mindfulness are recognised as ways to still the chattering mind and detach us from our habitual focus on the past, the future, the hypothesis, the analysis – all of which detracts from experiencing the present.

What we are trying to do in meditation practice is wake up to the changing flow of ex-

perience, moment by moment. Michael Stone17



Mindfulness is “the awareness that arises from paying attention … in the present mo-ment, non-judgementally, to things as they are”. 18 There is evidence to show that mind-fulness slows down brain waves and can even change the size of parts of the brain, such as the hippocampus.19

In this piece, I describe some personal experiences of mindfulness and their impact on ‘knowing’ and consider Maurice Merleau-Ponty’s reflections on the role of silence.

One approach to mindfulness is to sit quietly and pay attention to what is going on around you.20 I went for a few days to Exmoor to stay in a remote hut to do just this. My hope was to experience a greater degree of inner stillness and see how that impact-ed my writing. When I arrived, evening was falling and I sat on the porch, listening to the silence as the light faded. Someone had aptly left behind Henry David Thoreau’s Walden, and I read the following, written in his solitude:

This is a delicious evening, when the whole body is one sense, and imbibes delight

through every pore. I go and come with a strange liberty in Nature, a part of herself. …

Sympathy with the fluttering alder and poplar leaves almost takes my breath away; yet,

like the lake, my serenity is rippled but not ruffled. Henry David Thoreau21



162 Section V On Experiential Knowing in a Complex World



As I sat in the gathering dusk, I noticed the gradual silencing of the birds in a way I that I would often miss through being too busy thinking. I wrote:

The dark darkens

An owl already calling

And still the chirrups of the day birds.

The last walker gone,

The dogs called in from the garden.

Soon I will no longer be able to write in this drizzling dark-deepening stillness.

The owl is insistent as the day birds fade.

Another example: A friend discovered some diaries of her late husband, Miles Godfrey, who, when he was twenty-four, had spent several weeks alone in a remote part of Canada. He wrote the following:

Not a plane or a boat to break the silence that must have been here since the beginning

of time, a silence with its own sounds of life and movement. A dank coolness and dull

light, the rain not falling but moving nonetheless into everything. And yet a sense of

expectancy and alertness conflicting with the passing of time. An insignificant day, but

one I shall remember for a long time; the endless atmosphere against the quick passage

of the daylight hours. And now the water, too heavy for the air, falls once more. And

every so often a surprising and expectant stillness when the breeze dies. Like a wave

the sounds rise and fall.

He continued:

I wonder how many people have felt how relative time is, as I have today. By concen-

trating on each constituent of my view, the rivers, the trees, the mountain, the cache;

each has its own life span and its own time value, from a few seconds or minutes to

ages; all is in a constant state of change at its own pace, dominated and overborn by the

passage of light. To become, to be present and to pass away.22



I am struck by the poetry and subtlety of Godfrey’s description. He notices the way the rain falls ‘too heavy for the air’. He notices a sudden stillness. The prolonged solitude attunes him more deeply to the qualities of what is happening around him.

Furthermore – and this is a point expressed by Merleau-Ponty – Godfrey’s meditative silence does more than just reveal the world to him; it leads him to insight – in this in-stance, about the nature of time, of its different rhythms and scales.

Merleau-Ponty, a phenomenologist who was a contemporary of Sartre, was very interested in the role of silence and stillness as a way into what he called embodied perception.

We must see how silence is the primordial level of perception at the heart of the sense

of the world’. … Silence is required in order for the world to reveal its effulgent meaning

… taking in the silence allows a spontaneous responsiveness to the beckoning of the

world. Glen Mazis 23

38 The sounds of silence 163



For Merleau-Ponty, it is through silence that we open the door to perceive the essence of what is there. It is through such non-cognitive embodied perception that things re-veal their true nature, their deeper layers. Further than that, they reveal “their efful-gent [intense or radiant] meaning”.

Merleau-Ponty believed that by engaging with the world in this deep, embodied way new insights arise. He was not a fan of philosophy that was analytical, that comes out of rational thinking. He promoted a naturalistic, experiential approach to developing philosophy [2].

Listening to silence is named as what is necessary for philosophy, prior to the thema-

tising experience, to open access to ‘all the living relationships of experience’. Mazis24

This underpinning of silence and stillness can form an important starting point for en-gaging experientially with the world. It can help to still the rational mind and engage more directly with what is there – the flow, the whole, tiny fleeting moments and sud-den shifts. Stillness can also form the foundation that allow insights to emerge. And, more than that, as Karen Armstrong says, it can change you.

I was beginning to understand that a silence that is not clamorous with vexation and

worried self-regard can become part of the texture of your mind, can seep into you,

moment by moment, and gradually change you. Armstrong25





39


Artful art



The way art expresses and surfaces qualities in a complex world.



If the world is complex, where many of its aspects that we want to explore are pat-terned, diffuse, shifting and emerging, then it cannot be ‘scientific’ simply to rely on

objective methods that assume a more fixed and certain view of reality. Arguably, art, narrative and poetry offer practices that better reflect the patterned, complex world, a world impacted by context and perspective. I was delighted to find the following quo-tation from Ilya Prigogine and Isabelle Stengers, which resonates with this view.

Scientific knowledge, drawn from the dreams of inspired – that is supernatural – rev-

elation, can be seen today as at once the ‘poetic echo’ of nature and a natural process

within nature: an open process of production and invention in an open, productive and

inventive world’.26



In this piece, I explore the role of the arts to connect us with what is there, and to ‘know’ the world in ways that go beyond reason. This exploration, as you will see, was punctuated by visits to exhibitions.



Sculpture



In 2022, I visited an exhibition of Barbara Hepworth and, later that same year, one showing the work of Henry Moore. Both exhibitions included excerpts of writing from the sculptors, and I was intrigued by what they both had to say. Both of them wrote about their work in terms resonant with those I have been exploring, such as the im-possibility of separating the knower and the known, the nature of wholeness and the importance of context.

There was one sculpture at the Hepworth exhibition that caught my eye, an abstract based on a bird with its wings upheld. The nature of the piece seemed to vary with the angle from which you viewed it, and the information plaque that accompanied it made clear that Hepworth was intending to convey this intimate relationship between per-ceiver and perceived. She wrote:

There is no fixed point for a sculpture, there’s no fixed point at which you can see it,

there’s no fixed point of light in which you can experience it, because it is ever changing

and it’s a sensation which cannot be replaced.



166 Section V On Experiential Knowing in a Complex World



A sculpture might, and sculptures do, reside in emptiness, but nothing happens until

the living human encounters the image. Then the magic occurs – the magic of scale and

weight, form and texture, colour and movement. The encircling interplay and dance

occur between the object and human sensibility.27

I was also very struck by another information plaque at the exhibition, explaining Hep-worth’s reaction to watching an operation in a hospital. She describes how she attuned to perceiving the integrity and indeed beauty and grace of the operating team, working as a whole, in harmony. The quote below really brings home how Hepworth’s way of perceiving allows this intuitive sense of ‘the whole’ to come to the fore.

[...] in about the middle of 1947, a suggestion was made to me that I might watch an

operation in a hospital … from the moment when I entered the operating theatre I be-

came completely absorbed by two things: first, the extraordinary beauty of purpose

and co-ordination between human beings all dedicated to the saving of life, and the

way that unity of idea and purpose dictated a perfection of concentration, movement,

and gesture, and secondly by the way this special grace (grace of mind and body) in-

duced a spontaneous space composition, an articulated and animated kind of abstract

sculpture very close to what I had been seeking in my own work”.28

Moving to Henry Moore, Moore described the way he conceived of the relationship between space and form. This is an aspect of taking account of context – you do not just think of the thing itself, but the ‘thing’ in relation to its position in space. What is not there is as important as what is there. Moore says:

Eventually, I found that form and space are one and the same thing. You can’t under-

stand space without understanding form … to understand it ... you must understand the

space that it would displace if it were taken away. Moore29



In similar vein, Hepworth expresses the relationship between context and form, and the importance of light and colour:

I have gained very great inspiration from Cornish land and seascape, the horizontal line

of the sea and the quality of light and colour … which so excites one’s sense of form; and

first and last there is the human figure which in the country becomes a free and moving

part of a greater whole. Hepworth30



I feel, both in their work and in the way they conceived of their work, they bring to life aspects of the complexity of the world – the importance of context, including the re-lationship between ‘things’ and space, the way the knower is embedded in the whole, and the idea of ‘the whole’ having a sort of consummate integrity – a notion made prominent in Daoist writing [24:4]. Engaging with their work can deepen our perspec-tive and allow us to see the complex world with fresh eyes.

39 Artful art 167



Artists in Dialogue



Turning to the art of drawing and painting, I have been very entranced, in my local town, by an initiative, Artists in Dialogue, in which a group of local artists have established connection with a number of Ukrainian artists.31 They have found a way to dialogue through art by the use of an online ‘whiteboard’. Using this technology, one person shares a sketch or painting, and others respond to what is evoked through contribut-ing their own echoed sketches and words. The expressed primary aim of the project is to connect – to show in microcosm what happens when people with very different life stories, but united in outrage and distress at war and aggression, engage. The art and the concept boards have been shared in an exhibition in the town so that others, like me, can be privy to the interaction between these artists from their very different backgrounds. The art itself is often arresting, particularly the work of those who draw from their experience of war, loss and displacement. But the heartfelt response of art-ists here in the UK is palpable too. And, as a visitor to the exhibition, it changed me, and has aroused a sort of activism in me, derived from my visceral connection to the way these artists are impacted by war.

The work of one of the artists, Svitlana Poluyanovava, really affected me. She conveys in her sketches such a strong sense of feeling – of rage, terror and distress – that her pictures seem to scream. One in particular, included in this book, shows soldiers point-ing guns down a hill, and somehow towards me in the art gallery. It is as if I can sense into the feeling of that experience, and sense into what it might feel like to be the sol-diers too. The sketch is stark and simple, and yet, for me, exudes such a depth of viscer-al raw feeling that I find it hard to look at.

This ability to convey emotion through art really brings home to me the vital role of the senses when engaging with the complex world in all its interwoven complexity.

I talked this through with a colleague and we discussed the fact that some people ac-cept the notion of a world of interwoven connectivity – but view it in a dispassionate way, as a concept accessed by thinking. Those in ‘thinking mode’ may accept that the world manifests in patterns, and may be motivated to observe those patterns, but feel-ing does not play a strong part in their sense-making. But, as I’ve argued earlier, we lim-it what we can know if our senses and emotions and hearts are not in play too. When the ‘heart-mind’32 is engaged, our knowing is enhanced and more en pointe. And art can be masterful at engaging our heart-mind. Svitlana’s sketch brings me into contact with the fear and brutality of conflict in a way I cannot avoid, and in a way that has changed my perception, shifted my understanding and changed ‘me myself’. Knowing and trans-formation are deeply entwined.

The feeling dimension is present in many situations in the world, acknowledged or not. It plays out in the boardroom, in families and in ministerial meetings. If we become impervious to empathic response, we are creating our understanding and shaping the future with one hand tied behind our back, or worse, with our eyes closed. This is the subject of ‘Thyme for a Sage’ [54].

168 Section V On Experiential Knowing in a Complex World



Poetry



Poetry and poetic expression are also ways of conveying ‘that which cannot be pinned down through rational ways alone’. The Greek root, poeisis, means making or crafting.

The Dao de Jing, for example, is written poetically, and sinologist David Hinton has this to say:

Narrative, reportage, explanation, idea: language … that looks out and thinks about the

cosmos as if from some outside space. But poetry pares language down to a bare mini-

mum, thereby opening it to silence. And it is there in the margins of silence that poetry

finds its deepest possibilities – for there it can render dimensions of consciousness that

are much more expansive.33



Many people have written about poetry and the need for a poetic appreciation of the world, and I am particularly interested in the work of Maurice Merleau-Ponty [38]. Merleau-Ponty explores the difference between language, “which is designed as a common tool to convey agreed specific references”, and poetic language which achieves “primal contact and a reverberation with things of the world”.34 He differenti-ates between a “communication of ideas or information” and the way poetry can offer “an encounter with another being”, a “gesture that points beyond itself to what it might convey”, and a “pre-reflective experience”.35

Poets and artists let things be, but they also let things come out and show themselves.

Sarah Bakewell36



Sarah Bakewell in At the Existentialist Café suggests that: “We help things to emerge into the light by being conscious of them, and we are often conscious of them poetically, which means that we pay respectful attention and allow them to show themselves as they are, rather than bending them to our will.”37

Merleau-Ponty suggests that the essence of what the poet or artist is experiencing and aiming to convey has a sort of stillness or silence about it. For example, artist Sarah Gillespie has this to say:

After a period of settling, several hours into the drawing, I find myself becoming absent.

In quieting myself, something shifts. … On a really good day, the presence of the tree, or

the nest, or the light on the water … comes up and towards me. There’s nothing meta-

physical or theoretical about this; it is a physical feeling of empathy … it is my way in.38



In ‘The sounds of silence’ [38] I explore the way silence can be a starting point to ex-periencing the nature of the complex world in a way that is pre-reflective, subtle and tentative. Merleau-Ponty asks how poetry can convey “the silent world that is beneath language” without destroying “silence itself”.39 Poetry, he is saying, is trying to use lan-guage to describe and connect with something in the world that we can attune to be-fore we bring in our conceptions and models. We ‘let it be’. Poetry, you could say, is

39 Artful art 169



right-brain language not left-brain language, something “closer to the truth”, less taint-ed and shaped by our pre-formed ideas. “Poetic speech … reveals the nexus of relations that comprise the world”.40



Drama



A few days before I completed the manuscript for this book, a colleague, Jed Hopkins, shared another interesting approach to knowing.41 He was interested in how a group of teachers can gain knowledge about their practice but also surface their implicit ped-agogies. He asked a group of teachers each to compose a fictional but believable les-son as though it were a scene from a play in which they played the role of the teacher. The group then shared and discussed their scripts. Hopkins found that the scripts did indeed illuminate their practice – how each teacher went about teaching, the extent to which each was sensitive to the subtle reactions of students and picked up on the dynamics in the classroom. The fact that the stories were fictional made them easi-er to critique and facilitated shared learning. But, more than this, Hopkins found that the group was able to reflect on the deeper issues concerning their underlying beliefs and assumptions about how to teach – their implicit pedagogies. This exemplifies the idea mentioned in ‘Metaphysics and chocolate’ [2], of a naturalistic approach to sur-facing ontological frames concerning ‘the way things work’. As Hopkins commented, ‘the teachers discovered a new way to talk about the conceptual framework in which teaching sits’. He felt this was transformational for those who took part.



And so …



In this piece, I have explored briefly how art – sculpture, painting, poetry and even script-writing – offers ways of knowing in a complex world. Art captures something about the holistic, patterned nature of life; it captures too, the importance of detail – the way a bird entering the scene brings it to life, the way the angle and framing of a photograph change what we see and how it makes us feel.

Art animates the idea of the relationship between thing and context, between figure and field; it shows how the actuality of the creation is relational and happens between the object and the perceiver. Art offers a way of experiencing that undercuts our habit-ual conceptualising and labelling. It does not sever the connection between the emo-tional and the cognitive, between the thing and its context, between the perceiver and the perceived. Art works on the level of the imaginative, the intuitive, the unconscious, the unsayable. It surfaces that which is difficult to surface, it handles paradox and ambiguity.

These are all aspects of a complex, patterned, processual world. To ‘know’ this complex world, we need subjective methods. The tools of objective measurement are inevita-bly going to discard a great deal that is pertinent to our knowing. And if science aims 170 Section V On Experiential Knowing in a Complex World



to uncover that which is salient, that which has agency to create the future, ignoring aspects just because they are subjective and subtle, does not make for good science. In a complex world, art has a ‘scientific’ role to play.

As well as offering a form of knowing, art connects and transforms. It allows us to perceive, but facilitates a dialogue between perceptions which can then, reflexively, change those perceptions, all without recourse to concrete analysis. We are changed, moved, see differently – but we cannot necessarily say why or how. We are sensitive to the art as a holistic expression of what the artist perceives. And we are also sensitive to subtle difference – and a dialogue through art can reflect back both similarity and difference, both wholeness and nuanced particularity. Art provides a medium to facili-tate the emergence of shared meaning-making, as we explore with others what we see and experience.

Works of art can communicate patterns of culture, not only by emphasis, coloration,

context and imagery but by what is unsaid. Gregory Bateson42

Art, because it is not subject to purposive language-bound rationality, is capable of

re-linking us with our context. Peter Reason43





40


Wholesome fare



Holistic ways of engaging with patterns and why we should bother.



The world according to complexity theory is not just an overwhelming, random, in-terconnected, mess of which we can be sure of almost nothing. Nor does it suggest that, in order to get to grips with it, we have to collect so much detailed information that we are tempted to give up before we start. In practice, the world displays a degree of structure; it has a tendency towards patterning – like the swirls and ripples and rap-ids and stagnant pools of a flowing river. Such patterns emerge from the interconnect-edness of their constituents, their interdependency and reflexivity. These reflexive re-lationships create something more than themselves, and have qualities which manifest systemically, and exist in the pattern as a whole.

If the social and natural world tends to be constituted of patterns or wholes – patterns which are not entirely independent and not entirely analysable in objective ways, then one key aspect of knowing about the complex world is to apprehend these patterns. This does not require us to collect every detail, but it is nevertheless not straightfor-ward. Patterns may be relatively stable and identifiable, but they are not forever. They interact, affect each other and co-evolve, and their form, edges, depths and cores are not fixed, objective qualities.44

In this section, I explore what sort of processes of knowing or engaging we can use. What does it mean to experience holistically? How can our method mirror the qualities of that with which we are engaged, and recognise that we are part of this picture, not in general a passive observer?

Goethe was one of the first philosophers to consider the way natural phenomena seemed to manifest as organisms that have a sense of wholeness and integrity. He was interested in how we appraise or discern such wholes; he referred to them gestalts, or meaningful patterns.45 He suggested that we need to become one with the thing we are experiencing – through the medium of our senses coupled with our intuition. One example of this holistic appreciation is listening to a piece of music. On one level the music is just a series of notes; it is our sensory experience of those notes which turns it into music – music that has an integrity, form and meaning.

We might also ask: Is the piece of music a ‘whole’ on its own, or is the whole in the re-lationship between ourselves and the music? Are we, the perceiver, part of the whole?

Brian Goodwin, an admirer of Goethe, focused on the role of intuition in this sensing process.46 He explored how our intuition coupled with our sensory experience can resonate with ‘organismic wholes’ in nature and society.

172 Section V On Experiential Knowing in a Complex World



The touchstone … is experiential, participative knowing. … Human presence meets giv-

en reality through participating in its being. Peter Reason and Brian Goodwin47

Goodwin described how he would spend time in nature, inviting the whole of his sens-es to meet the whole of what he was experiencing. 48 He was interested in what comes to us when we still our busy minds, inhabit our bodies, stop our activities and try to attune to ‘what is’ [38].

I do the same. For example, I wrote this after sitting late one evening by my local river:



The river

The stretch of river is quiet and still, the moon shines through the trees. There are

bats. Then a screech and something slowly swimming across to the opposite bank and

then to right in front of where I am sitting. An otter. It crosses again to the opposite

bank, gives another loud screech and is gone. It is a beautiful, prolonged, peaceful

moment. The river too seems peaceful; the reflection of the trees on its surface is

clear in the light of the moon. A breeze travels down the river and suddenly the

atmosphere changes. I feel as if the whole – river, trees, and me – takes in a breath. It is

as if I can feel into what the river feels.



Sitting silently in nature gives me a sense of being less of an observer and more part of the experience. I seem more able to notice small movements and sounds, feel more deeply into my surroundings and become affected by them. This deep engagement is what underpins the tenets of deep ecology.49 The premise is that if you feel your inter-connection with nature, it is much more difficult to see nature as something separate from you, as a resource at your disposal.

Goodwin engaged deeply with the idea of intuitive knowing and the need to include a place for subjective qualities in science. He was a complexity scientist coming from the fields of mathematics and biology, and he was concerned with what is omitted in the methods of inquiry consistent with classical science. He emphasised that classical science struggles to include aspects of the world such as colour, texture, and aesthetic sense and he developed the idea of a science of qualities.50

Goodwin takes the example of a medical practitioner assessing someone’s health or wellbeing. The practitioner pays attention to the patient’s subjective experience of pain and takes note of skin tone, posture, tone of voice and handshake, as well as the quantitative hard data such as temperature and blood pressure. Health is a systemic property of the body and cannot be reduced to the summation of hard data relating to different parts of the body.

As Goodwin says, what is not commonly taught to science students is the “systematic cultivation of the intuitive faculty”, which he feels can develop “the capacity to rec-ognise the coherent wholes that emerge from related parts”.51 Practitioners, he says, require experience and practice to “hone their intuitive faculty” and come to a “holistic judgment” about the problem and how to proceed [51].

40 Wholesome fare 173



The intuitive way of knowing ... is a definite way of knowing about the world. ... All the

great scientists, Einstein, Feynman ... would say intuition is the way they arrived at their

basic insights. ... The rest of us have to pretend that we’re really basing everything on

hard fact, proceeding to generalize by induction ... not seeing a new whole intuitively.

Goodwin52



Goodwin applies the same thinking to ecologies. He says: “The health of an ecosystem is reflected in the quality of the birdsong as well as in the [quantitatively measurable] diversity of species.”53



So, what should we do?



How are we to explore and engage with wholes and patterns? These patterns are not objectively existent and there is a degree of judgment in exploring what they are, what qualities they have and where their boundaries might lie. These judgements, however, are more than matters of opinion. Analysing a time-lapsed photograph of a meadow or a pond, for example would give some indication of where to judge its edges lie. It is not exact, but neither is it just a whim of the observer.

Wholes are not fixed certain things, and we need to use our intuitive and embodied responses to try to attune to their qualities. Furthermore, there is recognition that, in actuality, the ‘whole’ we are exploring is not just ‘out there’ but is a co-creation with ourselves. The whole is held in the relationship with the perceiver.

A not-dissimilar approach underlies skilful engagement with groups. Imagine I enter a room where a meeting is going on. Can I take in the sense of the group – the way people are engaging, the energy, the tone – as a whole? Can I quieten my tendency to analyse and assess and instead just attune to what is happening?

Tarthang Tulku calls this panoramic viewing.54 Instead of letting my eyes and other senses capture and categorise the detail, I aim to take in the panorama and use my intuitive sense to tune into the mood, culture and modus operandi of this group. When I do this well, it informs me as to how to join, what to say or do. I can notice how things shift as I start to interact or, equally, notice the impact if I decide to say nothing and sit quietly. Does the group shift anyway due to my presence?

A useful resource for engaging holistically with organisations is Working Whole Sys-tems.55 Pratt and his colleagues share ways to work with dialogue and stories and en-gage with issues of power. They give some very practical exercises as to how to tune in to the current culture and ways of working in an organisation, to surface sub-cultures and the less tangible aspects of patterns and norms. They emphasise that uncover-ing these norms and what drives them is an intuitive process for which there are no recipes.





41


From morning twilight comes the dawn



Spotting the glimmerings of new beginnings.



Process complexity provides an image of the world as comprised of shifting, inter-penetrating but nevertheless at times quite stable – and sometimes even unbeliev-

ably rigid – patterns [14]. But things are nevertheless in constant flux.

In this piece, I am focusing on how and why to pay attention to any glimpses of emerging newness. Could it be that we can be effective through aligning ourselves with nascent potentialities? This may seem an unusual thought in a world that wants hard evidence that change is happening and does not imagine that alignment with any incipient irreg-ularity could be an option to pursue. We may be working with just a hint, an instinct that something new is here, in a sort of liminal space that we cannot pin down.

Juliana Scheider emphasises the value of noticing small changes.56 She builds on the work of Goethean scholar Henri Bortoft, who, in Taking Appearances Seriously, talks of looking ‘upstream’ rather than ‘downstream’.57 As Schneider explains: “To see up-stream is to join the arising of the phenomenon whilst downstream is to see it after it has already appeared”.58 In other words, we should be interested in watching and accompanying things as they are in the process of emerging and becoming – not just wait until they are fully formed.

François Jullien, in The Treatise of Efficacy, uses similar language in speaking of glimpsing change way upstream. He says:

Behind the opportunity that seems to arise unexpectedly … can be glimpsed another,

way upstream, that is the starting point of the process from which the later opportunity

eventually emerges. … The moment at the beginning ... was the point of the appearance

of a cleavage that caused the potential gradually to shift to one side rather than

the other.59



There is ambiguity in this process. Not all tiny differences turn out to lead to some new outcome. They may be fluctuations which merely subside again, as transient rip-ples on a pond. But the point being made by Schneider and Jullien is that by noticing early we have more agency. We can perhaps protect some emerging shoots and allow them space to grow. Or we may, in some cases, choose to suppress or ignore them. This latter is reminiscent of training a puppy; ‘undesirable’ behaviour caught early can be quenched or re-directed before it has time to develop into a full-blown problem. I am reminded of those people who say ‘this puppy is so cute he nibbles my fingers’!

So, if we can be alert to the ‘beginning of the beginning’ of a change, we may be able to influence the process, and perhaps be able to nudge it in this direction, not that.

176 Section V On Experiential Knowing in a Complex World



But first, we must hone our ability even to notice them. What alerts us, as we enter a situation, that something is not right here? What makes a social worker for example, feel that the situation warrants decisive action even if she can’t entirely articulate why she feels that way? How do we sense danger and how do we sense its opposite, when there is a subtle shift in a group of people, maybe a sort of momentary emptiness or luminosity which heralds a coming together, a building of community [19]? The Dao de Jing has lots of advice to offer on this front. Leaders are encouraged to hone their in-stincts and cultivate the ability to spot signs of difference and take them seriously [27].

Schneider has this to say:

Anyone who wishes to inquire into their experience at work can practise and culti-

vate the art of paying attention to small shifts in everyday conversations or scenes as
