they are occurring. By doing so, practitioners can become more sensitive to ill-defined

disquiets and potential openings and respond to them in the moment. These fleeting

moments matter, because they can be a doorway to moving on together in new and

sometimes constructive directions. Conversely, when we fail to pay attention to the

small movements in such everyday scenes, we risk missing these potential turning

points altogether. 60

Here are other examples of noticing ‘glimmers’ and acting on them:



Glimmers of change

Your child has been having a hard time at school. You think she might be being bullied

but she refuses to talk about it and shuts herself away, eats little and is becoming quite

remote. One evening she comes downstairs and sits down. Nothing is said, but you feel

this is an important moment. Can you find a way to create the conditions for her to

feel able to even make eye contact? How can you respond to the gesture of her coming

downstairs in a way that is not too obvious, that opens up the space, does not close it

down?

You are in a meeting at work where some big changes are being discussed. The mood of

the meeting is angry but then there is a sudden lull. Without really planning to, you find

yourself saying to the group that you know how difficult this will be, you feel upset and

concerned yourself, but you hope there might be a way to find merit in the new situa-

tion. Somehow your sense of timing was well-judged, and your intervention was, in part,

able to create an opening for acknowledging and imagining a new future.



Benjamin Knight has explored the nuances of how children learn, and how teachers can create the conditions for such learning.61 He remarked to me that: ‘Spotting the earliest possible signs of change resonates with my concept of ‘soon-to-be learning’ in classroom contexts.’62 He shared a transcript of a filmed conversation between pupils trying to make a rocket that could fly over the classroom. He explained: ‘A first viewing of this short episode would lead to a conclusion that nothing significant was occurring, firstly because the reasoning behind the pupils’ utterances remains tacit throughout. However, closer observation reveals that a range of learning behaviours – including

41 From morning twilight comes the dawn 177



reasoning, hypothesising, constructive disagreement, problem-solving and testing – are evident beneath the surface.’

Knight recognised that this learning was hard to evidence in a concrete fashion, as it ‘lacked structure’ and was not ‘fully formed or made explicit’. But he explained that an attuned teacher can sense that there is a move towards gaining knowledge and under-standing when it is at ‘the beginning of the beginning’ of developing knowledge and un-derstanding. He feels it is very important to trust in the teacher’s sense of judgement as to how create conditions in the classroom to enhance such ‘soon-to-be learning’ op-portunities and notes it is easy to overlook its importance.



But giving voice to difference can be a political act …



What I am describing in this piece is noticing and giving voice to something new, some-thing different. But bringing attention to ‘difference’ can, of course, be a political act. For example, at the beginning of the Russian invasion of Ukraine in 2022, a very brave editor on a prominent Russian news programme held up a placard behind the news-reader which read, ‘stop the war, don’t believe propaganda, they’re lying to you’.63

Dictators often take the eradication of difference or dissent very seriously and seek to annihilate it before it gets off the ground.

Even in less extreme examples, pointing to newness or difference can require a stance against the norm, a reaching for something subtle, unacknowledged and less main-stream. Artist Erin Manning calls this ‘working in a minor key’ and points out:

The grand is given the status it has not because it is where the transformative pow-

er lies, but because it is easier to identify major shifts than to catalogue the nuanced

rhythms of the minor.64



Deleuze and Guattari also talk about the use of ‘the minor’, which they define as the “gestural force that opens up experience to its potential variation”.65 Margaret Gearty builds on this idea. She asks: ‘What can you do to ‘gesture towards’, or ‘hold the space’ so that different voices can be heard?’ ‘Maybe in this ‘hearing’’, she suggests, ‘there may emerge a more collective response?’ 66 She makes the point that naming or bringing attention to something that is under the radar can give it support. She asks:

How can we refer to [these tiny shifts] or share them with others whilst all the while

leaving them alone? Is there a way of naming them that helps us to tap into or orient a

collective responsive gesture, to nurture connections not previously made?67



I am reminded of the work of Miranda Fricker on epistemic injustice.68 Fricker empha-sises that prejudice can diminish the level of credibility of a speaker. This can occur, she suggests, in reporting sexual assault or discrimination. Sometimes the issue is about the lack of available language to name or even conceptualise an experience. Fricker 178 Section V On Experiential Knowing in a Complex World



uses the example of children who cannot tell you about abuse because they have no conceptual framework or language to express or make any sense of it.

The ability to bestow meanings – to name things, acts and ideas – is a source of power.

Ray Ison 69



A thought to end with



To notice and pay attention to difference is one thing, but to see it as important is an-other. And to act, protect and nurture the nascent shoots and tiny shifts so they have room to grow takes yet another step. This piece started with the question of how to ‘know about’ tiny fractures of newness. But it ends with recognising that endeavouring to shape the future through protecting ‘difference’ and giving it a chance to flourish, can be a political act.





42


Staying with the trouble70



How following the path with all its details leads to insight and change.



In ‘From morning twilight’ [41], I explore how following the path of what happens allows us to spot the first glimmerings of change. Equally, through paying attention to

the minutiae of experience and staying with it as it unfolds, we may also be able to spot aspects that do not easily change. These may be things that we habitually gloss over without noticing. Noticing them may lead to new insights and to change.

In this piece, I want to explore ways to pay attention to ‘the path’ and show what this can achieve.

I use as an example an approach called embodied writing.71 The invitation is to trust there is value in observing the path of thoughts, activities and feelings as they happen without evaluating or making sense of them. Just keep writing – recording what hap-pens, what you feel, what you think.72 I share here a personal experiment of doing this. I am describing one morning of writing this book, when I paid attention to the writing process itself.



An example of embodied writing

Only ten minutes in, I break off and wander downstairs and out into the garden. I in-

spect the flower beds and pick a few purple flowers with their silver leaves. ‘So what’,

I think, as I do this, ‘what is the point in noticing I am picking flowers’. My mind goes

unbidden to a recent meeting. I remember something I said, and I lose energy; my shoul-

ders sag and my mood drops. I berate myself for handling it badly. I think: ‘I always say

too much, I never judge it well’. I feel miserable. I go back upstairs to continue writing.

Back at my desk, I start to edit something I’ve already written and then almost imme-

diately stand up and go downstairs, find some chocolate, then put on the washing. Sud-

denly I am back again in the memory of that meeting, the way people were, the way I

was. Why am I returning to this again and why now, when I am so desperate to get on

with writing? I feel hopeless.

In frustration, I throw down my metaphorical pen and go outside to eat some lunch. I

pick up The Wild Edge of Sorrow73. Weller suggests, in dealing with upset, we should “stay

attentive to the minute alterations”. He speaks of “allowing things to mature in the con-

tainer of the heart … [in order to] hear the subtle character and nuanced complexities of

our inner life”.74 I am moved by this, feel softer towards myself and sad.



As I reflect on this experience, a number of things strike me. The first is that my identi-fication with my behaviour in the meeting, which I deemed to have been ‘bad’, has the sort of fixed quality that complexity thinking, and Daoism are disavowing. I felt, during that morning of writing, that there was no solution, that I was doomed never to change. 180 Section V On Experiential Knowing in a Complex World



What struck me in a sudden flash, when I read those pages from Weller, is that I was limiting and indeed punishing myself with this idea that I can never change. It came to me that this is in direct contrast to the tenor of complexity thinking which points to the opposite – the contingent, fluid nature of things. Patterns can, it is true, get stuck, but equally they can change. People change; situations change; why shouldn’t I?

To borrow Donna Harroway’s phrase, it was ‘staying with the trouble’75 – following what happened and paying attention to its ‘minute particulars’ – that led me to insight and change. Paying detailed attention can both surface what is going on and at the same time bring a degree of closure to it, as emphasised in Gestalt thinking [47].



Staying with the story in organisational contexts



I have focused on a story of personal insight and change. But this ‘staying with the trou-ble’ is equally powerful in an organisational context – working on team development, developing business strategy or evaluating a project. It can lead to insight, and it can also lead to change. A sensitive facilitator, for example, can have a good sense, in real time, of how to spot and seize the moment; she intuits when it is helpful to confront and when to support or to wait. She is attuning to the group process and skilfully using this attunement to recognise, as the meeting progresses, where there are critical junc-tures and opportunities. She is not just driving relentlessly through a set agenda [23:4].

Let me give a further example.

I worked with a colleague on a method of impact assessment that did not assume ab initio that the project was necessarily the driving force in the change that occurred. We developed a method of homing in on critical junctures.



Changing the law in East Africa

We were exploring what had caused a change in financial regulation in a country in

East Africa.76 We interviewed various stakeholders and asked them to take us through

the history that led up the change. The interviews were very open-ended. We asked:

When did the idea for this law change arise? Can you tell us who was there and what

happened? And then what happened? And what else was going on in the country during

that time? What were other agencies doing? Were there key events and key players?

One aspect of the responses that shone out was the way people described what they

saw as turning points or ‘critical junctures’. We found that people tended to describe

such moments in great detail, with colour and emotional engagement. Furthermore, it

was striking the extent to which the stakeholders tended to mention the same turning

points. Nearly all those interviewed, for example, referred to a particular international

conference, which brought together local decision-makers with others from around the

world. This conference made people feel that the project was feasible and necessary and

42 Staying with the trouble 181



was seen as very significant in changing attitudes. The way the conference was

highlighted in the narratives about the project, together with what happened next (a

number of swift changes followed this event, and attitudes really seemed to shift due to

hearing about the experiences in other countries, and engaging with a ‘critical mass’ of

key players), reinforced that this was an important moment.



The interesting question raised by this example was whether identifying such critical junctures, and also exploring what led to them, could form an adjunct to more tradi-tional methods of impact assessment. Clearly, such an approach can be distorted by the fact that people tend to remember what happened in a way that emphasises their own agency. But this can be explored by triangulating these stories with each other and with accounts by people who were not actors in the project, as well as checking out written reports and other factual information. Such narrative approaches can also explore what happens between such critical moments, what lays the foundations and creates the conditions for change. In this project, we referred to this as ‘building the ground’. As complexity theory emphasises, ‘agency’ does not just reside in the critical juncture but is buried in the way the conditions and actions along the way have shifted and loosened the dynamics and brought the system to a place where it will not take much for the situation to tip into a new configuration.



And so …



Sometimes the ‘devil is in the detail’. Sometimes it is only through following the details of what is emerging that we can understand what led to what and how change hap-pened. Sometimes the very uncovering of the detail, as in my example of embodied writing, is what leads to change. Telling the story, living the story, feeling the story are vital aspects of gaining knowledge and insight.





Section VI




Becoming Ourselves





43


Introducing becoming ourselves



The individual is a complex, interwoven, relationally shaped ‘becoming’; theories of the

brain and the mind-body are very resonant with complexity thinking.

Only someone who is open to everything, who excludes nothing, even the least explain-

able, will experience a living connection to others and will, from that, create his own

authentic existence. Rainer Maria Rilke1



If the science of complexity, the science of open systems, gives a picture of the func-tioning of complex webs of inter-relating ‘things’, then we can expect this science to

offer a perspective from the smallest to the largest scale. Individual humans – as well as communities, regions, or ecologies – are open, relational, woven, diverse entities. We humans have ‘form’ and yet continue to be shaped and energised by the wider world of which we are a part. We are not fixed, but honed by everything that happens along the way. The insights gained from complexity theory can provide guidance and insight not only at the level of society or organisation, but also at this level of the individual.

This is not to suggest that these so-called scales – individual, organisational, global – are independent of one another. It is individuals in relationship that constellate societ-ies, and societies that engage with wider ecologies and economies to shape the global world. Indeed, there are times when particular individuals and unique events can have cataclysmic, global impacts. And vice versa, we – as individuals, families or communi-ties – are hugely impacted by the norms that have become established in our global and more local societies.

In this section, I am focusing on the way each of us is a complex pattern. Each of us is unique, complex, constituted through the relationship with our environment, and im-pacted by the people and pets and possessions that surround us. We are shaped by our past, shaped by our present – yet always becoming. In some ways we can feel caught in patterns – of behaviour, of expectations, of social norms – from which, at times, it can feel there is no escape. At other times, we adapt, transform and surprise ourselves.

Sometimes we experience ourselves as a complex congregation of characters, arguing between the part that wants another cake and the part that does not, or between our tendency to be selfish and our capacity to be other-centred. Sometimes we find our-selves a conundrum; we recognise a certain persistence in our behaviour, and yet at the same time experience ourselves disconcertedly morphing and shifting in relation to those around us, responding to changing circumstances, swayed by our feelings and moods. We cannot stop ourselves being irritated by the behaviour of someone close to us, and yet we can display a sudden spontaneity of affection, astound ourselves by speaking out, or find ourselves strangely entranced by a new interest.

186 Section VI Becoming Ourselves



All the experiences in your life – from single conversations to your broader culture –

shape the microscopic details of your brain. Neurally speaking, who you are depends

on where you’ve been. Your brain is a relentless shape shifter, constantly rewriting its

own circuitry – and because your experiences are unique, so are the vast detailed pat-

terns in your neural networks. Because they continue to change your whole life, your

identity is a moving target; it never reaches an endpoint. David Eagleman2

In my explorations I have found much that resonates with a complexity perspective. For example, consider the following quote:

It is not just that we are entangled in matter—we subjects who read, write, and rumi-

nate on what ‘we’ are. We are materializations entangled in other materializations; we

happen in our mattering. What matters in our ethics, our politics, our worlds entangle

us in and as new materializations. Catherine Keller and Mary-Jane Rubinstein 3

We affect each other and shape cultural norms, and then ‘culture’ impacts back on us. What we need to decide is what ‘matters’.

The work I describe in this section brings to life how the individual is constituted as a complexly woven pattern. These ideas do not apply to the ‘outer’ world alone; the less tangible ‘inner’ world of memories, gut instincts, beliefs and fears also behaves in ways that are in tune with a complexity perspective. Indeed, what makes us who we are is a complex interweaving of the tangible and intangible, the mind and the body, the inner and the outer.

Man grows according to his interpretation of himself. Viktor Frankl4

These realisations can help us to navigate more skilfully the world of which we are a part. The insight that we are always becoming – rather than seeing ourselves as fixed, unchanging, independent units – can inform the way to live and work.

In this section, I start with the science of the brain, drawing mainly on David Eagleman. Then, I touch on the work of Iain McGilchrist, particularly his exploration of the differ-ing personalities of the left and right hemispheres of the brain.5

Next, I focus on the effects of trauma. This illuminates how the past can get wired into persistent patterns of feeling and response and suggests how these seemingly unshift-ing patterns can be disentangled and lose their power.

Then I describe Gestalt psychology; the ideas intrinsic to Gestalt psychology are very in tune with complexity thinking and offer particular insights into ways of knowing and ways to engender change in both individuals and organisations.

Finally, building mainly on the memoir of Karen Armstrong,6 I explore the value of embracing both the uniqueness of our own nature and the uniqueness of the life that turns up for each of us. I consider what it means to lead the life we have, rather than focus on the life we want.

43 Introducing becoming ourselves 187



These pieces, quirky and particular, reflect my own interests; I have followed my nose. I am not trying to convince you that any of this is the ‘right’, or the ‘best’ way to look at things, or even that it is the most relevant or valuable. I am aiming to illuminate how theories of the individual often resonate with a view of the world as complex – pat-terned and sometimes locked-in, impacted by history, interwoven with others and with the context, and yet still able to change. My suggestion is to approach these pieces with a propensity to be interested and provoked, rather than viewing them as a definitive collection of ideas, or as presenting optimal solutions.

A final note: The pieces in this section collectively give a picture of what is called a ‘strong’ process view.7 People are not fixed entities; they do not remain untouched when engaging with each other and with the demands of the world around them. It seems fitting to suggest that people are processes – constantly shaping and being shaped, constantly becoming. I find it a relief to recognise that however fixed I feel by my history and circumstances, the reality is I am always able to change – even if the only difference I can make is to my attitudes.





44


Brain science



The brain is plastic; it displays patterns of relationships which morph over time depending

on circumstances. This accords with a processual view of complexity.



Some years ago, I watched a television series about the brain by neurologist David Eagleman and was struck by the resonances with a processual view of complexity. For example, his description of the development of the brain illustrates the importance of context and history in the formation and persistence of brain structures and pat-terns. I draw primarily on his book The Brain.8

Eagleman starts by describing how babies are born with their lifetime’s allowance of neurons. At the beginning there are few connections between the neurons, but these connections grow rapidly over the first two years and then start to wane. As he says, “the process of becoming who you are is defined by carving back the possibilities that were already present … our brains form fewer but stronger connections”.9 Patterns of connection evolve, depending on what the child experiences and on what the envi-ronment offers; and as certain patterns emerge, other patterns that could have been formed are no longer available. In this way, the particularity of individual brain struc-ture and patterns of connections becomes established.

The process of becoming who you are is defined by carving back the possibilities that

were already present. Eagleman10

In Eagleman’s words: “The indelible, microscopic impressions accumulate to make you who you are, constrain who you can become.”11 This is an example of the irreversibility of path dependency. I explain in ‘Path dependent’ [15] that what makes things irrevers-ible is that we – or an ecology, or in this instance, our brains – can never go back to what was. As one path is taken, other possibilities fade away. The paths that could have been taken are no longer available.12

Our brain and its structures and neural patterns are never fixed, and Eagleman ex-plains that even in adulthood, our brains continue to change. Nevertheless, there is a tendency for patterns to persist, due to the plasticity of the brain. Eagleman defines plasticity as “something that can be shaped and can hold that shape”;13 so, whilst the brain has a tendency to retain its shape, it can shift into new patterns of connection.

Although the brain loses cells and connections as we age, we are able to strengthen interconnections and neural pathways through diversifying and seeking out ‘learning experiences’ such as taking up an instrument or studying a new language. Unstimu-lated brains atrophy; stimulated brains show increased resilience and are more able to shift functions to other parts if there is brain disease, or brain damage due to an ac-cident. Indeed, the brain, Eagleman says, is like a city. Its functions are not “the sum of 190 Section VI Becoming Ourselves



activity in a collection of well-defined modules”.14 The brain works like a complex inter-connected organism, not like a machine with well-defined parts and fixed connections. Its properties are not held in one place but are systemic and holistic. Continuing the city analogy, Eagleman asks, where is the economy of a city situated? As he says, “the economy emerges from the interaction of all the elements – from the stores and banks to the merchants and the customers”.15 The brain works in a similar way.

Eagleman goes on to explore how this systemic networking of the brain extends into our relationships with others, and with the wider world. He says: “Although we typi-cally feel independent, each of our brains is a rich web of interaction with one anoth-er.”16 Neuroscientists have discovered an underlying process that underpins the way interacting with others shapes our brains and behaviours, and this is through mirror neurons. “Mirror neurons are one of the most important discoveries in the last decade of neuroscience” say Sourya Acharya and Samarth Shikla.17 Essentially, mirror neurons respond to actions that we observe in others, and more importantly react as we infer the intentions behind others’ actions. It is thought that the system of mirror neurons underpins our ability to empathise, and indeed is the basic mechanism from which language evolved. The impact of our interactions with the wider world, as observed through magnetic resonance techniques, create visible changes in the brain.

Brain science echoes our experience that we are an individual, but not an island; we connect to and are influenced by those around us. Society is “built on layers of com-plex social interactions”.18 Our brains are patterns of connection that can structurally change; these patterns adapt as a result of atrophy or accident, and also respond to who we are with and how we relate to our environments. A great example of environ-mentally driven structural change is displayed in the brains of taxi drivers in London who, through studying ‘The Knowledge’ of street names and routes, grow the hippo-campus where spatial memory is stored.19



In summary



The brain, like a city or society, is a complexly interwoven set of patterns of relation-ships. It has a tendency for patterns to sustain and yet nevertheless can adapt and change. The patterns are shaped by history and context. This work from the field of neuroscience is very much in tune with our understanding of complex open systems. And both remind us that while we may feel that we and others do not easily change, that we are ‘just the way we are’, change can and does happen, and this can be aided by the experiences we choose and the people and places we seek out. At the very least, we have some power over the way we frame and respond to our situations, however hard that may seem to achieve.

What is my ‘take home’ from this? A reminder that I am not stuck but can and do change. I react to circumstances, but also, and more gradually, I develop – in response to life, and to the people I interact with, and through my own intent. How I manifest myself at any given time is relationally held. I am less a ‘me’ than I imagine myself to be. I like that view; there is still all to play for.

44 Brain science 191



Finally, I am delighted to find the similarities between an understanding of complexity when studying galaxies or ponds or societies and an understanding gleaned by neu-roscientists from peering into the brain. For myself, that strengthens each body of knowledge, and offers the possibility of ‘flirting with’ and ‘trying on’ insights from each sphere.





45


I’ve looked at life from both sides now20



The brain is asymmetrical, and we can enhance our ability to handle complex problems and

assess complex contexts through loosening up on our belief in the power of reason.



As discussed in Section II, process complexity presents a view of the world as com-plex, systemic, patterned, emergent, surprising, creative and messy. Its core mes-sage is that the world does not work like a machine; things cannot in general be reduced to separate independent domains, and logic and analysis do not lead to definitive an-swers. The world is complex whether or not we choose to embrace that complexity.

Iain McGilchrist is a psychiatrist who has spent decades developing an understanding of the way the brain works, particularly focusing on the role and ‘personality’ of the two cerebral hemispheres, known colloquially as the left-brain and right-brain. He is keen to show why we are so attracted to the hope that the world will demonstrate that it does indeed have machine-like characteristics, amenable to being understood through rational logic. He asks what causes us to cling to a view of the world as controllable, predictable and objectively ‘there’. McGilchrist addresses this question by contrasting the differing worldviews of the two hemispheres.

McGilchrist describes the signature of the left hemisphere as focused on the detail of the known, the fixed and that which is categorizable. Left-brain processes, he explains, tend to isolate things from their context and de-animate them. “The left hemisphere”, he says, “gives us a representation, a map of that which has gone before.”21

In contrast, McGilchrist describes how the right hemisphere deals with things as inter-connected, situated in context, flowing and changing, unique and animate. The right-brain, he says, is in touch with what is alive and emerging now, in all its richness and complexity; it is attuned to the big picture, is imaginative and intuitive.

I found this very intriguing, as these right-brain/left-brain signatures accord pretty much exactly with the comparison of the machine worldview and the complexity worl-dview. Whether starting with the science of open systems or, as McGilchrist does, with the processing of the brain hemispheres, we arrive at a very similar perspective.

McGilchrist argues that right-brain processing is most able to apprehend what is there and attune to the scene or the situation. The right-brain is conditioned to undertake reconnaissance, to engage on a quest to spot patterns and register nuance. The left-brain, he argues, is best at working out how to act. Left-brain processes are adept at converting into a useable map the complex terrain uncovered by the right-brain.

I believe that nowadays we live no longer in the presence of the world, but rather in

a representation of it … this representation has all the qualities of a virtual image:

an infinitely thin, immobile, fragment of a vast, seamless, living ever-flowing whole.

McGilchrist22

194 Section VI Becoming Ourselves



This categorisation of left-brain and right-brain can seem rather stark. We each weave a complex mix of ways of understanding, ways of knowing, and ways of determining action. I am not competent to critique McGilchrist’s neuroscience, but I do not believe he is disputing the brain’s complexity. He is emphasising that the brain is not symmet-rical, and its two hemispheres are relatively physically independent. He explains that experiences of people with brain damage (which points to which part of the brain leads to what) demonstrates that we have these two rather different brain personalities to call on. We may be skilled at moving between the two and we may use both at once. We can indeed, he argues, create a disposition towards connecting the two. But many of us, he contends, has a tendency to prioritise the use of one hemisphere over the other.

Each hemisphere has inevitably a distinctive ‘take’ on the structure of the world, which

is why we have two hemispheres at all. McGilchrist23



In The Matter with Things, McGilchrist develops his work on brains into a wide-reach-ing metaphysical quest – engaging with science, philosophy and art, and leaving few stones unturned. His conclusion is that the nature of the world mirrors the worldview of the right-brain. I would add that, in the main, this right-brain perspective mirrors the worldview embedded in process complexity.

The problem, says McGilchrist, is when the left-brain is in the lead. If that is the case, we may start to believe the world really is machine-like – not a model, but the truth. In that case, the important complexity, detail and nuance is likely to be discounted at the outset [55].

Complexity is the norm, and simplicity represents a special case of complexity, achieved

by cleaving off and disregarding almost all of the vast reality that surrounds whatever

it is we are for the moment modelling as simple. McGilchrist24

The problem is that the very brain mechanisms which succeed in simplifying the world

so as to subject it to our control militate against a true understanding of it. Meanwhile,

compounding the problem, we take the success we have in manipulating it as proof that

we understand it. McGilchrist25



McGilchrist suggests that a right-brain preference was more prevalent when we were hunter-gatherers – when, to survive, we needed to read the landscape and learn the habits of our prey. We needed to be able to spot their subtle movements within that landscape. The left-brain – the emissary in McGilchrist’s terms – is the tool most capa-ble of planning and executing actions after the right-brain – the master – has surveyed the scene and located the prey. The left-brain can cut through detail and make deci-sions that galvanise us into action. It stops us from being doomed to wallow in shallows and miseries, to quote Shakespeare.26 There is a danger, if we immerse ourselves to such a degree in the totality of the complex picture, that we may feel overwhelmed and unable to determine what to do.

45 I’ve looked at life from both sides now 195



Another point McGilchrist makes is that it is the right-brain that deals with values. He says that the only ‘value’ the left-brain recognises is power. If the left-brain dominates, we are more likely to see decision-making as essentially about analysing data and mak-ing logical technocratic decisions – for the sole purpose of achieving our goals. We be-lieve there is a right answer, and that we will get there through detailed processing. By contrast, the right-brain is more comfortable with ambiguity and subjectivity, can juggle sometimes-incommensurate values, and make complex judgements.

Over centuries Man seems to have found greater reward in preferencing the left-brain over the right. He has found he can win the games he plays through hard-edged ratio-nal thinking. When such an approach is expected and rewarded, then Man will focus on short-term goals and worry less about the indeterminate future. He may, for example, go all out to fulfil the purposes of an organisation even if these are questionable. I re-member a conversation with an executive of a tobacco company whose job it was to find barely legal ways of selling cigarettes to Indian children. The saying is that people in such roles need to ‘leave their values at the door’ when they go to work and pick them up again on the way home.

The left-brain is likely to feel there is an ‘either-or’ choice to be made – either the world goes to plan, or it is in chaos; either data analysis will lead us to the answer, or we won’t know what is going on. The right-brain is comfortable with juggling both qualitative and quantitative data, and with making judgements when there is a need to weigh up conflicting goals. There used to be a management maxim that, ‘everything that can be measured can be managed’. Maybe that sounds sensible. The problem lies with the fact that not everything that is there, that materially affects outcomes, is unambiguously, objectively measurable. If we only manage what we can measure, we may ignore many vital subjective and nuanced factors just because they cannot be pinned down.

Much more could be said, and I recommend McGilchrist’s writing to you and also the work of Jeremy Lent, who argues in similar vein in the opening section of The Web of Meaning.27 In a nutshell, in handling complex problems, we should aim – in first sizing up the situation, then deciding on action and acting, and finally reviewing the consequences – for right-left-right, and avoid at all costs left-left-left!



Stories of reflection



It is often my experience that when I start to engage with a set of ideas, examples in the real world pop up and provide some sort of mirroring. The first example came by way of a text from one of my nephews and got me thinking about the role of imagination in science.



196 Section VI Becoming Ourselves



String theory by text

My nephew asked me if I could explain string theory to him in a few sentences. (This is a

theory, by the way, that the basic building blocks of matter are stringlike and have sev-

en dimensions.) I answered that I couldn’t. He persisted and started to explain that he

could understand the idea of three dimensions, and make sense of the fourth dimension

of time, but he couldn’t get his mind around the idea of the seven dimensions referred to

in string theory. It occurred to me that he was doing what we are trained to do with left-

brain thinking: analyse the problem in terms we already understand, then seek to build

on the new parts on top. He wanted to reach a model that was amenable to objective

rational analysis. He was very disappointed that I couldn’t oblige. There will most likely

be such a left-brain ‘answer’ if you have access to the mathematics of string theory, but

it occurred to me that any ‘understanding’, unless you are steeped in the maths, is more

likely to come pictorially, holistically.



This story of ‘string theory by text’ reminds me of physicist Richard Feynman, who came to an insight through watching someone spinning plates. His initial inspiration did not come from the left-brain, although he certainly needed it for the maths that followed.

Some guy, fooling around, throws a plate in the air and I notice the red medallion of

Cornell went around faster than the wobbling. … I start to figure out the motion. … Ev-

erything flowed out effortlessly. … The diagrams and the whole business I got the No-

bel Prize for came from that piddling about with the wobbling plate. Richard Feynman28



The second example revealed itself in a wonderfully engaging book, Muppets in Mos-cow.29 This is a powerful story, and I am sure there are parallel stories from many places in the world. The example shows that change happened when the logic of the left-brain – both in the planning and in the discussion – gave way to a story which connected with the right-brain, painted a picture that participants could connect to emotionally, and aroused their values. From that point on, things changed, different objectives were suggested, and different decisions were reached. Furthermore, the change emerged holistically, was felt viscerally in the room, and shifted the patterns of meaning and connection. But of course, as the example illustrates, without proper planning, objec-tives and agendas, that point of transition would not have happened. We can’t have one without the other.



Muppets in Moscow

Natasha Lance Rogoff describes how, in the early 1990s, a US children’s programme

Sesame Street was introduced into Russia and the former USSR as a way to help chil-

dren explore what it means to live in a post-communist era, where they would likely

encounter democratic and economic freedoms not previously experienced. The story

that caught my attention describes a workshop held to define the curriculum of the pro-

gramme and discuss the values and behaviours that the series would aim to illuminate.

45 I’ve looked at life from both sides now 197



The workshop, which included educationalists and programme designers from the US

as well as Russia, was meticulously planned and well researched, utilising the left-brain

at its best.

But all did not go to plan. The author recounts a particular moment where everything

became very tricky. The issue centred on whether disabled children should be included

in the filming. One person felt that would be exploitative; another argued that it would

be depressing and that children should be allowed to ‘forget about those hardships’ and

just enjoy the fun of the programme. There was an impasse. Finally, a woman spoke up.

She said: “For decades … our town, populated mostly by Turkic people, was used as a

dumping ground for hazardous chemicals … the rate of birth defects is one of the high-

est in Russia. … I work with wheelchair-bound children every day … you can’t imagine

how much [they] long to live in a world where they are not treated with contempt.”30

The author of the book, decades later, recalls that moment with great clarity. She said:

“It was as if an angel had descended into our presence … the harsh language moments

earlier evaporated into thin air. I sense everyone realising that something otherworldly

is taking place. … We are overcome with a deep sense of our responsibility and an un-

derstanding of how Ulitsa Sezam [Sesame Street] can help fight prejudice and encourage

tolerance.”31



The third example comes from listening to an audio version of Ian McEwan’s Machines like Me.32 The novel explores the way a robot, purchased by the protagonists, a man and a woman, develops a form of consciousness. The robot declares he loves the woman and becomes deeply involved in their lives. The dénouement comes when the robot reports her to the police. The robot loves her and is driven by the logic that she will be best served if her crime comes to light.

What McEwan is exploring and questioning is whether you can programme a machine to handle complex judgements that weave ethics, justice and love and is sensitive to circumstance. There is no logic-based algorithm, no left-brain method that can be pro-grammed to make such nuanced judgements as to what constitutes ethical behaviour, and what is fair. In other words, it is well-nigh impossible to turn right-brain, holis-tic, nuanced thinking into machine-like logic, no matter how complicated that logic becomes.



And so …



The purpose in writing this piece is not to champion the distinction between left and right-brain, nor to see this framing as the ultimate truth about brain science. Rather, I find myself saying: ‘Isn’t it interesting that McGilchrist, from his neurological starting point, ends up in a similar place to those of us focused on the distinction between ma-chine and complexity thinking.’

I also note that art, including the use of film and story as in Muppets in Moscow and Machines Like Us, gives a way of both describing and connecting us to a right-brain type of engagement, as I explore in ‘Artful art’ [39].

198 Section VI Becoming Ourselves



Art, because it is not subject to purposive language-bound rationality, is capable of

re-linking us with our context. Peter Reason33

The world is patterned, emergent, dynamic, subjective and contextual. Whilst, in some instances, we can manipulate the world by simplifying and ignoring some of this com-plexity, the underlying complexity remains. I am encouraged by McGilchrist’s emphasis that there is a difference between how we apprehend the world (where right-brain pro-cesses are most suited) and how we then develop plans to act in that world (accessing the strength of left-brain processes). The plasticity of the brain, as discussed in ‘Brain science’ [44], encourages the view that we have some choice over how we use and develop our brain; potentially we can change our habits of thought.





46


Shadows from the past



How work on trauma illustrates how patterns can become ‘stuck’ and how they can be

loosened, both for individuals and potentially for societies.

There are instincts which are deeper than reason. Arthur Conan Doyle



In ‘Brain science,’ [44] I drew on neuroscience. Here I am drawing on theories of the person that have emerged from the fields of psychology and psychotherapy, with

their many traditions and long histories. In particular, I explore the need to pay at-tention to the sometimes deep-seated ‘shadows’ or ‘complexes’ in our own personal stories, buried within our psyches. I finish by considering the relevance of this work to ‘letting go of the past’ at a societal level.

Conceptual frameworks regarding the nature of the person have been in circulation in the West since the Ancient Greeks. For example, Hippocrates, in the fifth century B.C.E., conceived of the personality as being made up of a mix of four temperaments – sanguine, choleric, phlegmatic and melancholic. More recently, there has arisen a view of the person as a community of sub-personalities.34 Then there are models which dis-cuss, variously, the self, the ego, the subconscious, the unconscious, the persona, the super-ego, and the id.35 The common theme is that each of us does not display a uni-form, stable, well-defined personality; we can be gripped by differing aspects of our-selves in differing circumstances. Often, we experience more than one aspect at once, and struggle to work out what we feel or think because different parts want different things. And things change; whatever seems salient, unassailable and vital now can shift over time – or, indeed, can sometimes shift in a flash.

Some theories emphasise that what we become is shaped by the way memory is held not just in the brain, but in the integrated nervous system of the brain-body. The brain-body works systemically, and our histories fashion the development of our particular traits and quirks. What we are becoming is personal and path-dependent – moulded by our nature, our nurture, and by the things that happen and the actions we take along the way. It is conditioned by the norms of the cultures in which we live.



Trauma



Trauma and traumatic events can create particularly potent ‘shadows from the past’. Trauma encapsulates the response in the body-brain system to distressing experiences. Children who experience such events or have had to handle ongoing problematic con-ditions with little support, may develop trauma – and this can impact their develop-ment and their adult lives. Unresolved trauma can lead to deeply entrenched patterns called complexes. These operate like whirlpools in our body-brain neurology; these 200 Section VI Becoming Ourselves



can become locked-in and shape future behaviour in ways that can be very powerful and difficult to mediate. They can form part of what Jung named our shadow – aspects of our unconscious operating under the wire of our conscious minds; the shadow can powerfully drive behaviour.36 Complexes can be hard to budge, and yet shifts can be made, demonstrating the plasticity of the psyche.37

Trauma can result from threat, stress, or disruptions to intimacy and bonding. As chil-dren, we developed survival mechanisms to keep us away from difficult feelings, such as control, pacifying others, becoming numb, or adopting addictive behaviours. As adults, situations that remind us of early trauma can trigger sudden outbursts of seem-ingly atypical behaviour when our defences don’t hold.

There has been much interest in recent times in so-called somatic therapies which work on the understanding that trauma can be held in the body tissue. This can lead to long-standing muscle tension or patterns of body posture, such as habitually hunching the shoulders. Peter Levine discusses how animals that have had a fright tend to shake the body and release the tension; humans however find it harder to ‘shake things off’.38 We are more likely to resort to problem-solving to try to overcome difficulties and find it less easy to imagine that the body holds the key. Consequently, trauma can become trapped in the body-mind and continue to haunt us. Working with body sensations can loosen these patterns in the body-mind; some believe such an approach is more ef-fective than ‘talking therapies’ as these can actually reinforce persistent patterns of thinking.

Even when early childhood events were less extreme, they can nevertheless influence what we become – more cerebral than inclined towards relationships, more likely to want to please others than ourselves, or maybe more dominant than submissive. The suggestion is that many of these tendencies operate in ways that we find difficult to understand and consciously choose. We are driven by our animal instincts more than we like to recognise. We can become ‘in their grip’.39

‘Having been’ is also a kind of being. Viktor Frankl40



Mark Epstein, in The Trauma of Everyday Life,41 further explains that entrenched com-plexes resulting from childhood experiences are held as unformed, inchoate memories, which he calls intrinsic memories. They differ from normal memories, as they are not attached to a narrative, nor accessible consciously, but are ‘split-off’. He suggests that it is only by consciously re-experiencing these childhood traumas later in life, and feel-ing their affect, that we transfer them into conscious narrative memory, where they then can be attended to. This is easy to say but not easy to do, as we experience these split-off traumatic feelings as if we were small children, and they can feel overwhelm-ing. Equally, more recent events which mirror the losses of the small child, can trigger these inchoate memories, these complexes, and make it hard to distinguish the impact of recent occurrences from deeply buried responses to early trauma.

46 Shadows from the past 201



Handling loss – a personal example

I became particularly interested in the power of locked-in patterns in the psyche after

dealing with an episode of personal loss. This catapulted me into a level of grief that

took me by surprise and seemed to my adult reasoning to be rather out of proportion

to what had happened. My experience of these distressing feelings seems to exemplify

Epstein’s theory of inchoate memories in the sense that there was no easily accessible

story or memory attached to them. Following his advice, I tried to stick with the feelings

rather than distract myself or dismiss them.

At the same time, I found that my mind took me back, unbidden, to previous upsetting

events. I recalled images related to these earlier events in great detail – the colours and

light in the room at the time, who was there, what was said, how I felt. It occurred to me

later that I was transforming these earlier memories, as I grieved this current loss, into

Epstein’s narrative memories – memories of which I started to become more conscious-

ly aware and could then situate in time. This led to insight, and I was able to get a handle

on the backstory that was affecting my reactions. It also resulted in a diminishment of

the intensity of feeling triggered by the more recent loss.

As I reflected later on this, I noted that change occurred by ‘staying with the process’

[42]. I tried not to distract myself or dismiss all this as self-indulgent; I gave myself time.

It also seemed to me that this was the stuff of my body as much as my mind; when in

the grip of these memories and griefs, I might feel shaky or my heart race. Long walks

helped me to escape from the ruminating mind, with its tendency to circle around and

go over events, looking for explanations.



I find this work on trauma fascinating. This perspective on complexes in the human psy-che chimes with the ideas from complexity thinking as to the way patterns and norms can become locked-in. In these examples of personal trauma, the patterns reside in the intangible realm of thoughts and ideas. But these persistent patterns of our inner worlds are likely to translate into outer behaviour. And given that society is shaped by our collective behaviour, it begs the question as to how unresolved trauma might impact the development of societal norms.



What do I take from all this that may have wider applicability?



Trauma as the root of sociopathic behaviour, and how to deal with sociopaths



First, it has been suggested that such deep-seated traumatic complexes may underpin the behaviour of leaders who have sociopathic tendencies – ones who are willing and able to operate in ways that seem to pay little heed to human or environmental cost.42 Insatiable greed, unresolving hatred, or the never-ending desire for revenge can be viewed as unconscious responses to childhood trauma. Experts in trauma advise that such individuals are unlikely to heed a call for compromise, or an appeal to reason. It is 202 Section VI Becoming Ourselves



better to define limits and boundaries, and to stand up to such behaviours in ways that neither appease, nor provoke, but are firm and decisive. The point is that you cannot reason with someone in the grip of such primitive complexes.

Staying with the trouble



Secondly, I am interested in how my own work to handle loss required me to follow the unfolding of the minutiae of experience, and required me to stay, as much as I could, with the associated unravelling of uncomfortable feelings. Shifts were often tiny – a small insight, a sudden release from whirring tension, or from repetitive fixed thoughts. The resolution and change seemed to require this ‘staying with the trouble’. The idea of paying attention, when working with groups, to tiny happenings, in order to effect group change, is explored in [42]. This is a theme that is also very resonant with Daoist practice [27]. The point is that by sticking to the moment-by-moment connection of what is happening, something, somewhat miraculously, resolves [47].

Surfacing and building on the positives ...



Thirdly, my focus on personal grief and what it surfaced for me, paradoxically brought to the fore other aspects of who I am, aspects which are sustaining and supportive – such as loyalty, a dogged determination to see things through, humour and creativity. This was very important to me at the time and stopped me from feeling defeated by life’s difficulties. Post-Jungian psychotherapist James Hillman, in The Soul’s Code, takes this further.43 He suggests that we should take the time to explore and articulate for ourselves what we see as our essence, the core of who we are. We do this, he suggests, through noting our long-standing interests and recognising what seems vital to our nature. Have we always wanted to be an artist, or an activist, or to work with children? Are we happiest alone or in the centre of things? For what do we yearn? Hillman feels we should seek to surface, and then embrace wholeheartedly, the task of becoming what we feel at heart we should be – a view of ourselves untrammelled by others’ and society’s expectations, and undiluted by our own doubts and sabotages. Jung called this individuation. The emphasis is on ‘becoming our best selves’ rather than aiming to become ‘normal’ in the eyes of society.

The point is that, through focusing on what we do well, on what we are proud of in ourselves, we can build positive structures in the body-mind. Preferencing thoughts that appreciate our talents, and emphasise what we like about ourselves, can promote virtuous circles in the same way that self-criticism and ruminating about the past can do the opposite.

… whilst not ignoring the ‘negatives’



But herein lies a trap. Being positive and ignoring the negative will not make these deep-seated neurological complexes merely go away. There needs to be a balance between attending to what is problematic or difficult, and nurturing those aspects of ourselves that offer positive resources. In a society relentlessly addicted to progress,

46 Shadows from the past 203



there can be a tendency to focus only on the positive, to want to forge ahead and let the past be the past. But Jung and others emphasise the importance of reaching into the shadows and complexes, to release their energy and liberate the insights they contain.

[We must go into] the despair and darkness and fear that grips us all, and dispel the

notion that denial, numbing, or escape are valid options. Mathew Fox44

What disintegrates in periods of rapid transformation is not the self, but its defences

and ideas. We are not objects that can break. As open systems we are ‘but whirlpools in

a river of ever-flowing water. We are not stuff that abides but patterns that perpetuate

themselves’. … Our ‘going to pieces’, however uncomfortable, can open us up to new

perceptions, new data and new responses. Joanna Macy45

Grief dissolves judgement, it is a solvent to self-righteousness.



Working collectively to let go of the past …



History shows that unacknowledged impacts of racism, slavery and colonialism don’t just go away, but fester for generations. If unattended, the shadow of people and of societies will hold energy in the system and contribute to the way the future unfolds. Through seeking to reveal and acknowledge the shadow, we – both individually and collectively – can move forwards in a way that builds on more solid foundations.

Forgiveness projects in prisons,46 and practices of truth and reconciliation in post apartheid South Africa,47 are examples of working with collective trauma. Acknowl-edging, collectively as well as personally, past traumas and the indignities that were inflicted on the powerless, can release the past.

Francis Weller, in The Wild Edge of Sorrow, describes the way such collective rituals can provide both containment and release.48 Together, participants can pay witness to the past, and commit to future actions and intentions.

... even when those past memories do not seem of concern



I was also reminded that these childhood memories are not always negative, and yet can still hold sway in unexpected ways. George Monbiot notes the surprising power of children’s stories based on farms and farm animals.49 As he says: “The stories they tell are a version of an ancient idyll [in which] livestock farming is a place of safety, harmony and comfort.” His point is that this warm, romantic, collective memory from our childhood reading can colour what we feel is the solution to current ills – a return, perhaps, to “medieval production systems” which, as Monbiot says, “cannot feed a twenty-first-century population”. Monbiot tells us that Jeremy Lent calls such an im-age of Daisy the Cow a root metaphor, an “idea so deeply embedded in our minds that it affects our preferences without our conscious knowing”.50 This was a new thought for me. It had not occurred to me that idyllic images of times past may be as unhelpful in shaping our attitudes as buried prejudices.

204 Section VI Becoming Ourselves



In conclusion



The practice of paying detailed attention to experience seems to reveal a great deal about what it is to be a living being. Our unique complexities can be understood as the emerging, stabilising, and sometimes burying of locked-in patterns. Despite their deep-seated nature, such patterns can be made conscious and then shifted and dis-solved through accessing the subtle, intricate-yet-frozen energies held in the memo-ries of the body-mind.

This perspective has relevance in endeavouring to change organisations and societies, as well as for families and individuals. There is power in embracing a holistic, embodied view of what it is to be a living being. It can help us to find the courage, individually and collectively, to face the shadows of the past. Furthermore, paying detailed attention to the unfolding of thoughts and feelings which arise as we aim to face stuck patterns, is a very powerful way to create change.





47


Riding on Gestalt cycles51



Gestalt thinking, Gestalt field, cycles and patterns.



I came across gestalt psychology in the early 1990s, a few years before I discovered complexity theory, and I wonder if the one led me into the other as their ideas are so

connected.

The German word gestalt translates as whole or pattern.52 Ludwig Von Bertalanffy53 was very aware of the connection between systems thinking and gestalt psychology. As he said: “Gestalt psychology showed the existence and primacy of psychological wholes which are not a summation of elementary units.”54

Whatever else personality might be, it has the properties of a system. Von Bertalanffy55



The early gestalt psychologists, such as Wolfgang Kôhler,56 were interested in percep-tion and concluded that we are “hard-wired to recognise a whole, meaningful pattern rather than to see individual constituent parts”.57 In other words, we see a picture rath-er than a group of separate objects on a canvas; we hear a tune rather than individual notes. This fits very much with Iain McGilchrist’s description of the way the right-brain has an ability to see connections, patterns and wholes [45]. I describe, in ‘Wholesome fare’ [40], how we can use this ability to sense into patterns and gestalts to gain a ‘sense of knowing’ in the world at large.

In the 1960s, Fritz Perls took gestalt thinking into psychotherapy. He considered the personality to be an organismic whole – embedded in the sensations in the body as much as in thoughts and emotions. Thus the therapist, in working with the client, must work systemically, asking about the physical sensations of the body and about what is emerging in the imagination in addition to exploring thoughts and feelings.

The focus is on working in the ‘here and now’, as opposed to recalling stories or re-hearsed interpretations of the past. In that way, both client and therapist are engag-ing with experience as it is happening, not analysing remembered and possibly dis-torted fragments. Furthermore, the therapist takes note of her own reactions, both of thoughts and sensations, and considers whether these might be projections of the disowned feelings and reactions of the client. Gestalt psychotherapy suggests that, by working, as much as is possible, with the systemic entirety of experience in relationship, change becomes possible.

The theory supposes that any projections and counter-reactions are transmitted through the gestalt field, defined as the relational space through which we mutual-ly impact each other. This field theory is mirrored in some theories of physics – akin to the way ripples spread out and intersect on a lake [6]. Gestalt psychology sees the person not as fixed, but as more impermanent and relational – affected by and affect-206 Section VI Becoming Ourselves



ing what is happening around her. This view has its roots in organismic conceptions of biology, seeing the living system as unified by the relationships between its aspects and also fuelled by the exchange of energy, resources and information with the wider environment in which its sits. Gestalt psychology takes this systemic, holistic view of the person and pays attention to how the client is responding to, and situated within, her environment – in effect theorising the person as a shifting pattern or process in an open system. This is very in tune with process complexity.



The Gestalt cycle of experience



Working with immediate experience centres on the ‘gestalt cycle of experience’.58 What Perls (who developed this idea) is saying is that we, as biological organisms, are continually trying to self-regulate and be part of harmonious comfortable cycles of ex-perience. If these reflexive cycles are flowing well, then, when we act, we are likely to find that the world responds in ways we expect. We feel in harmony with our modus operandi; our responses come naturally and gain the reactions we expect.

However, when these cycles of experience are incomplete, when we are tripped by some unresolved aspect of our past, then life does not proceed in such a harmonious fashion. If we can identify where the ‘break in the cycle’ occurs and find ways to com-plete it, then, the theory goes, it will lose its power and the issue will retreat into the background. It is as if there is a boulder in a river or a fallen tree that gives rise to an eddy; if the obstacle is removed, the flow of the river becomes more uniform. This is reminiscent of the notion of complexes and inchoate memories discussed in [46] and accords with the idea of ‘staying with the trouble’ described in [42]. This ‘both-and’ of effecting change through engaging with patterns of behaviour – while at the same time paying attention to the ‘minute particulars’ of unfolding experience – is a recur-ring theme.



Stuck patterns in organisations

As well as working at the level of the individual, this gestalt perspective is also useful in

working at the organisational level. For example, I worked, as a consultant, for a com-

pany in Holland exploring what epitomised their culture and how it differed from their

partner organisation in the UK. One of the issues that became apparent in the inter-

views I undertook was the reluctance of staff to share creative ideas with the Board.

Board members could not understand why they were not able to entice staff to seek

support for any innovative ideas they might be hatching and gain Board support to de-

velop them. When I explored the reasons why, staff told me about a former Chief Execu-

tive who was so challenging and critical of presentations at Board meetings that people

decided never to put themselves through that again. Despite him having left some years

before, the memory of those humiliating presentations had remained in the organisa-

tional culture and still held energy; people continued to recount the stories of these en-

counters with detail and verve. The new Board needed to acknowledge and articulate

this cultural echo head-on and allow it to be surfaced and re-positioned as something

47 Riding on Gestalt cycles 207



from the past. Then the Board could aim to create fresh ways to progress innovation in

the organisation, less distorted by this cultural memory.



The value of ‘reading’ complexity through other lenses



When I explore ideas that resonate with complexity thinking, yet emerged from differ-ent stables, I sometimes find nuance and new depth in concepts I think I have already mastered. For example, the idea of the gestalt cycle of experience caused me to reflect more deeply on the notion of patterns. If a gestalt cycle is complete, it sinks from view. So, does that mean there is no pattern? For patterns to sustain they need a degree of variation, or disturbance. This can be ‘good’ when the focus is on maintaining resil-ience in ecologies and ‘bad’ when inadvertently sustaining past trauma. I found that intriguing.

Thinking between bodies of knowledge, exploring similarity and difference, can ward against landing on tidy understandings which can then be packed away as ‘under-stood’. Tussling with different perspectives stops, to use gestalt language, the cycle of understanding being completed, and makes the quest to understand concepts a living, never-ending process.





48


The life that I have is all that I have59



Embracing our uniqueness and stepping into the flow of life as it presents itself, does not

mean we cannot change, but rather that we work with our uniqueness and do not feel driv-

en by cultural conditioning which asks us to ‘be normal’.

It’s not a bug, it’s a feature.60

Life is what happens to you while you’re busy making other plans. John Lennon and

Yoko Ono



Each of our lives is unique, unfolding in ways that are contextual, path-dependent and emergent. To see life this way can feel a relief compared with the myth of prog-

ress, or the myth of being able, with a bit of effort, to overcome limitations and become ‘normal’. If we focus on normality, we may feel that, if only we could deal with our lim-itations, or apply for the right job, or live in the right place, then all would be well. A culture focused on normality can lead us to believe that, with a little effort, we should be able to do anything – and it must be our fault if we do not succeed.

From where does this idea – of striving to be perfect, or normal – originate? In ‘Uni-ty and uniqueness’ [5], I describe Plato’s theory of ideal forms and how he saw Man’s task as striving for Perfection. Perfection, like Truth or Beauty, was seen as a universal concept; this stands is in opposition to the idea that we should aim to be perfect in our own way, contingent on circumstances. It is one small step to say that if we are to be Perfect according to a universal standard, then we should, in similar vein, aspire to be Normal. Interestingly, this theme, in the early days of psychoanalysis, contributed to the split between Freud and Jung. Freud’s focus was more on aiming to help each per-son put right what had gone wrong in order to become ‘normal’. Jung’s saw the person in a more complex way – impacted (through the collective unconscious) by the collec-tive past, affected by childhood, shaped by individual personality and talents, as well as by personal aspirations. Jung’s thesis centred on ‘becoming your best self’ through individuation.61

A normative view can be both punishing and unrealistic. It can lead us to feel inade-quate as leaders, or parents – or children, for that matter – because we do not excel in all aspects of what is expected. We take for granted those things that come naturally to us, but we berate ourselves for those that do not.

The question is: What would happen if we relaxed into being who we are and living the life we have, rather than the life we want?

Some years ago, I came across The Spiral Staircase,62 the memoir of Karen Armstrong. In it she tells the story of her unusual life. She became a nun, then left the order and struggled to re-enter secular society. She studied at Oxford but felt thwarted in her academic career and moved to school teaching. Subsequently, her teaching career also 210 Section VI Becoming Ourselves



came to a grinding halt. She finally focused, with great success, on writing about reli-gion and spirituality. She has never married and lives, she says, a life of solitude. In her memoir, she reflects on what it has taken for her to embrace her unique nature and accept and attune to what life has thrown her.

She tells of the impact of reading Ash Wednesday, a poem by T. S. Eliot.63 The poem speaks of giving up on “desiring this man’s gift and that man’s scope” and ceasing to mourn the “vanished power of the usual reign”. Armstrong says:

I had … given up hope, and yet [recognised] that could be the way forwards. … Ever

since I left the convent, I had tried to be normal, to be just like everyone else. It was

time to give up ‘desiring that man’s gift’ or ‘that man’s scope’ and to refuse any longer to

submit to the ‘usual reign’. 64



The poem continues: “I cannot drink there, where trees flower, and springs flow. … Be-cause I know that time is always time and place is always place, and what is actual is actual only for one time and only for one place. … I rejoice that things are as they are.”

The poem captures the particularity of time and place and asks us to accept things “as they are” rather than wishing for a better place “where trees flower”, or wishing we were endowed with “this man’s gifts”. The poem is a homage to embracing the unique-ness in ourselves, and in our situation. To Armstrong it suggests we should live “as in-tensely as possible in the here and now”.65

Armstrong discovered that, by surrendering to the particular ‘life that we have’, there are unexpected gifts. In her case, as she surrendered to a life of aloneness and focused on study and writing about spirituality and religion, she feels she has experienced, at times, a sort of ecstasy. She says:

Occasionally, when poring over a dusty tome, I would experience mini seconds of awe

or wonder that gave me some sense of what was going on in the mind of the theologian

or mystic I was studying. At such times, I would feel stirred deeply within, and taken

beyond myself. I was finding in study the ecstasy that I had hoped to find in those long

hours of prayer as a young nun. 66

This reminds me of the notion of consummate integrity explored in Daoism, and the sense of ‘just rightness’ musicians or artists can find in their work (see [23:4]) – an at-tunement to the flow of life.

Armstrong does not believe she has had an easy life, but she suggests that sinking into where life seems to have taken her – into this world of writing and solitude – has a rightness about it. Moreover, she feels that solitude has given her a gift; her aloneness has attuned her to empathy and compassion for the world at large. She says: “Solitude is a teacher. It is lonely. Living without intimacy and affection tears holes in you.” 67 But this solitude, she says, through “stripping away a skin”, has broken down “the protec-tive shell of heartlessness which we cultivate in order to prevent ourselves from being overwhelmed by the suffering of the world”. This “identification with suffering” has

48 The life that I have is all that I have 211



paradoxically led her to joy. She continues: The pain of connecting to this suffering “is a small price to pay for the spirituality of empathy”.

Karen Armstrong finally embraced solitude, surrendered to her nature and accepted the life that ‘turned up’ for her, even when that was a life that she did not always con-sciously choose or want. Hers was not a passive acceptance, nor a depressed fatalistic stance. She was willing to face herself and continued to strive to ‘cultivate her best self’ [25]. Her example is very much an invitation to start by living the life we have and not diminishing that life against a vision of the ideal.

A more extreme example of ‘living the life we have whilst not losing our humanity’, comes from Viktor Frankl, a psychiatrist who survived Auschwitz. He writes of how those prisoners who accepted their lot and yet found tiny ways to stay human within it – through sharing a piece of bread or contemplating a tree – were able to maintain a sense of dignity and meaning, even, in some cases, as they died.68 He calls it tragic optimism.69

Everything can be taken from a man but one thing: the last of human freedoms – to

choose one’s attitude in any given set of circumstances, to choose one’s own way. Frankl



This brings me to a final theme to ponder: Daoist thinking is underpinned by the phrase ‘the path is made through walking’, meaning that we co-create the future through our collective actions. We join a path that already has some shape due to what has gone before. The victims of Auschwitz, or the children starving and losing their families in brutal conflict, are walking a path shaped to a large degree by others; they have little personal agency to change the nature of that path. Our lives are not all of our own making, and sometimes we only have control over our response.

I wonder, too, what causes the sense that some people possess of a desire to strive towards something. Is this personal, or somehow held more collectively in some mys-terious way? Priscilla Stuckley,70 in her autobiographical writing, quotes from Marge Piercy’s poem To Be of Use:

The work of the world is common as mud.

Botched, it smears the hands, crumbles to dust.

But the thing worth doing well done

has a shape that satisfies, clean and evident.

The pitcher cries for water to carry

and a person for work that is real.

Stuckley says: “I know that cry of the heart, that yearning to be of use. In my twenties I searched for water to carry, fearing that my pitcher would forever be empty. But in the end, all I had to do was keep following what I enjoyed most. All along, it seems, the water that wanted to be carried was finding me.”





Section VII




Perspectives on Organisational Practice





49


Introducing perspectives on organisational practice



A wide-ranging and eclectic perspective on issues of concern for those working in and with

organisations, with a focus on leadership, strategy and change, paradox, hope, and the

importance of outliers; some reflections on the downsides of overly relying on structures

and controls.



Complexity theory has seeped into my bones and irreversibly changed my perspec-tive. It offers an invitation to see the world differently. If the world is complex, then embracing that complexity will impact all aspects of what we do – how to get to know about the world, live and work effectively, design and respond to management processes, and evaluate outcomes. Complexity offers no universal ways of working; it depends on the task, and it depends on the situation.

In this section, I want to bring alive some of the implications of ‘seeing the world through fresh eyes’. I draw a great deal on my personal experience, acquired over rath-er too many decades of helping organisations and communities to develop strategy, effect change, refine leadership and so on. I share the thoughts, perspectives and prac-tices that have emerged for me. My approach has been marinated in complexity, ex-panded by Daoism and honed by experience.

This section covers a huge amount of ground; my hope is that it brings alive the im-plications of engaging with the world as complex, and makes you think. But I am not aiming to cover every hectare. I am wandering the landscape of the real world, sitting on interesting-looking hills, and visiting the odd bog.

The pieces in this section broadly cover four themes:



(a) The Pernicious Power of Machine Thinking



The implications of assuming certainty and stability.

It seems very hard, for us Westerners in particular, to give up on machine thinking. The desire for certainty, rules and objective ways to measure outcomes is very strongly embedded in our cultures. But tasks – such as planning, measuring, linking outcomes to inputs, controlling what happens next – are not straightforward if the world is com-plex. Indeed, if blindly applied, these management practices can become mere fictions or talismans, proffering the illusion of control. The unspoken fear is that if we do not act as if we are in control, then we will end up with its opposite – chaos, anarchy, stag-nation, vulnerability or even danger.

216 Section VII Perspectives on Organisational Practice



Some of these pieces explore aspects of the downside of adopting machine thinking. But I am not advocating that we should just abandon structured approaches to man-agement. We need systems, processes and rules to manage public services, for ex-ample, and these will have to be standardised to a substantial degree, such as when dealing with pensions and tax. The balance between necessary standardisation and the ability to handle and respond to unique people and unique situations is a very real conundrum. So, I am not arguing for its opposite – a laissez-faire, ‘do it your way’ ap-proach – but I am suggesting we need to loosen up a bit, consider the downsides of overly standardising design and control, and place more importance on learning, trust, and judgement. The four pieces in this section are:

Start with the hard case, not with Norm: How classical statistical thinking tends to as-sume we should focus public policy on ‘most’ people whereas there is merit in starting by thinking about those who are outliers.

Are we trying to turn machines into people or people into machines: The dilemmas of over-reliance on AI-based diagnostic tools, in this case, for those in the medical profes-sions. Can machines handle incommensurability, intuition and love?

Bending the rules: How rules that are too rigid can cause professionals to cover up any variations, with the result that the ‘system’ struggles to learn and adapt.

Cranking up control or engendering trust: Are we overly relying on rules to control and are we not paying enough attention, when considering governance, on building trust, community values and compassion?



(b) Embrace the Whole Picture



Allow clarity and simplicity to emerge by working, as much as possible, with the whole

picture, rather than pre-defining what information is pertinent and what is not.
