The first piece in this section considers the extent to we should consider a person ‘in the round’ when we are attracted to his or her ideas. The next two pieces consider the importance of looking widely and systemically at the terrain, in order inform strategy.

Thyme for a Sage: When philosophers philosophise, do they leave aspects of their person ‘at the door’ and does that cause their ideas to be suspect? Do theories that emerge from a more woven-together life, from the whole person, contain more wis-dom and insight than if we bracket off and devalue all but ‘pure reason’?

Simplicity on the other side of complexity: By embracing, as far as we can, the whole picture, we may find a degree of simplicity ‘on the other side’. Grappling with the com-plex richness of the full picture may more clearly reveal patterns and leverage points, than if we limit, at the start, the scope of the data.

Strategy in context: The importance of diagnosing context and connecting to organi-sational intentions and capabilities.

49 Introducing perspectives on organisational practice 217



(c) It all Depends



Action is contingent on the situation, and nothing lasts forever.

Complexity theory emphasises that the particularities of conditions and contexts strongly influence how best to act. So, we need to get to grips with the situation we are dealing with – its history, its dominant patterns, its quirky opportunities and limita-tions, and its positioning in the wider world. What we do depends on circumstance. In part we are seeking to explore the predominant nature of the context – whether things are essentially chaotic; or stable and balanced; or rigid and locked-in and in danger of collapse [14]. We are in addition looking for patterns, and we are looking for critical junctures – these may consist of ‘cracks’ in the status quo, or places where many fac-tors cohere. All these factors are pertinent to deciding where to direct interventions.

This contingent approach impacts how to approach change, strategy, the shaping of organisations, and leadership. And we need to bear in mind that interventions in a tur-bulent world are always provisional as we can never be sure in advance what will work, nor for how long. It is highly likely that there will be unintended consequences in even the most well-researched or well-thought-through approaches. Even if we ‘get it right’ at the beginning, things inevitably change over time; new things emerge, broader shifts happen in the wider world, and people adapt. But even though any intervention is pro-visional, this does not mean it has to be tentative or half-hearted.

The art of systemic change: What type of approach to change fits the situation? When should we aim to strengthen what is there, and when should we be aiming to disrupt or even engender collapse?

The sagacity of leadership in complexity: The leadership qualities required to handle complexity require maturity and a breadth of skills. We cannot each be adept at every aspect of complexity leadership and will need to involve others, depending on the re-quirements of the situation.

Organisation: the shape of things to come: A discussion about the changing nature of organising for work and the worrying loss of middle-sized organisations in the organ-isational ecology.



(d) It’s neither One Thing nor the Other



Sometimes aim to transcend paradox, but sometimes settle into the less exciting

‘middle ground’.

The complex world, as well as being sensitive to context, provides many examples where there are paradoxes and few easy answers. Complexity embraces paradox – two incompatible things may both be present and pertinent; there is no right way to do things, and, whatever we decide, there is likely to be some truth in its opposite. Daoists state, for example, that ‘the end is entailed in the beginning’ [28].

218 Section VII Perspectives on Organisational Practice



Paradox, polarities and the middle ground: Tussling with ‘both-and’ can lead to solu-tions or insights which transcend paradox and provide a fresh position that emerges beyond the beige or grey compromise of the middle ground. And yet, sometimes, the middle ground is what is needed.

Let one hundred flowers bloom: Defining strategic goals seems, in general, a sensible approach – but what are the virtues of ‘letting one hundred flowers bloom’, or of allow-ing strategies to emerge rather than pinning them down at the outset?

Abandon hope all ye who enter here: A wide-ranging exploration of the usefulness or otherwise of hope.

Seeing the world through new eyes; some simple approaches: How to work in organ-isations that are not embracing complexity; how to use standard tools in a new way.



And so …



In general, the world is complex. Even when things are stable and we are confident we can use simple methods, the world is still complex underneath, at its roots. The swans may look serene, but they are paddling hard below the surface. Relying on simplicity, or hoping that stability will continue to last, is a hostage to fortune. Something may work here, and it may work now, but it is unlikely to work everywhere and forever.

Whilst methods of implementation may often need to be contingent on circumstances, this is not to ignore the place of working with hard data. Statistical analysis proved that smoking causes cancer, for example. Measurement shows that the Antarctic sea ice is disappearing at a rate never before seen since records began in the 1970s. De-mographic data helps us to plan services for the elderly. But, as I consider in ‘Strategy in context’ [56], whilst this hard data can usefully form part of the diagnosis and help to reveal what is going on, solutions often need to be more systemic, contextualised and adaptive. Yet even this assertion needs a caveat. Some solutions do need a design which is more machine-like – for example a benefits system. There is always a tension; how do you design a benefits system that is streamlined, standardised and clear, and yet does not unintentionally disadvantage certain groups?

Methods relevant to embracing the complexity of the world are often imprecise and contextual – more about principles and rules of thumb rather than procedures to apply one step at a time. Often, the advice is to use existing methods differently, to come from a fresh mindset, rather than to reach for an approach which is entirely new. Grappling with practice raises as many questions as answers; indeed, asking new questions and different questions is, arguably, a key tool of engagement in a complex world. I hope you enjoy wandering through this section, Perspectives on Organisational Practice.



VII (a) The Pernicious Power of Machine Thinking





50


Start with the ‘hard case’ not with Norm



When designing strategies and policies, wrestle first with how to tackle the atypical.

Policies for public sector services – policing, public health, primary health, education – often take a utilitarian stance. The government aims to get the most benefit to the most people for the least cost. Which, on the face of it, seems perfectly sensible.

One perspective endorsed by complexity thinking is that it can be more fruitful – both in cost terms as well as in efficacy – if we focus on the whole person and their complex needs1 rather than pick off particular issues and deal with each issue separately. One way to explore this approach is to start thinking about ‘outliers’ rather than ‘norms’. Outliers and norms are terms from statistical analysis. If you analyse something and plot the results on a graph, you tend to find that there is a large cluster of ‘average’ characteristics centred around the norm; the distribution then tails out into ‘outliers’ from the pack and these outliers display more unique and less typical characteristics. In the instance of provision of public services, for example, outliers are likely to be those people with complex needs living in more difficult circumstances.

Here are a couple of examples:



Strategies to stop smoking

The UK government, like many governments, wants people to stop smoking. This is

primarily because the ill-health caused by smoking is expensive to treat, and the im-

pact on social services, as well as on medical services, is substantial. The government

wants to dissuade the maximum number of people from smoking for the least cost. One

programme offers would-be quitters a number of counselling sessions over a twelve-

week period, together with free nicotine patches. This can be highly effective, leading

to three times the likelihood that a participant will stop smoking compared to relying

solely on willpower.2 It is cheap, time-bound and effective for the ‘Norms’ – those mo-

tivated people with stable lives who find it easy to turn up for appointments and can

commit to using the patches appropriately. What’s not to like?

This norming of policy was questioned during a meeting I attended with a group of

public health professionals. They were describing the problems of helping vulnerable

people to stop smoking when they had multiple problems – mental health, drugs and al-

cohol. As they told me, people in this vulnerable group do not always have a stable home

base and may also have problems with mood and impulse regulation. Those coping with

multiple issues often struggle to commit to regular counselling sessions, and may use

patches ineffectively and, indeed, continue to smoke at the same time. Helping this

group to stop smoking is a whole other ball game compared to working with the Norms.

222 VII (a) The Pernicious Power of Machine Thinking



Vulnerable people trying to stop smoking whilst coping with many other issues are ex-amples of outliers in the mathematical sense – but they are also often seen as ‘social outliers’, indeed almost as outcasts. In a society focused on easily implementable pol-icies and easily measurable outcomes unambiguously linked to specific interventions, such groups with complex intersecting issues are often placed in the ‘too difficult’ box – too expensive to tackle, unlikely to be motivated and hence unlikely to succeed.



The UK Post Office – a public service?

I’ve had reason to get to grips with the policy seemingly being followed by the UK Post

Office, a public body which runs the local post offices in the UK. There is an intention,

so I have been told, to run down the number of post offices and replace them as much

as possible with self-service machines. This strategy – minimise post offices in other

than major towns and replace them with machines – will save money. But – and this was

our concern – at a cost to the quality of public service. The post office is more than a

place that dispenses stamps; people go there for advice, support and reassurance. Such

a streamlined approach will probably work well enough for people who are confident

with technology or, if need be, can travel to bigger towns to access services there. But it

will not work so well for the vulnerable, the homeless, the elderly, and those with learn-

ing difficulties or issues of anxiety. This group may have neither access to the internet

nor confidence to use it, and if they don’t have family members to help, can be seriously

disadvantaged. These outliers run the risk of being left stranded by policies which aim

to get the most for the mainstream at the lowest cost.



There are a number of points that spring to mind in considering these situations:

Look for synergistic solutions that integrate services: When it is possible, if we look at cost and value for bundles of services rather than optimising costs for each service sep-arately, we may develop approaches that both have good impact and offer good value for money. There may be synergistic solutions – for example running a nursery in a care home, using mobile libraries to offer post office facilities, or integrating the myriad of services for children struggling to attend school. Such service integration does, to a degree, already happen, but the budgeting for each service tends to stand alone, and the preference to link services – rather than integrate and reconceptualise – can set limits on the art of the possible [56].

Look at knock-on effects downstream to other services: Equally, squeezing or stream-lining one particular service, such as public health, can have expensive knock-on ef-fects on other services, such as primary healthcare. In particular, the outliers – those with complex needs who are harder to reach – are the very ones who are more difficult and costly to treat when health risk factors turn into ill-health. Thus the ‘norming’ of public health, and the reduction in focus on ‘outliers’ is likely to amplify the costs down-stream in primary healthcare.

If you start by thinking about outliers not norms, you are forced into thinking sys-temically; this may help to craft effective solutions: This is a point of particular inter-est; we may be helped in designing effective services if we start by thinking about the

50 Start with the ‘hard case’ not with Norm 223



outliers rather than the norms. Taking as a starting point those with complex needs could surface solutions not only to their complex problems, but also illuminate better ways of delivering mainstream services for average users.

Taking such a systemic approach would require the weighing up and weaving together of many disparate factors and would need to draw on a wide range of perspectives from different professionals and service beneficiaries. This stage of exploration can surface linkages, identify unintended consequences and may reveal novel solutions. It can allow for the consideration of knock-on impacts downstream, as well as enabling the sharing of experience from many perspectives.

Think place-based, not siloed service-based: There may be a need for a greater fo-cus on systemic, integrated, place-based approaches where there is more flexibility to adapt – albeit within established principles and guidelines – than is the case when each service is driven centrally. This is not radically new thinking, and there are already ex-amples where something along these lines is attempted. For example, in shelters for the homeless, there is often support and signposting for help with addictions, issues of mental health and debt management.

At the very least, undertaking the diagnostic phase in a systemic fashion may throw up solutions that are novel, customised to particular situations and yet still efficient with resources. Even if the conclusion is reached that it is still best to deliver services individually, this diagnostic phase is likely to throw up helpful perspectives.

These examples are from the public sector, but the private sector is also prone to siloed thinking. I worked as a consultant for one global manufacturing organisation, during which I interviewed managers in Indonesia. These managers explained that they struggled to get heard against the centralised power of Global Manufacturing Tech-nology and Global Human Resources. They told me that they were being asked to buy approved manufacturing equipment that was too expensive for the size of their mar-ket. In addition, they told me, it required levels of expertise in engineering and main-tenance that was not readily available locally. Rather than reach a local solution, they feared the organisation would merely decide not to manufacture there. Maybe that was the correct solution, but the ability to brainstorm local solutions was, they told me, hard to raise as it cut across the power structures of silo management.

The problem with solutions that cross siloed boundaries is that they may raise the threat of structural change, and that will challenge the power base. But the suggestion to start with the outliers could provide a new lens and allow for the raising of novel approaches that go against the limitations of thinking ‘within the box’.

The suggestion is to start with thinking about solving the outlier problem before turn-ing attention to ‘Norm’ and his mates. Joined-up solutions are not only likely to be bet-ter at addressing the needs of the powerless and the vulnerable but might also throw up novel ideas that have traction for the mainstream and could indeed end up saving money overall.





51


Are we trying to turn machines into people or

people into machines?



Automated analysis of data sets has an important role but is not the whole picture.



Turning people into machines



We are able, in medicine, dentistry and veterinary science, to use the immense processing power of computers to analyse enormous quantities of data, thus increasing confidence that we can identify the links between causes and effects and distinguish pathways of disease progression. Based on this analysis, students in the medical professions can be kitted out with checklists and decision trees to support di-agnosis. In many ways this form of artificial intelligence (AI) – finding structure in big data sets – is a real step forwards. Such AI systems underpin online medical diagnostics which, through asking a series of linked questions, determine whether the questioner has a serious problem and needs to be seen urgently. This ability to analyse masses of data counters the risk of relying too much on the inevitably-more-limited experience of the medical practitioner.

However, an over reliance on AI can create at least three problems in this medical con-text. First, if, as a patient, your symptoms and your own sense of what is happening to your body do not fit the ‘training data’, the risk is that your own perspective may be discounted against the unassailable power of this body of knowledge. You, the unique, non-average, living patient, can feel unheard and sidelined. Secondly, the ‘training data’ may be based on certain groups of patients and hence may be biased. Thirdly, a nor-mative system, looking for average trends and based on past data, mitigates against placing credence on ‘that which does not fit’; hence new trends or symptoms which are emerging in the population may be missed.

I asked my dentist to what extent medical training focuses on data and logic. His view is that we train people primarily in left-brain, analytical processes of thinking [45]. We provide less help, he said, in developing confidence to make judgements when the case in hand does not fit the textbook – judgements that take into account the wider cir-cumstances and the particularities of the patient. He explained that these relational, reflexive aspects to dentistry were, in part, skills learned over time, but he also felt they needed to form a greater aspect of formal training.

Robert Caplin, in Dentistry – art or science,3 explains that clinical reasoning, based on judgement, takes place at a largely unconscious level as the dentist is interacting with the patient. His concern is that too much focus on guidelines and expert opinion may undermine the dentist’s confidence to learn through experience, and can downplay the importance of intuition, which he defines as “rapid, non-analytical reasoning”.4 226 VII (a) The Pernicious Power of Machine Thinking



This discussion – about the need to integrate the rational and intuitive, the hard and soft, the lived experience and the results of analysis – brought to mind, in a very dif-ferent context, a story told by the department manager of the manufacture of a drug called Contac 400. This story is described by Philip Streatfield in The Paradox of Control in Organisations.5 No matter how hard the researchers tried, Streatfield explained, it was never possible to pin down the process of mixing the ingredients and get the me-dicinal pellets to form in a way that anyone could reliably make it. Some workers were better than others at this cookery and they “just knew when a batch was going to turn ‘clumpy’ … or when ‘powder would fly’”.6 It reminds me of my Mother teaching me how to make Yorkshire pudding mix, showing me when it reached the optimal consistency. In the end, I knew when it was ‘right’, and still do! As Bent Flyvbjerg explains, exper-tise cannot be reduced to rules – and even the expert himself cannot translate his own “pattern of practice” into “theories, maxims and rules of thumb”.7

Experts’ behaviour is intuitive, holistic and synchronic, understood in the way that

a given situation releases a picture of problem, goal, plan, decision in one instant.

Flyvbjerg8



Turning machines into people



So far, so good. The advice is to use the power of AI to act as an adjunct to support the wisdom of the medical practitioner – wisdom she has gained through experience, through her ability to tune in to the patient – and then respond in an integrative way to the often-complex picture that each person presents.

This begs an interesting question: To what extent will machines of the future be able to replace skilled humans, and for what? The ability of AI to analyse awesomely extensive data sets is stunning. Reading an MRI scan, for example, and picking out anomalies, most probably will be achieved more reliably by an AI system than by the average ra-diologist. The ability of an AI system, having been trained through analysing several snapshots, to, in seconds, pick someone out from thousands of surveillance cameras, as the protagonist wanders around a city, is already quite remarkable.

One area that is likely to remain of concern is bias. Carissa Veliz, an expert in digital ethics, cites a case of an AI system making recruitment decisions.9 The historical data on which it had been trained showed that mainly white males had been recruited. So, the AI system, not surprisingly, preferenced white males in its selection process! This is a problem that is well-recognised and, to a degree, can be dealt with. But the issue remains that the people who select the training sets on which AI systems learn have the power, if they so wish, to inject bias in a way that may not be obvious to users of the system.

Apparently, some AI companies are employing workers to trawl through the internet to try to ensure dubious material is not mined by the AI system in its learning process-es. There is a similar issue in embedding values into AI systems. AI systems can, in a

51 Are we trying to turn machines into people or people into machines? 227



sense, make value-informed decisions, but their ‘values’ will merely mimic those they have learned from their training material.10

It is interesting to ask in what ways machines are likely to continue to struggle to mimic human capabilities. Consider meeting an old friend you haven’t seen for years. Con-tained in that moment of meeting is the sense you used to have of him all those years ago; maybe some memories – some more visceral than actual – of what he had been to you. Then, at the same time, you are engaging with him as he is now – his appearance, what he says, how he looks at you. He is doing the same to you, and his responses mir-ror back and impact how you react next. In that moment of meeting is a co-evolution of the memory of your shared past, coupled with the current reflexive mirroring be-tween you and him in the body, heart and mind. Imagine you had to make a decision – he wants something from you. Your decision will be mediated through this interwo-ven, historically and contextually shaped, reflexive interaction. It is hard to see how a machine could mimic this complexity [40].

We should never substitute a computer system for a human function that involves in-

terpersonal respect, understanding and love. Joseph Weizenbaum11

Perhaps, you might retort, you are not looking for the machine to be your best friend. If the machine is deciding if you are ill or whether to lend you money, these more subtle cues arguably do not matter. And, arguably, asking a machine to make a more ratio-nal decision on, say, recruitment could actually sidestep the inevitable biases that are prevalent in us humans too.

Nevertheless, although machines can ‘learn’ the values or express a level of empathy buried in the information from its learning set, it is not obvious how we can know what values it has introjected and how, indeed, these might evolve. And if there are errors or unintended biases in the training material, trusting the logic of the AI machine can have devastating consequences. A well-documented case of a flawed automated system in Michigan falsely accused thousands of benefit fraud.12

Anyone can write any kind of algorithm, let it loose in the world without any kind of su-

pervision or regulation. We wouldn’t allow pharmaceutical companies to do that [with

drugs]. Veliz13

Are we in danger of valuing process and logic over experience and intuition? Do we tend towards the ‘machine view’ that only that which is easily measurable, statistically significant, and clearly linked to inputs can be used in decision-making? It is easy to devalue our human ability to handle complex information. We may take for granted the way we can skilfully and almost miraculously weave and balance together objec-tive ‘fact-based’ analysis, with subjective, qualitative perspectives and weigh up some-times-incommensurable values. Do we run the risk, first, of emulating machines, and then allowing machines to replace us? Do we remain in thrall to the ‘machine world-view’ and continue to distrust our natural ability to handle life’s rich complexity?

228 VII (a) The Pernicious Power of Machine Thinking



Endnote



A note to end on: The field of AI is moving very fast and writing about it is a hostage to fortune. Things will inevitably change in unimaginable ways and I wonder how this piece will stand in ten years time. Will I be eating my words – or will my robot do it for me!





52


Bending the rules



Where you need to have rules, don’t make it impossible to challenge them.



In 2005, during Hurricane Katrina, a public-minded pharmacist broke into sever-al pharmacies before they were flooded and removed morphine and other serious

drugs. He took them to a place of high ground so he could provide them to the am-bulance and fire services who were rescuing people. Of course, he was breaking the rules. In the same year, four suicide bombers struck London’s transport network. At one point, it was reported that there were sounds of screaming from further along the tunnel but the men in the rescue service were reluctant to advance without formal permission. Someone jumped onto the line to show it was not live and to urge them on – but they found breaking the rules hard to do.

Another story: A research colleague tells of a social worker who is called out to a child one Sunday evening and feels she is in immediate danger. The social worker is not al-lowed just to remove her but is concerned that the situation is so grave that he should not wait for due procedure. In the end, he phones a friend who runs a hostel and takes the child there. He is in serious breach of the rules. When he writes his report, he hides this ‘misdemeanour’.

The research colleague found this example was not uncommon. Reports tend to be written in a rule-abiding way because the consequences of breaking the rules, even with good reason, are career-limiting. The learning that could have arisen about the case, and the potential for shifting procedure to accommodate such anomalies, did not happen. When the researcher raised this with managers in the service, they told him their own tales of having to conform, or pretending to conform, to rules which some-times ran counter to good judgement. The implication seems to be that we build an un-bending block of rules which can never adapt, as any rule-breaking tends to be hidden from sight.

I have been reading Call the Midwife – reminiscences of a midwife working in East London in the 1950s.14 The midwives followed procedures but were equally encour-aged to improvise when the social circumstances made that impossible. They were ap-plauded for using their initiative and did not fear being disciplined if they had to take some risks as they struggled to deal with real-world situations. Equally, the procedure manual was regularly updated as the team shared and learned from the practical ex-amples of working in the community. Has it been an improvement to move to a more rule-based approach?



230 VII (a) The Pernicious Power of Machine Thinking



Encouraging judgement, creativity and caring



The dominant paradigm in the West is that the way to efficiency and quality is through the design of and adherence to detailed, clear ways of working which can be monitored and measured. Procedures are, without doubt, necessary, but are we paying sufficient attention to encouraging employees to use their judgement to deal with situations as they present themselves? Is there the space to develop creativity? Do we value the talent, commitment and experience of professional people or do employees feel that they must not deviate from procedure to such an extent that they start to leave their creativity, compassion and humanity at the door? And, furthermore, are we embedding a system which cannot learn – as any deviation from the rules is so frowned upon it has to be hidden?



An afterthought:



These are not new thoughts. Peter Senge’s The Fifth Discipline15 which introduced ‘the learning organisation’, came from very much from the same viewpoint as I am express-ing here.

The [Learning Organisation] was a compelling vision of an organization made up of em-

ployees skilled at creating, acquiring, and transferring knowledge. These people could

help their firms cultivate tolerance, foster open discussion, and think holistically and

systemically. Such learning organizations would be able to adapt to the unpredictable

more quickly than their competitors could.16



However, a recent review of learning organisations concludes that the vision of their uptake has not been realised due to the desire to proceduralise and institute such learning processes, then measure their impact and show how learning contributed to the overall strategic goals.17 In other words, it has largely failed because managers wanted to take a living, creative, relational learning process and insert it into the usual mechanical box, constrained by rules.





53


Cranking up control or engendering trust?



Engendering adaptation, trust and humanity can be more effective than tightening the

controls on the machine.



The dominant Western paradigm, when it comes to designing and running manage-ment practices in the public sector in particular, is to believe that efficiency and effectiveness is best met through standardisation, defining objective measures and seeking hard evidence. The UK seems to have a particular liking for standardisation and simple measures. For example, the UK school inspection regime confers a one-word assessment on a school after a two-day inspection – outstanding, good, ade-quate, or failing.18 The stress caused to head teachers is legendary, as the implications of being awarded a ‘bad’ label are far-reaching.19 One friend who is a UK Head Teacher confesses that as well as coping with the huge anxiety of waiting for the inspection, she does not feel it conveys a fair representation of the qualities of a school. She urges prospective parents, whatever the outcome of the official process, to visit the school of interest, meet the staff and parents, and gain their own sense as to whether children seem happy, motivated and safe.



Holistic teaching



By contrast, Finland’s approach to education appears to be more holistic.20 It “breaks the boundaries of existing subjects” through cross-disciplinary projects involving the wider community, such as measuring the water quality of a lake. There are no standard tests and no school inspections. Teachers have freedom in the classroom to experiment and find ways of teaching that work for themselves and their pupils.21 The espoused philosophy is that you invest in educating and encouraging good teachers – and then you trust them.

Three important aspects of the Finnish System compared with general Western model:

flexibility and diversity rather than standardization, emphasis on broad knowledge includ-

ing all aspects of individual growth and learning, and culture of trust through profession-

alism. Ustun and Eryilmaz 22



Olli-Pekka Heinonen, Director of The Finnish National Agency for Education, talks about “giving children the ability to cope in the future, a future that we adults cannot imagine” and says the emphasis is not on learning facts but learning through play and experience.

Finnish education, with its focus on preparing students to handle an uncertain future and on learning how to learn, is a good example of adopting a complexity mindset. A key question is to ask whether this looser, trust-based, contextual approach results in 232 VII (a) The Pernicious Power of Machine Thinking



a compromise of standards. The statistics say otherwise.23 Finland scores higher than the UK on numeracy, literacy and other educational outcomes.24 And, regarding chil-dren’s happiness, Finland scores near the top of the ratings for Europe and the UK is at the bottom.25 Interestingly, Finnish children also start school later than in the UK and have shorter school days.

A word of warning, though: A colleague, Mike Collins, working in educational research told me that recently Finland’s ratings are starting to drop a little and the UK’s rat-ings on numeracy and literacy have been going up. Why is that? Has the UK moved more towards a more holistic adaptive approach? Have UK teachers increasingly gone their own way and usefully ‘bent the rules’? Has the top-down prioritising in the UK of numeracy and literacy showed some return? Or have other social factors negatively impacted Finnish children? Teasing out how outcomes emerge is a complex issue, and neat expositions are always to be treated with caution. Mike was emphatic, however, that holistic teaching coupled with adapting to the uniqueness of particular groups of students, does tend to be more effective than too great a reliance on standardisation and simplistic controls.



Natural governance in healthcare



To turn to another example, another colleague, Aine Carroll, working in the health sec-tor, described her despair over the response to an awful situation in a UK hospital, in which a nurse murdered a number of babies over a period of several months.26 ‘It’s not the first time such an awful thing has happened’, she said, ‘but the policy response is always the same; to crank up the rules, checks and protocols’.27 She continued: ‘I have no confidence this really works. We need a fresh approach, one that takes into account the complexities of the situation, one that is more human.’

We agreed that what is needed is the development of a form of natural governance with an emphasis on developing principles rather than rules, building stronger more supportive teams, and at the same time making it more acceptable to voice concerns.28 It reminds me of the saying, ‘it takes a village to raise a child’ – meaning that respon-sibility is shared, parental behaviour is legitimately challenged, and problems are not just exported upwards to ‘the system’.

Natural governance is an emergent process generated through the reflexive interac-

tion of multiple stakeholders, coupled with enabling constraints, the creation of ‘adap-

tive space’ to facilitate processes of feedback, sensemaking and reflection. Carroll29



One model, from a very different setting, but also dealing with issues of safety, sug-gests that a culture needs to be engendered which is ‘just’ (defined as a culture which creates “an atmosphere of trust where people are encouraged and even rewarded for sharing safety-related information”), flexible, supports learning and encourages staff to ask ‘what if’.30 This seems a good starting point to consider how to implement natu-ral governance.

53 Cranking up control or engendering trust? 233



Dealing with falls

Following complaints, a hospital undertook an experiment in group-based natural gov-

ernance, aimed at reducing falls.31 This was led by a senior nurse, who worked along-

side other nurses to open up conversations, coach them and review any incidents. The

senior nurse would ask questions of her colleagues such as: ‘What do you think would

happen if you left the frail elderly person alone on the toilet’, or ‘what do you think your

colleagues were doing at the time’, or ‘is that the kind of care you would have want-

ed for your Mother’? The idea was to help people think through consequences and see

things through others’ eyes, and, in so doing, reflexively shift the culture to one which

grappled more comfortably with the tensions between mutual support and trust, care

for patients, and willingness to ‘call out’ and challenge each other. Apparently, over the

course of the first year, the number of falls reduced by thirty percent.32 Unfortunately, a

few years later, the senior nurse’s time was redirected away from coaching and towards

writing incident reports, as these provide more tangible, measurable evidence that ac-

tion was being taken. Fall rates started to rise again.



It sometimes feels that structured aspects of organisational life – planning, setting objectives, writing reports, measuring outcomes – take over from nurturing the rich potential of human warmth and creative endeavour. The structures and strictures of machine management dominate. But herein lies a danger: If we feel treated like bits of a machine, we start to act accordingly. We save our humanity and creativity for our life ‘outside’. We start to turn up at work and just do what is asked or respond to what will be measured – no matter how uninspiring, how pointless, or, indeed, how risky we sometimes feel it is.

Do we need to review, in governance processes, the balance between structuring and ‘culturing’? Would not a greater focus on engendering a ‘just’ culture of support, agility and caring actually create safer environments and better outcomes than relying pri-marily on processes, reports and numbers?



VII (b) Embrace the Whole Picture





54


Thyme for a Sage?



Be cautious of the elegance of the Thinker and look for the more complex, tender, embodied

wisdom of the Sage.



The life experience of a philosopher or thinker will inevitably colour his or her the-ories in ways that are not immediately obvious. And whilst we are all, inevitably, blind to some of our prejudices or predilections, these sometimes-hidden aspects are important to dig for. Complexity theory suggests that ‘all that we are’, acknowledged or not, will shape our ideas and philosophies. Thus, should we not take into account the path of the philosopher’s life, the way he or she has lived, when we engage with and evaluate his or her philosophies?

I’m going to take a particular example, and that is Martin Heidegger. Heidegger devel-oped his seminal work in the 1920s in Germany. I don’t find his writing easy to follow, and I certainly cannot do it justice here, but one central theme developed in Being and Time was to explore the essence of ‘beingness’, the nature of existence.33 He advocated that this was undertaken through paying attention to experience in the ‘here and now’. He also stated that “before you knew the truth about things, you have to care about them”.34 Caring, he said, is what motivates us to explore and take notice, in order not to be blind to key aspects.

These are important ideas and fit well with the admonition, from complexity thinking, to see things in context and to see things in relationship. But … Heidegger was a Nazi and was anti-Semitic. His black notebooks, published in 2014, way after his death, leave no doubt as to his deep-seated anti-Jewish stance. As he shows in his notebooks, he saw Jews as both racist and rootless – and even suggested their own racism was to blame for their treatment during the holocaust. “Jews, he writes are ‘uprooted from Being-in the World’ – that is [they are] incapable of authentically caring and knowing”.35

The question this raised, when these notebooks were published, was whether his seemingly profound anti-Semitic views bring into question his work in general. Can you carve off the bits of him you don’t like and keep the rest? Or, as someone said at a panel discussion in the US on Heidegger and Nazism soon after the publication of the notebooks, “anyone who is capable of that sort of argument cannot be trusted to think”.36

I find Heidegger’s work rather abstract and disembodied, and yet, paradoxically, the focus of his philosophy is phenomenological – advocating the paying of attention to the relational particularity of immediate experience. It is as if he ‘thinks about’ experience ‘top-down’ rather than reflects on experience ‘bottom up’. Richard Bernstein, in The New Constellations, expresses the same concern.37 He argues that Heidegger relied on 238 VII (b) Embrace the Whole Picture



‘thinking’ and ignored phronesis, Aristotle’s term for learning through paying detailed attention to our involvement in the world.

I wonder if I can trust the philosophical ideas of someone who wrote about compassion and connection and yet deemed them not to apply to a particular group of people, in this case, the Jews. And this is despite him having colleagues, friends and indeed a lover who were Jewish.

Often what is thought is less important than who thinks it. Carl Jung38

We need to recognise the knower in generating knowledge. Guy Loubser39

Would I throw away everything Heidegger thought? No. But would I rather trust the ideas of some of his contemporaries who wove their life experience into their ideas and showed themselves in their writing – such as Hannah Arendt40 or Philippa Foot41 – then yes. Arendt’s wisdom is less polished, less elegant, more ambiguous, more caveated – but, to me, feels more whole, more to be trusted. I would suggest that wisdom is an emergent aspect of the systemic nature of who we are and how we live. Relying pri-marily on the logic of the abstract mind and theorising from there is, perhaps, like mak-ing a cake without all the ingredients.

Thinking analytically is like going on a diet. Its aim is a healthy austerity of thought, a

certain trimness of mind. But when it’s carried too far, we are left with only a skeleton.42



Conversely, if we actively cultivate what the Chinese call xin or heart-mind, if we con-sciously marinate our ideas in lived experience, won’t this enrich and infuse our in-sights and philosophical conclusions [25]?

Freya Mathews is thinking along the same lines. She says:

The Theorist picks out concepts from his thinking and “by further abstraction, sharpens

them into well-defined abstract categories”. In so doing, he “shifts the focus from the

world itself” and, “in accordance with abstract principles of inference and evidence”,

develops a schema that is then “judged to be true”.43

Mathews contrasts the Theorist with the Sage and suggests that the Sage “focuses not on abstract schemas at an inner remove from reality but on the immediate field of ac-tual, outer influences and concrete particulars in which he is immersed”.44

Should we not be wary of the sometimes-gripping, analytically elegant, sophisticated and well-argued philosophies of the abstract Theorist, even if they excite us? Wouldn’t we do better to trust the more tentative but maybe more holistic offerings of the Sage who develops wisdom using the heart-mind, and who brings her ‘lived life’ into the de-velopment of her insights and perspectives? I leave the question with you.





55




Simplicity on the other side of complexity



Keep the diagnostic broad, rich and complex; then how best to intervene may reveal itself as

surprisingly obvious.

For the simplicity on this side of complexity, I wouldn’t give you a fig. But for the sim-

plicity on the other side of complexity, for that I would give you anything I have. Oliver

Wendell Holmes Sr.



It is tempting to simplify a problem at the outset to try to make it tractable. We tend to define boundaries and limit the types of information we deem relevant. But what

if, in so doing, we run the risk of throwing out the baby with the bathwater? What if we make it harder to apprehend the features, patterns, nodes and discontinuities that we are trying to uncover – those features that will aid both understanding and action? The complexity worldview suggests that it is through engaging with as complete a picture of a situation as we can, that the relative simplicity of its features may be revealed. And then, by working with this relative simplicity, we are most likely to home in on what is there and let that guide us as to where best to intervene. This is what is meant by the ‘simplicity on the other side of complexity’.

As an analogy, imagine you have only a grainy, monochrome, cropped picture of a land-scape. There might be a building in that shadow from the hill, but nothing is clear. But if you were able to widen the boundaries of the picture, as well as increase the pixel den-sity and maintain the whole range of colour, you might suddenly see that the picture has two key potentially connected features – a crevasse right over on one side which might just, if it widens, threaten the farmhouse that was indeed lurking in the shadow of the hill. Now, you can assess this dilemma and decide what to do. The complex messy data resolved itself, in this instance, into two key objects – crevasse and farmhouse.

How does this translate into a practical example? Imagine a company is exploring a new market for washing powder in Asia. It might seem sufficient to focus on the current market – competition, demographics of the customer base, supply chain options and so on. But if that perspective is widened to include, for example, the geo-politics of the broader region, together with immigration policy and food security, such a broader perspective may point to unexpected opportunities or threats; it may also reveal ways in which the socio-politico-economic landscape is stable or unstable. Such features might have been missed if the approach had focused too narrowly on the more imme-diate issues of the market. The ‘patterns in the landscape’ come, in this example, not from economic factors on their own, nor from features in the washing powder market per se, but from the systemic interplay of the economic, the political and the environ-mental.

Such an approach will require many types of data and many perspectives; it requires judgement, and it is imprecise and subjective. But to simplify at the start and then 240 VII (b) Embrace the Whole Picture



work analytically with a restricted data set can make us precisely wrong! In contrast, to open up to a richer, more inclusive perspective which allows systemic patterns and systemic features to be seen may reveal opportunities and stumbling blocks not apparent through more conventional analysis.

In surveying the complex picture that comes from such an inclusive and far-reaching exploratory phase, in seeking patterns and resonances, nodes and leverage points, it can become clearer what is stuck, what is fluid, where there are entry points, and where there be dragons!





56


Strategy in context



How to approach strategy development in a complex, dynamic uncertain world.

How to deal with an emerging future and recognise that the realised strategy is never

what was intended.



When I ask managers whether the strategic plans in which they have been involved delivered on their goals, the vast majority say no. When I ask why not, they play back to me a scenario of new entrants, shifting markets and contexts, lightning bolts from left field, and opportunities or threats that either had been overlooked or were newly emerging. In other words, they surface a complexity worldview of the context in which their strategies were enacted.

There is a theme running through the whole strategy process, but it often lurks beneath the surface. It is the tension between the desire to control what unfolds – through set-ting goals, creating plans and undertaking analyses – and the unpredictable reality of change in complex situations. Indeed, the strategy process is replete with paradox. However well we understand the terrain, things will change; however much we artic-ulate strategy, what is enacted will differ from what was intended; and however thor-ough we are at matching opportunities to our capabilities, our ability to spot emerging shifts in the market, and adapt to them, may in practice trump everything else.

But despite operating in a dynamic and fast-changing world, we do need to decide, as best we can, how to start, and where we hope to go. We have to acquire resources and capabilities, and provide a degree of clarity of role and purpose. We have to be adept at determining where we are going, and be equally adept, when circumstances require it, at adapting.

I start with the issue of diagnosing the context, then consider how to build on that di-agnosis to develop the initial strategy. But this strategy can only be the starting point. How can we adapt to an emerging future, and accept that what strategy is realised in practice will be neither what was hoped for nor what was planned?



Diagnosing the context in a complex world



Do organisations spend enough time getting to grips with the ‘terrain’ before deciding on a course of action? In ‘Simplicity on the other side of complexity’ [55] I consider the importance of widening the view, in order to reveal systemic patterns and features. Here I take things a bit further.

The insights from complexity thinking concerning the nature of this terrain are that it is:

242 VII (b) Embrace the Whole Picture



• patterned: the context – the market, the society, the environment – shows up in

systemically woven patterns.

• featured: there may be quirky opportunities, hiding in plain sight, some of which

might be newly emerging; there might also be a few chasms.

• dynamic: the context is rarely static and sometimes changes rapidly and radically.



A great deal of strategy work is trying to figure out what is going on. Not deciding

what to do but the more fundamental problem of comprehending the situation.

Richard Rumelt45



This diagnostic phase – arguably the most important part of the strategy or change process – begins with ‘pattern-spotting’. We need to view the terrain systemically and as widely and comprehensively as possible to spot the systemically woven patterns that, as I have argued in [14], constitute that terrain. In addition, we need to be able to spot movement and get to know the terrain so well that we can spot any minor changes [41] – any cracks or developing new shoots, or leverage points – which may confer advantage.46

Get the beat … listen to the wisdom of systems. Donella Meadows47



Start with the past



The past is an important place to start when looking at dynamic, ever-changing contexts, for several reasons:

First, it contains nuggets of wisdom. It isn’t just a dark country from which we have escaped. We can mine it for good ideas and take heart that if we could do it then, we can do it now.

Secondly, history can illustrate what might happen in the future. Histories of the rise and fall of empires, for example, show the extent to which very similar factors tend to trigger their downfall and display the common features in what tended to happen post-collapse [see 68: history’s crisis detectives].

Thirdly, the past, in many ways, lives on in the present. Unresolved conflicts can fes-ter under the surface and erupt at a later stage. Equally, cultures that have displayed ethnic concord over centuries will have the memory of that tolerance in their cultural psyches, even if it seems to have gone underground. Gathering information about the past enriches our knowledge of the terrain of the present – the depth of its valleys and the extent of its quagmires.

Finally, paying attention to the past can save time. Talking to people who have left an organisation or moved within an organisation can provide learning, contacts, old reports and stories of pitfalls and successes. These can really enrich understanding.

56 Strategy in context 243



Pay attention to the dynamic



The point at which the importance of viewing the dynamics of situations came home to me was when I came across the idea of fragile states.



Fragile states

A fragile state is a regime which is unstable and at risk of internal conflict or collapse.

There are published indexes which grade the fragility of countries.48 The characteristics

on which the grade is based include governance – either weak central government or

one which has little legitimacy in the eyes of its citizens; lack of public investment in

infrastructure; failure to establish rule of law and instability; and significant shifts in

economic trends.

Around the time of the Arab Spring uprisings in 2011, Syria was deemed, in these index-

es, to be stable. But then conflict broke out. This was initiated by violent crackdowns on

youths demanding greater democracy. But there was also an emerging perfect storm

caused by the drought of 2012 and loss of livelihoods for farmers in the Southeast, cou-

pled with the loss of a social ‘safety net’, all entwined with the desire for democracy. This

loss of safety net was due to a shift, over time, towards free market ideologies and an

associated minimising of state support. The conflict also became mired in a much wider

set of interests of other states and extreme religious groups. So, when viewed systemi-

cally and historically, the potential for instability becomes much more visible.



It is always easy to comment in hindsight, but this example of evaluating whether a state is fragile gives a good example of the way that developing a systemic, historical and dynamic perspective can offer a richer picture of the situation. It makes more prominent, perhaps, the systemic determinants of fragility rather than the signs or mea-sures of stability.

I’m suggesting, in similar vein, that for organisations developing strategy, a diagnosis that incorporates the past and also views things systemically and dynamically, can provide an essential perspective. It allows judgement as to what is stable, what is deeply entrenched, what may be unravelling and where there may be new opportuni-ties and threats.

In The Five Percent, Peter Coleman explores ways, through working systemically, to find solutions to seemingly impossible conflicts. His advice, relevant in the broader field of strategy too, is to:

See the system. When you find yourself stuck in an oversimplified polarized conflict,

a useful first step is to try to become more aware of the system as a whole, to provide

more context to your understanding of the terrain in which the stakeholders are em-

bedded, whether they are disputants, mediators, negotiators, lawyers, or other third

parties. This can help you to see the forest and the trees; it is a critical step toward

regaining some sense of accuracy, agency, possibility, and control in the situation.49 244 VII (b) Embrace the Whole Picture



Building on the diagnosis to create an adaptive strategy



We start with a divergent diagnostic phase and welcome the whole messy complex interwoven picture; we then look to uncover its features. We use it to build a strategy that will reflect and respond to the patterns and dynamics in both the organisation and its context. In a nutshell, we need to match the opportunities and threats brought to light by the diagnostic with the capabilities, intentions and values of the organisation.50

But there is a dilemma here. If the strategy becomes too fixed, it can blind organisa-tions to new information, new opportunities and changing contexts. However, if the strategy is too loose, how can organisations amass resources, create clarity for its staff and boldly step into the market? The balance between direction and adaptation is the paradoxical conundrum in a dynamic complex world. For we want to do more than re-spond to changing circumstances, we want to shape the future too.

We need to view the world as continuously producing newness, within which we cre-

ate patterns of relative stability. Bill Sharpe51



We have to build into our strategic processes ways to continually engage with the un-folding future; in that way we continue to notice - as new information emerges and as actions have an impact – and keep on reviewing the whole picture. We must keep an eye out for any ‘weak signals’ of change or opportunity. This process is never-ending.

We can never be sure about the future. We often have to make some leaps of faith in order to proceed. It can sometimes be preferable to try out reasonably well-informed hunches than to put all our efforts in to analysing available information for the pur-poses of predicting the future. This is particularly pertinent when asking for opinions about products or services that are radically new. In this territory of ‘unknown un-knowns’ predicting the future is almost never effective, as discussed by Christensen, in The Innovator’s Dilemma.52

We must move forwards and yet, at the same time, expect the unexpected. The entre-preneur knows this well. Many entrepreneurs have strong hunches and develop strong intentions. Yet they seem able to flex their activities to achieve their visions, and also modify those visions if other opportunities arise, or if there are unexpected difficulties. Many entrepreneurs have many failures as well as successes and, even if successful, have very diverse portfolios. It is the act of balancing opportunity and intention that seems to be key.



And/but what gets enacted in practice?



As well as recognising that the external world is dynamic and changing, we are also dealing with the complexities of the organisation that created the strategy. What

56 Strategy in context 245



actually gets implemented – the enacted or realised strategy – is often very different from what was espoused or intended, for many reasons.53 These include: the impact of the biases of senior managers, new leadership, a chance opportunity that caught everyone’s imagination, bad luck, ill-thought-through investments, shifts in govern-ment policy, the arrival of new entrants or the impact of some aggrieved customer or supplier. Equally, what we imagine the future to hold will shape our decisions and actions in the present. As Prigogine said, the future contributes to the present through how we anticipate it.54 The past, in our constant re-interpreting of it, also continually reshapes the present. As we reinterpret the past or reimagine the future, we are likely to change what we do.

Organisations often adapt incrementally to such influences, and it is important to pay attention to what strategy is enacted in practice and how this changes over time. In this way, the emerging direction can be reviewed and potentially redirected if the enacted strategy has wandered too far off course.



In conclusion



Developing strategy in a complex world presents a good example of the dilemmas of planning or designing anything. On the one hand, we need to decide on the direction an organisation is heading in order to plan resource allocation and direct activities. On the other hand, if we are not flexible and do not notice and adapt to what is happening, we run the risk of either missing opportunities, or trampling on innovative or effective activities that we have stumbled across. Equally, flexibility is costly and becoming too flexible can make it hard for staff to prioritise. We may end up with an expensive mud-dle. The appropriate middle ground between clarity and adaptation will depend in part on the nature of the market and in part on the strength of the organisation to carve its own path.55

A complexity perspective on strategy places attention on the value of exploring the dynamics of the landscape in which the strategy is to be implemented. It exhorts us to pay attention to history as well as to the emerging future and to work systemically – to uncover what has stabilised, where there are leverage points and where ‘new shoots’ may be emerging; and to remain alert and attuned. The future will emerge in ways that are hard to imagine. Adapting to and exploiting changing circumstances can offer as much if not more advantage as will constantly refining our analysis of the present.



VII (c) It all Depends





57


The art of systemic change



Systemic change is not always achieved by ‘acting’ systemically; evaluate when and how to

strengthen ‘the system’, when and how to disrupt it.



In this section, I focus on change. I have in mind organisational change, and the sort of change that takes place at the level of communities – place-based, social change.

In an organisational setting, this may focus on the development of a more innovative or customer-focused culture or aim to improve happiness or teamwork; in community settings, it could centre on improving well-being or prosperity.

This is a vast subject, and in this short piece I am drawing on my own experience of working in the field of change, informed by ‘Daoist-flavoured’ complexity.

The term systems change pervades the ether these days, although it is probably safer to talk about systemic change as it removes the lure of thinking that ‘a system’ – some-thing clear and boundaried – necessarily exists [13].

To speak of systemic or holistic change implies that the aim is to create change in all or most of the interwoven aspects of what is there, rather than merely affect one partic-ular aspect. Systemic change is expected to be more sustainable and resilient than if only parts or aspects are changed.



Holistic diagnosis but more focused interventions?



But if we want to create systemic change, does that necessarily mean we should aim to work with the whole?

It is certainly the case that, when planning change interventions in an organisation or community, a holistic approach to the diagnostic phase is most likely to reveal the fea-tures and patterns of what is there, paying attention to history, culture and context. This is discussed in ‘Simplicity on the other side of complexity’ [55] and ‘Strategy in context’ [56].

But when it comes to building on this diagnosis and moving into action, it is by no means self-evident that trying to work with the whole is the best option. It may be more effective to identify leverage points and focus on those, as Donella Meadows suggested.56 Or perhaps we should aim to create the conditions in which people can feel empowered to take their own initiative and let ‘one hundred flowers bloom’, as discussed in [61]?

250 VII (c) It all Depends



Strengthen or disrupt?



There is another question to consider too: Should we aim to support and strengthen positive aspects of the status quo? Or are there times when we should go all out to disrupt and destabilise power structures, or other obstacles to change?57

How to strengthen?



Participation and consensus building are often seen as the route to strengthening those aspects of the status quo we want to revitalise or develop. This is often approached through a comprehensive stakeholder engagement to define goals and create plans. Sometimes, however, it may be more effective to operate with less fanfare, in a less overt fashion – perhaps bringing people together from different stakeholder groups more informally – to share learning, develop new relationships and feel inspired to go away and experiment. Evidence that such less-coordinated processes are starting to gain traction, may arise when thought leaders and those with power and influence start to take an interest.

Sometimes, too, strengthening is less about focusing on the status quo and more about nurturing and protecting any emerging ‘new shoots’ of change. In effect, we are trying to grow something new alongside the old in the hopes that the new will supersede it.

We can also aim to find critical moments or opportunities where change can be trig-gered. Such moments may arise through an unexpected failure or unintended success, a change of leadership, a new entrant into the market, or a change in the law. Perhaps our focus should be on spotting opportunities and ‘seizing the moment’?

How to disrupt?



There are also occasions when the task is less to preserve and develop what we have, and more to destabilise and disrupt aspects that are blocking change. Being measured or seeking consensus can be ineffective when trying to shake up very locked-in situa-tions or effect a radical redistribution of power.

Change can be catalysed when people resist and refuse to be defeated by prevailing norms. They ‘stand against the crowd’ and speak ‘truth to power’. Sometimes the ne-cessity for a radical rethink is triggered by consumers or citizens deciding they have had enough; they force change through collective buying choices, boycotts or protests.

Some brave journalists and activists seek to expose powerful cabals or corruption and bring attention to ‘bad practice’ or discriminatory processes, as discussed in [71]. The same can happen – or not happen – in organisations. How much totally worthless ac-tivity goes on because nobody wants to stand up and say that something is pointless or ineffective? David Graeber in Bullshit Jobs: a Theory, committed a whole book to this topic.58 However, this is hard to do if you fear isolation, persecution, or loss of liveli-hood.

57 The art of systemic change 251



So, we should not shy away from the role of disruption and challenge and imagine that creating systemic change is always about enabling, connecting and strengthening.



Agonism: the value of productive arguing



I want to say a bit more about participation and collective decision-making. I some-times wonder whether our desire to ensure that we are seen as respectful and fair, can actually hinder progress. We may fear that if we take the lead, or say too much, or display our particular talents or knowledge, we appear overly dominant. But sure-ly, effective participation requires ‘productive disagreement’, which Chantal Mouffe calls agonism [70]? Indeed, if too much emphasis is placed on harmony, some may be tempted to subvert change through inaction and silence. Equally, discussions may go on forever and, in the desire for consensus, it can become hard to reach a decision. Or others, who have a ‘bee in their bonnet’, or a clear view of what is needed, may start to work ‘under the wire’. Deviance may not seem desirable, and yet there are times when those mavericks who break the rules are actually the ones who create movement.

Standing up for something, speaking out and being seen, can often take much cour-age. But evolutionary theory emphasises that change, and adaptation often start with events that destabilise the status quo; change is generally not a tidy process.



Conclusion



Perhaps the core message from this short foray into the topic of creating systemic change is that it is paradoxical. Whatever you say, there is some truth in its opposite. Systemic change is not always achieved by working systemically. Aiming to build con-sensus in a participative fashion has its downsides as well as its positives. Nonconfor-mance, conflict, disruption all have their place; it is not just about being ‘good’. And however well-conceived the plans and methods, things inevitably will not happen the way we imagined or intended; it all depends. When dealing with change in a complex world, there are no easy answers. What might work best is contingent on circumstanc-es. The current situation can be brought to light through a diagnostic phase, but we have to remain alert to the inevitable shifts in context over time.

But this does not mean we should give up; it means we should engage very specifically with each situation, consider what is needed, try things out and be prepared to modify direction as we go [27]. And we should beware of anyone who thinks they have a meth-od that holds true in every situation!





58


The sagacity of leadership in complexity



Leadership in a complex world requires high levels of wisdom and skills, plus teamwork.



Complex and sophisticated competencies



Those in positions of leadership in a complex, uncertain and fast-changing world ide-ally need the highest levels of cognitive skill, coupled with superb emotional intelli-gence and sophisticated skills of relationship-building.

Embracing complexity requires a willingness to engage with uncertainty, ambiguity and paradox. It is enhanced by an ability to contemplate the big picture, and yet, at the same time, dive into the details and particularities at the sharp end. It calls on cogni-tion, instinct and imagination in equal measure. In the main, solutions will not be sur-faced by analytical means alone.

This can seem to require – in the language of Rooke and Torbert in Seven Transforma-tions of Leadership – that we become Alchemists, or at least Sages.59 But how many of us achieve this level of wisdom, intelligence, emotional maturity, skill, foresight and humility?

Mastery hears its own heartbeat and the heartbeat of others simultaneously.

Camaren Peter60



So, what to do? If I am in a position of authority in an organisation, I need to take note of this required breadth of competencies and practices, but I cannot expect to be gifted in them all. If I am worried about my own emotional intelligence, then it will be important to work with people I trust to take the temperature of the room and help to engender good communication and collaboration. If I feel unskilled at conceptual thinking, or in-expert at finding creative solutions, then I must find people who are. To embrace the complex world effectively requires, at the very least, working closely with a diverse team to cover all bases, and often suggests the need for processes of shared leadership.

But we are at our best when we are ourselves. Any of us required to step up and lead are most effective when embracing our own uniqueness, at the same time as putting in place ways to deal with those parts of the process at which we are less adept. As Aris-totle said, “knowing yourself is the beginning of all wisdom”.



254 VII (c) It all Depends



Dealing with power and dominance



Embracing complexity, though, is not the only game being played. If we look around us, those in positions of power are often selected due to their ability to convey certainty in a vision or process, and by so doing, make people feel protected and secure. It can sometimes seem that adopting a stance of certainty is rewarded above all else, even if the future shows it was unfounded.

Seek clarity not certainty. Julian Baggini61



In addition, global commercial organisations have an inbuilt requirement to focus on profit and shareholder value – and the stock market drives a short timescale on these. Leaders – business leaders and politicians – are not in general rewarded for grappling with the long-term future of the planet or striving to reach their outlying and less valu-able customers or citizens. They are also, of course, not applauded for maintaining a healthy balance amongst their fellow firms or ensuring the political ecology remains vi-brant and democratic. They are there to grow, to dominate their markets and put their opponents out of action [68]. Indeed, ‘killing off’ the opposition is seen as an entirely legitimate, indeed laudable, strategy in the name of competitive advantage. Leaders of industry, and perhaps also leaders in politics, may feel under pressure to ‘leave their values at the door’ in order to do what is necessary to win [70].

In order to be effective, leaders have to engage with the reality of powerful others – others who may have very different motivations to their own – and be prepared to influence, or even out-manoeuvre, those with other values and different goals. It can be important to accept and consciously address the political nature of the ‘stage’; oth-erwise, it is possible, for example, do a great job of creating a fabulously effective, cre-ative and agile team, only to have it shut down by a senior manager who either does not understand what had been achieved, or realises that it does not suit his purpose.

How to be effective in handling power and politics needs careful consideration: how to widen your spheres of influence; how to imagine ‘walking in the shoes of others’ so you can plan how to approach them; how to become alert to the levers of power and influence that you do have available, and thus increase your agency? These are import-ant questions.



Grab the simplicity on the other side of complexity, and yet keep

an eye out for newness



It is also a relief to recognise that, on a day-to-day basis, it is not necessary to continu-ally splash about frenziedly in the sea of complexity. Partly, this ‘sea’ is often composed of relatively stable patterns and so is relatively straightforward, much of the time, to characterise. Partly, whilst the appraisal of complex situations requires grappling with a wide and messy picture, clear strategies and actions can be developed that ‘hold’,

58 The sagacity of leadership in complexity 255



at least for the short term [56]. So, the task of the leader, once the appraisal stage is completed for now, and a way forward has been decided, is to allocate roles and actions and give people space to undertake them, whilst ensuring that review process-es and opportunities for discussing unexpected obstacles or opportunities are also established.

It is of equal importance to establish ways to spot changes, opportunities and threats and ensure processes are in place to then address their implications. How, for example, can the collective intelligence of the ‘hive’ be harnessed to see early signs of changes in the wider context? The Daoists emphasise above all else the importance of the wise leader’s ability to spot change ‘at the beginning of the beginning’; they regard this as proffering great advantage [27].



Leadership as a social process



I have so far focused on the pressures that the complex world places on those who are in formal positions of leadership. But, in many ways leadership, like ‘organisation’, emerges. How decisions are actually made is a social process that is impacted but not determined by those in formal leadership roles. As Sharon Varney explains, leadership is “co-created by mutual recognition” and “emerges from the interactions of organisa-tional actors in social networks and social movements”.62 It can be thought of as a ‘pat-terning’ that is impacted by, but may also cut across, formal and legitimate authority.63

Like water, the leader is yielding. John Heider64



Some approaches to leadership take the route of abolishing permanent roles alto-gether; leadership responsibility is rotated, and leadership is participative. However, coping with too little structure can end up being time-consuming and, particularly if consensus is the aim, can result in decisions that are ‘beige’ and safe. Equally, highly participative processes can lend themselves to being ‘worked’ by those who are adept at the politics of organisations, and who are able to influence and corral support be-hind the scenes, as discussed in [57]. The best level of structuring will be contingent on purpose and context. It is important to embed the practice of paying attention to the way leadership is emerging and ask whether what is emerging is effective.65



Be bold but pragmatic



Recognising that the world is complex and oftentimes fast changing can cause us to grind to a halt. We prefer to wait for certainty before acting. Sometimes, though, hav-ing assessed the situation as clearly as possible and having reached tentative conclu-sions, the best approach is to act with confidence. Seizing the moment is a key part of success. At the same time, it is important to stay in tune with what then starts to unfold, so that we can adapt if things do not evolve as hoped [56].

256 VII (c) It all Depends



This shift in worldview requires more than recognising the messiness of the world and ‘coping’ with it or aiming to ‘manage’ it. Complexity is the source of creativity, adapt-ability and innovation. Embracing complexity contains an invitation to become ener-gised, stimulated, thrilled even, by the opportunities opened up by this shift in view.



In conclusion



Zoe Arden pointed me to Leadership for a Sustainable Future.66 In this report, she and her colleagues define leadership as:

A dynamic social process that shapes behaviour and culture, requires a meaningful

purpose, and is embedded in and shaped by context.



I think this sums it up nicely. This definition fits neatly with a process complexity worl-dview [10]. Leadership is a process not just a style or something associated with one person; and as a process it will always be changing and adapting. It is contextual – it will both be shaped by context but also needs to respond to context. And leadership shapes and is shaped by purpose. For purpose to be meaningful, it has to have what Daoists call consummate integrity [23] – a sense of wholeness that ‘makes sense’. Purpose, too, builds on intention, but is also affected by the values that determine how those inten-tions are enacted. In other words, it encompasses both where you are trying to head and how you are going to get there. As Daoists point out, in leadership, as in life, the path is made by walking [15], and the steps you take are influenced by where you want to get to – even if you never arrive, or you change your mind along the way.





59


Organisation: the shape of things to come67



A complex and processual perspective on organisational design, and why, in an

ever-more-complex and connected world, we should think in terms of organisational

ecologies.



Individual organisations, power and process



Ideally, the choice of organisational form or structure should enable the tasks organi-sations perform to be achieved in a way that fits the dynamics of their market. Often

though, the selection of form is driven by the mindsets of those in charge. If senior managers believe that the best approach is to mimic the workings of a machine, then bureaucratic, hierarchical structures may be established, seeking standardisation, clarity and efficiency. By contrast, if the prevailing belief is that an ability to adapt to changing external circumstances is the key to success, then a decentralised design may result. Henry Mintzberg asked whether designs are led by fashion or fit.68 Indeed, as these approaches go in and out of fashion, organisations sometimes flip and flop be-tween centralisation and decentralisation, leaving traces of the former behind.

If organisations do not properly address the polarity between control and flexibility, the resulting structure may become very complicated. The tension between a pull to centralise against a pull to localise is dealt with by adding more and more tweaks and processes on top of the existing ones. This can make it impossibly hard to get anything done. I was involved with one such organisation and it finally dawned on me that the reason I was there was to legitimise the muddle, to suggest to employees that ‘complex-ity’ meant that muddle was inevitable. But I disagree. We cannot escape the complex-ity of the environment, but we can aim to design organisations that have a degree of consistency. We should not leave it to employees to navigate the ambiguities emerging from the unresolved tension between two paradigms.
