I remember one employee tell-ing me that it took her two years to work out where power really lies before she could get anything done!

It is a challenge, though, to design organisations that are effective, resolve conflicting priorities (such as between cost and customer focus) and are poised to respond to an unknown emerging future. Furthermore, trying to change organisational structures is not helped by the fact that any proposed change almost inevitably leads to shifts in power at the senior level. It is interesting that the topic of organisational design – something that used to be taught in business schools and was a key specialism offered by management consultancies – seems to have almost disappeared from view. And yet, 258 VII (c) It all Depends



matching the organisation’s form to its purpose, task and market has not lost its im-portance. If this results in a muddle or mismatch, it hinders effectiveness. When staff spend more time talking about the inner world of the organisation rather than turning their attention to the outer world of the market, there is cause for concern.

A processual view of organisations



Ideally the structure of organisations should follow and not dictate the flow within key ‘business processes’, the paths through which products and services move from incep-tion to the market. This epitomises a process orientation towards organisations.69 I am not advocating there should be an exhaustive analysis resulting in detailed and com-plicated flowcharts of pipelines and pathways; but I am suggesting that evaluating the effectiveness of decision-making and communication having identified those core pro-cesses, and aiming to preference and streamline their operation, is worthy of consid-eration. Otherwise, if it is unclear which processes are core, the ‘tail may wag the dog’.

There are others who adopt such a processual view of organisations, for example Ha-ridimos Tsoukas and Robert Chia.70 They see the purpose of organisational design as streamlining and corralling activities in order to achieve desired outcomes. They say: “Organization is the attempt to order the intrinsic flux of human action and channel it towards certain ends.” 71

But they also make the point that what organisations become is always a dance between what is intended and how people respond. They emphasise the co-evolving, emergent nature of organisation, and see organisation as “a pattern that is constituted, shaped and emerging from change”.72 Whatever is designed or intended, what constitutes the actuality of organisation depends on what people do with that design – how they shift, ignore, or work round it – or indeed decide to conform to it. James March suggests that because people are “intelligently attentive to their environments and jobs” they will naturally find ways to make it work and adapt how they do things.73 His view is that what pragmatically constitutes the organisation – what actually emerges – is a com-plex weaving between the constraints imposed by structure and process, and what people actually need to do to get things done.

This makes a lot of sense; but, of course, another force is also in play, and that is power. The desire to hold on to power can work against the pragmatic desire of the ‘doers’ to adapt. Power plays can cause an organisation to have certain immovable structural blocks around which the ‘water of activity’ must flow.

In summary



Organisational reality will never accord with what was designed and intended, but if the design is aligned as much as possible with task and environment, and if its form makes sense to people, it can aid rather than obstruct. In particular, it is important to agree in principle whether the organisation needs to move closer to the market and enable flexibility at the sharp end; or whether it is best served by a more centralised

59 Organisation: the shape of things to come 259



and often siloed approach. I think of the stories people have shared of the tensions caused when sales processes are local, stock management is regional, and manufactur-ing strategy is global. Local teams report that flexible decision-making at the sharp end is hampered by the constraints these differing centralised siloed structures impose. Confusion in levels of decision-making impedes fast and adaptive decision-making and the ability to balance many factors; such confusion can hamper achievement of the primary goal – to deliver goods and services efficiently to customers.

But organisations need to say alive and adaptive. It is helpful to undertake a regular re-view of the reality of organisational life (rather than the organisation design as set out on paper). In part, this helps to surface particularly inimical, power-preserving block-ages, and identify unnecessary ‘complicatednesses’. A review can also explore whether there is need for a new approach in response to any shifts in the environment or chang-es to working practices.



Ecologies of organisations



Thus far, I have discussed individual organisations and I now want to consider organi-sations as forming ecologies.

The first formulation of a theory of organisational ecology was undertaken by Michael Hannan and John Freeman in 1977.74 This ecological perspective is relevant as we con-sider how the world of work is changing. Remote working – enabled by increased IT connectivity, flexibility in working hours, outsourcing specialist services and increased automation – is changing the game. The nature of interactions and alliances between organisations – their relative size and the way responsibilities and specialisms are di-vided up across organisations – is changing too.

The demise of the middle-sized?



Organisations with global reach are able to lock-in their dominance as they have the weight – both economic and political – to quash or purchase any aspiring new rivals.75 Since the trend towards the deregulation of markets in the late 1980s, coupled with the opportunities afforded by the internet, it would appear that there has been a rise in the numbers of mega-organisations and a reduction in the numbers of the mid-dle-sized. My observation is that the ecology of organisations consists, increasingly, of giant ‘whales’ surrounded by myriads of tiny ‘minnows’.76 The tuna and cod of the or-ganisational world are becoming endangered! Minnows can provide specialisms such as leading-edge and risky research, specialised consultancy, clever IT-driven solutions to logistics and so on. A different sort of minnow – an independent clothes designer, a restaurant with a novel theme, a dance studio – can create or exploit a local niche but can find it hard to weather variations in demand, and struggle to grow to become mid-dle-sized. Mega-organisations are able to absorb or kill off any emerging new players. This loss of ‘size balance’ is of concern, as it impacts the resilience of ecologies, both natural and organisational; this is discussed further in [64].

260 VII (c) It all Depends



Meanwhile, the world is increasingly in the hands of powerful, internet-driven organ-isations that provide communications and social media, deliver almost every product at the click of a button, and leave little room for other enterprises to emerge. Such mega-organisations have more power than do governments to shape the global future and are very hard to regulate [71].

Ecological thinking and governance



Max French and colleagues, in Harnessing Complexity for Better Outcomes in Public and Non-profit Services, consider the public service system as an ecology of organisations, where their impact is “dispersed across the relationships amongst them”.77 Working with an ecological focus – establishing processes to determine how organisations in-teract, designing methods to agree how to share resources and power and work adap-tively – would allow societies to deploy resources more effectively and achieve the collective outcomes required to serve the public needs. The same thinking has rele-vance in the corporate world too and would be useful in considering issues of global governance in the face of the rising polycrisis, given the power of mega-corporations to dominate the future, as discussed in [71]. Which is not to say it would be welcomed, easy to implement or popular!



VII (d) It’s neither One Thing nor the Other





60


Paradox, polar opposites and the middle ground



When starting to reach a conclusion, explore what truth lies hidden in its opposite. By danc-

ing with paradox, fresh insights may emerge.



I am struck by how easy it can be, in conversation, to drop into generalisations and absolutes. Statements such as: ‘young people are fearful these days’, ‘no one really

changes’ or ‘people are more selfish than they used to be’. Such maxims can greatly influence what we believe needs to happen and how we think it can be achieved [65]. Maxims provide the comfort of a sort of certainty but may blinker us to engaging with their opposites. For example, if we think people need to be controlled, then we will fo-cus on creating rules. If we think individual freedom is the most important thing, then we will defend a system that imposes few constraints on that freedom.

If we allow that things may be complex, nuanced and situational, then are there ways to burrow underneath our sometimes unconscious ‘conventional wisdom’, and support us to think more broadly?

Whatever you think, think the opposite. Sharon Varney78



One approach: When you find yourself quoting some absolutist wisdom, ask what truth or value there might be in its ‘polar opposite’. It is useful to be specific. If you feel that ‘no one really changes’ then can you think of times when you, or others, did change? Trying on the opposites of any statements you or others make can help develop a more nuanced perspective and trigger a wider inquiry.

D. H. Lawrence … was prepared to allow contradictory thoughts to coexist, to push ev-

ery thought to its extremity in order to try it out and then think its opposite.79



For example, I was taken aback when I heard someone say, with great conviction, that everyone wants to be happy. It really got me thinking. How much do I value happiness? Do I think happiness is either achievable or laudable? What is the downside of focusing on happiness, as opposed to other goals such as ‘living a meaningful life’ or ‘fulfilling my potential’? For what would I like to be remembered?

Complexity theory, as well as pointing to the particularity of different situations, indi-cates that there are no easy answers, but many paradoxes [18]. Too much control and we lose adaptability and suppress innovation; too little control and we risk a muddle where no one knows what to do. Paradoxes such as these are centre stage in complex-ity thinking: freedom yet responsibility, control yet adaptability, standardisation yet response to particular circumstances, short-term versus long-term. What works here 264 VII (d) It’s neither One Thing nor the Other



may not work there. Sometimes it is good to go with the flow, sometimes it is better to stand against it. Working in this middle ground, maybe working out where to start in this situation; these are the sort of judgements that the complex world demands.

The test of a first-rate intelligence is the ability to hold two opposed ideas in mind at

the same time and still retain the ability to function. F. Scott Fitzgerald



Sometimes working with paradox requires discernment as to where to land on the continuum between opposites – the right shade of beige, the right compromise. But sometimes, it requires explicitly grappling with that paradox, and in so doing, allowing new solutions and understandings to emerge; these solutions may be radical and novel and ‘transcend’ what was there before.80



A third runway

Some years ago, I was party to a conversation about building a third runway at Heath-

row in London. The economic argument was for it; the environmental argument was

against it. And in general, in such situations, the economic argument wins. However,

the expert I was talking to pointed out to me that even if the environmental lobby won

and there was no third runway, the same amount of traffic would arrive in the region

and would merely arrive in Paris or Amsterdam. But, so he suggested, if we used the

opportunity of a new runway to tighten up on standards of pollution and install an ex-

emplary air traffic control system to minimise circling whilst waiting to land, we could

kickstart a step change in the industry and by so doing help to reduce carbon emissions

and pollution elsewhere. This struck me as an example of working creatively with what

can feel like irresolvable polar opposites (the needs of the economy versus the needs

of the environment) and coming up with a ‘third way’ (as opposed to a third runway … ).



Paradoxical, context-sensitive problems are the stuff of the complex world. The com-plex social and natural world – of diversity, openness and reflexive relationships – does not reduce down to a simple form where there are unequivocal solutions. When work-ing in complex situations, there is a need to tussle with opposites and ‘home in’ on solu-tions that are often contextual and particular to circumstances.

Irresolvables define where metaphysical opposites differ from each other, and [res-

olution] requires more than theory. It requires learning, participation, integration

and negotiation – strategies which engage directly with the contextual specifici-

ties and can possibly lead to resolution under certain context-specific conditions.

Camaren Peter81



Compromise, transcend or work in the middle ground?



There are various approaches to handling paradox, some more successful than others.

Compromise: The first is to aim for compromise. But if we are not careful, this may lead to the worst of both worlds. This approach seems to be adopted quite frequently in

60 Paradox, polar opposites and the middle ground 265



organisations, where processes are layered upon processes with the aim to compensate for the limitations of one by adding another on top [59]. This can lead to a very compli-cated muddle, in which it becomes really difficult to work out how to get things done.

Another kind of compromise ends up with what I call ‘the beige solution’. It builds on a story of a community group who were trying to agree on the colour of a new carpet. The only colour they could agree on was beige. In other words, compromise can lead to the lowest common denominator.

Transcend: The second is to adopt an intuitive, creative way to work explicitly with po-lar opposites with the aim of creating a ‘third way’ that transcends the initial polarities. The approach is to hold in mind the relevant polar opposites and allow new solutions to surface; Barry Johnson, in Polarity Management, provides a useful source of methods.82

Work in the middle ground: Thirdly, sometimes the way forward can be more prag-matic – where there is a need to find a balance between, say, clarity and flexibility, or local and global. This is ‘working in the middle ground’. Approaches are likely to be con-textual and particular to circumstances.

For example, imagine you are managing a group of hospitals, all based in different parts of the country, with very different demographies and needs. Is there a way to define the principles and outcomes that are required at the same time as allowing a degree of flexibility for each hospital to engage with the institutions in its own community and make the best of the particular local resources to respond to particular local needs? This is not a compromise – rather it leads to an approach which sorts out (a) what needs tight global control; (b) what aspects are best served by clarifying principles within which a more adaptive and contextual response can be taken; and (c) what is most ef-fectively managed and designed at local level.

Working in the middle ground, does not mean aiming for the half-way point; it re-quires working out, in this space between such polar opposites as control and flexibil-ity, where the most effective approach lies. This more flexible way of working is often well served by creating forums whereby practitioners from, say, different hospitals, can come together to share practices and learning. In this way a degree of consistency emerges through sharing practice, rather than through imposition from above.



And so …



In general, the goal is not to be satisfied with compromise , nor end up ‘going beige’. The real gold is uncovered when finding creative ways of transcending polar opposites and finding new solutions or perspectives that ‘go beyond’.





61


Let one hundred flowers bloom83



Sometimes, when things are impossibly locked-in, random acts are what is needed.



One of the ideas that emerged from the work of those researchers and journalists exploring the way oppressive regimes crumble was that ‘letting a hundred flowers bloom’ was often what catalysed their downfall [70]. However, allowing a free for all would seem to sit in contradiction to the assertion that clear intentions and shared values are vital drivers of change. Are there times when ‘letting things emerge’ is just what is needed to break out of rigid and hierarchical structures?

Vaclav Havel, in The Power of the Powerless, which became a manifesto for dissenters in Czechoslovakia, Poland and other communist regimes, reported that in the end people just felt compelled to ‘be themselves’ – to play their own music, dress how they wanted and embrace their uniqueness.84 He explained this was a pre-political stage, with no leadership or coordination or collective intent, and, due to that, it was very hard for the regime to oppose. A regime cannot arrest everyone.

The multiple consists of noise, but within the noise are heartbeats, rhythms, move-

ments – sustained or periodic – a thousand drums in the noise. Camaren Peter85

A colleague recounted the story of a meeting convened to discuss ways of tackling global warming. Apparently, participants were bemoaning the huge number of unco-ordinated and sometimes overlapping initiatives popping up from all sorts of differ-ent spaces. Some felt this was a waste of effort, but one had this to say: “We’re doing something unprecedented, in every corner of society and industry. There will need to be many initiatives. The number [of initiatives] will be too much for any one person to stay on top of. That’s probably a sign of success.”86

So, which route to take and how to decide? Getting out of locked-in, rigid situations, where power is held in the hands of the few, might benefit from ‘random acts of activ-ism’. By contrast, when a community is trying to become established, agreeing shared intentions and ways of working may be vital. The best approach is contingent on the prevailing conditions – whether there is rigidity, or chaos, or something in between [14]. There are times when setting the conditions for experiment, entrepreneurism and self-expression is more important than corralling it all into a meaningful direction.

One thought to wrestle with: Engaging with the complexity of the world also has some-thing to offer those who are intent on attack and destruction – rogue regimes and ter-rorist organisations. If the plan is to bring down a society from the outside, then ‘light-ing one hundred fires’ is an effective way to work. You can be opportunistic in offering military support to weaker regimes or finding weaknesses in cyber security. Fake news and taking opportunities to discredit others work well too.

268 VII (d) It’s neither One Thing nor the Other



Which takes me to this: Values are probably the most vital factor to hold on to when acting in the complex, interconnected, emergent, paradoxical world in which we live. If the future is co-created through the path we walk, we need to pay attention to the quality of each step.





62


Abandon hope all ye who enter here?



In a complex world, there is always hope and yet hope can get in the way of living the life

we have.



Of all the topics I have researched and pondered, inquiring as to whether or not hope is a ‘good thing’ has provoked the greatest divergence of views.

I also notice that this inquiry into hope exemplifies how I tend to explore paradoxical, complex questions. I seem to carry the question – in this case as to whether or not hope is useful – around with me into all situations. I often find that odd snippets of informa-tion surface – on the radio, in the newspaper, when looking into something else. I get into conversations about it, and I argue with myself. Through allowing this diverse ma-terial to ferment in my brain, I seem to arrive at an understanding that is more subtle, nuanced and contextual than when I started.

This is how the inquiry unfolded.



Is hoping ‘good’ or ‘bad’?



If things are difficult, do people feel better if they keep alive the hope that things will improve? Or could that breed discontent and prevent them from making the best of to-day? Do they see today merely as a stepping-stone or waiting game until their hoped-for future arrives?

People are different and adopt their own ways to handle hope and disappointment. But it makes me wonder, given we can never really know what the future will bring, and cannot change the past that brought us to where we are, would not embracing today be the most fruitful? Or, as someone said to me, perhaps we should aim just to ‘keep a little bit of hope for our cherished dreams, but only peek at it once in a while’.

Think about the hope for love: Philip Larkin writes about the desire that is encapsu-lated in love songs and asks whether this romantic vision help when ‘life and love fall short’? 87 Might it not amplify any sense of failure or disappointment?

The glare of that much-mentioned brilliance, love,

Broke out, to show

Its bright incipience sailing above,

Still promising to solve, and satisfy,

And set unchangeably in order. So

To pile them back, to cry,

Was hard, without lamely admitting how

It had not done so then and could not now. Philip Larkin88

270 VII (d) It’s neither One Thing nor the Other



Consider how we long for recovery from illness: Neurosurgeon Henry Marsh writes about the value of giving hope to patients, even to those with poor expected outcomes. He says: “Hope is one of the most precious drugs doctors have at their disposal … hope is a state of mind, and states of mind are physical states of our brains, and our brains are intimately connected to our bodies.”89 Giving hope, exaggerated or otherwise, ex-tends people’s lives through helping patients to manage stress and endure treatment.

What about the hope that we will reverse global warming? One research paper shows that giving people too much hope that all will be well limits their motivation to change. What leads to action is the fear that time is running out. They conclude that emotional distress is strongly correlated with the motivation to take action; hope is not.90

Hope can drive us forwards. But what happens when our hopes are dashed – when our longed-for career never materialises, or when our plans are thwarted by catastrophes not of our making? Buddhist writer Jack Kornfield writes of the transformative power of loss of hope, of disillusionment. He says, “disillusionment … is a powerful and fiery gateway. To be disillusioned is to be stripped of our hopes, imaginings, and expecta-tions”; he explains that loss of hope can lead to a more solid base from which to move forwards. In contrast, he believes that if we merely cling to the hope that the future will turn up something better, and never fully enter into these pits of despair, these un-resolved losses and traumas may continue to haunt us.91 The paradox is that, through giving up on hope and embracing disillusionment, we may find new promise ‘on the other side’.



Hoping against hope: in a complex world, there’s always hope



Complexity thinking has an interesting contribution to make to this debate in that it speaks to the difference between probability and possibility. To give an example: The dire consequences of global heating are very likely to be disastrous beyond our wildest imaginings. But we can still legitimately cling to the possibility that there is yet a way forwards, still some collective will or brave government or leading corporation that can tip the balance. Things may look doomed but sometimes they change on the head of a pin; sometimes a number of factors come together at the same time, and some-thing seemingly impossible becomes possible. I imagine Nelson Mandela, in his twen-ty-first year of imprisonment, did not really expect to get out alive, never mind become President of a post-apartheid South Africa. But it seems that Mandela continued to live life according to his values and did not give up. Keeping alive a little bit of hope can help to endure today and attune us to any unexpected possibilities as they arise and motivate us to keep moving forwards.

Ayana Elizabeth Johnson, a marine biologist, has this to say:

While I’m not a fan of hope as a guiding principle, because it by definition assumes that

the outcome will be good, which I know is not a given, I am completely enamoured with

the amount of possibility that’s available to us. So that’s the word that I try to embrace

when I think about what if we get it right, is how much possibility remains.92

62 Abandon hope all ye who enter here? 271



Rebecca Solnit agrees that it is the innate unpredictability of the world that is a cause for hope. She says: “It is easy to forget in retrospect how astonishing was the fall of the Berlin Wall in 1989 … or how marriage equality seemed like a long shot just before it became a reality in countries all over the world.” Solnit points out how unimaginable 2022 would have seemed in 1972 and how little our modern world resembles either prediction or science fiction. She reminds us that “we see no farther that the little halo that is cast by our lanterns, but we can travel all night by that light”.93

To be hopeful in bad times is not just foolishly romantic. It is based on the fact that

human history is a history not only of cruelty but also of compassion, sacrifice, courage,

kindness.

What we choose to emphasise in this complex history will determine our lives. If we see

only the worst, it destroys our capacity to do something. If we remember those times

and places – and there are so many – where people have behaved magnificently, this

gives us the energy to act, and at least the possibility of sending this spinning top of a

world in a different direction. And if we do act, in however small a way, we don’t have to

wait for some grand utopian future. The future is an infinite succession of presents, and

to live now as we think human beings should live, in defiance of all that is bad around us,

is itself a marvellous victory. Howard Zinn94

I give the last word to complexity theory, which, if it could, would say that nothing is fixed forever, and things do change. Hope springs eternal!





63


Seeing the world through new eyes:

some simple approaches



Pragmatic suggestions of how to ‘embrace complexity’ in organisations that do not.

The real voyage of discovery consists not in seeing new sights, but in looking with new

eyes. Marcel Proust



People who start to ‘get’ complexity tend to have one of two reactions. One is a sense of relief; they always thought things were that way and had for a long time ques-

tioned the orthodoxy of mainstream management methods. They feel empowered. By contrast, ‘getting’ complexity makes others feel bewildered and despondent. They ask ‘well, given I still have to adhere to the methods in my organisation which don’t allow such freedom, what am I supposed to do’?

Below are some pragmatic suggestions as to how to embrace complexity in a world that does not always want to.



Uncovering



In what ways can we uncover both what is newly emerging in the landscape and what has been overlooked – such as unexpected successes, or emerging opportunities or threats in the wider context?

Questions: Ask more questions, particularly of outward-facing staff. Do they detect a shift in mood or a change in the habits of customers? Are they picking up anything surprising or exciting? Such questions can give people the permission to share their intuitions; these intuitions can always be followed up more rigorously and, if sever-al people notice not-dissimilar issues, then confidence builds that there is a change emerging.

Micro-sharing: Add five minutes to the end of project or review meetings to ask about concerns, ideas, or share what is on the grapevine. Encourage people to discuss what isn’t working too. If people become used to this slot, they can feel that their opinions and insights are valued, and they may consequently develop a greater sense of respon-sibility for making things work. Such early warnings can usefully surface problems within projects, or spot changes within the organisation or its environment.

Network: Search out and sustain contacts with people who have a handle on the fu-ture, not just those in the mainstream. Build your own thought-leadership network.

Manage by walking about (MBWA): This is a phrase traced to Hewlett Packard in the 1970s and captures the value of informal encounters to sense into what is going on. I 274 VII (d) It’s neither One Thing nor the Other



think of MBWA when waiting in the reception areas of organisations. How do reception staff engage with me and with each other? Is the area welcoming? I’m already forming opinions about the organisational culture before I have properly set foot inside.



Expect the unexpected and look at situations dynamically



Allow some leeway to experiment: Strategies finely attuned to today almost by defi-nition will not be so well attuned to tomorrow. In addition to doing ‘sensible’ things supported by market analysis, build on a few hunches and take a few risks. You may un-cover hidden niches, or even create new niches through acting into the environment.

Create long-term intentions but make detailed plans for the short term: Review of-ten and reposition ‘not going to plan’ as learning, not failure. Failure is an inability or unwillingness to adapt.

Create steering groups with diverse stakeholders: If projects or strategies are steered by people with a diverse range of perspectives – including junior staff, staff from dif-ferent departments, independents from outside or a customer or supplier – the con-versation can be much richer; it can both allow a robust discussion of what is working and what is not, and also facilitate a creative exploration of what needs to change. If a regular steering group of this nature is not practical, then convene a review meeting on a regular basis to really dig deep and take more than a superficial view as to whether things are going to plan.



Context and history matter



Aim to maintain some consistency in approach over different parts of your organisa-tion, whilst recognising that there are differences in history and context. A school or hospital in a city, for example, has different problems and opportunities to one in a ru-ral setting. How to benefit from consistency, and yet allow some freedom to adapt?

Develop good practice by sharing, not by diktat: Share learning and good practice in conferences, or convene regular get-togethers with people from different geogra-phies, or from different parts of the organisation. Rather than ‘roll out’ good practice, encourage mutual influencing through swapping stories and examples.

Mine the past: The past is a good source for understanding what works and what doesn’t. It seems to have gone out of fashion to chat with people who used to do your job or contact people who have moved on. But it is an amazingly efficient way to learn some of the pitfalls, be provided with useful contacts, and gain the perspective of those who no longer have to be careful what they say. Also, the present is built on the past, so these conversations will enrich your understanding of the current territory on which you are operating.

63 Seeing the world through new eyes: some simple approaches 275



Use existing methods, but mediated through a different mindset



As an example of being required to use an existing method, suppose you are faced with a rather mechanical performance management system. You have to submit to Human Resources a set of objectives for each member of your team and show that you have reviewed these objectives. What can you do to make this approach more ‘complexity friendly’?

You could:

• Set some shared goals: Have a discussion with the whole group about what is

needed, what feels more certain, what feels more up in the air. Out of this could

come some joint objectives, together with a list of ‘things to watch’.

• Set some aspirational goals couched in developmental language: Rather than

be tempted to make objective setting ‘safe’, is it possible to couch ‘stretch goals’

in terms of developing the person, so she is not disadvantaged if these are not

achieved?

• Review more often: Review more regularly than the official form requires,

and if possible, on review, modify or re-weight existing objectives. Use review

discussions to consider the impact of shifts in the outer world, or surface problems

with other departments, so that response can be timely.

These suggestions are not ‘rocket science’, but hopefully they do illustrate ways to work more adaptively, and in a more connected fashion, within the constraints of cur-rent management systems.



Asking questions



I really like the following story shared by a junior manager in a company that makes plastic materials. He wanted to stimulate conversations that caused people to ques-tion the status quo, without overly rocking the boat.



Working under the radar: biding your time and sticking to your values

A former student, inspired by complexity thinking, wanted to see what he could do

to get his colleagues in the organisation where he worked – a company that made

plastic – to pay more attention to environmental concerns. He managed to get

permission to set up a future-thinking team to brainstorm ways to modify existing

materials and also discuss non-plastic options. The senior management were not very

open to this, and he didn’t make much impact, although the group continued to meet.

But he kept asking questions about issues of pollution and waste whenever he got the

chance. I saw him a year later. The company had been taken over by another, one that

was much more interested in new materials. His quiet activism and determination

had got him noticed. He was promoted into a role which aligned with his values and

concerns and gave him a platform to put them into practice.

276 VII (d) It’s neither One Thing nor the Other



This next story illustrates how a head teacher changes the culture in his school through a shrewd use of questions.



Leading questions

A head teacher describes how he engenders change in his school.95 His aim is to nudge

the organisation’s values through judicious choice of questions. In so doing, he indicates

what he sees as important (reminiscent of the approach to natural governance dis-

cussed in [53]). He might ask: How do you demonstrate the value of honesty to pupils?

How do you show appreciation of unusual or expected answers? How do you show the

class you care? Paradoxically, his approach both opens up a space for dialogue through

legitimating a new language (caring, for example, now becomes an ok word); at the same

time, he shapes and constrains that space through clarifying what is deemed important.



The power of questions: Both these stories, above, really emphasise for me the power of questions. Asking questions can raise difficult issues in a way that is not too con-fronting. For example, asking ‘what are you worrying about’ can encourage people to share concerns and bad news earlier so that things can be tackled. Being curious and interested can also encourage a more relational approach that sets the ground for ask-ing for help or sharing learning. It can be a way to get into ‘why’ questions and into problem-solving. And the direction of questioning can display what is deemed import-ant and encourage and legitimate new topics for discussion.

Helping others to ‘get complexity’: Asking questions can also provide a means to en-courage others to notice the complexity of situations. For example, asking why a strat-egy did not go to plan is a very effective tool for surfacing issues of context, emergence and interdependency. Such discussions can help people to engage with the complexity of the world and start to explore new ways of working. Once you’ve seen the world as complex, it is hard to unsee it.



And so…



I hope these simple thoughts on how to embrace complexity in environments which do not immediately see its value – and would rather avoid its challenge – offer some pragmatic suggestions for practice in organisational settings.

Finally, leading in our complex world can seem a daunting, and overwhelming chal-lenge. It is impossible to control complex situations, so it is pointless to aim to do this. Similarly, you cannot predict the future. What you can do, is behave with integrity, in line with your values and beliefs, and sleep soundly knowing you did the best you could.





Section VIII




On the Global





64


Introducing on the global: world without end?



Complexity thinking applied to the global emphasises the systemic interdependencies of the

social, environmental, political and economic; seeks a middle ground between laissez-faire

and control; raises concerns over the potential for lock-in of power and growing inequality

and provides a challenge to unremitting economic growth.



My growing preoccupation is with the global. As I’ve been writing this book, we have emerged from a pandemic, the biggest thing to happen to the global economy –

not to mention its impact on global health and wellbeing – for many years. We are ex-periencing global heating, leading to desertification, rising sea levels, increasing fre-quency of extreme weather events, loss of polar ice and rising temperatures. There is mass extinction, ocean acidification, pollution and ozone depletion – all problems of the global commons. Recent and ongoing conflicts and wars show the extent to which food security and energy supply are global concerns; production, to be efficient, has tended to become more concentrated by region – and hence control of resources, in-cluding their transport, becomes both a goal and a weapon of war. Social media, with its global reach, can inform and connect, but can also manipulate, incite, disinform and embed means of surveillance. There is growing economic inequality, both within coun-tries and between countries, and seemingly ever-worsening geopolitical unrest. Con-templating all this together does not conjure up a happy picture.

These issues are complex, interpenetrating and vast. If complexity theory is good for anything, it must be able to throw some light on this global landscape and help us to think through what we need to do. Many of our urgent challenges are truly global; no country, region or people can isolate themselves from the global reach of disease or greenhouse gases, or from the impact of those gigantic, dominant global companies, or escape the will of superpowers.

Exploring these global issues not only provides a perspective in its own terms, but also serves to illustrate and bring to life principles at play in a complex world that have ap-plicability at different scales and in different contexts. These include the self-organisa-tion and self-regulation of societies, their path dependence, the dangers of lock-in and the way societal patterns are shaped through the systemic weaving of the economic, the political, the social and the environmental.

This section offers some insights and perspectives and suggests some ways forward.



280 Section VIII On the Global



In this Section



‘It’s only make-believe’ explores the way values, beliefs, and intentions shape social and economic policy. Beliefs (in individual freedom or the centrality of equality), fears (of those of different creeds or backgrounds), and attitudes (seeing nature as a re-source to consume or as something to preserve) are of fundamental importance. They shape what we view as appropriate goals and methods, and what we regard as effec-tive political or economic structures. Beliefs have immense potency; they can provide the fuel to push for change, cement in the status quo, cause us to ‘bow to our fate’, or legitimise our treatment of others ‘not like us’.

The second piece, ‘Our home, the earth’, considers what creates the wherewithal for the earth to self-regulate and find balance, and questions the viability of unfettered economic growth.

‘Run, run, run, run, runaway’ discusses the nature of runaway change and why we find the concept so hard to embrace when developing policy to address pandemics, climate change or potential environmental collapse.

‘Free markets: free for whom?’ discusses free market ideologies and how complexity thinking illustrates that minimal regulation can lead to the widening of inequality in the absence of mitigating ‘counterforces’ such as the role of the state and the role of values and intentions to mediate this tendency.

In ‘Does the economy have to be predicated on growth?’, I discuss the reasons for ‘lim-its to growth’ and reflect on alternative economics models.

The next piece, ‘On the political’, considers the reflections of various political writers who have lived through the rise and fall of dictatorial regimes. They explore, through these experiences, both how to arrest the path-dependent journey towards the dilu-tion of democratic freedoms and also how to shift away from locked-in power.

In the final piece of the book, I discuss what is being called the rising polycrisis. I con-sider the need to strengthen global governance and address what this crisis demands of us all.



Three overarching points



Before I head off into the substance of the Section, I want to examine three points in particular which are relevant to what follows:

The first focuses on the value of the unexciting-sounding middle ground – also known more enticingly as the Goldilocks zone. The second reflects on what it means to con-sider situations systemically, and the third reflects on the issue of scale. What is the necessary balance, in a global world, between working at the scale of the globe, the nation and society?

64 Introducing on the global: world without end? 281



1. Goldilocks and the middle ground



And then she went to the porridge of the Little, Small, Wee Bear, and tasted that; and

that was neither too hot nor too cold, but just right.

There is one difference in emphasis between the organisational and the global that is worthy of mention, and that is the way we humans have chosen to engage at these different levels. When considering how to manage organisations, the tendency is to-wards control through imposing a machine-like order, with the intention of enhancing clarity and efficiency. Section VII, Perspectives on Organisational Practice, gives many examples as to where the imposition of machine thinking at the level of the organisa-tion has unintended and unhelpful consequences.

Meanwhile, at the level of the economy, the focus has moved, since the late 1980s, in the direction of trusting that ‘natural law’ will give the freedom needed for ‘the best’ to survive – so-called laissez-faire or free market economics. In many parts of the world, by following free market thinking, the state’s role in providing services, owning and managing resources and regulating the economy has been diminished. In ‘Free mar-kets: free for whom?’ [68], I consider, amongst other things, the implications of leaving it all to the market.

It is interesting that the machine view of organisations and the free market approach to economics both have their roots in classical physics. The economists chose the phys-ics of equilibrium thermodynamics in their assumption that economic systems find bal-ance and ‘stationarity’ [8], and the management theorists were allured by the deter-ministic physics of Newton [3].

However, whether starting from too much control or too little, the desired end state is not dissimilar – freedom, but not unfettered freedom; processes of control but not overarching control.

As I have argued in Section VII, organisations often need to allow more flexibility, trust people more, and take more note of the particularities of context and history to shape management processes and structures [53].

In contrast, when considering processes that operate at the global level, such as eco-nomic processes, the starting point is often centred on too much freedom rather than too little. In that situation, I conclude, it implies we need to find ways – through reflect-ing on the purpose of the economy, exploring methods of governance and reinstating some methods of regulation – to re-introduce some forms of control.

Complexity theory does not equate with laissez-faire: A point to emphasise strongly, discussed in ‘Free markets: free for whom? ’[68], is that complexity theory does not advocate for laissez-faire and does not suggest that self-organising processes necessar-ily find balance. Rather, complexity theory shows how the big can get bigger, and the powerful more powerful. Over time, even forests end up with less diversity, and can 282 Section VIII On the Global



become more rigid and less resilient to shocks and changes around them, as Holling demonstrated [14].1 Positive feedback loops, if unfettered, can accord more power to the powerful and more wealth to the wealthy; the hope for a ‘natural’ reinstatement of balance is a hope in vain.2

The pieces in this section reflect on different aspects of the global world and, in the main, suggest that we need to consider ways to mediate the way power can concen-trate. Path dependence is at work here, so the advice is not to leave it so late that things have locked-in and thus become very hard to shift.

What do we value: Furthermore, as well as considering these more structural aspects of society, these pieces also bring into focus the importance of what we, as individuals and societies, value [68]. How do we think about the balance between personal desires and community needs, between the short-term and the long-term? What is the role of citizens in governance in order to hold governments and institutions to account? What services and responsibilities are best handled more locally, through a combination of public institutions, civil society and not-for-profits? Values and beliefs play a huge role in creating the global future [65] and exploring and mutually reflecting on values can be effective in moving towards a more inclusive and balanced future.

Not too much, not too little: I am arguing that societies, to be resilient, need to seek the ‘Goldilocks zone’ in the middle ground [60]. Too much direction will stymie, if not eradicate, the natural tendency for a community or society to be creative and able to find mutually beneficial ways to form synergies and cooperations. But too little direc-tion, or scant attention to values or rules, can allow an unstoppable co-option of power by those for whom the values of cooperation or fairness are not high on their agenda. Child and slave labour, inhumane farming practices and exploitation of workers con-tinue to happen in today’s world, when laws are either not in place or are not enforced. We need to find solutions that sit within the space between controlling everything and controlling nothing. We must ask: What kinds of regulations, laws or structures are needed? Conversely, what are the circumstances in which things are best left to sort themselves out? The solutions are always contextual – rarely are there answers that fit all circumstances.

2. Thinking systemically



Complexity theory takes a systemic perspective. Politics and the economy do not op-erate separately but are very interwoven, both in terms of ideology and identity, and in the way they function; the social, economic, political and environmental are all co-de-pendent and mutually affect each other. Despite this, certainly for the West, the focus on economic growth can trump almost anything else. If we choose between the econom-ic value of drilling anew for oil versus its impact on global heating, it invariably seems to be the case that the economic argument wins. Indeed, the environmental or social are often reframed in economic terms, such as in ‘ecosystems services’ or ‘gross national happiness’.

64 Introducing on the global: world without end? 283



By paying attention to the systemic nature of the global world, we underline the need for the interweaving of policy across all spheres and for ensuring the voice of the fu-ture is not lost in the debate. There are those in academia who are pushing for more transdisciplinary approaches to scholarship to allow for such systemic thinking and to counter the practice of delving deeper and deeper into ever-narrower specialisms.3 Creating strategic visions, setting systemic goals and intentions is also of great value, as Mariana Mazzucato emphasises [68]. These strategic visions can shape our thinking about the determinants of such outcomes; such visions are best tackled systemically, taking note of many interwoven strands.

The crises feed on each other in a sort of polycrisis—ecological, economic, political, so-

cial, civilizational—and it is escalating. Edgar Morin4



3. Scale



The third introductory point I want to make is about scale. To begin with the exam-ple of ecologies, mature ecologies, to survive, have to find a mutual balancing between species. But this does not mean that all species are of similar scale. There needs to be a balance between the large, the smaller and the tiny. Such an ecological balance can and does emerge in natural ecologies, both in the ocean and on land. However, when this balance in the population ecology is distorted – through, say, over-fishing whales – then the ecology as a whole loses resilience. What happens in this instance is that when the whale population substantially reduces, the population of krill, the tiny fish on which they feed, reduces too. This seems counterintuitive. But, apparently, the ac-tivity of whales diving deep brings up food resources from the depths for the krill to feed on, so having fewer whales does indeed result in there being fewer krill.5 Ecolo-gies require the giant, the medium and the tiny in dynamic balance – not too much, or too little, of any.

This concept of the need for balance in scale translates into the social world too. I dis-cuss this in relation to ecologies of organisations in ‘Organisation: the shape of things to come’ [59]. In similar vein, E. F. Schumacher, it is said, did not want his book to be called Small is Beautiful, because he recognised the need to select the appropriate scale for the issue.6 This ideal of working at the appropriate scale, known as subsidiarity, was adopted for a while as UK government policy in 1992. In 2010, the notion of ‘big society’ was promoted as a way to help society regenerate through giving citizens the means to look after their own communities; it continues today with devolution to cities and counties.7

Working locally to tackle locally determined, systemic goals concerning wellbeing or prosperity can be really effective and efficient. A local focus can open up ways and means for the public, private and not-for-profit sectors to work together to handle complex issues, making use of quirky local opportunities in the best possible way. But a local focus doesn’t help the design and upkeep of transport networks or energy 284 Section VIII On the Global



provision. And the issue we now face more than ever is that many of our challenges – global heating, pandemics, geopolitical unrest, economic, environmental and political migration – have to be tackled globally and collaboratively in a world not used to operating in that way.

Considering in what ways we need to think and act locally and where we need a region-al or global focus is an important factor to explore when engaging with the complex world.



Key strands



The pieces in this section, On The Global, raise many questions. I summarise below some of the strands that emerge as particularly salient:

• It is vital to dig for and bring to the surface the beliefs that underpin a particular

view of politics or economics before getting entranced by its mechanisms.

• If we are to sustain society and the environment into the future, we need economic

policies and practices that value more than profit and growth and in particular

tackle inequality.

• We have to ‘challenge early’ if iniquitous economic or political systems are not

to get locked-in and become unassailable, at which point can we only wait for

collapse?

• There is always power in the people.

• Values matter; we lose our humanity at our peril.





65


It’s only make-believe



What we believe about human nature, about the way things work underpins policy devel-

opment and the design of economic and political structures. It is vital to explore beliefs and

search for the beliefs of others when evaluating approaches to policy.

The belief that there is only one truth, and that oneself is in possession of it, is the root

of all evil in the world. Max Born



Beliefs matter. A belief of one sort or other lurks beneath almost everything we choose to do. I have elected to discuss beliefs in this Section, ‘On the Global’, be-

cause beliefs fundamentally impact political choices and shape social and economic policy.

In Section I, I discussed ontological beliefs, beliefs about ‘the way things work’. Does the world work like a and machine and so require hierarchical processes of control? Or is the world more complex and better served by social policies that are adaptive and contextual?

But, as well as these scientific worldviews, we have beliefs about the characteristics of ‘the other’. What are our views about the import of race, gender, disability, intelli-gence, religion or class? Who do we trust, who do we fear, who do we value? Who is ‘civilised’ and worthy, and who is not? Do we believe the strongest has an inalienable right to win? Do we have little sympathy for those who struggle because we think that ‘if we can do it, then anyone can’? Are we prepared to shut our minds to those who are negatively impacted by our ways of life? What value and meaning do we place on the natural world?

Some of our beliefs concern basic human nature – is Man good or evil or merely selfish and determined to win? Jean Hardy, in A Wiser Politics, explores in depth the way ideas about human nature drove foundational political and economic ideologies, and I rec-ommend her short book.8

Hardy discusses two of the key thinkers who shaped political ideologies: Thomas Hobbes and Jean-Jacques Rousseau. Hobbes expressed the view that human nature is essentially competitive and acquisitive.

Men love themselves best, have insatiable desires and want more and more and are

never satisfied. Hobbes9



In contrast, Rousseau said that “everything is good when it leaves the hands of the Cre-ator; it degenerates in the hands of man”.10

But is there any evidence to support the views of either Hobbes or Rousseau? I consid-er this question, from many angles, below.

286 Section VIII On the Global



Is Man ‘good’ or ‘evil’: some evidence

Anthropologist David Graeber and archaeologist David Wengrove, in The Dawn of Ev-

erything, argue that there is little anthropological evidence to suggest that Man’s pri-

mordial state was either of innocence or of ‘war of all against all’.11 They make the point

that Hobbes and Rousseau were hazarding opinions, as opposed to developing views

based on evidence.

Socio-biologists David Sloan Wilson12 and Edward O. Wilson13 view Man’s nature

through the lens of evolutionary biology. They suggest that, for packs of men to hunt

down animals more powerful than themselves, they needed to cooperate, to work as

a team. Yet they also recognise that Man, from earliest times, had the capacity to kill

‘the other’ – other tribes and groups who might take over their hunting grounds. Their

conclusion is that Man seems able both to cooperate with and take care of members of

his own tribe but also, with impunity, to kill, rape and pillage those who are different,

those whom he fears, or who have what he wants. In similar vein, Jane Goodall, in her

research on chimpanzees, was disappointed to find chimpanzees could show great ten-

derness and compassion for their tribe but would attack tribes who might impinge on

their territory in the most brutal manner, even if they were relatives.14

Looking at the field of psychology, Donald Winnicott explains that babies are born self-

ish and omnipotent and then, if all goes well with their development, they mature into

children and young people who can balance this selfish desire with the desire to think

of others. We are born selfish but have the capacity to cultivate our other-centredness

along the way. 15



I found this brief search for evidence about human nature – and there was more I hav-en’t shared – very illuminating. It suggests, perhaps not surprisingly, that Man can be both ‘good’ and ‘bad’, both compassionate and selfish. But we have some agency in this. If we also take into account the plasticity of the brain and the way it can change as we make different decisions and adopt new behaviours [44], it would appear that we have some choice as to whether we cultivate our best or worst traits [25]. What each of us becomes is path-dependent – a function of personal decision-making, although also shaped by what happens along the way, and shaped by the beliefs of our peer groups and culture.



Why does this matter? Beliefs shape ideology, policy and practice



As Hardy emphasised, what we believe about human nature has a strong impact on our chosen ideologies, and these in turn affect our views on policy. For example, if a politi-cian has to balance spending on health and education against spending on defence, po-lice and prisons, how do her beliefs about human goodness sway her decisions? Simi-larly, fear or distrust of ‘the other’ – fearing they are ‘bad’ – is likely to amplify concerns over immigration or the right to protest. On the other hand, believing too much in the good in others might allow someone to overly trust leaders when they are, in actuality, playing by different rules; a civil servant, for example, may develop regulatory policies that unrealistically rely on ‘gentlemen’s agreements’ to act fairly and openly.

65 It’s only make-believe 287



Justifications for adopting free market ideologies



As well as the (questionable) belief that it is in the nature of things to find balance, discussed in [68], there are further assumptions and assertions that build the case for free markets. Hobbes, for example, viewed society as essentially a series of market re-lations between selfish individuals and competing institutions.16 A free market, it then follows, makes a virtue out of the inevitable self-centredness of the individual, and pro-motes competition as the only game in town.

Interestingly, Friedrich Hayek was also a strong proponent of free markets, but his be-liefs centred on our rights rather than on human nature. Hayek believed, as did Kant, that the sovereignty of the individual, the right to individual liberty and freedom, was paramount. He felt that the market was the best invention of human society as it made people free.17

Some argue that, in a free market, there is equality of opportunity if not equality of outcome.18 Although some do better than others, free markets cause the playing field to be level, so the argument goes. Others argue that equality of opportunity does not really exist. Hashi Mohammed, for example, points out that equality of opportunity is driven in part by education and ability, but that ‘getting on’ is strongly facilitated by the path-dependent process of growing up in the right milieu and associating with the right people.19

The point I am making is that the beliefs held by those who design and implement eco-nomic and political systems will strongly determine what they see as the right way to ‘do’ economics or politics.



Hidden and dangerous beliefs



Sometimes, rather prejudicial and judgemental beliefs are hidden. For example, do we view those who are struggling as mainly deserving of support with just a few shirkers who slip through the net, or do we imagine there are mainly shirkers with only a few who are deserving? Do we regard ‘the masses’ as largely capable of understanding the governance issues at stake, or do we take rather a dim view of people’s abilities to evaluate the arguments? Such beliefs often remain undiscussed or undisclosed, apart from in private. But beliefs form the starting point for our thinking and can have a huge impact on policy development.

A lot of rich people expect poor people to be stupid. Gary Stevenson20

Questioning the beliefs of those in power, those who are making the rules, is often not an easy process, in part as commonly held beliefs tend to be seen as self-evidently true and unremarkable. For example, Clive Hamilton, in Requiem for a Species, points out that a tendency to deny global warming stands on the belief that the rhetoric around climate change constitutes an envious attack on preserving comfortable lifestyles, and is hence politically motivated.21

288 Section VIII On the Global



Standing apart from the crowd with respect to our beliefs is often uncomfortable and even, in some circumstances, dangerous. Think of the Suffragettes, or conscientious objectors, those who challenged apartheid, or those who call out the loss of democrat-ic freedoms [41] [70].



Beliefs matter



History shows that speaking out, being prepared to challenge accepted beliefs and narratives, is a vital tool in creating fresh and inspirational new solutions. Beliefs play a central role in the development of societal norms and social structures. Without sur-facing and being prepared to evaluate and challenge some of these beliefs, the possi-bility for change is constrained. We don’t change the game; we merely play it better.

Complexity thinking, through the concepts of path dependence and lock-in, provides a reminder that when beliefs and ways of doing things become institutionalised, it can become hard for alternatives to be heard and gain traction. This is in part because those who benefit from established norms tend to resist change, and in part because we just tend to take for granted that there is no alternative to the status quo.





66


Our home, the earth



Exploring the systemic interweaving of the environmental and the social and describing

how the behaviour of a rogue species can destabilise the whole.

Ooh baby it’s a wild world. Cat Stevens

And you don’t know what you’ve got ‘til it’s gone. Joni Mitchell



The earth is an interwoven system, a huge ecosystem that has been able, over mil-lennia, to find a dynamic, resilient balance. It is balanced in the sense that, whilst various aspects – ocean and air currents, temperature differentials, distribution of flora and fauna – vary, they tend not to vary too far; they are able, collectively to main-tain the earth within certain limits. The earth functions in a similar way to the human body, which, through adapting to changes in external conditions such as high or low temperatures or variations of intake of food and water, is able, within reason, to keep its temperature, salinity, heart rate and so on within bounds. This is self-regulation. It engenders resilience – staying within limits in the midst of variation in conditions, providing those variations are not too extreme. Heat or starve your body to too great an extent, and you will pass the point of no return and be no more.

We know from studying the ecology of, say, forests, that certain conditions facilitate the maintenance of such a dynamic resilient balance. These are: sufficient diversity of species, the establishment of synergistic food chains between species that are neither too fixed nor too chaotic, and an openness to the wider context [14].

The ecosystem’s balance is maintained so that the diverse members live in intricate

collaboration and competition and the complex whole of the ecology is dynamically

stable. Peter Reason22



Mature ecologies that have developed over millennia have evolved a mutual balancing. However, even in the natural world, this dynamic balancing can go awry. Self-regula-tion does not occur under all circumstances. Rabbits, say, introduced into New Zea-land, wipe out native fauna and, every few decades, go through explosive plagues of population growth. These die down as they eradicate the vegetation on which they live. But, as this causes soil erosion, many areas never recover.23

Akin to the rabbits in New Zealand, there is one species that is having huge impact on the whole global ecology and that is we Humans. We are using up resources – water, minerals, land – at a vast and accelerating rate, and in the process, creating global heat-ing. The rate of the rise of carbon emissions has slowed down a little over the last few years, but the amount of carbon in the atmosphere continues to rise.24 Carbon survives in the atmosphere for thousands of years.

290 Section VIII On the Global



[There] is a new age in the history of the Earth, which definitively began with the “great

acceleration” of the 1950s. The basis of the Anthropocene hypothesis is the concep-

tion of the Earth as a single dynamic, self-regulated complex system characterized by

physical, chemical, and biological, as well as human, components … nature and society

have become one single thing. Mauro Ceruti25



One of the sobering thoughts for us as humans is that ecological health – the ability of the planet to self-regulate – is a function of the whole system, of which our economic system is only a part. We do have the power to destabilise the whole, but we cannot control it. In the end, it is the globe that will respond systemically to all its constituent interwoven aspects – heat from the sun impacted by greenhouse gases, water, land, forests, fauna, and of course, our own behaviour. The earth will have the capacity to adapt – or not. It will have the last word, so to speak. Like any other ecological system, there can be points of no return for the earth as a whole, from which there is no easy reversal. This is a central tenet of complexity science. Complexity science explains the conditions under which self-regulation is effective – but it also emphasises that at tip-ping points, things can get irredeemably out of balance and irreversible change can take place [17].



Global tipping points

One example of a global tipping point is the way, from 2020, that cases of Covid 19 rose

exponentially in a very short space of time. Politicians were having to adjust to the fact

that if there were signs of a rise in cases, they only had days to react to try to reduce

social contact and curb the spreading. One day’s delay made a huge difference to the

rise in cases.

Global heating has the same characteristic dynamics as a pandemic, and there are sim-

ilar dynamics leading to the point at which an ocean or soil is no longer able to sustain

life, or sea ice is no longer able to reconstitute itself. Extreme weather caused by cli-

mate breakdown has led to increased deaths from intensifying heatwaves in all regions,

millions of lives and homes destroyed in droughts and floods, millions of people facing

hunger, and ‘increasingly irreversible losses’ in vital ecosystems.26



The potential for global irreversible change should not be overlooked. And, as we are seeing, our economic activity has the power to endanger global ecological self-regula-tion. If we want the earth’s ecosystem to remain in balance, we need to put the spot-light on the economy; the form of our economic processes, together with the levels of growth, need addressing if we are to avoid irreversible catastrophe.

We’re at a tipping point in the relationship we have with ourselves, with each other and

our world. A world which is so intricately connected, so interdependent, so intrinsically

linked. So delicate. The power struggles between huge organisations, economics, de-

velopment and the species we share our planet with is growing … out of control. Dara

McAnulty27





67




Run, run, run, run, runaway28



Change tends to be either fast and furious; or things can seem stuck forever.

A 2023 article in Nature explains that Antarctic sea ice has “entered a new regime”.29 The very low sea ice coverage is no longer reversible but is here to stay. As they ex-plain, this has huge knock-on effects on coastal erosion, ice shelf stability and deep ocean ventilation. Thinner ice is also less effective in blocking the sun’s rays, and so what results is positive feedback between reducing sea ice coverage and increasing ocean temperatures. The whole interconnected system finds a new, more concerning dynamic and leaves the past balance behind. There has been a so-called ‘phase change’.

In similar vein, a 2024 study of ocean currents in the north Atlantic describes the shift-ing situation in much the same language. The report explains that the Atlantic Ocean circulation is nearing a “devastating tipping point” and that the expected “collapse in the system of currents that helps regulate global climate” is likely to be at such a speed that “adaptation would be impossible”. 30

Regime shifts or phase changes occur when there are radical irreversible system-ic changes. Such changes can be sudden and catastrophic. Examples are the end of apartheid or the fall of the Berlin Wall; systems persist for a long time despite internal weakening and loss of resilience, but the final collapse often takes us by surprise. Some phase changes lead to break down, like the ocean currents; others lead to runaway change, like a pandemic. There can also be a more gradual shift towards a new set of characteristics and conditions; think of a garden – gradually, as the climate warms, dif-ferent plants start to flourish, different insects find a home and over time the garden ecosystem reaches a new balance.

Natural systems often show a surprising resilience and manage to adapt in such a way that their key patterns and relationships sustain despite changing conditions. But, as they become less robust and resilient, at some point they may break down and some-thing new may replace them. Such a change trajectory – which can seem as if nothing much changes then, all of a sudden, everything changes – is called episodic or punc-tuated change [17]. We observe in the fossil record, for example, that there are times when there is a prolific explosion of extinctions with a myriad of new species emerg-ing; then there are other periods which remain stable for millennia. This is punctuated equilibrium.31

One version of this irreversible shift into a new regime is that of runaway, nonlinear, exponential change. But one of the issues that policy makers face is that we humans are not habituated to imagine irreversible shifts or runaway change. Partly we have an implicit belief that ‘things’ naturally find balance so that environmental or indeed eco-nomic collapse are really quite unlikely [68]. Equally, our brains, shaped by the modern-ist era of mechanical thinking, tend to expect change to be incremental and reversible. 292 Section VIII On the Global



We expect that change will be like weight gain; if we eat a little bit too much, all we have to do is to eat a bit less and we will return to normal. We are conditioned to think that there is a straightforward relationship between what we do and what happens as a result. Handling the idea of time delays between input and output, or of grappling with the concept that positive feedback loops between systemic causal factors can create runaway change, seem alien to how we have been conditioned to think.

We saw this in the response to the pandemic. Politicians wanted proof before reacting, but there was no time to wait for incontrovertible evidence; locking down one day ear-lier or later had a massive impact on the rise of cases.



Implications for practice



The question I am raising is: Given catastrophic or runaway change is becoming even more common and problematic in a world that – for a variety of interconnected en-vironmental and social reasons – is experiencing weakening stability and resilience, what practices are needed to ensure we do not react too late?

Allow ‘runaway thoughts’: First, we have to allow into our worldview the idea of un-stoppable, sudden catastrophic change. In a complex world, such change is not unlike-ly, particularly when taken-for-granted social or environmental stabilities are showing signs of wear. But we prefer to push this thought away, in part due to the feelings of loss of control that it evokes. The international development community is aware of this tendency in relation to ‘calling’ a drought. If a drought is called too late, it becomes impossible to arrest the numbers of deaths; but the counter argument is that if it is called too early, it will be very costly.

Build early alert systems: Analysis ‘after the fact’ of the drought of 2012 in Northeast Africa suggested that part of the problem was that it can be hard to trust and rely on the views of people ‘on the ground’ – those who have seen it before and can predict reasonably accurately when things are dire.32 We are often uncomfortable to work with qualitative data and respond to subtle signs. We prefer to use concrete data – temperature readings and rainfall – in order to be sure. But in these fast-changing situations, while we wait to be sure by objective means, or overly rely on predictive models, it can become ‘too late’ if change is fast and exponential.

We thus need to invest in building relationships with those nearest to the issues, those who might have a more intuitive sense of change born out of experience. This includes experts who have studied disease trajectories or environmental collapse, as well as those on the ground who have a more historically shaped and embodied sense of what is happening. Setting up ways to sense the environment, and to engage with a wide range of actors and experts, is a pre-cursor to sensing into runaway change. These are ‘early warning systems’.

67 Run, run, run, run, runaway 293



Yesterday is often not a good predictor of tomorrow: Returning to climate change, it is as if the human mind does not easily accept or grasp that yesterday and today are not always good predictors of tomorrow. But, when several different indicators of chang-es to climate show substantial shifts in very short timescales, and when measures on many fronts far transcend the averages and standard deviations that we have seen be-fore, we need to take the situation seriously.

There are, of course, many reasons why facing up to signs of global heating is so dif-ficult quite apart from the problem of embracing the exponential. I’m thinking of the power of vested interests, and the short-termism of both politicians and business lead-ers alike. But the point remains, the hope that things will change a little, incrementally, and then, if things get too much, we can make a few adjustments and things will go back to normal, does still tend to lurk within conventional thinking.

Embracing the possibility, indeed, increasingly, the probability of nonlinear runaway catastrophic change, is one necessary shift in mindset that we ignore at our peril.





68


Free markets: free for whom?



Considering the justification for a free-market ideology and illustrating that complexity

theory suggests this is likely to lead to increasing inequality. Exploring the importance of de-

termining the goals of economic systems, the importance of values and the role of the state.



Complexity theory demonstrates that resilience in systems requires diversity and balance. When some aspects hold too much sway, systems can lose this balance and lose their ability to adapt to changing circumstances and mediate shocks. This is the case for ecologies, and it is equally the case for societies and economies. In this piece, I focus on resilience in the economy and ask whether the adoption of free mar-ket economics has had an impact on that resilience.



Free market economics and the ‘lock-in’ of inequality



Since the 1980s, led by the US and UK, free market ideologies have become adopted as policy by the large majority of societies. The approach is based on the argument that if markets can be as free as possible, then choice and competition will drive inno-vation and excellence. More than that, the ‘invisible hand’33 in line with the principles of ‘natural law’, will supposedly ensure that the economy self-organises into a resilient, self-balancing system. There is a belief that there is something natural about balance – and hence systems, including economic systems, will spontaneously tend to such a balance.

This natural law is felt to exercise some sort of coercive surveillance … some sort

of meliorative trend over the sequence of events and give it a spiritual stability”.

Thorstein Veblen34

Free market economics is further validated through taking the view that people tend to act in their own self-interest. A free market is thus deemed to take advantage of, and indeed encourage, such self-interested choice-making. The argument goes that if we each choose what we, selfishly and immediately, want, then the market, according-ly, can seamlessly respond. Regulation is viewed as a distorting factor on this natural process towards balance.

Neoliberal [free market] orthodoxy holds that economic freedom is the basis of every

other kind. That orthodoxy is not only false, it is devouring itself. Joseph Stiglitz35



The adoption of free market or laissez-faire36 economics sets the trend for deregulation and, in many parts of the world, for a diminution of the role of the state. All is to be left to market forces and governments are to be viewed primarily as bodies who deal with 296 Section VIII On the Global



so-called market failures – aspects of society that are better served by means other than market competition, such as education, or the maintenance of law and order.

What we learn from complexity theory, though, is that self-organising processes do not necessarily lead to resilience, even in nature. There is potential for the strong to get stronger; they lock-in their advantage and lock out any competition [14].

So, it will come as no surprise that unregulated self-organising processes in econom-ic systems do not inevitably lead to a diverse, balanced, innovative economy offering choice. In the words of Joseph Stiglitz, “there is no invisible hand”.37 If firms start to have some success and win out over their rivals, they can use their economic power and wealth to control supply chains, or artificially lower prices for a while to put small-er firms out of business; they can lobby governments and sway consumers through their advertising campaigns; they can capture the market and lock-in their advantage.

Once an outcome begins to emerge it becomes progressively more locked-in.
