Brian Arthur38



Richard Bronk in The Romantic Economist, agrees. He says that economies almost nev-er “settle down into a predictable equilibrium” because, and he quotes Brian Arthur, economies are “process dependent, organic and always evolving”.39

Where does this lead? We move towards an economy with fewer players and with less choice. We ‘inherit an economic process that polarises and accelerates economic inequality – where there is little to stop those that have power using their power to protect their patch and strive for a bigger share of the pie. This tendency for the rich to get richer and the powerful to get more powerful has not escaped the notice of various commentators, as the following quotations show.

The hegemony of uncontrolled profit (a major cause of the ecological crisis) increases

inequalities within each nation and across the entire planet. The qualities of our civili-

zation have deteriorated, and its deficiencies have increased, notably in the spread of

selfishness and the disappearance of traditional solidarities. Edgar Morin40

Within a market economy, there are no transactions between equals. Some traders en-

joy the backing of huge military machines, others have access to subsidies not enjoyed

by their competitors … competitiveness is thus less a reflection of the ‘natural advan-

tages’ enjoyed by traders as of the historical, geopolitical and organisational advantag-

es they enjoy. Colin Hines41

Capitalism claims … to allow people to act as one pleases without oppressive restric-

tions, however, the reality is quite the opposite when the majority of the population

becomes ‘locked-in’ by poverty and the systematic exploitation of their labour.42

There is also hard evidence, some listed below, of this trend towards inequality; it makes for uncomfortable reading.

68 Free markets: free for whom? 297



Has the world become more unequal?

It is interesting to note that (in 2022) the ten wealthiest people, all men, have a com-

bined net worth of $1.5 trillion.43 That is half the economy of Africa, an economy of 1.37

billion people. The ten biggest companies manage about $16 trillion44 of the world’s

wealth and just under half of the world’s wealth is held by 1% of its population.45 There

are capital concentrations in Sovereign Wealth Funds, the Chinese State, Russian oli-

garchs, South Korean chaebol, asset management firms and billionaire entrepreneurs.46

As Cliff Bowman says, “this level of capital concentration has not existed on a global

scale before”.47

There is also evidence of growing inequality between leaders and workers. For exam-

ple, in 1965, CEOs in the US earned twenty times an average worker’s salary; the figure

in 2019, was 278.48 Total CEO compensation growth (remuneration plus benefits) rose

1,007.5% from 1978 to 2018, and for workers over the same period increased by just

11.9%.49



Increasing inequality – does it matter?



It is only fair to ask whether this tendency towards growing economic inequality matters? One argument is that, if the economy keeps growing, then it can be deemed acceptable if the gap between the ‘haves’ and the ‘have-nots’ widens, as those at the bottom still have more than they used to have; they are ‘pulled up by their bootstrings’. This ‘trickle down’ argument does, however, depend on continuing growth; I explore, in [69], whether never-ending growth is either feasible or desirable.

Putting aside the way growth has, to a degree, masked increasing inequality, growing inequality still seems problematic for at least two reasons.

First, ‘the poor’ may come to feel aggrieved. They are told they are better off than they used to be, and they are told that there is equality of opportunity [65], hence it is large-ly their own fault if they are poorer, or feel poorer, than they would like. This can breed dissatisfaction and social unrest, drive migration, and is seen as a key reason for the rise in populism reported worldwide.50

With regard to social unrest, I note, for example, in the UK in 2023, that nurses went on strike over pay for the first time in thirty years. In that same year, the then UK Housing Secretary suggested that failure to solve the housing crisis would threaten democracy and could turn young people towards authoritarianism.51 If societies become too un-balanced, if too many people cannot manage economically, eventually people start to react. And history suggests this can have serious consequences, as illustrated by the following study.



298 Section VIII On the Global



History’s crisis detectives

A study by Daniel Hoyer looks back at earlier points of societal crisis and pinpoints

what led to collapse.52 Hoyer explains that “extreme inequality shows up in nearly every

case”. He continues: “When big gaps exist between the haves and have-nots, not just in

material wealth but also access to positions of power, this breeds frustration, dissent

and turmoil” and ushers in an age of discord.

Hoyer points out that one of the most surprising factors is that inequality seems to be

just as corrosive for the elites themselves. This, he says, is because the accumulation of

so much wealth and power leads to intense infighting between them, and this ripples

throughout society. Typically, wealth is related to power, as elites try to secure top po-

sitions in political office. He explains that history shows how this rivalry can lead to the

breaking of rules and social taboos in order to stay ahead of the game. And, he finds,

once the taboo of refraining from civil violence falls – as it too often does – the results

are typically devastating.

Put together, fiercely competitive elites alongside scores of poor and marginalised peo-

ple create an extremely combustible situation. This is because elites tend to capture the

lion’s share of wealth, often at the expense of both the majority of the population and of

state institutions. So just when a society most needs its leaders in government and the

civil service to step up and turn the crisis around, it finds itself at its weakest point and

is unfit for the challenge. This is one of the main reasons, Hoyer concludes, that so many

historical crises turn into major catastrophes.



Secondly, as inequality grows, the future of the planet is held increasingly in the hands of a relatively small number of wealthy individuals and institutions, whose main objec-tive is to win at the economic game. These business leaders are not tasked with being the guardians of the future, or the champions of the poor – but they now have more power over the future impact of growth and inequality and its consequences for the planet than any government or global institution.



Can we redress the balance?



If inequality is problematic, then are there ways to counter its drift and redress the bal-ance? I address two related aspects: one centres on the role of individual and societal values, and the second focuses on the role of the state.

Values



Values, some would argue, are intrinsically linked with the economy. Clearly, a sup-posed value-free laissez-faire economy places a high value on the freedom to make money. Mark Carney, the former governor of the Bank of England, highlighted, in his Reith lectures of 2020, the importance of reflecting on values in relation to the econ-omy.53 He expressed the view that Adam Smith’s moral sentiments have been replaced by market sentiments, and that societal values have become equated with financial

68 Free markets: free for whom? 299



value.54 His point is that what the economy delivers depends on what we want it to deliver. If we preference short-term profit above all else, we will reach a different out-come than if we also value long-term resilience, the husbanding of resources, fairness and social justice. As he says, “markets don’t exist in a vacuum, the market is a social construct whose effectiveness is determined partly by the rules of the state and partly by the values of society”.55

Why do financial markets rate Amazon as one of the world’s most valuable companies,

but the value of the vast region of the Amazon appears on no ledger until it’s stripped

of its foliage and converted into farmland?



And how can we reconcile our celebrations of the extraordinary values of public ser-

vice dedication and the heroism of healthcare workers with their low wages and per-

ilous working conditions? These are all issues of how we get what we value. Carney56

In the UK, after the Second World War, there tended to be a strong collective societal desire for people to pull together, transcending issues of class and education (if not, at that time, race and gender). There was acceptance that to maintain a resilient and fair society, people needed to consider each other and cooperate, to balance personal needs with the needs of the community. This was the time in the UK when the wel-fare state, the health service and free education were all established. Indeed, from the 1950s to the 1970s, working class people really were able, for the first time before or since, to create a new life for themselves and really were upwardly mobile.57

This approach is an example of what has come to be known as social capitalism. Social capitalism is structured to engage with the tensions between liberty, equality, and jus-tice. It proposes an economy that values quality of life, happiness and the natural world as well as profit and growth.58

The role of the state



Second, there is the question of role of the state.

Self-regulating markets never work; their deficiencies, not only in their internal work-

ings but also in their consequences (e.g., for the poor) are so great that government

becomes necessary. Stiglitz 59

The state to shape economic priorities: Mariana Mazzucato, an economist current-ly having great impact on government policy worldwide, advocates “stronger more empowered states”.60 Governments are not just there, she says, to tackle market failures, but should be setting broad goals and articulating the key priorities for our time – goals, she suggests, such as tackling climate change, or inequality, or the chal-lenges of an ageing population. Setting such systemic long-term strategic “missions”61 as she calls them, allows public and private sector to work together to achieve them. This both calls out and shapes the direction for investment and innovation, enabling “bottom-up experimentation and learning”. She believes these missions are often first 300 Section VIII On the Global



identified and lobbied for by civil society and then are picked up by political leadership. She is clear that these missions “should focus on creating system-wide transformation across sectors”.

Mazzucato gives an example of Germany’s Energiewende policy, initiated by bottom-up green movements, which culminated, she says, in political understanding and eventu-ally in leadership from above. She explains that Germany’s development of green steel, using its state development bank KfW, made loans conditional on the introduction of environmentally friendly technologies.



An example of missions in action: Wales Future Generations Commissioner

In 2016, Wales established a Future Generations Commissioner whose office is setting

a number of future missions.62 For example, its manifesto states that: “We will make it

our mission to help transition Wales to an economy that puts people and planet first. As

a result, governments at all levels, communities and businesses are making this happen.”

It is establishing similar mission statements for climate and wellbeing, sharing stories of

good practice, and convening and funding initiatives that bring together public, private

and not-for-profits.



The state to protect public goods: Another question in relation to the role of the state regards the protection of the public commons. Guy Standing, in The Blue Commons, describes how common land and oceans used to be regarded as a shared heritage, a resource belonging to everyone. Governments used to steward and defend such re-sources, for now and for the future. These days, he says, the commons are treated as the property of governments to be exploited and ‘plundered’. 63 A notable exception is the way the Norwegian government has treated income from their oil fields; they invested it in a sovereign wealth fund to be used to fund Norway’s social costs.64

Governments should also, arguably, resist the temptation to sell off public goods for ‘ready cash’. For example, in the UK in 1989, water companies were privatised. Was the way this was undertaken in the best interests of the public, given that the companies manage and control public goods for private profit? How well do we hold these and other companies to account for polluting rivers and seas? The dumping of raw sewage is becoming a huge issue for the UK, one which water companies seem unable to re-solve, given their shareholders are starting to abandon them as their profits diminish.65



Resilience and the middle ground …



Is it not for the government to ensure that there are effective governance frameworks to mediate between the drives of the commercial sector and the interests of the peo-ple? Commercial organisations indeed often argue for rules that create a level playing field, such as a carbon tax or a minimum wage.66 Ha Joon Chang explains, in 23 Things They Don’t Tell You about Capitalism, that even in largely deregulated economies, there are always exist some regulations, laws and practices.67 The trick, ideally, is to ensure

68 Free markets: free for whom? 301



that these act to support economic balance and do not just ease the path of the win-ners. These may include, he suggests, anti-monopoly legislation, a focus on human and workers’ rights, the right to protest and the right to a fair trial.

What makes for societal and economic resilience with the long-term future of the plan-et in mind, tend to be middle ground solutions, with not too much power in the hands of the state and, equally, not too much power in the hands of the private sector. Equally, there needs to be a balance between valuing individual freedom and emphasising the needs of the whole community. Mature ecologies are resilient if they are able to main-tain diversity and maintain dynamic balance. If they become too unbalanced, they can-not adapt. In similar vein, societies that become too unequal are hard to change organ-ically as power is locked-in either by the state, or by the wealthy. And, of course, there are many examples where political leaders become extremely rich as well as politically powerful. Resilient economies encourage innovation and enterprise, thus maintaining a balance between the small, medium and large and the dynamic interplay between them.68 There is also a need for defending independent voices. Resilient societies pro-tect the right to speak out and preserve the freedoms of mediating bodies such as the judiciary and the press. I explore this further in ‘Who got the power’[70].





69


Does the economy have to be predicated on growth?



Questioning the possibility of unfettered economic growth and exploring other options.

Our current global economy is structured around growth and depends on growth to function. Governments rely on growing taxes to fund welfare. Firms need to demon-strate growth in profits and increased market share to attract and retain investors. Many corporations prioritise the short-term interests of shareholders in order to maintain their loyalty and attract new investors.

There are companies who do recognise and address their adverse impact on society and the planet. The rise of interest in the UN sustainable development goals, and cor-porate success in managing impact, show there is some progress, albeit slow and in-consistent.69 But growth is rarely questioned as the ultimate target to ensure success. Does it have to be this way? Is growth both necessary and inevitable? The intense drive for growth is predicated on the particular form of capitalism that has evolved in a par-ticular fashion, and then been embedded and instituted through custom and practice, and the law. But its form, and the rules that structure the economy, are not set in stone, although it can feel that way. Usury, for example, used to be illegal and now is a central feature of our economics processes.70 The rules that economics processes follow are not incontestable ‘laws of nature’; the economy could, and, in some places, does operate in different ways. It is hard, but not impossible, to challenge and reframe.

This is a topic that remains difficult to raise. Economic growth has, without doubt, im-proved the living standards of hundreds of millions. For example, over the past forty years, the number of people in China with incomes below $1.90 per day – the Inter-national Poverty Line as defined by the World Bank to track global extreme poverty – has fallen by close to 800 million.71 We wealthy Westerners hardly have the right to inform the developing world that their citizens cannot have the ways of life we have engineered for ourselves although, as I will argue, growth does not have to be the only way out of poverty. Growth, too, is partly driven by increases in population, which, globally, has more than doubled in the last fifty years.72 And with the current system of investment and taxation, fear of stagnation engenders much cause for concern.

Here’s the hard truth. We are living in the twilight of this age [of exponential growth]

Matthew Syed73



The problem lies, however, in whether the planet can continue to provide the resourc-es that are needed to sustain unfettered growth. Technology enthusiasts believe that technological advances will stave off the point at which resources run out, as we find alternative and less resource-intensive ways of doing things. The expected reduction in the global population will have a positive impact too and surely, at some stage, we will decarbonise?

304 Section VIII On the Global



But … the scientist in me is convinced that, in the end, be it in decades or longer, we will eventually run out of land, minerals and water. There is no infinite source of supply. Coupled with the distinct possibility, indeed the likelihood, of runaway global heating, the availability of these resources will further reduce as land becomes unusable for farming, as forests die, and as the ocean becomes too warm or too saline to support marine life. Global heating is also likely to accelerate migration — as regions are sub-merged under water or become too hot or too dry for habitation. Climate change is also likely to lead to iniquitous ways of gaining control over ever-scarcer resources. For example, Ukraine is regarded as a mineral superpower. It has the second largest gas reserves in Europe, the Donbas region boasts one of the largest coal deposits in the world, and Ukraine has 117 of the 120 most widely used metals and minerals.74 Is the current invasion of Ukraine by Russia driven primarily – or at least in part – by a desire to control these resources?



Are there ways to limit growth?



The issue of Limits to Growth was posed by Donella Meadows and others in 1972.75 Can we limit growth, and indeed does it make sense to consider degrowth? A recent article in Nature, Degrowth can Work,76 explores how this could function. The article suggests, for example, that governments should find more creative ways to fund them-selves than primarily through taxes on profits and income. They advocate that govern-ments stop subsidies for fossil fuel extraction and instead tax ecologically damaging industries. This is in line with Mazzucato’s suggestions that governments should take a stronger lead in creating a future vision and in setting the parameters in which the private sector operates [68]. This is of course complexified when political leaders are entwined in various ways with the business world.

Kate Raworth in Doughnut Economics has developed an elegant way to think about lim-its to growth.77 She starts with the argument, in line with Carney [68], that the econ-omy’s purpose should be to promote human prosperity and happiness. It should pro-vide everyone with the material resources to pursue their highest dreams – but not through irreversibly depleting the planet’s resources. Raworth likens the economy to a doughnut. The inner circle represents the zone of deprivation, and the outer circle represents the limits of economic growth as set by the planet’s natural resources. We have to remain in the dough of the doughnut!



‘Sweating’ natural resources through intensive farming?

One way that technology can extend the limits of resource usage is intensive farming.

But are there consequences? Philip Lymbery78 tells the story of going for a walk in rural

southeast England; he watched a huge field being ploughed and noticed, as did the

Walrus and the Carpenter, in Lewis Carroll’s poem, that ‘no birds were flying overhead’.

Lymbery realised this was because the soil was inert – there were no worms to attract

69 Does the economy have to be predicated on growth? 305



them. He explained that factory farms rearing thousands of animals require huge

factory farming of grains to feed them. These grains tend to be grown as monocul-

tures, and this depletes the soil and relies on enormous quantities of fertiliser. Such

an approach is efficient in the short-term but has concerning long-term implications.

Lymbery advocates crop and animal rotation to naturally enrich the soil.

This tendency to create efficiencies of scale for single crops impacts soil health and of-

ten also impacts livelihoods for local farmers, particularly in regions of the world where

much land is held as commons and then can be sold by governments to foreign corpora-

tions.79 Furthermore, the concentrating of crops in particular regions of the world, such

as wheat in Ukraine, can also lead to issues of food security as well as the weaponizing

of food and other crops in times of war. So, the interconnections between economic

gain, unscrupulous political practices at the expense of powerless citizens, environ-

mental degradation, questionable animal-rearing practices, and the weaponizing of

food availability globally, proffers yet another example of the need to consider issues

systemically and not let one factor – in this case, cheap meat – run out of control.



Examples of economies that are less dependent on growth



Are there alternative economic models? The German Mittelstand seems to provide one type of solution, an example of social capitalism in action. The Mittelstand, made up primarily of privately-owned medium-sized firms, and employing sixty percent of the workforce, is often described as the heart of the German economy, accounting for the bulk of economic output.80 The German economy thus is less driven than in the US and the UK by the stock market, with its need for short-term profits to satisfy share-holders. Private ownership allows for longer timescales (not needing to show profits to shareholders every quarter), allows firms to bed into their communities, care for their workers, invest in education for the next generation of workers, and protect the local environment. The Mittelstand has engendered a more systemic approach that balances economic success with social and environmental needs. It has held its place against economics giants. And it has proven itself to be more resilient to crisis.81

Another approach that is gaining traction is the idea of a circular economy.82 A circu-lar economy is a model of resource production and consumption that involves sharing, leasing, reusing, repairing, refurbishing, and recycling existing materials and products for as long as possible. There may be local ‘share shops’ where items not often used – such as a chainsaw or a carpet cleaner – can be donated and then borrowed. There may be community fridges where local shops and restaurants can donate food that would otherwise be thrown away; there may be clothes swaps, plant and seed swaps, repair shops, and lessons in remaking clothes. Often there is a focus on buying locally made goods, using local renewable sources of energy, and sourcing raw materials in the vicinity as much as possible. The philosophy of the circular economy also applies to industrial processes as exemplified by demonstrator projects in the Tees Valley.83 It was announced yesterday (as I write this in Spring 2024) that the UK is to establish the 306 Section VIII On the Global



world’s first UN-backed centre for circular economy research.84 I am not suggesting an economy can be entirely local, but a move in that direction can both reduce growth and limit carbon emissions.

One reason why even socially-minded governments are so concerned to even raise the issue of limiting growth is that the funding of public services would need, in large part, to come through taxes. Taxing those of middle income and higher is without doubt an option that needs further discussion, particularly in the light of increasing inequality. Today’s middle classes are often very comfortable indeed. In principle, poverty can be reduced by tackling inequality as much as by relying on growth. Easy to say and hard to implement, given locked-in interests, but surely this is a topic that should be on the table?

In terms of approaches to change, one promising aspect of circular economy mod-els is that they are appearing within and alongside business as usual. As discussed in [57], change can sometimes be triggered by new approaches emerging from within the current situation. Furthermore, in our social history, we have a resonance with this circular approach through the norms in the time of our (great) grandparents and forebears. It used to be customary to ‘borrow a cup of sugar’, so to speak, as well as to make and repair clothes, grow and swop food, and barter for various goods and ser-vices. Re-purposing approaches such as this – approaches which used to be common practice – can instil us with the confidence that they can work, and thus makes them easier to adopt.

The real difficulty in limiting growth is to find ways to challenge those with current economic power, particularly when many also have political power. But if and when we start to reach the limits of the planet to fund our habits, if we get close to environmen-tal collapse, the potential for major global social unrest becomes a distinct possibility. Jared Diamond in Collapse85 tells many stories of what happens to deeply unequal so-cieties when the population starts to starve, and when they no longer have anything to lose. William Dalrymple and Anita Anand, in their podcast Empire, give several instanc-es of the rise and fall of empires – some of which held huge sway over large regions for decades, if not centuries, and are now virtually forgotten.86 The issue we face today is that the whole earth is so interconnected that it can almost be viewed as a single ‘em-pire’ in the way it functions.

Doesn’t this global weaving of the social, economic and environment, and the implica-tions of widening inequality in the face of diminishing resources, require greater atten-tion? We have to take seriously the possibility of global collapse and, surely, we have to challenge the inevitability of unfettered growth.





70


Who got the power? I got the power87



Various political writers describe the world in terms very recognisable to the complexity

thinker, and proffer both an analysis of how democratic freedoms erode and suggestions as

to how to stop this before ‘power in the hands of the few’ becomes too locked-in.



A focus on the dynamics of power



As a global citizen sporting a complexity hat, I am concerned to understand what con-tributes to the coalescing of political and other forms of power into the hands of the few. This is driven through the accumulation of wealth, and also through the dynamics of political processes. Indeed, the two are often closely interlinked. In the following, I explore:

Is this move to power inevitable? What are the steps towards power-grabbing?

How can we prevent it from happening? What can we do when power has become

entrenched?

Pursuing these questions has led me to uncover a number of very interesting political writers and I build in particular on their work.

Political writers building on lived experience



One such political writer is Maria Ressa; her book How to Stand up to a Dictator is well worth reading.88 Ressa, a Filipina educated in America who returned to her country of origin, set out to challenge political corruption. I find it intriguing that her insights fit so well with those of process complexity. What also attracts me to her writing, and that of other political writers I will mention, is that these are the deliberations of thought-ful people seeking to lead impactful lives. These are not theoretical discourses. They are contemplating their ‘lived experience’, and their reflections tend to be nuanced and provide a subtlety of perspective that really illuminates how to navigate this complex world. I like the fact that they sometimes disentangle complex issues in ways that are fresh to me, and hence lead me into a whole new set of considerations.

I also came across Turkish journalist Ece Temelkuran’s How to Lose a Country: Seven Steps from Democracy to Dictatorship,89 Chantal Mouffe’s works on populism,90 and The Power of the Powerless by Vaclev Havel.91 Havel became Czechoslovakia’s President af-ter the Velvet Revolution and this book gives his take on how small acts of resistance, and giving sway to ‘being authentic’, can make a difference.

308 Section VIII On the Global



Path dependence and lock-in



As always, I am interested in the links with process complexity, and I suggest that the core idea that underpins these expositions on power is path dependence [15]. The future is co-created by the actions that we, collectively, take. These actions are influ-enced by our interactions, by how we engage with and impact each other. And our in-teractions are both influenced by, and give shape to, the cultural milieu in which we find ourselves. By cultural milieu, I mean the norms and beliefs of the societies, fam-ilies and institutions of which we are a part. These norms and ways of ‘thinking and being’ have developed over time and tend to become relatively stable. We may re-gard them as so self-evidently ‘normal’ or ‘obvious’ that we are hardly aware of them. These cultural norms, however, shape to what we, and our society, pay attention; they shape our choices, decisions and actions. As with process complexity, the idea of the path is central to Daoism; in Buddhism, path dependence is an expression of collective Karma92 – collectively, we reap what we sow [15].

These political writers proffer a common warning: If we let things pass – things of which we don’t really approve – it becomes increasingly hard, and even dangerous, to oppose them at a later stage. They are talking about the steps on the path towards the growth and consolidation of power. Hannah Arendt, for example, coined the phrase “the ba-nality of evil” to describe the most extreme failures of personal moral awareness.93 As Sarah Bakewell explains in At the Existentialist Café: If you do not respond when times demand it, if you show a lack of imagination and attention, these behaviours become as dangerous as “deliberately committing an abuse”.94

This path-dependent consolidation of power leads us to the related concept, in com-plexity language, of lock-in. There comes a point, a so-called tipping point, beyond which the powerful can invincibly consolidate their power through terror, or through changing laws and institutions, or stuffing the system with cronies [17]. Their power becomes increasingly impossible to challenge and overturn. This can occur in democ-racies as well as in more expressly autocratic regimes, and the warning is that dem-ocratic processes can gradually and imperceptibly and – ultimately irreversibly – be eroded.

The lure of power



Why do people want this sort of political power? Political power seekers may merely enjoy the feeling of power and become addicted to their ability to sway the masses; they may have no obvious ideology or intention and may indeed shift course and be prepared to do or say whatever it takes to remain in power, just for the thrill that they can. Yet others may have strong beliefs or intentions – to purify the race, to make their empire great again, or to oppose the ‘infidels’. The authors I describe point to the view that all beliefs and ideologies, when they become singular and oppositional, when they propose ‘there is only one right way’ with little nuance or attention to alternatives, can lead even well-intentioned leaders to become ‘economical with the truth’ and take actions not congruent even with their own espoused values. This elision of the truth

70 Who got the power? I got the power 309



is justified as a way to create the conditions whereby the outcome they believe to be in the public good can be realised. The desired ‘end’ is used to justify the sometimes shady and questionable ‘means’ they are prepared to take. Complexity theory warns that, whilst the outcomes may not be achieved, the ‘means taken’ will impart an inevi-table signature on that which unfolds [15].

The end cannot justify the means, for the simple and obvious reason that the means

employed determine the nature of the ends produced. Aldous Huxley



I want to focus on two key questions addressed in the works I have mentioned:

What leads to the concentration of power in the hands of the few; and what

do you do about it?



How does power become concentrated in the hands of the few?



Here is a checklist of what you would do well to do if you want to capture political power.

(a) Control the truth



Ressa and Temelkuran describe the ascendence of the so-called post-truth era. Te-melkuran exemplifies this with a quote from the New York Times, in which an aide of George W. Bush, was mocking those in “what we call a reality-based community … people who believe that solutions emerge from the judicious study of discernible real-ity”. He went on: “That’s not the way it works any more … we create our own reality.”95 This was back in 2004; now, in 2024, talk of post-truth and fake news is in common parlance, and, as Temelkuran says, many would argue these days that it is naïve even to expect politicians or leaders to tell the truth when a lie or obfuscation will be more effective.

Studies show that lies laced with anger and hate spread faster and farther than facts.

Ressa96



Ressa is exercised by this same issue, and she explores in particular how social me-dia has amplified this slide into disinformation and fake news. She says that, earlier on, social media used to do the opposite of this. For example, social media, around the time of the Arab Spring and Occupy movements in 2011, helped to surface untruths promoted by power seekers; it also allowed challenge, the pooling of group knowledge and provided platforms for connecting people together. Ressa herself set up Rappler in 2012 – an online news site in the Philippines, which invites citizens to provide sto-ries, pictures and information that help to combat any official line. Rappler continues to work to expose corruption and disinformation and Ressa received the Nobel Peace Prize in 2021.

310 Section VIII On the Global



I saw then [in 2011] how powerful participatory media can be: how citizens using their

cell phones are enfranchised to demand justice and accountability. It showed me how

technology could be used for good: for citizen empowerment, voting and democratic

engagement, and integrity and truth. Ressa97

Ressa is particularly concerned about the automated processes used in social media; these can achieve massive influence by attacking those ‘speaking truth to power’ and can spread slurs and disinformation. Corrupt politicians are able to make enormous use of such processes. She points to the way algorithms aimed at increasing traffic on social media find that incendiary posts which attack or are extreme are the most pop-ular. Social media companies are thus not incentivised to remove such posts, or ban their perpetrators, as such posts bring in traffic and hence profit. But these algorithms, through feeding us what we want to hear, can increase polarisation of perspectives and work against tolerance, pluralism and the handling of the real paradox of living in a complex world. Maria Ressa does not mince her words. She says:

Those very same developments that I welcomed in 2011 would soon be fine-tuned by

the platforms’ business models, co-opted by state power, and turned against the peo-

ple, fuelling the rise of digital authoritarians, the death of facts, and the insidious mass

manipulation we live with today. We are conditioned to prefer sensationalism over

objectivity.98



That is not to suggest that there is Certain Truth [5]. But equally, that is not to say that there are not untruths, deliberately spread to achieve particular outcomes. Some types of ‘facts’ do seem pretty certain. For example, aerial photographs can reliably show the reduction in the size of the Amazon rainforest, and we have reasonable certainty about the numbers of deaths due to disease, or levels of population growth, or changes in corporate profits. Searching for facts and aiming to fact-check may still not lead to Truth but it may expose untruths and trace ways in which untruths have been gener-ated and spread.

(b) Rewrite the past



Ressa describes how actions in the present can work backwards to reshape old memo-ries and perspectives; we can rewrite the past. Ressa explains how she became alerted to this idea through an essay by T. S. Eliot. In this, Eliot describes how “the way you read William Shakespeare is affected by the last novel you read, and the last novel you read is affected by the fact that you read Shakespeare”.99 What it made Ressa understand is that memory of the past can be distorted by new influences – influences which may indeed be designed to mislead. Disinformation can rehabilitate the track record of ear-lier leaders and bring back to popularity those with a sometimes-questionable public record;100 it can be used as a form of collective and intentional gaslighting.

70 Who got the power? I got the power 311



I realise that the past is re-examined regularly, as we explore the narratives around, for example, colonialism, where history was in the main written by the victors; deciding what is ‘true’ is not straightforward. But that is not to ignore the deliberate burying or misrepresenting of past events with the sole intention of gaining power.

(c) Create an ideology



Havel, in The Power of the Powerless, reflected on what led to the success of Soviet-led communism. His thoughts, though, are relevant to the role of any entrenched ideology. He explains that, as power coalesces around them, ideologies start to be treated as un-assailable truth. He argues that this is problematic when it becomes impossible to take an opposing view, or to challenge or even discuss it. If you want to join the ‘winners’, you may need to act as if you swallow whole the requisite ideology.

This intention of the state to dominate and shape the culture of a society – the beliefs, perceptions, values and mores – so that the required worldview becomes the accepted cultural norm, is known as cultural hegemony.101 People accept current norms as natu-ral and cannot imagine an alternative.

By not confronting systems built on a questionable ideology, Havel says people “live within a lie”. “But”, he continues, “they need not [even] accept the lie. It is enough for them to have accepted their life with it and in it. For, by this very fact, individuals con-firm the system, fulfil the system, make the system, are the system”.102

It is one thing to recognise the power of ideology in the starkness of a totalitarian re-gime, but Mouffe and Temelkuran further suggest that this argument applies to the adoption of any ideology. Belief in the free market, or in individualism or growth, can equally become problematic if it becomes politically inexpedient and impossible to challenge them, in order to remain ‘within the camp’. In this way, Havel says, ideology “has a natural tendency to disengage itself from reality, to create a world of appearanc-es, to become ritual”.103

(d) Let them laugh at the horror



In her book How to Lose a Country: The Seven Steps from Democracy to Dictatorship, Te-melkuran, basing her themes on observations of what has happened in modern-day Turkey, identifies “let them laugh at the horror” as a technique to concentrate power.104 Early on, as she remarks, people are prone not to take seriously rather extreme lead-ers who are setting out seemingly ridiculous agendas. People – other politicians and citizens – laugh at them. She makes the interesting point that laughing at someone can make you feel that you are taking action against them. But, she reminds us, you are not. Those hungry for power may be inured to mockery or even embrace it for what it gives them – publicity and a sort of disguise.

312 Section VIII On the Global



(e) Enfeeble the processes of ‘checks and balances’



Another step in Temelkuran’s list focuses on how, when power is gained, those seeking to cement their leadership will work to reduce the institutional powers of bodies such as the judiciary or the civil service – bodies which serve as ‘checks and balances’ in relation to the executive. Power seekers will endeavour to promote cronies and those of like mind into positions of power. They may indeed seek to change laws in order to criminalise active dissent or relax opposition to monopolies in press ownership; they may make themselves immune from the law. We may experience a weakening of the legal challenges to politicians when they accept gifts or advantageous loans, or a lessening of consequences if they are exposed for telling untruths or breaking rules of conduct.

The emergence of an ever-more-powerful Executive can, Havel argues, ultimately take on a life of its own. Leaders may become “the blind executors of the system’s own inter-nal laws”. Furthermore, the system is likely to become “far more powerful than the will of any individual” and hence, when instituted, hard to shift.105 Havel continues: If some-one does possess a more independent will, he will need to “conceal it behind a ritually anonymous mask in order to have an opportunity to enter the power hierarchy at all”.

(f) Establish a Faustian pact between the powerful and powerless



Another perspective that has really stayed with me since I came across it a long time ago, comes from When Society becomes an Addict by Anne Wilson Schaef, published in 1987.106 Schaef starts with an exploration of the mechanical worldview [3]. As she says, treating the world as if it works like a machine suggests that we can understand it using reason and logic alone. And she argues that if things are objective and certain, with clear links between cause and effect, then we can analyse and consequently control this machine. Hence, it makes sense to have people in charge of this machine, people who know ‘best’ – because there is a best to know, and because machines are run effi-ciently by hierarchies in which someone is clearly in charge.

Paradoxically, it is not this machine perspective, but the dynamics of systemic, complex societies that create the next step in her argument – which is that when people get to be in charge, they are able to lock-in their power. In other words, the machine world-view predisposes us to accept hierarchy and the necessity for power at the top; and the complexity worldview advises how to lock it in.

Schaef goes on to say that to create stability for those in power, it is not enough to wield power. It requires others to accept that ‘power over’ is necessary and normal; ‘the peo-ple’ have to accept and expect the inevitability of their own powerlessness. Further-more, she argues, we, the people, can become addicted to powerlessness. Part of the process, she says, is to medicate the powerless through consumerism (you are being a good citizen if you consume) and through growth (actually, ‘they’ are doing a good job, evidenced by the fact that ‘we’ are getting richer). Helplessness is also medicating: ‘I can do nothing, I am just a tiny cog in this machine, so I will enjoy myself, get on with my

70 Who got the power? I got the power 313



life and leave it up to those at the top who know best.’ In these ways, we come to regard a division between the powerful and the powerless as normal; and indeed, we don’t really feel powerless as wars, climate change, poverty, pollution are ‘their’ problem, not ours. In Western democracies, we are told that we are ‘free’ individuals, with equality of opportunity, free to choose what we want, free to succeed [65]. All we have to do, we are told, is to keep our heads down and leave important matters to those in charge.

Schaef suggests that the powerful and the powerless are in a Faustian pact whereby both get what they want. The entitled ones that have taken power are allowed to do what it takes to retain that power in order that the powerless can remain innocent and avoid responsibility. If such leaders can manoeuvre themselves into the role of the protective Father, then their behaviour is often not challenged.

Authority can give us the freedom to be our worst selves. Ressa107

Each person [is capable of] succumbing to a profane trivialisation of his or her inherent

humanity … In everyone there is some willingness to merge with the anonymous crowd

and to flow comfortably along with it down the river of pseudo-life. Havel108



Fiona Hill, in her 2022 Reith lecture, also discusses the conditions that lead to a rise of authoritarian rule. People, she says, feel loss, grievance and anxiety when they sense they are losing out or getting left behind. They experience fear. Populism derives from authoritarianism, when the seemingly protective Father is building on these feelings of fear; he promises to provide security and safety as well as prosperity – as long as people acquiesce to his ultimate authority.109

(g) Appeal to technocracy



One of the ways the power machine can be held in place, according to Mouffe, is to adopt a technocratic approach to politics. Mouffe explains this approach was exempli-fied by Tony Blair, the UK Prime Minister in the 1990s. The proposition, again in line with the machine view of society, suggests that there are solutions to most of the prob-lems that governments face. If governments focus on finding these solutions and im-plementing them, then, the reasoning goes, we can all rise above party politics. Blair said: “The choice is not between a left-wing economic policy and a right-wing economic policy, but between a good economic policy and a bad one.”110 Havel covered similar ground and pointed out that “political parties run by professional apparatuses [re-lease] the citizen from all forms of concrete and personal responsibility”.111

The point is not that analysis, logic, data and expertise do not have their place. Rather it is to say that if we believe there is a ‘right answer’, we have yet another reason to leave things in the hands of those at the top, those who we can believe know more than we do, to the extent that we can become lulled into not even questioning their logic or ev-idence or intention. Blair, or any politician, may indeed have alighted on a ‘good’ strat-egy – but we, as citizens, need to continue to engage and make our own assessments, and not just assume the clever guys at the top have got it right.

314 Section VIII On the Global



Mouffe, in contrast, points out that the real issue does not reside in defining the solu-tion, but in the impossibility of agreeing on the problem. As she says, the agendas on the left in particular are much more diverse than was the case in the ‘old days’, when left/right ideologies were rooted in economic power. Now, she argues, there are many issues – climate change, gender, race – not only jobs, wealth and economic inequality; issues are prioritised and held differently by differing groups. As she says, there is no “rational answer to the different problems of society”.112

Political questions are not merely technical issues to be solved by experts; they always

involve decisions which require us to make a choice between conflicting alternatives.

Mouffe113

This is not an attack on ‘experts’ but a recognition that many factors combine, and land-ing on a solution, or indeed agreeing on the problem, is not trivial or obvious.

First, not everyone agrees on priorities: Should we focus on growth or climate change; should we prioritise ageism or racism? Secondly, values and ideologies play a part; there are disparate views as to what would be acceptable mechanisms to achieve out-comes, even if desired outcomes could be defined. Thirdly, these complexly interwo-ven social-economic-environmental problems are non-trivial and are unlikely to have clear pathways and unambiguous solutions. Political processes should, of course, work with evidence and expertise – but in the knowledge that the balance of priorities and the complex interweaving of the social with the environmental, the short-term with the long-term, means there is no simple, unique solution.



What do you do to stop power growing or catalyse its collapse?



I’ve described, based on the work of various political writers, how we become en-trenched in situations of locked-in power. I recommend their books to you, and I think their insights have an applicability for contexts wider than the political sphere, as I will discuss later.

The next question is, what can be done about it? I have been really intrigued as to how these writers advise both how to arrest the emerging of behaviour when it becomes increasingly, inexorably dictator-like, but also how to reverse situations where public freedoms are starting to disappear.

(a) Challenge early



The first suggestion is to challenge early. It must be very tempting, as a politician, to back a leader who is popular and adept at charming the public, even when you neither agree with his or her views nor see him or her as acting with integrity. The reason-ing goes that you, the politician, cannot challenge everything and, even if the leader’s methods are a bit askew, at least she is popular. Backing her might enable you yourself to be re-elected in the future. And what use will you be with no seat? So, you keep quiet

70 Who got the power? I got the power 315



and go along with things. But what both Ressa and Temelkuran are pointing out is that this acquiescence to questionable methods and behaviours is the path through which a ‘do whatever you need to succeed’ culture emerges and becomes normalised. People start to say, ‘well all politicians lie, they always have’. It can even come to seem that act-ing honestly and with integrity is weak and naïve, rather than laudable and important. Ressa calls this “the death of democracy by a thousand cuts”.114 By the time we notice, it is too late.

(b) Set your personal moral compass and ‘draw your line’



The political writers discussed here display their interest in a world that is compas-sionate, equal and sustainable. They express the view that, in order to get there, we each need to reflect on our personal moral stance. Ressa talks about “drawing the line”.115 Do we consider the basis of our own moral stance? Do we reflect on the cir-cumstances in which we would stand up for fairness or against corruption? How do we balance the importance of engendering “smooth interpersonal relationships” against calling out misconduct? 116 To what extent would we compromise if we felt intimidated and feared for our own safety? To what extent do we tend to adopt unchallenged the social mores of our culture? Pondering such issues can help us to draw our own ‘line’.

Ressa explains that deciding where to draw the line has been a core underpinning of her stance against corruption and the misuse of power. Furthermore, as well as being effective, she feels that maintaining her personal moral integrity has been life affirm-ing and bestowed meaning to her life.

I learned that drawing the line, calling out unfairness and being honest … often means

moving life forward, bringing something new into fruition.117

Meaning is not something you stumble across or what someone gives you; you build it

through every choice you make, the commitments you choose, the people you love, and

the values you hold dear.118



The importance of taking care of ‘every step you take’119 is intrinsic to path dependence [15]. In one sense, path dependence is neutral; it does not of itself take a position on whether we should be moral or otherwise, but merely demonstrates that, collectively, what we believe and how that shapes what we do, is how the future is created. ‘But’, Ressa is saying, ‘if we want a reasonable crack at a future that is sustainable, fair and just, then it is the ‘moral means’ more than the ‘moral ends’ that matter’.120

Why bother having a law if you are not going to stick to it? If a democracy is to survive ...

the insidiousness of corruption, does that mean that lines have to be drawn every time,

that every cut against the truth has to be resisted? Ressa121



316 Section VIII On the Global



(c) Be authentic



The importance of being authentic is emphasised in particular by Havel. Havel points out that when regimes are founded on the suppression of dissent, or on the disallowing of a diversity of perspective, such regimes are in fact vulnerable, as they rely on power and terror to suppress opinion and hide or distort the actuality of what is happening. Such regimes are, says Rupert Read, based on lies,122 whereas, as Havel emphasises, “immense power … comes from telling the truth”.123

[They] may be struck at any moment … by the force of truth … it is a bacteriological

weapon. Havel124



Havel further suggests that when there is only a single accepted narrative or ideology, then, paradoxically, we citizens may look more widely and deeply at alternatives, or we may address more fundamental questions than we would have done if the political discourse were more complex and less coherent.

Havel’s key point is that many people, in the end, feel at odds with themselves if their authenticity is compromised. For instance, they may have to pretend that all is well when it is not, or feel it is dangerous to voice their views or express themselves through their actions and choices. Even when citizens cannot entirely point to the reason for their malaise, even when they are habituated to the dominant way of seeing things and its consequences, something does not feel right. As Havel says, their “authentic existence” is violated.125

Havel noticed that, as dissent grew in communist Czechoslovakia, individuals, in small ways, found they could no longer bear not to be themselves. He speaks of this phase as pre-political and describes how some focused on “self-education” and others on “free creative expression”.126 As he says: “There may be writers who [start to] write as they wish”, others who “practice independent scholarship” or “organise private discussions”. Dissenters may include “clergymen who try to carry on a free religious life” as well as “painters, musicians and singers”.127 “Young people”, he said, wanted no more than “to play the music they enjoyed, to live freely in dignity.”128

Iran has a long history of repressing dissent. But a movement [referring to the recent

surge of young women who want to be free to express themselves] that has no stated

goals and no leadership, but deep cultural roots, may be hard to control.129

Havel described how these almost unconscious but determined attempts at authentic-ity, which took place just out of view and were not mobilised or coordinated, provided the pre-political milieu for more deliberate resistance. He felt that “attempts at polit-ical reform were not the cause of society’s reawakening but rather the final outcome of that reawakening”.130 This diverse, personal expression, driven by the necessity of living one’s personal truth, and by a need to “regain control over one’s own sense of responsibility”, Havel saw as a moral act.131 Through being yourself, you “be the change you want to see in the world”.132

70 Who got the power? I got the power 317



(d) Encourage agonistic dissent



I now return to Chantal Mouffe and the concept of agonistic dissent. Mouffe’s critique is not aimed at those living under dictatorships but at the ideology of modern liber-alism. The core of her concern is that modern liberalism tries to stifle difference and antagonism in its struggle for consensus. Liberalism, she feels, does not regard antago-nism as a fundamental aspect of being human, as “constitutive of human societies”.133 Evidence suggests, as discussed in [65], that humans embody both a tendency towards competition and antagonism and a tendency towards compassion. Mouffe however is not suggesting we set out to create antagonistic political processes that are polaris-ing and breed conflict; these would work against robust but constructive debate. She leads us towards the notion of agonism – a way to surface and live with difference with-out it either going underground (and stifling debate, making it hard to challenge the status quo, and even harder for new social and political structures to emerge) or lead-ing to violence. She asserts that “acknowledging the ineradicability of the conflictual dimension in social life, far from undermining the democratic project, is the necessary condition to which democratic politics is confronted”.134 Far from seeing ‘the political’ as a space of power, conflict and antagonism, she says that liberal theorists, for exam-ple Hannah Arendt, envisage the political as a space of freedom and public delibera-tion, as a place where we can allow a multiplicity of perspectives and ‘structure in’ the opportunity to disagree and dialogue.



So, where does this take us?



A pause for breath: This has been one of the longest pieces in the book and has pulled together material from a wide range of sources and covered a lot of ground.

Exploring the writings of those who have experienced the impact of loss of democratic freedoms and can warn of the stealth with which such freedoms are eroded, highlights the threats and the opportunities we face. These writers describe the world in ways I recognise from complexity thinking. They emphasise path dependency and the lock-in of power; they clarify the conditions under which resilient societies sustain their resilience.

The notion of path dependency – that the future path is created one step at a time and builds on the steps already taken – arises again and again in these stories and writ-ings. It explains how power grows. But it also explains how ‘walking’ in accordance with one’s own moral compass, and in the truth of one’s own authenticity, has the capacity to ‘erode’ such power structures and eat them away from the inside.

The lessons learned – beware single-stream ideologies; inform yourself through mul-tiple diverse sources; speak truth to power; tackle early; embrace diversity of opinion and encourage debate – have wider application than to political systems alone. These lessons provide food for thought for any collective human endeavour. The idea, for ex-ample, that change comes because people feel ‘the lie’ occurs not only in dictatorships 318 Section VIII On the Global



but can also occur in situations where people are told that to be happy loyal citizens they must be good consumers and buy more. Or they are assured they have equality of opportunity, even though they feel dispossessed and helpless [65]. Populism is based, so Mouffe would say, on popular leaders connecting emotionally with these feelings of unfairness and dislocation and then providing a target – immigrants or unions or ‘the socialists’ – for people to blame.

I continue to be mesmerised by the way in which these writers, from very different times and worlds, see things in such similar ways. There is so much resonance with a processual view of the complex world and I find in these writings sources of inspiration as to how to act. Clearly, a worldview cannot, of itself, offer nuanced solutions honed through life’s experience, but experiences as described by these writers, that chime with and affirm a complexity worldview offers a powerful combination both to unravel concepts and inform action.





71


In the face of a polycrisis, what should we do?



Taking a global perspective through the lens of complexity emphasises limits to growth and

raises concerns about increasing inequality. It underlines the need for global governance,

the centrality of values in shaping behaviour, and the importance for each of us to play our

part in co-creating the future.



This in the last piece in this section ‘On the Global’ but it is also the ‘last piece’. I am submitting this manuscript in two days. Early this morning, I walked into my local town to the one coffee shop that opens at the crack of dawn. As I walked there, I found myself looking back over the last five years during which I have been working on this book. I asked myself: ‘If I had to summarise the key points in a few sentences, what would I say?’ Here are those sentences:

The social and natural world is complex whether we like it or not. The same under-

standing of the dynamics of complexity emerges time and again in very different tra-

ditions – from brain science, to Daoism, to the science of open systems. Hence under-

standing the nature of our complex world is worth the effort.

The triumvirate of mindsets, intentions and values shapes our behaviour more than

almost anything else and fashions the collective path that we take.

We are making the future; it is not just imposed on us. And our future is looking precar-

ious. The essential connectedness of everything means, first, that “no one is safe until

we are all safe”135 and second, I can’t just sit back, drink another coffee, and leave it all

to ‘them’.



Turning to the theme of this final section, ‘On the Global’, it is appropriate to ask on what basis I assert that the future looks precarious? March 2024 has been the hottest March yet, following a ten-month run of ‘hottest months to date’,136 and eight years of the hottest on record – yet we are still investing in fossil fuels and emissions are still rising. Increasing drought, flooding, extreme weather events and geopolitical conflicts are driving poverty, starvation and homelessness and rendering millions unsafe. Fear, fuelled by spiralling economic inequality, is causing rising xenophobia and its associat-ed politics. Food and energy security have become weapons of war. The word ‘polycri-sis’ – a cluster of crises that interact and reinforce and can impact every aspect of the future – has entered the common language.

Last night, I went to a book launch for Growth by Daniel Susskind,137 at which he challenged – indeed mocked – the view that never-ending growth is not possible on a finite planet.138 As will be obvious from the arguments developed earlier in this section, I disagree for three reasons:

First, to whatever degree innovation and technology lead to more productive use of resources, or create more imaginative services for us to buy, ‘stuff’ still lurks beneath. 320 Section VIII On the Global



The envelope of possibility can be extended through innovation, but it cannot be end-less. There is no ‘perpetual motion machine’.139

Secondly, the current growth trajectory is very ‘stuff’ intensive. For example, two-thirds of US adults are overweight or obese.140 One-third of the total projected global popu-lation is expected to be obese by 2025. Herein lies a wonderful business growth strate-gy: Get people to eat more than they need through providing addictive ultra-processed food which is difficult to resist. This has the added benefit that the pharmaceutical in-dustry then has a growing market of those suffering from the health impacts of obe-sity. The global obesity epidemic is predicted to cost $1.2 trillion annually by 2025.141 Meanwhile, 783 million people are chronically undernourished worldwide.142

My question is: How do we stop these corporations not merely benefitting from such trends – but, in the name of Growth and Profit, often pulling out all the stops to create them? Innovation in these industries may lead to lower unit costs but it is not directed at lowering volumes. It is one thing, as Susskind suggests, to say that we should shift the nature of growth away from ‘stuff’; it is another thing to persuade the ‘stuff ped-dlers’ to give up their ‘gravy trains’.



Fahrenheit 451

I am reminded of the dystopian novel Fahrenheit 451 by Ray Bradbury, written in 1953.

In it he imagines a world where owning books becomes a criminal offence as reading

causes people to think and makes them unhappy. Instead, people are encouraged to

spend all their time watching ‘happy’ programmes on TV screens and commit any spare

cash to buying a screen for each wall.



Thirdly, as Susskind himself explains, the current growth trend is strongly correlated with widening inequalities. He confirmed that those who own the ‘capital’ are continu-ing to increase their share of the ‘wealth pie’ compared with the workers. And, more than that, there is widening inequality between the very rich owners and the less rich owners, just as there is between the high earners and those at the bottom of the earn-ings ladder. Susskind was not complacent about inequality, but he was adamant that it was not an issue to be left at the door of commerce; inequality, in his view, is not their problem.

My point is: I’m not convinced that the planet can offer unlimited resources howev-er much we improve their efficient use through innovation. I’m not convinced that current corporations, whose growth is very much predicated on ‘stuff’, are going to change their ways. And I can’t see any reason why the accelerating inequality will slow down on its own.

I draw on complexity theory to substantiate these statements. As I’ve explored throughout the book, complexity thinking emphasises the systemic nature of our world and the need for balance between its differing aspects if it is to maintain resilience and health. Humans are behaving like rogue rabbits and, like the rabbits, are quite capable of eating all the food and destroying their habitat at the same time. Self-regulation is

71 In the face of a polycrisis, what should we do? 321



not a given, and it is hard to see how unremitting economic growth can be sustained, however much it is ‘greened’ or ‘innovated’.

Equally, path dependence and lock-in explain how power inequalities tend to grow – as the powerful gain and maintain sufficient power to overwhelm their opponents, in both the economic and political spheres.

The hegemony of uncontrolled profit (a major cause of the ecological crisis) increases

inequalities within each nation and across the entire planet. The qualities of our civili-

zation have deteriorated, and its deficiencies have increased, notably in the spread of

selfishness and the disappearance of traditional solidarities. Edgar Morin143

Much infectious disease has its roots in ecological change. That means that the health

of the planet and the health of humans must be considered alongside that of animals.

It also means we should invest in public health as never before, stop cutting down the

forests, address climate change and phase out intensive farming. A ‘one health’ plane-

tary approach is the best hope we have. John Vidal144



So, what should we do?



It seems to me that the ‘what should we do’ question focuses on the need to find ways to push back against greed, spiralling inequality and growth. And this translates into asking the question: How to wrest the fragility of our future out of the hands of pow-erful elites who do not appear to feel responsible for our collective future? Typical of these are global corporations that often go unchallenged, except by their profit-orient-ed shareholders. Indeed, rather than be challenged, corporates may even be aided and abetted by politicians focused on their personal short-term political careers, and con-cerned not to do anything that might upset their sponsors. And, of course, we consum-ers are not innocent in this pact – it is our desire for ‘stuff’, our entrancement by a life of plenty – that is part of the current self-perpetuating economic model, with all its allure.

I think there are three related issues that need to be addressed. All three focus on how to return to economic balance and a fairer society, and how to stop us humans destroy-ing the planet.

These are: The need for global governance, the need for leveraging action and the need to attend to our values, our ‘moral sentiments’, to borrow the term from Adam Smith.145

(1) The need for global governance



We need to strengthen global governance. In part, this can and should arise through the function of independent, international institutions – but governance is also imple-mented through protest, consumer choice, freedom of speech, civil society, campaigns, journalism and so on. Governance is driven, too, through the will of us citizens to en-gage and to recognise our part in the problem. Unless we can rely on cooperation and 322 Section VIII On the Global



fairness to balance societies and economies – and history suggests we cannot – then there is a role for governance.

The institutions that have the most power to shape the future are the giant corpora-tions. But they have no mandate to preserve the future, and they have no global con-stituency to whom they are answerable. Particular countries can impose their laws locally but cannot control the global movement of profits or the tendency to shift manufacturing to regimes with the most favourable legislative climate, or the ability of global information companies to monetarise our personal data submerged in ‘data lakes’. Corporations, as we know, are instituted primarily to grow, to dominate their sectors and to increase profits for shareholders. Susskind argues that we should not ask corporations to take any moral responsibility; corporations should merely focus on growth at all costs and wait to be ‘governed’. His view is not untypical.

George Monbiot, in The Age of Consent,146 underlines the way giant corporations have enormous influence in the political as well as the economic sphere, a point also made by Susskind. Monbiot says:

The shift of power to the global sphere is the reason why almost every major politi-

cal party on earth now has the same policies. Their policies are pre-determined by the

banks and financial speculators, the corporations and the global institutions. At the

national level, there is democracy but no choice. At the global level, there is choice but

no democracy. The great question of our age is what the hell we intend to do about it.

He goes on:

We leave our future in the hands of these men of commerce “who have appointed

themselves to run the world”. 147

So, what can be done? How can such mega-giants be held to account, regulated or even challenged? Can we imagine effective global governance, and what would it look like? How can ‘we’ institute ways to tax the mobile rich, control the shipment of toxic waste, sustain the ban on landmines, prevent the proliferation of nuclear weapons, and op-pose the destruction of forests that function as the lungs of the world? How can we call into question the maxim of growth at any cost?

It is not that the field of global governance is entirely barren. In 2015, the United Na-tions created seventeen sustainable development goals with the aim of “peace and prosperity for people and planet, now and into the future”.148 Bodies such as the UN, the World Health Organisation, and the European Court of Human Rights play a role. But power is accorded them via national governments and we are still waiting for these governments to see the seriousness of global issues such as the climate heating, and show themselves prepared to curb the free rein of the corporations. One positive ex-ample: The Sackler family has just been fined $6bn for their role in the opioid crisis which led to half a million deaths over ten years.149

71 In the face of a polycrisis, what should we do? 323



There is also a role in global governance for all of us as citizens. Indeed, it is heartening to find stories of citizens making an impact. The achievement of 2000 Swiss women who changed the law, which happened as I bring this book to a close, is a very import-ant step.



Is climate protection a human right?

In 2023, a group of 2000 women accused the Swiss government of violating their hu-

man rights through its failure to act on climate change. The case was heard in the Euro-

pean Court of Human Rights, the first time the court had heard such a case. They asked,

“is climate protection a human right”?150 In April 2024, it was announced they had won,

the first ever climate case victory in the European Court of Human Rights.151



We citizens are also consumers. Do we pay sufficient attention to where our products come from and in what we invest? Journalists, academics, activists and authors can share information, expose questionable practices and galvanise action. Civil societies, charities, and other relatively independent bodies can equally play their part in expos-ing, lobbying, and campaigning. We lose the freedom to speak out at our peril, and it is salutary to recognise the extent to which those ‘speaking truth to power’ or even just trying to report on conflicts and protests, place their lives in danger. But governance is part of our duty as citizens, and we need to stand against any creeping erosions of the right to protest.

Inequality demands oppression. The more concentrated wealth and power become,

the more those who challenge the rich and powerful must be hounded and crushed. In

other words, economic inequality is mirrored by inequality before the law. … The best

measure of the health of a political system is who gets prosecuted. Monbiot152

One final point: The owners of the capital, the shareholders, are also citizens. One of my colleagues wondered if the handful of people who are super-rich, and really could make a difference, might see the light and take action to change things. Indeed, at Da-vos in 2021, 102 members of the global super-rich, including Disney heiress Abigail Disney, did actually express the view that the current tax system is rigged in their fa-vour and that governments around the world should “tax the rich and tax us now”.153



(2) Leveraging crisis; taking action



The second issue concerning ‘what to do’ centres on the need for each of us to take ac-tion and shoulder responsibility. We are all in this together, we are all in the soup [24]. Complexity thinking emphasises that ‘small things can have big effects’. We may feel insignificant – but consider the possibility that it could be your actions and behaviours that make a difference. The world is often impacted by singular events or by the ac-tions of particular individuals. Maybe our comments to the woman sitting next to us 324 Section VIII On the Global



on the train really had an impact. Maybe she is in a position of influence and our words created the courage for her to make a stand? We shouldn’t allow feelings of insignifi-cance or impotence to let ourselves off the hook.

People do have power. The work of historians such as William Dalrymple, Vaclav Havel and Jared Diamond suggests that, in the end, when there is little left to lose, when dif-ficulties become catastrophes, people rise.

Will we have to wait for such a serious crisis that the need to act is unequivocal and there is nothing to lose, nothing to hold people back? Will we wait ‘until the bitter end’ or will we find ways to create change before it is too late?

Crisis is a liminal point; it can signify the last straw before collapse, but it can also mo-bilise action.

George Monbiot is interested in the idea of promoting social tipping. He suggests we should start by working with “pockets of resistance”, engaging with people who are starting to think differently. We should “preach to the choir” rather than start with the gainsayers. We should start, he says, by connecting with people who think like us and then aim to “push out the concentric circles”.154

Individual action can engender change. For example, Greta Thunberg, the Swedish climate activist, has had a significant impact on many.155 It was the campaigning by sub-poster master Alan Bates that led to a recent docudrama that exposed the failure of the UK government, over twenty years, to compensate Post Office managers for wrongful conviction for fraud following a computer error.156 The film so enraged the public that the government took action virtually overnight, after years of prevarica-tion. Writing, both fiction and non-fiction, and art in general, can inform, change opin-ions and beliefs, and create the impetus to stop us sitting on the sidelines. Activism takes many forms.



(2) The importance of values



The third issue I want to raise is the importance of our values. What we value, both indi-vidually and collectively, drives the future. The future is co-created one step at a time. We can’t control the future, but we can give it shape. What steps are we going to take in this creation of the path to the future? Do we value others, do we value nature, do we value fairness and truth – or are we more intent on greedily satisfying our own desires?

I find myself returning time and again to this theme of values. It is inescapable that the collective path of the future is shaped by what we, individually and collectively, do on the way. What we do matters – and what we value shapes what we do.

Edgar Morin, a French philosopher with an interest in complexity thinking, is now 102. His perspective comes from witnessing the whole of the last century. He has this to say:

71 In the face of a polycrisis, what should we do? 325



We are heading towards a probable catastrophe. The polycrisis we are experiencing

across the planet is an anthropological crisis: It is the crisis of humanity failing to be-

come Humanity.157



Morin’s view is that we need to return to our humanity, to the values of “fellowship, life and love”. He suggests we should see this as political resistance akin to the resistance movements that he would have experienced during the Second World War – resis-tance against the forces of “dislocation, disintegration, conflict and death”. This, too, is the message embedded in the work of Havel, Ressi and Temelkuran [70].

Morin again:158

The first and fundamental resistance is that of the spirit. It requires resisting the intim-

idation of every lie asserted as truth, the contagion of every collective intoxication. It

requires never yielding to the delirium of the collective [hegemony] of a people or an

ethnicity. It demands resisting hatred and contempt.



A sobering thought



Some thinkers are talking not just of a polycrisis but of a metacrisis. Jonathan Row-son describes this as “the crisis within and between all the world’s major crises” – an emerging systemic outcome that becomes ever more impossible to counter.
