He sees the root cause as a “multi-faceted delusion … that … manifests institutionally and cul-turally to the detriment of life on earth”.159 These are strong words. They endorse the theme of this book: the need to rethink our fundamental beliefs and worldviews. We cannot just tinker around the edges.

Our current ‘deluded’ way of life may indeed just implode. Both history and complexity remind us that the present will not last forever. It is sobering when listening to discus-sions of past empires on William Dalrymple’s podcast Empire,160 to find that I am hardly aware of some of the empires that ruled vast swathes of territory for centuries, such as the Persian Empire in the fifth century B.C.E. or the Mughal Empire in the seventeenth century. And, as discussed earlier [67], collapse is made more likely by inequality, limits to resources and breakdown of mutuality. We are getting perilously close to the point at which at least some aspects of our current social and natural world could descend into chaos, if they are not already doing so. We have to face that possibility and consid-er how to behave in the face of that possibility – both how to ward against it and how to act if things unravel.

In particular, orthodox economic thinking has, surely, to be addressed. There are many academic challenges to economic orthodoxy, not least from students.161 In contrast, politicians worldwide, from the left and right, seem reluctant to face this elephant in the room. We need, don’t we, a new way to ‘do’ economics that does not rely so fun-damentally on growth and does not lead so inexorably to widening inequality.162 Clive Hamilton tells us of an essay, written in 1939, by John Maynard Keynes, considering 326 Section VIII On the Global



what life would be like after another century of economic growth. Keynes imagined people would be choosing “wisely and agreeably and well” and would be cultivating the art of life and not selling themselves for the “means of life”. Hamilton asks: “Is it possible to imagine a society … in which we are no longer obsessed with growth and consumption and instead cultivate the art of life?”163

Which brings me back to the driving force behind this book: I have set out to put ‘flesh on the bones’ of a processual complex worldview and evidence my conviction that it provides a better fit with, a better explanation of, the world we see around us than does the mechanical view of classical science. As I say in the Introduction, my motivation comes from the desire to challenge both the orthodoxy and the hegemony of our cur-rent worldview. The ontology of process complexity provides a frame through which to understand change and stasis, unpick what engenders resilience, make sense of lock-in, reflect on the nature of emergence and consider the importance of our moral stance; through this, we may become clearer as to how, in Daoist language, to ‘cultivate’ our-selves, and how best to act. We need to challenge how we think, what we value, how we relate to each other, and how we treat the natural world.

The idea of metacrisis … draws attention to interiority (meta as within) and relational-

ity (meta as between) as spiritual features of what is typically assumed to be a political

challenge. Jonathan Rowson164



A call for action



For we have to act. We have to ask ourselves what part we are going to play as we face these challenging times. I am reminded of The Plague, a novel by Albert Camus,165 in which he tells an allegorical story of a town cut off from the outside world due to an outbreak of the plague. He describes how various citizens behave – some selfishly hoarding food, others carousing in the belief that there was no hope. But one charac-ter, the doctor, who understands more than anyone what might be in store, continues to work tirelessly to help the sick. Clive Hamilton, described this as active fatalism: “Despair, Accept and then Act … only by acting, and acting ethically, can we redeem our humanity”.166

This final section, then, presents a call for action – difficult, courageous, contentious action. It is a call to find our moral compass, face what is happening in the world, show up and play our part. It is a call to nurture our humanity, value life, value others, value the future and stay true. We must embrace our agency, however tiny that might feel. We are all complicit in co-creating the future.





Appendix





72


Science and indeterminism



How science, when applied to ‘real world’ problems, is not suggesting things unfold

according to universal laws.



It seems a contradiction to allow that most physics theories are deterministic – can predict the future – and yet argue that the universe, even when looking at it through

the eyes of classical physics, is not. Even physics, that bastion of reason and objective ‘truth’– when you put all aspects together and apply it to ‘real world’ situations – does not suggest that the universe merely unfolds in an orderly fashion. In practice, its unique features depend on messiness, history and context.

In this piece I explore various reasons why this is so. My motivation comes from the concern that we seem as inclined as ever to conflate an aim to be scientific and profes-sional – when ‘managing’ the social world – with the adoption of machine-like assump-tions of predictability, certainty and objectivity. And we look to the ‘hard sciences’ to justify this approach [3].

During the Renaissance and long after, the ideas of omniscience and omnipotence

were closely linked. Ilya Prigogine1

Our science is based on the principle of causality, and causality is considered to be

an axiomatic truth. [In nature] every process is partially or totally interfered with by

chance, so much so that under natural circumstances, a course of events absolutely

conforming to specific laws is almost an exception. Carl Jung2

The soundbite is: ‘Even physics does not support a machine worldview; even physics does not regard things as working like clockwork; Pierre-Simon Laplace’s Daemon3 was retired many years ago.’

Science is popularly believed to be the story of unfiltered access to Nature as it really is.

… resting at a prestigious remove from the tangle of subjectivity, as an objective world

of Facts. … Science is a superior … value-free practice. Ahistorical, neutral, stoic and

foundational. Bayo Akomolafe4



But surely physics is deterministic?



Science, according to popular parlance, is about certainty, prediction and objective truth. This view of science paints a picture of a universe that works like a machine – a universe that seems real, unambiguous and independent of our (or anything else’s) view of it, with mechanisms definable and unchanging. If we know the starting point of this machine-like universe, we believe we can know everything that will ever happen – because things progress, so it would seem, in accordance with fixed laws. This idea – 330 Appendix



that the future is already decreed and defined and merely unfolds – is known as de-terminism. But is science really suggesting that this is the case, that the universe really works in this deterministic way? Or is there more to it than that?

The picture from science is, at first glance, straightforward. If you look independently at individual science theories – such as electromagnetism or sound or gravity – then, in the way they are defined, they are universal and deterministic. The behaviour of light and sound, or the effect of gravity, are all described by laws that are fixed and univer-sal, and we do expect these laws to apply everywhere and to remain stable. They are based on equations which, if the starting point and the boundaries of the problem are known, do give exact answers and, in principle, do define how situations unfold over time. Einstein’s theories of gravity are similarly deterministic, if a bit more complicated than Newton’s original version.5

In very many ways, we can have much confidence in these equations. There are many examples where these equations do indeed lead to predictable outcomes, ones that can be investigated and evidenced. For example, Einstein predicted a ‘gravitational redshift’ in light as it leaves a star’s gravitational field. This was measured by Thomas Eddington in 1919 on the island of Principe, during a solar eclipse, and it was precisely in line with Einstein’s prediction.6 Another example: Penrose reports that “Astrono-mers have been monitoring the orbits of one double neutron star system – known as PSR 1913+16 – for around 40 years; there has been an agreement between the signals received from space and the overall predictions of Einstein’s theory to an astonishing 14 decimal places.”7

But, is this precision the whole story? Is there indeed an objective deterministic uni-verse out there, obeying unchanging universal laws? Are we doomed to live, as William James called it, in “an iron block, with no equivocation or shadow of turning”?8

In fact, this perfect – or terrifying – picture does not play out in practice, and this is for several reasons. In the following short sections I explore seven of these reasons, and then consider the implications.

(a) Even if things can be described mathematically, it doesn’t mean things

are certain



Physics theories, on the whole, are based on mathematics, and some argue that the universe really does accord with mathematics. But even if this were the case, mathe-matics does not necessarily lead to certainty. Penrose gives an example, nick-named the hairy dog theorem.9 He shows that it is never possible to brush the hair of the no-tional dog flat everywhere; there is always one point where it will stick up, although we cannot predict where. The hairy dog theorem is unusual, but there are many equations that have more than one solution, even simple ones such as x2 = 1.

One important historical example is the three-body problem.10 The three-body prob-lem is an idealised situation in which the initial positions and velocities of three bodies, represented as points in space, are defined, and their subsequent behaviour investi-

72 Science and indeterminism 331



gated. The application of the three-body problem at the time of Newton was to explore the movement, due to gravity, of the sun, moon and earth in relation to each other. Even though this problem is simple to define, it has no simple solution. It cannot be solved analytically (i.e., on a piece of paper, solving equations by hand). It requires a numerical11 approach to home in on the possible trajectories and it shows that each trajectory is very sensitively dependent on the initial conditions.

The point to take from these examples is that even if the universe could be described en-tirely by mathematics, this would not necessarily translate into certainty. And whether the universe as a whole can be described mathematically is questionable. Many factors transpire to mess this up, not least thermodynamics, as I will discuss later.

(b) Classical simplifications



When the maths of classical physics was developed in the seventeenth to the nine-teenth centuries, there were many simplifying assumptions. For example, Newton’s mathematics, which described the solar system, treated each planet as if it were a point. Newton also ignored any interactions between the planets and focused on their independent interaction with the sun. Another example: The theory of thermodynam-ics assumed that each collision between molecules was independent of every other collision.

Newton’s physics gives us a picture of a perfect machine; thermodynamics yields an image of a steady flow towards minimum energy. These seem contradictory and I re-member as a teenager studying physics and wondering when you apply one and when you apply the other. The first applies to situations in which there are only a small num-ber of things interacting with forces, and every movement can be tracked. The sec-ond applies to gases or liquids with trillions of seemingly identical particles, and you can look statistically at the whole. But when does the number of particles become big enough to move from mechanics to thermodynamics, I wondered to myself? There is not really an answer to this question, as these are mathematical idealisations which allowed the physicists of the classical era to make huge progress. The real world has more complexity than these analytical simplifications can capture.

Even if we can have confidence in physical laws, applying them to real-life situations opens up a number of contextual issues, and these add complexity, as the next sections will show.

(c) Beginnings, endings, singularities and discontinuities



In ‘The ticking clock’ [3], I touched on the problems of beginnings and endings and how Newton dealt with these by bringing God into the picture. Physics theories such as gravity or electromagnetism cannot easily be extended to deal with discontinuities or singularities – such as what happens at or before the beginning of the universe, or in a black hole, or at its end.12

332 Appendix



We can understand the universe back to the first microsecond … when it was just the

size of a tennis ball. As yet we have no experimental foothold on the very extreme phys-

ics involved [before that]. Martin Rees13



Beginnings and endings are one form of singularity. In mathematical terms, a singu-larity is where solutions to equations go to infinity or, colloquially, ‘blow up’. Another example is a gravitational singularity, in which gravity becomes so intense that space itself breaks down. At a singularity, the ‘normal’ laws of physics cannot be applied, and, as Penrose explains, “space and time end”.14 Singularities create discontinuities, analo-gous to ‘tears in the fabric’ or ‘sinkholes in the landscape’.

To talk about the age of the universe is already in a sense a non-Einsteinian con-

cept because the age of the universe only has sense if the birth of the universe is an

event, something irreversible, and that [event] does not form part of Einstein’s theory.

Prigogine15



Just to complicate things further, when you include quantum mechanics in the consid-eration of the centre of black holes, these black holes may end up technically not being ‘singularities’; however, they still remain ‘discontinuities’ where what emerges may be different in kind to what was there before.16

(d) Boundaries



Beginnings and endings are one sort of boundary, but there are also spatial bound-aries.17 It may not be an obvious point, but the classical laws of physics were derived for closed systems. A closed system is one which does not allow, inside the situation of interest, any energy, information or matter to leave or penetrate from ‘outside’. The total amount of energy, matter or electric charge is conserved in such isolated closed systems. It may make sense to consider the universe as a closed system (although this is itself contentious), but when considering more local situations – from galaxies to ponds – these are inevitably open to their environments, to their wider contexts. For example, stars emit light which leaves the galaxy in which they are situated, and stray asteroids wander their way in. And ponds are wonderful examples of open systems – they receive light and heat from the sun, they are subject to winds and rain, animals come and go, birds drop seeds and people drop litter.

To have open boundaries necessarily causes there to be a level of uncertainty about what is going on. We cannot be sure what might invade or leave through these open boundaries. The laws of physics still apply, but to gain certainty we would need to know everything that crossed those boundaries. Embracing the inevitability of open boundaries and the consequent implications, is a key aspect of complexity theory, as discussed in [8] and [11].



72 Science and indeterminism 333



If we think about the whole universe, we might argue that, as the universe must include everything, it must be a closed system. But this returns us to the point raised in ‘The ticking clock’ [3] – closed with respect to what? We could equally argue the universe is open. We can only decide whether it is open or closed in relation to something outside of it.

(e) Integration of forces in physics



Next, there is the issue of the integration of the differing forces in physics. The hunt for a so-called unified field theory has been the goal of physics since at least 1914 and it still eludes us. Although some physicists believe that, in the end, all the bits will tie up and we will have a ‘theory of everything’, there are many, including Roger Penrose, Martin Rees, and Carlo Rovelli, who think this is unlikely.

To make it even more complicated, the physical forces, particularly when venturing into the quantum domain, are not, in general, additive, but interact with each other. Their affects are synergistic; that is to say that the outcome of two or more different forces or fields cannot in general be reduced to the addition of their separate impacts, calculated separately.18

(f) Messy real-world situations



Even when the forces of physics are individually deterministic and well understood, they are operating on messy non-uniform situations in the ‘real world’.19 Indeed, in or-der to get the ‘clumping’ of gas and energy that leads to star formation, the starting clouds of ‘stuff’ have to be non-uniform. Stars are created from turbulence and vortices in clouds of simple molecules. Some clumped patches of these swirling clouds need to be dense enough for gravity to be able to amass enough force for nuclear reactions to be initiated.

In addition, these clouds tend to be strongly magnetic; magnetism resists gravity, hence more material is needed before there is a collapse into star formation, and this interplay allows the resulting star to be bigger than if the clouds were not magnetic. The particularity of the shape and size of a molecular cloud, and the exact timing of other matter arriving from the wider universe, will shape what sort of star is formed. And when there are births and explosions leading to the formation of new heavier el-ements, these events are complex and what happens is very dependent on context. Scientists have an understanding of the conditions under which stars can form – but knowing to where a particular situation might lead is another matter.

Why did [the universe] have the kind of irregularities that resulted in it not remaining a

uniform gas, but developing clusters of galaxies? Martin Rees20



334 Appendix



(g) Messiness and heat



‘Messiness’ is a particular feature of the theory of thermodynamics. Thermodynamics deals with the energetic movement of particles in gases and liquids, and it is this move-ment that leads to heat. The more energy the particles have, the more they bump into each other, and the hotter the liquid or gas becomes. It is a statistical theory, yet, for closed systems, the progression, on average, from hot to cool, follows a well-defined path. Over time, such an isolated system will run down, become featureless, and find its lowest energy state, known as heat death.21 This is described by the second law of thermodynamics.22

But although the ‘average’ trajectory towards heat death can be described by equa-tions which are deterministic – in the sense that, on average, it moves predictably and inexorably towards its lowest energy state – there are wobbles on the way. A bit like bringing soup to a boil, the process is not smooth – there are places which get hotter first and bubble up, and other areas will bubble up later. In general, the timing and na-ture and sequence of the ‘bubbles’ of emerging patterns that occur along the way is salient to what happens, and then what happens next. For example, if – just at the point that the interstellar ‘soup’ gave forth a bubble – a meteor shower arrived, then the out-come would be different than if the meteor shower arrived later or earlier. Classical methods of statistics ignore the history of the interaction between things; they assume that each event has no trace of previous events (there is no memory or hysteresis) and that something only interacts with one other thing at a time.

The point is that the particularity of what happens drives the particularity of what happens next. Messiness matters and its particular nature shapes the future. There are more sophisticated forms of statistics that embrace ‘possibility’ rather than ‘prob-ability’, but I won’t go further here [62].23

I look at thermodynamics of open systems and the way it formed the basis for complex-ity theory in [8].



Epistemological versus ontological indeterminism



This has been a very brief waltz through the sources of indeterminism in the universe. I have been intent on trying to illustrate that – even if at some future point, we man-age to connect up physics theories and create a unified field theory and even if we are able to push back our understandings to the beginning or even before the beginnings of the big bang or big bangs – there are sources of indeterminism that do not go away. Indeed, non-uniformity, fluctuations, and general messiness are not only inevitable but necessary conditions to enable clouds of ‘stuff’ to ‘clump’. It is this clumping that allows stars and galaxies to form; furthermore, each galaxy, star, asteroid and dust cloud is distinct. Such messy real-world situations are particular to their history and context and are complex.

72 Science and indeterminism 335



At risk of confusing matters, I want to add another nuance to this discussion on in-determinism and introduce the difference between indeterminism that is associated with our ways of knowing (epistemological in origin) and indeterminism that is intrinsic to the nature of things (ontological in origin).

Epistemological



Just because it is difficult to track everything, this still does not necessarily mean that the universe is not knowable in principle. Laplace gave substance to this idea when he dreamed up the thought experiment of a Daemon sitting out there in the universe, a Daemon who had a big enough brain so he could track everything. In principle, if the Daemon knew the starting point, at the beginning (if there was a beginning) of time, of every bit of energy or information or matter – however messy and non-uniform – together with “all the forces by which nature is animated,”24 he could work out how things would unfold.

But we – or any other entity in the universe – are not that Daemon! There is uncertain-ty caused by ignorance. This sort of uncertainty which is due to the practical limitations of knowing is called epistemological indeterminism. That is to say that the universe is so complicated that it is impossible to know in practice the movement of every mole-cule, every spark of energy, every dust cloud. The universe is not deterministic in any meaningful way.

Determinism is saved in principle, but it is inoperative since one cannot know exhaus-

tively the initial states. Edgar Morin25



Ontological



In fact, we can go further. Quantum physics confronts us with the idea of ontological indeterminism, a profound indeterminism that is intrinsic to its nature. I explore quan-tum physics and ontological indeterminism in [73].



In summary



So, to summarise, there are several reasons, coupled together – discontinuities, bound-aries, interplay between forces in ‘real world’ situations, intrinsic uncertainties due to the particularities of messiness and variations – that show how the universe, even if we only focus on its material nature, is really not the predictable machine that some might think science believes it to be.26 It all adds up to a view of the universe that is much more particular and contextual, and a universe in which novelty can emerge. As Leibniz said at the time, Newton’s laws cannot tell us why the planets and galaxies are where they are, and why there is such diversity of form in the world of galaxies and planets. We cannot know when and where a star will be formed, how big it will be and 336 Appendix



how long it will survive; we cannot predict the particularity of structures. And these fluctuations, interactions and uncertainties in the ‘real world’ are necessary for the uni-verse to emerge and evolve. They are not just incidental.

Darwin, in his thinking about evolution, similarly realised messiness, variation, and fluctuations are a necessary condition for the emergence of new forms [7]. Cosmologist Lee Smolin, in fact, suggests that evolution is the primary process not just for the living world but for the universe as a whole.27

In this piece, I have limited my arguments to the world of classical physics. But, as I’ve hinted, if you include quantum physics, you start to toy with the idea of intrinsic or ontological indeterminism – something more baked in and fundamental – and I will ex-plore this next in [73].





73


Smoke and mirrors: the paradox of quantum physics



The ways in which quantum physics creates intrinsic, ontological paradox and provides the

‘crack’ through which mystery enters.

There are more things in heaven and earth, Horatio, than are dreamt of in your philos-

ophy. Hamlet



A summary



I think this piece is the most dense of anything I have written in this book. I am trying to articulate what quantum physics is, what it has to say about the nature of the uni-

verse, and what that infers about the way we think about ‘reality’. Only read this in the morning with a cup of coffee and feel free to skip.

To summarise the key points in a few sentences:

The universe dissolves, at its smallest scales, into paradox; its properties are held in

relationship between ‘things’, not in the things themselves. Quantum physics offers a

relational, subjective and contextual view of ‘reality’. These are ontological, intrinsic

qualities. Quantum physics opens the door to ‘mystery’.



Introduction



Quantum physics changes the game. It causes us to conjure with concepts such as ‘on-tological paradox’ and ‘ontological indeterminism’. It forms a basis for a relational view of the universe and something I have called ‘ontological subjectivity’, which I go on to discuss. In these few pages, I want to give at least a hint of the implications of this the-ory, a theory which provides many verifiable outcomes and has stood the test of time.

This short piece contains a number of terms particular to quantum physics and, rath-er than try to define them all, I am leaving it to the reader to look up any terms that are unfamiliar (terms such as entanglement and action at a distance). I am trying to convey a sense of what this all implies rather than try to pin it down entirely. As Richard Feynman said “I think I can safely say that no one understands quantum mechanics”.

There are many interpretations of quantum physics, some of which still persist in the social sciences, even when they are no longer current in the physical sciences.28 I am basing my arguments primarily on the work of Carlo Rovelli, Roger Penrose and Chris Clarke.29

338 Appendix



What I am aiming to illustrate here is that quantum physics provides a concept of uncertainty or indeterminism that is more than epistemological in nature; is not just about ‘limits to knowing’ but seems to be ‘baked in’ to the way things are – an intrinsic, ontological sort of uncertainty. The nature of this uncertainty is not straightforward, though, as I will go on to show. And it goes beyond the much-talked-about uncertainty principle.30 In fact, the uncertainty principle – which defines the relationship between position and momentum, or between energy and time – is very specific in defining the way in which these so-called complementary pairs are connected. So, the uncertainty principle, peculiarly, is actually very exact.

Quantum physics is made even more difficult to grasp for the non-specialist as it pro-vides a rich source of metaphor and it can be tricky to distinguish the science of quan-tum physics applied in its realm from the metaphorical use of its concepts – of, for ex-ample, entanglement, action at a distance, or paradoxical cats in boxes. It is hazardous to assume that quantum physics concepts can map directly onto the experience of or-dinary life, for reasons I will explain.



Two parts to quantum physics: coherence and decoherence



I want to start by bringing attention to two contrasting aspects of quantum physics. They are both necessary aspects of the theory. First, there is the coherent, determin-istic part which can be expressed by a deterministic mathematical equation, such as Schrödinger’s equation. Such equations are relevant in situations where they are de-scribing a ‘pure’ quantum state in isolation from – and not interacting with – anything else. Second, there is the process of decoherence or ‘collapse’ where there is interaction, and something irreversible and unpredictable happens.

The coherent part



One of the representations of quantum physics is via a wave function – an abstract mathematical probability function that captures the information about possible out-comes. The wave function does not exist in any tangible sense and yet it leads to test-able predictions. The wave function is described as coherent, where “all parts of it have phases that are in step”.31

Although the wave function is capturing probabilities, it remains deterministic in the sense that what those probabilities are, and how they unfold, can be well-defined. However, these probabilities do not cover up our ignorance, as in classical physics; they are somehow intrinsic to nature.

To say that probability is intrinsic to the nature of things is of fundamental importance. It brings into question whether there really is an incontrovertibly existent universe. Do we have to change our concept of existence or of reality? Is there a way in which two incompatible things can both be true? This is illustrated by means of a thought experiment, nicknamed Schrödinger’s cat, which considers a notional ‘cat in a box’ and questions whether the cat is alive or dead.32 The probability embedded in the quantum

73 Smoke and mirrors: the paradox of quantum physics 339



wave function not saying: ‘The cat is alive or dead, we just don’t yet know which.’ It is saying that the cat, in some sense, is both alive and dead.

Putting the cat aside for a moment, the idea of a quantum probability function can at first glance seem unremarkable. But, as Rovelli tells us, it hides much that is bizarre. In ideal conditions, whereby photons, let’s say, are not being interacted with and remain in a ‘pure’ coherent state, they display behaviours that just do not make sense to our everyday experience, such as ‘action at a distance.’33 These behaviours derive from the ‘quantum interference’34 between distinct states within the wave function. For exam-ple, one state might be ‘spin up’ and the other ‘spin down’ – analogous to the cat being alive and dead. Rovelli describes, in Helgoland, an experiment with laser beams to il-lustrate this.35 I won’t try to describe it here, but it demonstrates that the concept of a universe made of ‘things’ interacting – even if those things are more like waves, more dispersed than the way we commonly tend to envisage things – is just not so. What is a ‘thing’ is paradoxical not because it is hard to measure, but because that is its nature. It is an ontological paradox. At the quantum level there is no ‘thing’ at all.

The decoherent part



Moving on to the second aspect of quantum physics, its process of decoherence, we ask what happens when these wave functions do interact with other ‘things’, for exam-ple with “heat vibrations.”36 In these situations, the coherence in the wave function is lost and something definite, ‘actual’ and irreversible occurs. In other words, the mess-iness of the real world causes the quantum probability waves to collapse or decohere and we return to the behaviour of the classical world.

This decohering process is often attributed to ‘the role of the observer’, and this can give a whole anthropocentric feel to the implications. It is the case that this decoher-ence can and will result from human measurement as in Rovelli’s example described above. But this process is not only attributable to human involvement. Quantum me-chanics operates perfectly well out there in the heavens, and operated before we came into existence. As Rovelli says, “to think that human beings, their minds, their tools or the numbers they use play any special role in the [ontological] grammar of nature is nonsense”.37

The equivalent process of ‘observation’ in deepest space, is when, let’s say, a quan-tum state interacts with a gravitational fluctuation. Penrose suggests this process is particularly relevant in a black hole, where conditions are so dense that gravitational fluctuations are at the same scale as quantum states. The outcome will depend on the interplay between gravity and quantum effects, and, in the words of Penrose, it is as if the universe “makes a choice” between options.38

Cohering and decohering



The characteristics of the quantum world emerge through the interplay of both as-pects of the theory. Pure coherent quantum states are ‘collapsed’ by interaction and 340 Appendix



relationship. The language and description of these two aspects will continue to evolve and continue to provide a source of mystery and potential.



A further look at coherent states: the importance of context

and relationship



I want to mention one more aspect of coherent states, which emphasises the impor-tance of context and relationship. Rovelli and Clarke introduce the idea of “context-de-pendent logic.”39 Clarke explains that this means that “two opposites can both be true – but not in the same context”40 and thus “reality depends on the context.”41 To put it another way, what manifests depends on the relationship between two (or more) par-ticular quantum functions. It is a relational outcome of that particular interaction in a particular situation. Rovelli says, in Helgoland, “we cannot separate the properties of the objects from the objects interacting with them in order for these properties to be manifested in the first place. … This is contextuality; things exist in a context”.42

I will attempt an example: Imagine you have three coherent quantum wave functions, A, B and C. A contains the quantum equivalent of the superposition of the dead cat and the live cat. When B interacts with A, the upshot is that the cat shows as alive. When C interacts with A, the upshot is that the cat shows as dead. Both are ‘true’ but manifest in different contexts. Or, to put it more graphically, the cat may be dead to you and alive to me.43

It is a world of perspectives, of manifestations, not of entities with definite properties

or unique facts. Properties do not reside in objects; they are bridges between objects.

Rovelli44



This idea of the universe as essentially manifesting in relationship is a very profound shift indeed.



Quantum fluctuations



There is one final point to introduce, which is on the subject of fluctuations. It seems that quantum fluctuations can pop up out of nothingness. Penrose suggests that this may lead to another universe being born when this one has run out of energy, or that many ‘baby’ universes can emerge out of black holes. It is also thought that this random process contributed to the lack of symmetry at the big bang (or bangs). As I discuss in [72], there needs to be some asymmetry, some messiness, for things to be able to clump together and form the stars and galaxies that evolved. Otherwise, all you get is a uniform expanding featureless cloud. As Clarke says, part of the messiness is due to these quantum fluctuations, arising in the initial cosmic fireball, which can “gave rise to the galaxies that would not otherwise have appeared”.45

73 Smoke and mirrors: the paradox of quantum physics 341



Making some sense of all this



I have been in two minds as to whether to include this piece on quantum mechanics. Let me try to summarise the key points I am endeavouring to demonstrate.

Ontological indeterminism



Quantum effects contribute to a fundamental source of indeterminism at the very tini-est scales of the universe. Rovelli asks: “Are we convinced that the world is determinis-tic, that the future and the past are univocally determined by the world’s present? Can we really squeeze the discoveries of quantum physics into the canons of metaphysical prejudice?”46 By metaphysical prejudice, he means, in this case, our fervent desire for a deterministic world.

Quantum physics conveys an intrinsic indeterminism in the ‘essence’ of the universe – an ontological indeterminism that is immanent, ‘baked in’.

One of the sources of ontological indeterminism embedded in the quantum world re-sults from the fact that quantum fluctuations can bubble up out of nowhere in random fashion, as noted above.

A second source of indeterminism occurs when there is a decoherent interaction, as we cannot be sure which, of all the possibilities, will actually occur.

The third reason for indeterminism comes from the paradox of ‘context-dependent logic’ mentioned above. Contradictory things may be true at the same time, but in dif-ferent contexts.

Paradox, relationships and ontological subjectivity



This idea that two contradictory things can both be occurring at the same time is a really profound thought. It suggests that paradox is woven into the universe, and that this paradox is ontological in origin.

This point about the paradox of context-dependent logic takes us into the territory of what I am calling ontological subjectivity. What is happening depends on the perspec-tive of those aspects of the cosmos that are interacting. The universe is not made up of things connected together in ways that make it possible precisely to define the present or precisely to predict the future; it cannot be understood as “a set of causal interac-tions ultimately reducible to matter and motion”.47 Just in the way that I have a subjec-tive view of my dog, a view that says as much about me as it does about the dog, then this gravitational fluctuation ‘sees’ that quantum fluctuation in a particular way and another gravitational fluctuation ‘sees’ it in a different way. The properties exist in the relationship between ‘things’ not in the things themselves.

As a fundamental underpinning of ‘reality’ these ideas are, I think, mind-boggling, al-most impossible to grasp entirely with the reasoning mind, as they move us beyond the sort of reason we are used to. But they do illustrate that the universe is not ‘real’ in the 342 Appendix



sense we imagine and not even Laplace’s Daemon could be sure of what is there and what might happen next.

In a relational world, the properties of things are not fixed absolutely … they arise

instead from the interactions and relationships among the things in the world. Lee

Smolin48

I mean to conjure a world that is not at all composed of ‘things’ already possessing

properties and features but a maelstrom of relationships in open-ended becomings.

Bayo Akomolafe49



Finally, mystery



I want to take one further step: Perhaps we can even say that the universe is mysteri-ous to itself, in an immanent way, imbued in its very fabric.50 John Kay and Mervyn King ask: “Does our world contain mysteries or puzzles?” Puzzles are solvable, mysteries are not.

This idea of mystery is given particular articulation by Chris Clarke.51 Quantum phys-ics, he felt, re-integrates the scientific with the mystical and the mysterious. If religion or spirituality is in part based on belief in this mystical quality, then quantum physics can be said to re-integrate science and spirituality.

The crack of complementarity left open what actually came into existence as a re-

sult of a quantum process, what actually becomes …. this emergence, this mystery.

Chris Clarke52



Maybe our sixth sense – our reaction to the atmosphere of an old church, a premoni-tion of danger, the synchronicities that sometimes seem to occur – are examples of feeling into the numinous aspects of existence that reach beyond reason. There are many examples of other living creatures – plants, animals, slime moulds – that seem to have incredible abilities to apprehend the intangible. Maybe we are sensing into those aspects of the ‘way things are’ that hide within quantum physics?



Summary



The key takeaway from this piece is that quantum physics changes the game in many ways: it questions the nature of reality, it introduces an ontological form of indetermin-ism, and it opens the door for mystery, for the reintegration of science and ‘spirituality’



73 Smoke and mirrors: the paradox of quantum physics 343



and a return to natural philosophy. If this has whetted your appetite, I recommend in particular the work of Carlo Rovelli and Lee Smolin.



A word of warning



A word of warning, though: It is always dangerous to assert anything about quantum physics as quantum physicists themselves do not all agree. And, of course, quantum physics will not be the last word; other theories will follow, which will include and tran-scend, or indeed disavow it. Indeed, the field of quantum gravity, where scientists are grappling with the interplay between the quantal and gravitational world, is surfacing yet new metaphysical insights. So, there is no need to take my word for it, but I hope at least it unsettles, if not unseats, any notions of what is real.

Endnotes: Section III 353



35 It reminds me of discussions about ‘African Philosophy’; for example, Tsenay Serequeberhan’s (1991) African Philosophy. It is often said that there is no or little indigenous African philosophy because there are no known African philosophers (Serequeberhan 1991:35). The point is that the philosophical tradition in African cultures tends not to be of the same kind as Western classical phil-osophical traditions, has not evolved by the same methods, and so it is overlooked and dismissed. By those writing more positively about it, African philosophy is regarded as having a “dynamic character to its ontology”, a “philosophy of unity and complete encounter of all things and beings” (Serequeber-han 1991:45) – which sounds rather resonant with complexity and Daoism. 36 Hall & Ames (1995:xiv)

37 Hall & Ames (1995:xiv)

38 A riff on ‘Yes we have no bananas’ – a song released in 1923. 39 The word acosmotic, whilst meaning there is no overarching connected oneness, does not imply everything is chaotic and disconnected. There is local coherence, but not total coherence (see [5] for discussion on coherence). The same point in made in complexity theory, which works with the middle ground between machine-like behaviour (we can know everything) and random chaos (we can know nothing).

40 Ames & Hall (2003:14)

41 Hall & Ames (1995:186)

42 The phrase ‘the ten thousand things’ is taken to mean ‘a myriad’ of things. There is an amusing story as to how 10,000 came to be the goal for the number of steps we should take in a day. Apparently, the idea of walking 10,000 steps a day was invented as part of the marketing campaign for an early pedometer ahead of the 1964 Tokyo Olympics. The Japanese character for 10,000 (which is the same as the Chinese character) looks rather like a person walking so the device was called the Manpo-kei or 10,000 steps meter. It stuck!

49 https://www.bayoakomolafe.net/post/what-climate-collapse-asks-of-us 50 When I say fabric, I am trying to find a word here which capture the local contextual inherent qual-ity, as opposed to a big picture overarching quality, like Purpose or Mystery. 52 Clarke (2010:137)





Glossary




Acosmotic: does not embrace the notion of an overarching cosmos; emphasises the importance of uniqueness, context and particularity.

Agonistic dissent: a concept in political theory where the focus is on acknowledging and embracing the inherent conflicts and differences within society as a necessary and productive aspect of democratic politics. Welcomes debate which neither suppresses conflict nor leads to violence; encourages the voicing of different perspectives and allows new solutions to surface. Autopoiesis: the inherent tendency of biological organisms to self-regulate and hence survive – through internal reflexive processes and interactions, coupled with engagement with their environment.

Axiom: a foundational principle or statement that is used as the basis for further reasoning or arguments within a particular field. Axioms are considered self-evident truths and are not thought to require proof.

Chaos: disorder and randomness. (Note that ‘deterministic chaos’ is a function of certain types of nonlinear equations and is entirely distinct from the general meaning of chaos.) Closed systems: systems that are isolated from their external environment, with no exchange of matter, energy, or information across their boundaries. Derivations within classical physics tend to assume boundaries are closed.

Coherence: no information is lost as things develop; there is perfect unbroken continuous connection – as would be found with motion on an unbreakable membrane or on the surface of a dish of mercury.

Context: the specific circumstances, conditions, histories and local environmental factors that surround and influence any event, situation, or interaction. Cosmology: in philosophy, cosmology involves the contemplation of the universe as a whole, considering its totality and the nature of existence; in physics, cosmology is the scientific study of the material universe, focusing on its structure, origins, and future development. Critical juncture: a pivotal moment, turning point or leverage point, where the potential for engendering change seems high.

Critical subjectivity: a deliberate and reflective engagement with one’s own, and others’, subjective experiences and interpretations, in a way that enhances understanding and leads to a considered view, often through actively engaging with and integrating multiple perspectives. Cybernetics: A field of study that focuses on how to control and stabilise systems using negative feedback loops.

Determinism: views the universe as unchanging and machine-like, governed by fixed, universal laws; the future is entirely predictable if one knows the starting conditions and the forces at play.

Discontinuity: occurs at singularities where traditional physical laws break down, such as at the beginning or end of the universe, or within black holes. These points are analogous to ‘tears in the fabric’ of space-time.

Dynamic stability: stability created by the combination of self-regulating interplay between elements, coupled with the propensity to interact with the wider environment.

378 Glossary



Emergence: the process through which novel properties appear, properties that have not previously existed and could not have been predicted; often (but not always) refers to systemic properties of ‘the whole’ that are not present in the constituent elements. (see Systemic emergence and Radical emergence)

Emptiness: a Buddhist concept used in certain texts (e.g. Milarepa text) to signify that form can only be understood in relational terms to is constitutive elements; form lacks substance. (see Pattern)

Entropy: a measure of disorder or randomness. The second law of thermodynamics tells us that that there is an overall decline towards disorder and increased entropy. Despite this, local decreases in entropy (increases in order) can occur in situations open to their surroundings, with which they can exchange energy, information and matter; it is this process that leads to star formation and indeed to life itself.

Episodic: a pattern of change that occurs in irregular intervals; typically marked by periods of relative stability interrupted by sudden, significant transformations. (see Punctuated) Epistemological indeterminism: the uncertainty and unpredictability in the universe due to practical limitations of our knowledge and understanding. This concept highlights that while the universe might be deterministic in principle, our inability to track every molecule, energy, and dust cloud leads to inherent uncertainty in practice.

Epistemology: the theory of knowledge, addressing questions such as how we gain knowledge, what counts as knowledge, and how we validate it.

Equilibrium: a state in which a situation retains balance and stability, with no net change over time.

Experiential knowing: a way of understanding the world through direct, immersive experience rather than through detached, analytical observation.

Flow: a central tenet of process philosophies that denotes the ongoing process of becoming, where change, and not stability, is the norm.

General complexity: an approach that emphasises engaging with the real-life complexity of situations, embracing their messy ambiguous paradoxical and emergent nature, rather than simplifying them into models or representations.

Hegemony: the dominance of one group over others, often manifested through cultural, ideological, or economic means. It involves shaping the culture, beliefs, values, and norms of a society so that the dominant group’s worldview becomes the accepted cultural norm, often to the detriment of diversity and equality.

Holism: a philosophical orientation that emphasises that the world tends to manifest as wholes or self-sustaining patterns; properties and behaviours of a situation cannot be fully understood by analysing the individual components.

Indeterminism: a view that the future is not fixed by preceding events and universal laws. Indeterminism allows for novelty and evolution within the cosmos, emphasising the effect of the particularities of history and of local conditions. (see Epistemological indeterminism and Ontological indeterminism)

Irreversibility: progression cannot be undone or reversed. Irreversibility is tied to the concept of path dependence, whereby what happens shapes what can happen next; it is implicit in ‘the arrow of time’, conveying the directionality of ageing and evolution. (see Non-ergodic) Local: emphasising the importance, in shaping the future, of factors present in the immediate situation and its surroundings, in comparison to a focus on the global or universal. (see Context)

Glossary 379



Lock-in: over time, certain patterns or behaviours become entrenched and resist change through ‘locking out’ the likelihood of any alternatives gaining traction; in social systems this is often facilitated through a reduction of diversity engendered through the constellating of power.

Machine thinking: a tendency implicitly to project a mechanical worldview on to situations and problems.

Mechanical worldview: a perspective that likens the world, or aspects of the world (such as organisations), to a machine composed of discrete parts operating in predictable ways according to definable laws.

Metaphysics: a branch of philosophy concerned with the fundamental nature of reality and existence. It encompasses a range of inquiries into what exists, how and why things exist, the nature of consciousness and the role of perception. It includes speculation about overarching questions that shape scientific and other considerations. (see also Naturalistic metaphysics) Mindset: a set of attitudes or beliefs held, often unconsciously, by individuals and groups and often seen as self-evidently ‘true’. It can include views on science, human nature, what we value and what we deem as acceptable and preferable behaviour. (see Worldview) Mutual entailing: the intrinsic interdependence and reciprocal influencing between entities and also with their context; in particular, the entailing of opposites – e.g. death is already present at birth, instability is not entirely separable from stability. Naturalistic metaphysics: a phenomenological approach to developing a metaphysics concerning the nature of reality; an approach that is grounded in empirical experience and direct engagement rather than initiated in abstract theorising. (see Phenomenology) Non-ergodic: future possibilities are shaped and constrained by the specificities of the path already taken; options are narrowed as events unfold, and choices are made; previous options are generally no longer available.

Nonlinear: a relationship between two factors that is not proportional; results in change trajectories that are not smooth, but may include runaway exponential change, or collapse, or result in periods of relative stability followed by sudden, rapid shifts. Oneness: Unity, the coherence of the whole, the absence of duality; the impossibility of aspects of the situation (the many) independently to display novel or unique behaviours. (Note this definition accords with a mathematical view of coherence, but there are traditions that view ‘the many’ as having a level of autonomy within the ‘One’.)

Ontological indeterminism: indeterminism that is inherent to the nature of the universe itself – due, in particular, to the paradoxical nature surfaced by quantum mechanics. Ontological resonance: a meaningful connection or alignment between different bodies of knowledge or perspectives that, although originating from diverse fields or starting points, arrive at similar conclusions or insights about the nature of reality. Ontological resonance can lead to a deeper, more nuanced understanding, and surface new ideas for practice from other spheres.

Ontological subjectivity: a form of intrinsic subjectivity introduced by quantum physics, suggesting that the universe has a relational contextual paradoxical quality at its tiniest scales; the objective ‘reality’ of the universe is brought into question. Ontology: a philosophical stance as to the nature of being and the nature of reality. It addresses, amongst other things: what is the nature of ‘reality’, is there a reality, are things stable or in flux.

380 Glossary



Open Systems: systems that interact with their surroundings, exchanging energy, matter, or information. Unlike closed systems, which have definite and impermeable boundaries, open systems are characterized by their ability to draw in resources from their environment, allowing for the emergence and stabilising of complex forms and patterns. Orthodoxy: the adherence to accepted norms, traditions, and established beliefs, especially within a particular field or system of thought. It often implies a preference for maintaining the status quo and a commitment to traditional beliefs and practices. Paradoxical: derived from the Greek para doxa meaning ‘against common sense’; the coexistence of seeming incommensurable factors, e.g. stable and unstable, structured and flowing.

Path-dependent: the future is shaped but not determined by its past, is sensitive to the details of the path that has been followed or has emerged.

Pattern: the systemic consequence of mutually reinforcing relationships; patterns are temporally or spatially situated, are sustained through the reflexive interweaving between their constituents coupled with engagement with their environment. Patterning: as a verb, the process of the formation, stabilising and dissolving of patterns; as a noun, signifies the processual temporary nature of patterns, held in place by reflexive relationships and through engagement with the wider context. Phase change: an irreversible shift in a system’s state, leading to fundamentally new properties. (see Tipping point and Regime shift)

Phenomenology: a philosophical stance that preferences the gaining of knowledge through direct experience thus avoiding overarching assumptions; emphasises the significance of individual lived experience.

Plasticity: the capacity for patterns and structures to adapt and reconfigure over time in response to circumstances, maintaining a balance between stability and flexibility. Polarity: involves two opposites that exist simultaneously. These opposites create a dynamic tension, which, if addressed simultaneously, can engender creative solutions and new understandings.

Processual: gives ontological primacy to change, flow, fluidity and becoming (as opposed to stability, fixedness and ‘being’).

Punctuated: highlights the episodic and nonlinear nature of change; associated with periods of stasis followed by periods of rapid change (in comparison with smooth or incremental change). Punctuated equilibrium: a term coined by evolutionary biologists who point to the way the fossil record shows that nature tends to have long periods of relative stability interspersed (punctuated) by times of major upheaval when many species become extinct and many other emerge. Extended to other disciplines, such as economics.

Radical emergence: new and unpredictable properties or behaviours arise, leading to a transformation that could not be predicted from the previous state; the entire landscape may be reconfigured. These emergent phenomena may or may not be systemic in nature. Reductionism or reductivism: the view that complex phenomena can be understood by breaking them down into their constituent parts.

Reflexive: the reciprocal, mutually impacting, reflective and iterative nature of interactions and relationships between entities.

Glossary 381



Regime shift: a shift to another regime or state, one which has novel properties not present in the earlier state. Regime shifts occur at tipping points – their novel properties may take time to emerge and stabilise. (see Phase change)

Reification: treating an idea or entity as self-evidently, unquestionably ‘true’ or ‘real’. Relational: the significance of interrelationships and interactions between elements, rather than viewing elements as connecting but unchanging; properties of resulting patterns or forms are co-constructed through their reflexive interactions. In quantum mechanics, relational quantum mechanics is concerned with the idea that properties exist in the relationship between ‘things’ not in the ‘things’ themselves.

Resilience: the ability to withstand and adapt to shocks, changes, and disturbances whilst either maintaining core functions and patterns or smoothly shifting to new forms more in tune with the developing conditions.

Restricted complexity: approaches to exploring complex situations that tend to rely on mapping and modelling and tend to be situated within the more reductive ontology of classical science.

Reversibility: the possibility of reversing or unravelling any change and returning to an earlier state. The mathematics of deterministic laws of physics have this characteristic; moving towards the future or towards the past cannot be distinguished. Runaway change: rapid, often exponential, irreversible change. Self-organisation: the process of the formation of patterns or forms that does not rely on external direction but arises from the mutual, dynamic interweaving between elements. Self-regulation: the process through which cells, ecologies or social systems maintain stability and dynamic balance through self-organising.

Singularity: points or conditions in the universe where normal physical laws break down. Associated with beginnings, endings and discontinuities, such as those found in black holes. More generally, in mathematics, a point at which the mathematical object ceases to be ‘well-behaved’.

Stasis: periods of equilibrium or relative stability.

Subjective: not entirely definable in objective terms, due to the perception of the observer, the inherent ‘fuzzy’ nature of what is being observed and the nature of ‘things’ as revealed in quantum physics.

Synergy: whereby the combined effect of multiple elements or forces is not additive but is greater than (or lesser than or different from) the sum of their individual effects. System: a set of elements connected together and working together, with defined boundaries; these may be constructed (e.g. a management system) or the term may be used more loosely (e.g. an ecological system). The reification of the notion of ‘systems’ as ontological aspects of the world is raised as a concern by many thinkers who situate themselves within the framework of general complexity.

Systemic: situations whose properties rely on the interweaving and reflexive interconnecting between its elements. Thinking systemically is to engage with problems through exploring many aspects and paying attention to their interplay.

Systemic change: approaches to change aimed at transforming the whole rather than just focusing on aspects.

382 Glossary



Systemic emergence: the process through which new properties arise through reflexive interactions. These emergent properties manifest at the level of the whole and are not present within individual components.

Systems thinking: paying attention to the collective and systemic properties of situations through consideration of the way elements interact; when referring to modelling and computational methods, may be situated within a restricted rather than a general view of complexity and may pay less attention to the processual, the qualitative and the emergent. Note the term is also used more generally to denote thinking systemically about situations. Tipping point: a critical threshold at which a final small change or series of small changes can cause a significant and irreversible shift, after which new properties emerge. (see Critical juncture)

Transdisciplinarity: an approach that transcends individual disciplines, going beyond interdisciplinary or multidisciplinary approaches in that it seeks to allow new holistic understandings to emerge that are not reachable within single disciplines. Unity: in science, equates to an overarching coherence and order resulting from unchanging eternal universal laws; unbreakable Oneness. (see Oneness) Weak signals: subtle, often early and easy-to-overlook indicators of emerging change; spotting them early, through paying careful attention, may convey advantage in deciding how to respond.

Worldview: a comprehensive framework of beliefs, values, and attitudes that shapes an individual’s or a group’s understanding of the world and influences their decisions and behaviour.

About the Author





Jean Boulton PhD, MPhil, MBA studied physics at the universities of Oxford and Cam-bridge and worked in applied physics before turning to management and the social sciences. She is a Fellow of the Institute of Physics and a visiting academic with the Department of Social and Policy Sciences at the University of Bath and with Cranfield School of Management. She is a former research fellow of Stellenbosch Institute for Advanced Study.

Jean has an international reputation for scholarship in the field of complexity theory and consults, writes, researches and lectures about its application to strategy, social change, economics and leadership. She teaches worldwide in a wide range of insti-tutions offering postgraduate programmes in international development, systemic change, engineering, holistic science, management, sustainability and community en-gagement. She is the lead author of Embracing Complexity (Oxford University Press) and author of a number of book chapters on complexity and strategy.

In her work outside academia, Jean has held executive management positions in large corporates and has consulted to global organisations in the private and not-for-profit sectors. She led the strategy and organisation division of an international consultan-cy firm before setting up her own practice. She is keenly interested in global futures and transformative change, engages in local politics and place-based initiatives in her home town in the UK, and has been chair of various organisations working in commu-nity health and sustainability.

She can be reached via www.daoofcomplexity.com or www.embracingcomplexity.com, where you can also sign up for regular blogs and updates.
